A lithe rider, dusty and worn, mounted on a superb bay horse, halted on the south side of the river to watch the Indians. &ldquo;Utes, I reckon,&rdquo; he said, answering to the habit of soliloquy that loneliness had fostered in him. &ldquo;Like the Kiowas they shore die hard. Doggone me if I don&rsquo;t feel sorry for them! The beaver an&rsquo; the buffalo aboot gone! The white man rangin&rsquo; with his cattle wherever grass grows!... Wal, Reddies, if yu air wise, yu&rsquo;ll go way back in some mountain valley an&rsquo; stay there.&rdquo; The rumble of the railroad train died away and the black snakelike string of cars wound out of sight between bold gray bluffs. A moment longer the Indians lingered, their lean and wild shapes silhouetted against the sky, then they wheeled their ragged mustangs and disappeared in red dust clouds over the ridge. &ldquo;Wal, come to think aboot it,&rdquo; mused the lone rider, &ldquo;they&rsquo;re not so bad off as me.... No money. No job. No home.... Ridin&rsquo; a grub line, an&rsquo; half starved. Nothin&rsquo; but a hawse an&rsquo; a gun.&rdquo; Brazos Keene&rsquo;s usual cool and reckless insouciance had suffered a blight. The outcast state he had bitterly avowed was far from new to him. It had been his fate for years to ride the trails from cow camp to ranch, from one cattle town to another. He could not stay long in one place. Always he had been driven. Wherefore the sadness of the hour scarcely had its source in this cowboy wandering. He put a slow hand inside his open vest to draw forth a thick letter, its fresh whiteness marred by fingerprints and sundry soiled spots. He had wept over that letter. Marveling again, with a ghost of the shock which had first attended sight of that beautiful handwriting, he reread the postmark and the address. Lincoln, New Mexico, May 3, 1880. Mr. Brazos Keene, Latimer, Colorado, ? Two-bar X Ranch. The Latimer postmark read a day later. &ldquo;My Gawd, but this heah railroad can fetch a man trouble pronto,&rdquo; he complained, and swallowing a lump in his throat he stuck the letter back. &ldquo;What in the hell made me go into thet post office for? Old cowboy habit! Always lookin&rsquo; for letters thet never came. I wish to Gawd this one had been like all the others.... But aw no!... Holly Ripple remembers me&mdash;has still the old faith in me... An&rsquo; she named her boy Brazos&mdash;after me. Aw! thet hurts somethin&rsquo; sweet an&rsquo; turrible! Shore as I&rsquo;m forkin&rsquo; this hawse heah thet&rsquo;ll be bad for me... or mebbe good!&rdquo; Lost in memory Brazos saw the green river brawling between its gray banks where the willows had a reddish tinge not all from the sunset. A brace of wild ducks winged swift flight over the water; coyotes watched the rider from the slope opposite; the willows shook with the movement of deer or cattle working down to the river; far across the valley on a rising slope black horses showed against the gray. The cold keen air, the fresh odor of the swollen river, the faint color along the brush-lined banks told that the time was early spring. Beyond the Purgatory the land climbed in level benches row on row, always higher and rougher, leading to the gray-ledged ridges, and these in turn to the shaggy foothills that ended abruptly in the mountain wall of slate cliffs and russet slopes and black belts above which the snow crown gleamed white and rose. &ldquo;Only five years!&rdquo; mused the rider, with unseeing eyes on the west. &ldquo;Five years since I rode along heah down the old trail from Don Carlos&rsquo; Rancho... An&rsquo; what have I done with my life?&rdquo; A savage shake of his head was Brazos&rsquo; answer to that disturbing query as also it was a passionate repudiation of memory. It had been his wont in dark hours like this to seek oblivion in the bottle. But with that letter heavy against his heart, with the past vivid and stingingly sweet on him, with the indisputable proof that Holly Ripple&rsquo;s faith in him would never die, he could not be so base, so treacherous. Not in the hour of his remorse and shame! If he could destroy the letter and forget... but that was vain and futile. Brazos rode on down the river trail toward Las Animas. He did not know how far it was in to town. His horse was lame and weary. This stretch along the Purgatory was not prolific of cow camps; nevertheless, Brazos hoped to run into one before nightfall. The sun set, a nipping wind blew down from the heights, the winding river lost its glow of rose to shade dark and steely under the high banks opposite. A coyote wailed out its piercing mournful cry. &ldquo;Purgatory, huh?&rdquo; muttered Brazos, somberly. &ldquo;Wal, the son-of-a-gun Spaniard thet named this heah creek shore hit it plumb center. Purgatory? River of Lost Souls!... Doggone if thet doesn&rsquo;t fit me proper. I&rsquo;m shore a ridin&rsquo; fool&mdash;a gone goslin&rsquo;&mdash;a lost soul!&rdquo; The trail worked up from the river to an intersection with a road. In the gathering darkness, Brazos&rsquo; quick eye caught sight of three horsemen riding out from a clump of dead trees which only partly obscured a dark cabin. The riders wheeled back apparently thinking Brazos had not seen them. &ldquo;Ump-umm,&rdquo; muttered Brazos to himself. &ldquo;Yu gotta be cuter&rsquo;n thet, my bocos.... Now, I just wonder what&rsquo;n hell kind of a move yu call thet.&rdquo; All the instincts and faculties of a range rider had been remarkably magnified in Brazos Keene. He reined his horse some rods before passing in front of that clump of trees. &ldquo;Hey, riders,&rdquo; called Brazos, curtly. &ldquo;I seen yu before yu seen me.&rdquo; After a moment of silence, Brazos heard the word &ldquo;Texan&rdquo; whispered significantly. Then one of the three rode out. &ldquo;What if you did, stranger?&rdquo; he asked. &ldquo;Nothin&rsquo;. I just wanted yu to know all riders ain&rsquo;t blind an&rsquo; deaf.&rdquo; Brazos&rsquo; interrogator halted just so far away that his features were indistinguishable. But Brazos registered the deep matured voice, the sloping shoulders, the bull neck. &ldquo;Thar&rsquo;s been some holdups along hyar lately,&rdquo; he said. &ldquo;Ahuh. An&rsquo; thet&rsquo;s why yu acted so queer?&rdquo; &ldquo;Queer?&rdquo; &ldquo;Shore. I said queer.&rdquo; &ldquo;Playin&rsquo; safe, stranger.&rdquo; &ldquo;Yeah?&mdash;Wal, if yu took me for a bandit yu&rsquo;re way off.&rdquo; &ldquo;Glad to hear thet.&mdash;An&rsquo; who might you be?&rdquo; &ldquo;I&rsquo;m a grub-line ridin&rsquo; cowboy. I&rsquo;m tired an&rsquo; hungry, an&rsquo; my hawse is lame.&rdquo; &ldquo;Whar you from?&rdquo; &ldquo;Texas.&rdquo; &ldquo;Hell! A deaf man could tell thet. Whar you ridin&rsquo; from?&rdquo; &ldquo;Montana. Straight as a crow flies.&rdquo; &ldquo;An&rsquo; whar you makin&rsquo; for?&rdquo; &ldquo;Mister, if I wasn&rsquo;t hungry an&rsquo; tired I wouldn&rsquo;t like yore pert questions. I&rsquo;m not goin&rsquo; anywhere in particular. How far to Las Animas?&rdquo; &ldquo;All night drill fer a tired hoss.&rdquo; &ldquo;Any cow camp near?&rdquo; &ldquo;Nope. Nearest ranch is Twin Sombreros, three miles from town.&rdquo; &ldquo;Excuse me for askin&rsquo;,&rdquo; went on Brazos, with sarcasm, &ldquo;but do yu fellars belong to an ootfit thet&rsquo;ll feed a hungry cowpuncher?&rdquo; &ldquo;My boss hasn&rsquo;t any use fer grub-line riders.&rdquo; &ldquo;Yu don&rsquo;t say. Wal, I reckon I don&rsquo;t eat. Small matter. But would yu tell me if there&rsquo;s any grass heahaboots for my hawse?&rdquo; &ldquo;Good grass right hyar, stranger. An&rsquo; you can bunk in the old cabin thar.&rdquo; &ldquo;Thanks,&rdquo; returned Brazos, dryly. The burly rider turned to his silent companions, just discernible in the gloom. &ldquo;Come on, men. If we&rsquo;re makin&rsquo; Lamar tonight we got to rustle.&rdquo; The couple joined him and they rode by Brazos too swiftly for him to distinguish anything. They took to the north, soon passing out of sight. Brazos kept staring in the direction they had gone. The thing that struck him on the moment was the fact of his insatiable curiosity. These three riders had not acted out of order, considering the time and place. They had a perfect right to be suspicious of him, as he likewise had of them. But there had been something wrong about them, something insincere, something hidden. A meeting with strangers on the ranges was nothing unusual for Brazos Keene. He had an uncanny instinct for recognizing dishonest men. That was one reason why he rode so many grub-line trails. He was honest himself, flaming of spirit, bitter toward the outlaws, the rustlers, and crooked cowmen who dominated the ranges from the Little Big Horn to the Rio Grande. Brazos dismissed the incident from mind. Dismounting, he led his horse off the road to the clump of trees. Long bleached grass of last year&rsquo;s growth appeared to be plentiful, and this fact relieved Brazos from worry about feed for his animal. The cabin proved to be close at hand. Brazos peeped in the open door. It was pitch dark inside and smelled dry. He removed saddle and bridle from the bay and turned him loose. Brazos carried his paraphernalia inside and deposited it upon the floor. He felt in his pockets for matches. He had none. Then he groped around, hands outstretched, until he bumped into a bench made of boughs. This, with his saddle blankets, would furnish a better bed than many to which he had of late been accustomed. Lastly, he went to the door to look out. Bay was cropping the grass near by. The sky had become overcast with dark clouds and the cold air had moderated. Brazos felt rain or snow in it. Coyotes were wailing. A few dead leaves rustled on the trees. The black melancholy range seemed to envelop the cabin. Brazos did not like the place, the night, the nameless oppression. But how many times had that very mood weighed upon him? He groped his way back to the bench where, heartsick and hungry, too bitter to care what happened, too weary to think longer, he lay down and fell asleep. Some time in the night he awoke. At first he imagined he had awakened from a vague grotesque dream, details of which he could not remember. Usually a light sleeper, he thought nothing of being aroused. But after a moment he felt that this was different. And he attended to outside sensations. He heard a drip, drip, drip of rain on the floor. Evidently the roof of this shack leaked. A low moaning wind swept by under the cabin eaves. The night was so black that he could not locate either door or window. Mice rustled the rubbish in a corner. The dry musty smell of the cabin appeared to have been permeated with a damp odor, which, of course, came from the rain. Drip&mdash;drip&mdash;drip&mdash;slowly the dropping sounds faded in his consciousness. From that hour on he slept fitfully, restlessly, harassed by strange dreams. One by one these increased in their morbid vagaries until finally a ghastly climax brought him awake, wet with cold sweat. Dawn was at hand. Through the window he discerned a faint blue of sky. Apparently the weather had cleared. But all of a sudden&mdash;drip&mdash;drip&mdash;drip. The drops of rain water were slow and heavy. They spattered on the earthen floor. It was now light enough in the cabin to make out a ladder leading up to a loft. The old yellow-stone chimney and fireplace had crumbled out of shape. How gloomy and still this square within its four log walls! Brazos wondered what had happened there. But for that matter, no log cabin in the West could be without its history, much of which was dark, violent and bloody. All at once a cold chill crept over his skin. That dank odor, dominating the pungent dry smell of the cabin, assailed his nostrils. Drip&mdash;drip&mdash;drip! Brazos was wide awake now, on the verge of being startled by he knew not what. Like his sight and hearing his olfactory sense had been abnormally developed by an outdoor life. Drip&mdash;drip! The odor he had connected with this sound did not come from dropping rain water. It was blood. Fresh blood! Brazos seemed suddenly transfixed with a sickening icy clutch at his vitals. He had smelled human blood far too often ever to mistake it. In a single action, he slid upright off the bench. That drip came from the loft just about the center of the cabin. Brazos could not see the drops, but by their sound, he located them&mdash;stretched out his upturned palm. Spat! Despite his steely nerve the heavy wet contact on his hand gave him a shock. He strode to the light of the doorway, there to confirm his suspicion. &ldquo;Blood!&rdquo; he ejaculated, his eyes fixed on the red splotch in his palm. &ldquo;Cold an&rsquo; thick.... There&rsquo;s a daid man up in thet loft.... Aha! them three hombres last night!... Brazos, I reckon yu better be rustlin&rsquo; oot of heah pronto.&rdquo; Hurrying back to the bench, Brazos wiped the blood on his saddle blankets, and carried these with his saddle to the door. Dawn had given way to daylight with a ruddy tinge in the eastern sky. And at that moment a clattering roar of hoofs swept up like a storm before the wind, and a group of riders pulled their horses to a sliding halt before the cabin. &ldquo;Ahuh. Jig aboot up! I savvy,&rdquo; muttered Brazos, and stepping out of the door he flung down the saddle and blankets to stand at attention. He needed not to see the rifles to grasp that this was a posse and that he was the object of their onslaught upon the cabin. &ldquo;Hands up, cowboy!&rdquo; came a harsh command. &ldquo;They&rsquo;re up,&rdquo; replied Brazos, laconically, suiting action to words. The leveled guns and grim visages of this outfit showed that they meant business. Brazos had seen many posses and had been a member of not a few. Most of these riders had the cowboy stripe, but some of them, particularly the harsh-voiced, hard-faced leader, appeared to be matured men. &ldquo;Pile off, Stuke, an&rsquo; you, Segel,&rdquo; ordered this leader. Whereupon two riders flung themselves out of their saddles to rush at Brazos from each side. &ldquo;Grab his guns! Search him.... Take everythin&rsquo;.&rdquo; &ldquo;Heah!&rdquo; flashed Brazos, hotly. &ldquo;Don&rsquo;t take thet letter!&rdquo; &ldquo;Careful, cowboy, or we&rsquo;ll bore you.... Search the cabin.... Jim, rustle up his hoss.&rdquo; Brazos&rsquo; rage had burst from his cool mien owing to the rude theft of his precious letter. But he was quick to recognize real peril and on the instant became his old self. He surveyed the group of horsemen to ascertain that they were all strangers to him and no different from any hard determined outfit of Westerners. In a moment, he made certain that not one of them had ever seen him. He had not been in that vicinity for six years, which was a long time on the range. &ldquo;Bodkin,&rdquo; called a rider from within the cabin, his voice queer. &ldquo;What! You found him?&rdquo; queried the leader, sharply. &ldquo;Yes. Up in the loft. Send someone in to help us let him down.&rdquo; Brazos listened with strained ears to the sounds and husky voices inside the cabin. Murder had been perpetrated. And he was to be held for it. The situation was critical and his life depended upon his nerve and wit. Presently three of the posse came out of the cabin, carrying the body, which they deposited upon the grass. Brazos&rsquo; startled gaze bent down upon a handsome youth scarcely twenty years old, evidently a cowboy from his garb, dark-haired and dark-skinned. He had been shot through the back. All his pockets were turned inside out. &ldquo;Allen Neece,&rdquo; burst out Bodkin, in surprise. He had not expected to see the owner of that name. &ldquo;Shot in the back.&rdquo; &ldquo;Robbed!&rdquo; &ldquo;Purty cold-blooded, I&rsquo;d say.&rdquo; &ldquo;Bod, I reckon we might jest as wal string this hombre up.&rdquo; These and various other comments greeted Brazos&rsquo; ears, and drew from Bodkin the harsh decree: &ldquo;Cowboy, you&rsquo;re under arrest.&rdquo; &ldquo;Hell! I&rsquo;m not blind or deaf,&rdquo; retorted Brazos, sarcastically. &ldquo;May I ask who yu air?&rdquo; &ldquo;I&rsquo;m Deputy Sheriff Bodkin of Las Animas, actin&rsquo; under Kiskadden&rsquo;s orders.&rdquo; &ldquo;An&rsquo; what&rsquo;s yore charge?&rdquo; &ldquo;Murder.&rdquo; Brazos laughed outright. &ldquo;My Gawd, man, air yu loco? Do I look like I am drunk or crazy?&rdquo; &ldquo;I reckon you don&rsquo;t,&rdquo; replied Bodkin, with glinting eyes taking stock of Brazos. &ldquo;Do I look like a hombre who&rsquo;d shoot a boy in the back, rob him, an&rsquo; hang aboot waitin&rsquo; for an ootfit to come get me?&rdquo; &ldquo;You can&rsquo;t never tell what a cowboy will do from his looks.&rdquo; &ldquo;Aw, the hell yu cain&rsquo;t,&rdquo; replied Brazos, contemptuously, with piercing glance of scorn flashing from Bodkin to his men. &ldquo;What kind of Westerners air yu?&rdquo; Brazos&rsquo; scornful stand, his cool nerve in the face of a critical situation obviously impressed some of the riders. &ldquo;Bod, I&rsquo;d recommend a fair trial fer this fellar,&rdquo; said one. &ldquo;It shore has a queer look, this whole deal,&rdquo; interposed another. &ldquo;Kiskadden has grown testy of late. Better let him be the jedge.&rdquo; &ldquo;Wal, if you-all ain&rsquo;t fer hangin&rsquo; him pronto, I&rsquo;ll hev to take him into town,&rdquo; said Bodkin, in gruff reluctance. &ldquo;Haw! Haw!&rdquo; laughed Bodkin, with a leering sneer. &ldquo;An&rsquo; what way was you goin&rsquo; to ride, cowboy?&rdquo; &ldquo;Hot for Las Animas, Mister Deputy Bodkin, yu can bet yore life on thet,&rdquo; rang out Brazos. &ldquo;You expect me to believe that?&rdquo; &ldquo;Say, I&rsquo;m an officer of the law an&rsquo; you&rsquo;re one of these rowdy windy cowboys, of which this hyar range is too thickly populated.&rdquo; &ldquo;Wrong again, officer. You may have the law behind yu, but yu&rsquo;re not very bright. I don&rsquo;t resist arrest. But I&rsquo;m innocent of this charge. I want a fair trial an&rsquo; a chance to prove it. What&rsquo;s more, if yu knew me yu&rsquo;d be most damn shore of yore proofs before yu pressed this charge.&rdquo; &ldquo;I would, huh? Suppose you acquaint us with your name an&rsquo; status in Colorado.&rdquo; Brazos made no reply to the deputy, and spent the following interval in a hard scrutiny of the members of this posse. It was significant that two of them in the background moved to hide behind those in front. &ldquo;Bod, you cain&rsquo;t hang this Texan on such heahsay evidence,&rdquo; advised the slow-spoken member. &ldquo;Why not? &rsquo;Cause you&rsquo;re a Texan yourself?&rdquo; &ldquo;Wal, as to thet, Texans, whether they&rsquo;re guilty of crime or not, ain&rsquo;t very often hanged. Personally, I reckon this cowboy is innocent as I am of this murder. An&rsquo; mebbe I&rsquo;m not the only one. If you hang him, Kiskadden will be sore. An&rsquo; if by any chance he ain&rsquo;t guilty an&rsquo; it comes oot&mdash;wal, it&rsquo;d kind of heat up the stink thet hasn&rsquo;t died oot cold yet.&rdquo; During the brief duration of that quiet speech Brazos gauged both men&mdash;the sandy-haired, sallow-faced Texan whose looks and words were significant&mdash;and the swarthy Bodkin, dark-browed, shifty of gaze, chafing under the other&rsquo;s cool arraignment of the case, and intense with some feeling hardly justified by the facts herein presented. &ldquo;All right, Inskip,&rdquo; rejoined Bodkin, with suppressed anger. &ldquo;We&rsquo;ll take him before Kiskadden.... Prod him to his hoss, men. An&rsquo; if he bolts, blow his towhead off.&rdquo; Brazos&rsquo; captors shoved him forward. Bay had been found and saddled. He did not like this crowd and pulled at his bit, held hard by one of the posse. Brazos mounted. The body of the boy Neece was lifted over a saddle and covered with a slicker. The rider of this horse essayed to walk, which gave Brazos the impression that Las Animas was not far distant. Presently the cavalcade started toward the road, with Brazos riding in the center. For a while, Brazos was too busy scrutinizing the faces of Bodkin&rsquo;s men to pay much attention to the lay of the land. When he did look beyond them, however, it was to see the Purgatory River winding down through a beautiful stretch which he remembered. At its extreme eastern terminus the sunrise over the horizon flooded the range with a soft rose light. Vast gray prairie lands rolled away to the north. Dots and strings and herds of cattle dominated the landscape. Columns of smoke rising about green field and red and white houses marked the location of Las Animas. Manifestly the town had grown since Brazos&rsquo; last visit there six years and more ago. At that time the railroad had just reached it. Brazos inquired of the rider on his left as to the size of Las Animas, but received no response from that worthy. The posse appeared to be a surly lot, not given to much talk. &ldquo;Say, pardner,&rdquo; he ventured, accosting the young man on his right, &ldquo;how big is Las Animas now?&rdquo; &ldquo;Right pert town. About twenty-five hundred, I&rsquo;d reckon,&rdquo; was the civil reply. &ldquo;Gee! Thet&rsquo;s shore a metropolis. Wide open like it used to be?&rdquo; &ldquo;Purty wide. I&rsquo;ve only been there a year. Folks say it&rsquo;s slow now.&rdquo; Bodkin turned to glare at the young rider. &ldquo;Shet up. This man is under arrest fer murder.&rdquo; Brazos felt the old gusty fire thrill along his veins, and he had to bite his tongue to keep from a sharp retort. But he would bide his time. He had seen numberless Westerners of Bodkin&rsquo;s type. His knowledge of them equaled his contempt. There would be men in Las Animas who would remember Brazos Keene and who would be certain to give this fourflush deputy a jar. The cavalcade traveled on at a slow trot and at length reached a site strangely familiar to Brazos. It was the head of the valley. He identified a grove of cottonwoods stretching far on each side of the river. Many a time had he camped there. The difference consisted in that the wildness of the sylvan spot had given place to a ranch that would have gladdened the eye of any cowboy. A long, low, red-roofed, red-walled adobe ranch house stood upon the north bank of the river, and below it, where the cottonwoods trooped into the valley, spread barns and sheds, corrals and racks in picturesque confusion. The droves of horses in the pastures, the squares of alfalfa, and the herds of cattle dotting the valley and the adjacent slopes attested to the prosperity of some cattle baron. &ldquo;Doggone!&rdquo; ejaculated Brazos in the enthusiastic appreciation of his kind. &ldquo;Whose ootfit is thet?&rdquo; Inskip, the Texan, riding second on Brazos&rsquo; left, took it upon himself to reply. &ldquo;Twin Sombreros Ranch,&rdquo; he replied, his dry drawl significant of something more than information. &ldquo;Operated now by Raine Surface, runnin&rsquo; eighty thousand haid of the Twin Sombreros brand. Used to belong to Abe Neece, father of the daid boy we&rsquo;re packin&rsquo; to town. Abe is livin&rsquo; still, but a broken man over the loss of thet ranch.&rdquo; &ldquo;Wal, I don&rsquo;t wonder,&rdquo; returned Brazos, feelingly. &ldquo;Gosh, I&rsquo;d rather be a grub-line-ridin&rsquo; cowboy.&rdquo; Bodkin turned again with malignance in his visage. &ldquo;You&rsquo;ll be a swingin&rsquo; cowboy before sundown.&rdquo; &ldquo;Yeah?&rdquo; drawled Brazos in his cool slow speech. &ldquo;Bodkin, I savvy thet if I don&rsquo;t swing, it shore won&rsquo;t be owin&rsquo; to yore kind offices.&rdquo; It so happened that when the cavalcade reached the crossroad to the ranch a sextette of riders, some of them cowboys, rode down from above to halt their mounts at sight of the posse. Brazos espied two young women riders and he burned both inwardly and outwardly. His proud, fiery nature rebelled at the indignity Bodkin had forced upon him. &ldquo;What&rsquo;s this, Bodkin?&rdquo; demanded the leader. &ldquo;Mornin&rsquo;, Mr. Surface,&rdquo; replied Bodkin, with both importance and servility. &ldquo;We been out arrestin&rsquo; a cowboy. Charged with murder. An&rsquo; I&rsquo;ve got the proofs on him.&rdquo; &ldquo;Murder! You don&rsquo;t say? Who?&rdquo; &ldquo;No other than Abe Neece&rsquo;s boy&mdash;young Allen Neece.&rdquo; &ldquo;Open up here!&rdquo; ordered Surface, and in another moment a few feet of unobstructed lane intervened between the rancher and Brazos Keene. For Brazos it was one of those instinctively potent meetings of which his life on the ranges had been so full. He turned from his long glance at the two girls, the older of whom had hair as red as flame, a strikingly beautiful face, with blue-green eyes just now dilated in horror. &ldquo;Who are you?&rdquo; demanded Surface, with intense curiosity, not one iota of which denoted sympathy. Brazos gave the rancher a long stare. Among Brazos&rsquo; gifts was the rare one of an almost superhuman perspicuity. The time had long passed in his eventful career when he distrusted that peculiar faculty. Surface fell under a category of Westerners far removed from the open-faced, eagle-eyed, great-hearted pioneers whom Brazos revered. &ldquo;Wal, who I am is shore none of yore business,&rdquo; replied Brazos, coldly. &ldquo;Cowboy, I&rsquo;m Raine Surface, an&rsquo; I have a good deal to say with the business of this county,&rdquo; returned the rancher, plainly nettled. &ldquo;I reckon. Do you happen to be in cahoots with this fourflush, Deputy Bodkin?&rdquo; The sharp unexpected query disconcerted Surface and elicited a roar from Bodkin. &ldquo;I put Kiskadden in office,&rdquo; said the rancher stiffly, putting forward a fact Brazos could see no reason for mentioning. &ldquo;I recommended to the Cattlemen&rsquo;s Association that we appoint deputies to help rid this range of desperadoes an&rsquo; rustlers&mdash;an&rsquo; rowdy cowboys.&rdquo; &ldquo;Wal, Mr. Surface, yu shore impress me powerful,&rdquo; drawled Brazos, scornfully. &ldquo;What&rsquo;s your name?&rdquo; &ldquo;Wal, I&rsquo;m not advisin&rsquo; yu to ride into town an&rsquo; find oot. Yu might, along with yore deputy hired hand heah, find yoreself disappointed.&rdquo; &ldquo;You insolent ragamuffin of a Texan!&rdquo; fumed Surface, evidently resenting his failure to be given due importance. &ldquo;See heah, Surface,&rdquo; flashed Brazos, his piercing tenor stiffening his hearers. &ldquo;I am a Texan an&rsquo; one of the breed thet don&rsquo;t forget insult or injustice. You&rsquo;re a hell of a fine Westerner to act as an adviser to a Cattlemen&rsquo;s Association. A real Westerner&mdash;a big-hearted cattleman who was on the square wouldn&rsquo;t condemn me without askin&rsquo; for proofs. You take this Bodkin&rsquo;s word. If he hasn&rsquo;t got some queer reason to fasten this crime on me, it&rsquo;s a shore bet he itches to hang someone.... Wal, I happen to be innocent an&rsquo; I can prove it. I could choke up an&rsquo; spit fire at the idee of my bein&rsquo; taken for a low-down greaser who&rsquo;d shoot a boy in the back to rob him.... An&rsquo; swallow this, Mister Raine Surface&mdash;you&rsquo;ll rue the day you insulted a ragamuffin of a cowboy who was only huntin&rsquo; for a job.&rdquo; The silence which followed Brazos&rsquo; arraignment was broken by Inskip. &ldquo;Surface,&rdquo; he said caustically, to the pale-faced rancher. &ldquo;You&rsquo;re new to this range. All you Kansas cattlemen need to be reminded thet this is western Colorado. Which is to say the border of New Mexico. An&rsquo; mebbe yore years oot heah air too few for you to know what thet means. All the same, Bodkin an&rsquo; you should have given this cowboy the benefit of a doubt.&rdquo; &ldquo;Bodkin said he had proofs,&rdquo; rejoined Surface, testily. &ldquo;I took his word.&rdquo; &ldquo;Texans hang together,&rdquo; tartly interposed the deputy sheriff, giving a double meaning to the verb. &ldquo;Inskip wanted to ride out on this hunt. I reckon he had a hunch. I really didn&rsquo;t want him.&rdquo; &ldquo;Bodkin, I&rsquo;m givin&rsquo; you a hunch,&rdquo; drawled Inskip, with tone and glance that warmed Brazos. &ldquo;Kiskadden is a Texan. Mebbe you didn&rsquo;t know thet.&rdquo; At this juncture, when a strong argument seemed imminent, the redheaded girl moved her horse close to Surface and put a hand on his shoulder. &ldquo;Lura, don&rsquo;t interfere here,&rdquo; snapped her father, impatiently. &ldquo;Mr. Surface, we&rsquo;ll ride on in,&rdquo; said Bodkin, and gave his men a peremptory order to move on. Before the riders closed in on Brazos, he gave the red-headed girl an intent look and a smile of gratitude for her championship. Her big eyes, still wide and dark, appeared to engulf him. Then the cavalcade started. Brazos soon regained his composure. Presently, it occurred to him that this situation had approached a provoking complexity. He cursed his luck. It was bad enough to fall into a perilous predicament without having it assume irresistible interest. He would fight that, and the hour he was freed, he would ride away from Las Animas. Before they had ridden many paces a clatter of hoofs behind and a call for Bodkin again halted the riders. The rancher Surface followed. &ldquo;A word with you, Bodkin,&rdquo; he said, reining his mount. &ldquo;Sartinly, Mister Surface,&rdquo; returned the deputy, hastening to fall out of line. &ldquo;About that suit of mine against&mdash;&rdquo; he began, with something of pomposity. But Brazos made quick note of the fact that that was all he could hear. Bodkin and Surface walked their horses out of hearing. Brazos was not missing anything. The two riders who had the disagreeable task of holding the corpse in the saddle and keeping it covered swore openly at this second loss of time. As Brazos turned to face forward again, he met Inskip&rsquo;s deep gray eyes in which there flashed a bright steely glint that could be interpreted in only one way. Brazos&rsquo; blood took a hot leap, then receded to leave him cold. This halt boded ill to him. Sight of Bodkin&rsquo;s grim visage, as he came riding back from his short colloquy with Surface, warned Brazos of the unexpected and the worst. But Bodkin took the lead of the cavalcade again without a word other than a command to ride. His tenseness seemed to be communicated to all. Their faces set away from the prisoner. Inskip took off his heavy coat and laid it back across the cantle of his saddle&mdash;an action Bodkin might have taken as thought-provoking had he noticed it. Brazos&rsquo; reaction revolved around sight of the two big gun butts sticking out of Inskip&rsquo;s belt. They spoke a language to Brazos as clear as had been the gray lightning in Inskip&rsquo;s eyes. The outskirts of Las Animas lay just ahead, beyond a bridge over a brook that brawled down to the Purgatory. Oaks and cottonwoods lined the west bank. &ldquo;Stop hyar, man,&rdquo; ordered Bodkin, wheeling his horse. &ldquo;Inskip, you ride on in an&rsquo; report.&rdquo; The Texan made no reply nor any move to act upon the deputy&rsquo;s command. &ldquo;Segel, you an&rsquo; Bill wait hyar with Neece,&rdquo; went on Bodkin. &ldquo;The rest of you come with me.&rdquo; He turned to ride off the road. &ldquo;Inskip,&rdquo; he said, suddenly, halting again. &ldquo;Are you takin&rsquo; orders?&rdquo; &ldquo;Not when it doesn&rsquo;t suit me,&rdquo; replied the Texan. &ldquo;What you up to, Bodkin?&rdquo; &ldquo;I&rsquo;m goin&rsquo; to finish this job right hyar,&rdquo; rejoined the deputy, fiercely. &ldquo;An&rsquo; if you don&rsquo;t want your Texas pride hurt, you&rsquo;d better not see what&rsquo;s comin&rsquo; off.&rdquo; &ldquo;Wal, I ain&rsquo;t so sensitive as all thet,&rdquo; drawled Inskip. Brazos realized the game now and what a slim chance he had for his life. That chance was vested in Inskip. An awful instant he fought the shuddering clutch on his vitals, the appalling check to his thought. It was succeeded by the desperate will and nerve, the unlimited resourcefulness of the cowboy whose flame-spirit had been engendered by such terrible situations as this. There would be one chance for him and when it came he must grasp it with the speed of lightning. Bodkin led down the west bank of the brook. The trees and rocks broke up the formation of the posse. Brazos&rsquo; sharp eye caught the rider behind Bodkin bending forward to untie his lasso from his saddle. They entered a rocky glade comminated by an old cottonwood tree with spreading branches and a dead top. Brazos had been under that tree before. &ldquo;Open up,&rdquo; shouted Bodkin. &ldquo;Prod his hoss out hyar.&rdquo; A moment of cracking ironshod hoofs on rock and Brazos&rsquo; horse champed his bit under a widespreading arm of the cottonwood. All the men faced Brazos, pale, with thin lips set. &ldquo;Boss,&rdquo; spoke up one of them, hoarsely. &ldquo;I&rsquo;m bound to speak out. This deal is too raw fer my stomach.&rdquo; &ldquo;Rustle then. Git out of hyar,&rdquo; yelled the leader, livid with passion. &ldquo;I sure will. Come on, Ben. We didn&rsquo;t join this outfit to hang a cowboy thet ain&rsquo;t proved guilty.&rdquo; The lean rider addressed detached himself from the group. &ldquo;Bodkin,&rdquo; he said, forcefully. &ldquo;You&rsquo;re too damn keen on this necktie party. Frank an&rsquo; me are slopin&rsquo;.&rdquo; &ldquo;Yellow, huh?&rdquo; shouted the deputy, as the couple rode off. &ldquo;All right. I&rsquo;ll bear it in mind.&rdquo; &ldquo;See heah, Bodkin,&rdquo; interposed Inskip, &ldquo;did you ride all this way to have yore mind changed by Surface?&rdquo; &ldquo;Inskip, you go to hell!&rdquo; hissed Bodkin, enraged at the sarcastic implication. Nevertheless, it could scarcely have been rage that paled the redness out of his malignant visage. Brazos read in Inskip&rsquo;s eyes what Bodkin failed to see; and it was that intelligence which sustained him. The Texan might have a trump card up his sleeve, but Brazos could only think of two desperate chances, one of which he was sure would be presented. &ldquo;Flip thet noose, Barsh,&rdquo; ordered Bodkin, sardonically, addressing a lean rider whose hat shaded his face. He had a coiled rope in his left hand. He gave the coil a toss. The loop spread to fall over Brazos&rsquo; head and lodge on his shoulders. Another flip and the noose closed round his neck. The feel of the hard smooth hemp against Brazos&rsquo; bare flesh liberated in him the devil that he had kept leashed. Brazos had never before suffered the odium of this phase of border law. Barsh plainly quailed before Brazos&rsquo; steady gaze. &ldquo;Pile off, all of you,&rdquo; shouted Bodkin, stridently, dismounting to lean his rifle against the tree. &ldquo;Barsh, throw the end of your rope over thet branch.&rdquo; This order issued from the Texan, whose hand obstructed Barsh&rsquo;s arm in his effort to toss up the rope. &ldquo;Wha-at?&rdquo; bawled Bodkin, rudely disrupted, glaring at Inskip. Bodkin was the only rider beside Brazos who had not dismounted. The others had laid aside their rifles and shotguns to crowd back of Barsh, nervously hurrying to get the gruesome job done. Inskip deliberately rode between them and Brazos. &ldquo;Bodkin, he might have a mother or sweetheart. An&rsquo; he&rsquo;ll want to send some word.&rdquo; &ldquo;Aw, hell! Let him blab it pronto then.&rdquo; &ldquo;Cowboy, do you want to tell me who you air an&rsquo; send some message?&rdquo; queried Inskip, calmly. &ldquo;I shore do. But I don&rsquo;t want this skunk to heah it.&rdquo; &ldquo;Wal, you can tell me,&rdquo; returned Inskip, and pulled his horse toward Brazos. The Texan leaned toward Brazos to whisper swift and low: &ldquo;Grab my guns, but don&rsquo;t kill onless you have to.&rdquo; Brazos&rsquo; clawlike hands swept out. As he jerked loose the two big guns, Inskip spurred his horse to lunge away. &ldquo;Hands up!... Turn yore backs!&rdquo; ordered Brazos, his voice ice-edged. &ldquo;Bodkin, tell yore men to fork their hawses. One move for a gun means I&rsquo;ll kill you first.&rdquo; &ldquo;Fellars&mdash;he&rsquo;s got&mdash;me cold,&rdquo; rejoined the deputy, huskily. &ldquo;Fer Gawd&rsquo;s sake&mdash;lay off your hardware.... Climb on.&rdquo; While they mounted stiffly, Brazos hauled the lasso in with his left hand and wound it around the pommel. &ldquo;Ride oot, you hombres.... Yu go last, Bodkin. An&rsquo; when we hit the road yell for Segel an&rsquo; yore other man to go ahaid.&rdquo; When the riders emerged from the grove Bodkin bawled to the couple on guard with the dead man. &ldquo;Ride on, you fellars&mdash;an&rsquo; don&rsquo;t look back!&rdquo; It might have been only a few moments and it might have been longer before the strange cavalcade entered the outskirts of Las Animas&mdash;Brazos never knew. But once having passed the portal of the town, he drew a deep breath and sat back in his saddle, lowering the big guns to rest on his knees. Bodkin had treasured his swarthy skin too dearly to make any false move, and his men evidently were not forcing any deadly issue. &ldquo;Turn in, yu-all, an&rsquo; set tight,&rdquo; called Brazos. A quick glance assured him that either he or something unusual was expected. Men were grouped about, and out in front stood a tall bareheaded man in his shirt sleeves. He had a silver star on his black vest. He stood significantly sidewise toward the street, his right hand low. Brazos breasted the hitching rail to see a broad lined face, deep piercing eyes, a thin-lipped, close-shut mouth, and bulging chin. Texas was written all over that wonderful visage. &ldquo;Wal, Bodkin,&rdquo; he drawled, in a dry crisp voice, &ldquo;you ride away in the daid of night withoot orders from this office, an&rsquo; you ride back with a daid man haidin&rsquo; yore parade an&rsquo; a shore enough live cowboy with guns at yore back. What the hell kind of deputy air you?&rdquo; &ldquo;Boss&mdash;I had this cowboy arrested fer murder,&rdquo; panted Bodkin, &ldquo;an&rsquo; thet &mdash;&mdash; Inskip double-crossed me&mdash;&mdash;&rdquo; &ldquo;Shut up, Bodkin,&rdquo; interposed Brazos. &ldquo;I still want to bore yu awful bad. It&rsquo;s shore only oot of respect for this office thet I haven&rsquo;t shot yu long ago.&rdquo; &ldquo;Cowboy, talk to me,&rdquo; said the man with the star. Brazos had not looked into many as clear hawk eyes as those with which this Texan took stock of him. &ldquo;Air yu Kiskadden?&rdquo; queried Brazos, sharply. &ldquo;Thet&rsquo;s me,&rdquo; came the curt reply. &ldquo;Did Inskip give yu a hunch aboot this?&rdquo; &ldquo;He told me you&rsquo;d be likely to ride in, but I&rsquo;m bound to admit I didn&rsquo;t expect you.&rdquo; &ldquo;Sheriff, will yu give me a square deal?&rdquo; &ldquo;You can rest assured of thet, cowboy. I&rsquo;m the law heah.&rdquo; &ldquo;My Gawd, but it&rsquo;s a relief to pass these over. Heah!&rdquo; burst out Brazos, and with a dexterous flip of the guns, he turned them in the air to catch them by the barrels and hand them to the sheriff. &ldquo;Sheriff, I shore haven&rsquo;t had many deals where I was more justified in throwin&rsquo; guns than in this one. But when Inskip gave me a chance to use them, he whispered for me not to shoot unless I had to. So I bluffed yore deputy an&rsquo; his posse.&rdquo; &ldquo;So I see. Wal, if you bluffed them, why didn&rsquo;t you ride the other way, instead of insultin&rsquo; my office this heah way?&rdquo; &ldquo;I happen to be a Texan an&rsquo; I&rsquo;m sore.&rdquo; &ldquo;I seen thet long ago. Go on. Why&rsquo;d you come?&rdquo; &ldquo;Last night I was held up oot heah by three men. I&rsquo;ll tell yu in private how they acted, what they said, an&rsquo; the lie they told me.... It was aboot night. I was cold an&rsquo; tired. Bay heah was lame. So when the three hombres rode away, I went to sleep in the cabin there. In the mawnin&rsquo; I found too I&rsquo;d been sleepin&rsquo; with a daid man. An&rsquo; I&rsquo;d just got ootside the cabin when Bodkin with his posse came tearin&rsquo; up. I had no idee what they wanted an&rsquo; they&rsquo;d covered me before I found oot. Wal, they arrested me for the murder of the young man they found in the cabin, shot in the back. Sheriff, you can bet yore life thet those three hombres last night an&rsquo; Bodkin&rsquo;s ootfit this mawnin&rsquo; knew the daid boy was in the cabin nine hours before I knew.... There was nothin&rsquo; for me to do but go along. I went. Bodkin is a surly hombre, an&rsquo; he&rsquo;s a hell of a queer deputy sheriff. First off it didn&rsquo;t look like he had any idee of hangin&rsquo; me. But he stopped at this Twin Sombreros Ranch oot heah, held up by the rancher Surface. An&rsquo; from thet moment Bodkin grew hell-bent to hang me. Inskip saw it comin&rsquo; an&rsquo; he tried to reason with Bodkin. But yu cain&rsquo;t reason with a bull-haided, fourflush, notoriety-huntin&rsquo; deputy sheriff who from some queer twist was daid-set to hang me. When they had the lariat aboot my neck, Inskip rode in so I could grab his guns. Thet saved my life, sheriff, I&rsquo;m innocent an&rsquo; I can prove it. I want my name cleared. Thet&rsquo;s why I took the risk of holdin&rsquo; up yore ootfit an&rsquo; ridin&rsquo; in heah to surrender.&rdquo; &ldquo;Who air you, cowboy?&rdquo; queried Kiskadden, searchingly. &ldquo;Thet&rsquo;ll have to come oot, I reckon,&rdquo; returned Brazos, reluctantly. &ldquo;I haven&rsquo;t been in Las Animas for six years. But there&rsquo;ll be men heah who&rsquo;ll vouch for me.&rdquo; &ldquo;All right. Get down, cowboy.... Bodkin, you look burstin&rsquo; with yore side of this story. Mebbe you&rsquo;d better hold in&mdash;&mdash;&rdquo; &ldquo;Aw hell!&rdquo; interrupted the deputy, his face working. &ldquo;Wait till you hear my side. He&rsquo;s a slick-tongued fellar, believe me. I&rsquo;ll gamble he turns out to be a range-ridin&rsquo; desperado. An&rsquo; it&rsquo;s a thousand to one thet he murdered young Neece.&rdquo; &ldquo;Neece! Not Abe Neece&rsquo;s boy?&rdquo; exclaimed Kiskadden, shocked out of his composure. &ldquo;Yes. Young Allen Neece.&rdquo; &ldquo;Aw, too bad&mdash;too bad!&rdquo; rejoined the sheriff, in profound regret. &ldquo;As if poor Abe had not had enough trouble!&rdquo; &ldquo;Boss, you just bet it&rsquo;s too bad. It&rsquo;ll sure go hard with Allen&rsquo;s twin sisters. Them gurls thought the world of him.&rdquo; &ldquo;Fetch Neece in,&rdquo; ended Kiskadden, and taking Brazos&rsquo; arm he led him into the office. &ldquo;An&rsquo; see heah, sheriff,&rdquo; spoke up Brazos. &ldquo;Will yu have my hawse taken good care of? An&rsquo; Bodkin took my gun, watch, penknife&mdash;an&rsquo; a personal letter. Thet&rsquo;s all I had, an&rsquo; the letter means most to me.&rdquo; &ldquo;Cowboy, I&rsquo;ll be responsible for your hawse an&rsquo; your belongings.&rdquo; &ldquo;Thanks. Thet&rsquo;s a load off my mind. An&rsquo; one thing more,&rdquo; said Brazos, lowering his voice so that the men carrying in the body of Neece could not hear him. &ldquo;I reckon thet letter will prove my innocence. I got it yesterday mawnin&rsquo; at Latimer, which you shore know is a hell of a long day&rsquo;s ride. An&rsquo; if I know anythin&rsquo; aboot daid men, young Neece was killed durin&rsquo; the day. Hold an inquest, sheriff, an&rsquo; make shore what hour thet pore boy was murdered. &rsquo;Cause the whole deal has a look of murder.&rdquo; &ldquo;You&rsquo;re a cool hand,&rdquo; replied Kiskadden, admiringly. &ldquo;I kinda like you. From Texas, eh?&rdquo; &ldquo;Shore. I was born in Uvalde.&rdquo; &ldquo;How old air you?&rdquo; &ldquo;Twenty-five.&rdquo; &ldquo;Wal, you don&rsquo;t look thet. Any folks livin?&rdquo; &ldquo;There was&mdash;a few years ago. But I&rsquo;ve been too unhappy lately to write home.&rdquo; &ldquo;Air you straight, cowboy?&rdquo; &ldquo;I am, sheriff, so help me Gawd!&rdquo; answered Brazos, passionately, meeting full the penetrating gray eyes, that had something of shadow in them. &ldquo;Wal, I promised you a square deal,&rdquo; concluded Kiskadden. &ldquo;Come with me. I&rsquo;ll have to lock you up.&rdquo; A corridor opened from the office. Kiskadden unlocked the first door on the right, to disclose a small room with one barred window. The only article Brazos could see at quick glance was a blanketed couch. Kiskadden escorted Brazos in and halted in the doorway. &ldquo;Cowboy, one thing bothers me. In case you air innocent, which Inskip swore you was, an&rsquo; I substantiate thet, you&rsquo;re liable to hold somethin&rsquo; against Bodkin an&rsquo; mebbe his men.&rdquo; &ldquo;Hell! Thet bothers me,&rdquo; flashed Brazos, sitting down heavily on the couch. &ldquo;Bodkin? I didn&rsquo;t know aboot him.... An&rsquo; thet Barsh, who put a rope around my neck. Only man who ever did thet!... But, Kiskadden, I&rsquo;ll be more interested in the three hombres who tricked me into this mess.&rdquo; &ldquo;Cowboy, you don&rsquo;t seem to concern yourself aboot why I&rsquo;m lockin&rsquo; you up.&rdquo; &ldquo;Concern? Say, I&rsquo;m tickled to death. What have I got to worry aboot now? You&rsquo;re a Texan an&rsquo; a man. You&rsquo;ll see through my part in this deal.... But when I get oot... Sheriff, I&rsquo;m askin&rsquo; yu&mdash;please get possession of my letter an&rsquo; please don&rsquo;t let anybody but yu read it. I shore couldn&rsquo;t stand thet.&rdquo; &ldquo;We&rsquo;ll see.&rdquo; The sheriff went out to close and lock the heavy door. Brazos lay down on the couch. As he composed himself, the sound of heavy boots and indistinct voices came through the walls from the sheriff&rsquo;s office. The window of his cell opened on the back. After a while, his blood ceased to race and his thoughts to whirl. &ldquo;Doggone!&rdquo; soliloquized Brazos. &ldquo;When did I ever have a closer shave than thet?&rdquo; He was well off in jail for a few days. He would be well fed and have a bed to sleep on. And meanwhile, he would piece the fragments of this case together. Something more would come out at his trial, or at least the perfunctory hearing Kiskadden would have to give him. He had not the least doubt that Kiskadden would not only release him, but establish his innocence. This Texan recalled other denizens of the Lone Star State that he had known&mdash;Cap Britt, for one, for whom he had ridden and shot himself into notoriety some years before. Inskip was another. These men knew their kind. Brazos wondered if this Abe Neece had come from the Lone Star State. Surface was certainly not a Texan. Brazos tried to side-track an insidious impression of intrigue involving the three riders who had accosted him out at the cabin, this deputy Bodkin, and the young man Barsh, who had been afraid to show his face, and Surface. Brazos had only slim pegs on which to hang these suspicions. But a remarkable career on the ranges had given him an experience far beyond his years. Always he had been thrown against a background of cattle dealing, with its multiplicity of angles. The vast plains of Texas, the Panhandle and the Llano Estacado, the silver-grassed ranges of New Mexico, the Colorado steppes and the many valleys of Wyoming&mdash;these he knew as well as any cowboy who had ever ridden them. Rustlers, outlaws, desperadoes, bandits, and the ever-increasing number of cowboys gone wrong&mdash;these had multiplied with the building of the cattle empire. Likewise, the strange fact of apparently honest cattlemen being in league with the evil forces had flourished since the first great herds of longhorns had been driven north from Texas. Brazos recalled a few he had known, the most notable of whom, though not the last&mdash;Sewall McCoy&mdash;brought a cold jerk along Brazos&rsquo; nerves. The redheaded daughter of Surface came back to mind&mdash;Lura Surface. She had certainly made him a target for wonderful blue-green hungry eyes. &ldquo;I know her kind,&rdquo; muttered Brazos. &ldquo;A flirt&mdash;for whom cowboys air apple pie. I&rsquo;ll shore have to see her again, risk or no risk. She saw I was no low-down murderer. An&rsquo; I&rsquo;ll have to remember thet.&rdquo; Brazos had innumerable questions to ask somebody about this Twin Sombreros Ranch, the Surfaces, and the Neeces. At this point he was interrupted by footsteps out in the hall. He heard a heavy bolt or lock shot back. The door opened to admit a man carrying a tray. &ldquo;Hyar&rsquo;s some grub, cowboy,&rdquo; he said gruffly, setting the tray down on the couch. Brazos rolled to a sitting posture, his boots clanging on the floor. &ldquo;Say, yu&rsquo;re one hombre I&rsquo;m gonna treat white in this burg,&rdquo; sang out Brazos. &ldquo;Hungry? Wal, look at thet grub!... Stay an&rsquo; talk to me, pardner.&rdquo; &ldquo;Agin&rsquo; orders,&rdquo; rejoined the guard, who went out to lock Brazos in again. Brazos made the most of that generous meal. A starved condition was not conducive to optimism and clear thinking. He felt vastly better. Pacing the narrow confines of his cell, he lived his experience over again and realized that he got more out of it. Then he lay down to rest, and anon, he stood up on the bed to peep out of the small window. It opened out upon a high-fenced compound rather than corral, at the back of which ran a long shed of stalls. He saw the flank of his horse Bay. The afternoon passed far from tediously for Brazos. Those heavy footfalls in the office and on the flagstone sidewalk had meaning for him. Unless Las Animas had more than doubled the population of his day, he estimated that very nearly every man in the town had visited Kiskadden. Late in the afternoon, two guards brought his supper. &ldquo;We&rsquo;ve orders to take you out back fer a little exercise, if you want,&rdquo; announced one. &ldquo;Wal, in the mawnin&rsquo;,&rdquo; replied Brazos. &ldquo;An&rsquo; if yu&rsquo;ll fetch me water, soap an&rsquo; towel, an&rsquo; a razor, I&rsquo;ll consider myself obligated.&rdquo; &ldquo;Glad to, cowboy.&rdquo; They went out in the gathering dusk. Brazos took off his clothes and went to bed, his eyes shutting as with glue. That night he made up lost sleep, and it was very late when he awoke. The stars told him that the dark hour before dawn was close at hand. The lonely silent hour Brazos had always chosen to guard the herd! If he could not sleep, that had always been a bad hour for him. His reckless life always spread out for him to review, the ghosts of dead men haunted him, the many opportunities for betterment that he had missed, the many times he had had to ride away from ranges and cowboys he loved, and lastly the dark-eyed lovely girl who had made him a wandering, line-riding cowboy. From now on his torture would be relentless and unabatable. That letter! He felt for it against his heart. Gone! And a wrench of pain and fury shook him. Would the sheriff read that letter aloud at the trial? Brazos did not believe the Texan would subject him to that ordeal. That letter had wrenched Brazos&rsquo; soul. And he had not dared to read it all or go back to it. He had been flayed. The bitter anguish of the past had softened. What hurt so terribly was to find that his name had been revered, that love and faith still abided out there beyond the Cimarron, that Holly Ripple had named her boy for him, that her husband Frayne and her foreman Britt, and all that hard-riding, hard-shooting outfit of cowboys, the like of which had never been known on the ranges, all swore by him, made him a tradition, and never ceased to believe he would end his reckless wandering and come back to them. Daylight brought a cessation of Brazos&rsquo; unhappy memories and resolves. But he divined that a leaning to evil had passed out of him during his dark hour. He felt himself transformed, gone back to the old gay cool Brazos Keene with something inexplicable added. The guards brought his breakfast, and the necessary articles with which to wash and shave, and make himself presentable. &ldquo;Your trial is comin&rsquo; off today,&rdquo; the kindly one of the two announced. &ldquo;An&rsquo; I reckon you needn&rsquo;t be ondue worried.&rdquo; &ldquo;Thanks, pardner. Thet&rsquo;s fine. Take me ootside for a stretch.&rdquo; All morning, however, he was left alone, waiting for a footfall that did not come. The fact of the omission of his noonday meal augured further for his release. Brazos paced his cell, finally achieving patience. At last a slow clinking step in the corridor ended his wait. That was the step of a Texan. Brazos was not disappointed. The door opened to admit Kiskadden, who closed and locked it. &ldquo;Wal, Brazos,&rdquo; he drawled, &ldquo;I&rsquo;m missin&rsquo; my dinner to have a confab with you.&rdquo; &ldquo;Yu know my name?&rdquo; queried Brazos, sharply. &ldquo;Shore. It&rsquo;s on the back of this letter. Brazos Keene. Wrote small an&rsquo; pretty, but I read thet much anyway. I&rsquo;m glad to tell you no one else has seen it. I reckon Bodkin&rsquo;s man, Segel, put no store on it. An&rsquo; heah it is, cowboy.&rdquo; &ldquo;My Gawd, sheriff, but I could die for yu&mdash;savin&rsquo; me the shame of disgracin&rsquo; a girl I once loved,&rdquo; replied Brazos, in grateful emotion. &ldquo;Wal, I&rsquo;m glad if it means as much as thet,&rdquo; returned Kiskadden, and he sat down on the couch to take out a black pipe. &ldquo;I always figger better when I smoke. Not thet I&rsquo;m not shore you&rsquo;ll go free. It&rsquo;s a pore case agin&rsquo; you, Brazos, an&rsquo; has some queer angles.&rdquo; &ldquo;Ha! I had thet hunch. Yu wouldn&rsquo;t be a Texas sheriff if yu hadn&rsquo;t seen thet.&rdquo; &ldquo;You got this letter the mawnin of day before yestiddy, at Latimer, didn&rsquo;t you?&rdquo; &ldquo;Yes, sir. An&rsquo; all by accident, or mebbe a hunch. I was ridin&rsquo; through aboot eight o&rsquo;clock. I went in the post office an&rsquo; was paralyzed to get it. I rode oot of town scared to death. But finally I sloped off under a tree.... Gosh, I must have been there for hours but I didn&rsquo;t have the nerve to read it all. But the sun was high an&rsquo; hot when I rode on again.&rdquo; &ldquo;Wal, we had two doctors make the inquest on young Neece,&rdquo; went on Kiskadden. &ldquo;Our Doc Williamson, who lives heah, an&rsquo; a surgeon from Denver, who was on a train. Williamson seen him an&rsquo; dragged him off. They found young Neece had been killed early in the evenin&rsquo; of thet day you rode oot of Latimer. The bullet hole in his back was shot there after Neece was daid. Both doctors agreed thet he had been roped&mdash;there were abrasions on his arms above his elbows&mdash;an&rsquo; jerked off his hawse on his haid. Thet caused his death.&rdquo; &ldquo;Wal, my Gawd!&rdquo; ejaculated Brazos, in wondering fire. &ldquo;I had no rope on my saddle.&rdquo; &ldquo;Brazos, I was convinced of yore innocence yestiddy, an&rsquo; now I know it. But for yore good, I reckon you better stay for the hearin&rsquo;. It&rsquo;ll show Bodkin up an&rsquo; I&rsquo;ll discharge him pronto. Another angle, it leaked oot somewhere thet Surface would jest as lief see you hanged, along with all the grub-line cowboys thet ride through.&rdquo; &ldquo;Hell yu say?&rdquo; queried Brazos, thoughtfully. &ldquo;Sheriff, I shore didn&rsquo;t take a shine to him.&rdquo; &ldquo;Surface is new heah. Claims to be from Nebraska. But he&rsquo;s from Kansas. Rich cattleman&mdash;an&rsquo; has a lot of stock. Same as all of us, for thet matter.&rdquo; &ldquo;Ahuh. How&rsquo;d Surface get thet Twin Sombreros Ranch from Neece?&rdquo; &ldquo;Wal, thet&rsquo;s kind of complicated, an&rsquo; never was cleared up to suit me. Neece was operatin&rsquo; big. He had five thousand haid comin&rsquo; up from Texas for Surface. The cash for this herd was paid Neece at the Cattleman&rsquo;s Bank in Dodge. More than fifty thousand dollars. Neece was fetchin&rsquo; thet sum over heah to our bank. But he got held up by three masked men, an&rsquo; robbed. Wal, the queer angle is thet the big herd jest vanished off the range. Neither hoof nor hair of them was ever found.&rdquo; &ldquo;But the cow ootfit!&rdquo; exclaimed Brazos, aghast. &ldquo;Same as the herd. They vanished. Neece made a blunder at Dodge. He hired a foreman thet he didn&rsquo;t know, let him pick an ootfit, an&rsquo; sent them south after the herd.&rdquo; &ldquo;Thet ootfit was bought off,&rdquo; said Brazos, abruptly. &ldquo;Wal, there was no proof of anythin&rsquo; except the longhorns were gone. Neece couldn&rsquo;t deliver to Surface. An&rsquo; he had been robbed of the money. Twin Sombreros was mortgaged an&rsquo; the banks wouldn&rsquo;t advance more. Neece lost all to Surface. He&rsquo;s a broken man now, livin&rsquo; oot of town down the Purgatory. An&rsquo; the twin gurls, Neece&rsquo;s joy an&rsquo; pride, air running a restaurant over by the railroad station.&rdquo; &ldquo;Twin girls!&rdquo; &ldquo;Shore. Eighteen years old&mdash;the prettiest gurls in all the West. An&rsquo; you cain&rsquo;t tell them apart&mdash;not to save yore life. June an&rsquo; Janis, they&rsquo;re called. Neece was powerful proud of them twins. He sent them back to Kansas City to go to school. Thet was ten years ago. An&rsquo; he didn&rsquo;t see them often an&rsquo; not atall of late years. He developed this Twin Sombreros Ranch for them. Thet was his brand. Two high-peaked sombreros. Wal, the gurls just got heah when the crash came. Hard luck fer them, everybody swore, an&rsquo; was sorry. But them gurls had spunk. They borrowed money an&rsquo; started a restaurant. Old Abe&rsquo;s Mexican cook stuck to them. An&rsquo; say, thet little restaurant is packed every mealtime, with a crowd waitin&rsquo; ootside. They&rsquo;ve paid back what they borrowed an&rsquo; now they&rsquo;re makin&rsquo; money.&rdquo; &ldquo;Stampedin&rsquo; mavericks!&rdquo; burst out Brazos. &ldquo;I reckoned I&rsquo;d heahed some range yarns in my day. But this one takes the cake.... I&rsquo;ll bet thet Lura Surface sticks up her nose at the Neece twins, huh?&rdquo; &ldquo;Wal, the wimmen folks all say Lura is a cat an&rsquo; powerful jealous of the twins. You see, she queened it over the range till Neece&rsquo;s gurls got heah. An&rsquo; now she&rsquo;s not got it all her own way.&rdquo; &ldquo;Kiskadden, what yu tellin&rsquo; me all this for?&rdquo; suddenly queried Brazos, sharp with suspicion. &ldquo;Aw, just range gossip, cowboy,&rdquo; drawled the Texan, with an evasive smile. &ldquo;Yeah? Wal, it&rsquo;s shore powerful interestin&rsquo; an&rsquo; yu don&rsquo;t strike me as the gossipin&rsquo; kind.... I figure Inskip&rsquo;s a friend of yores?&rdquo; &ldquo;Yes. We&rsquo;re pardners in a cattle business, but I&rsquo;m the silent one.... Wal, to come back to yore hearin&rsquo;, which is set fer two o&rsquo;clock, I&rsquo;d like you to read thet letter to me.&rdquo; &ldquo;Aw! Sheriff, you didn&rsquo;t open it?&rdquo; &ldquo;No.&rdquo; &ldquo;What yu want me to read it for?&rdquo; &ldquo;Brazos, I really don&rsquo;t have to heah it, if yu object. But it&rsquo;ll strengthen my conviction, I&rsquo;m shore. An&rsquo; I may have to talk turkey to Surface an&rsquo; some of his cattle association. All the same, I&rsquo;ll respect yore confidence.&rdquo; &ldquo;Shore. I&mdash;I&rsquo;ll read it to yu,&rdquo; replied Brazos, soberly, and as he opened the thick letter his lean brown hands shook slightly. At the conclusion of this reading, Kiskadden strode to and fro in the cell, while Brazos sat with bowed head over the letter. Once the sheriff laid a heavy hand on the cowboy&rsquo;s clustering chestnut curls, beginning faintly to show silver at his temples. The tread of boots outside brought Kiskadden to a halt. &ldquo;Aboot time for yore trial, Brazos,&rdquo; he said, consulting his watch. &ldquo;Sheriff, as I told you, I never read all this letter&mdash;first time,&rdquo; rejoined Brazos, gravely, as he carefully folded the pages and put them back into the envelope. &ldquo;Honest I didn&rsquo;t.&rdquo; &ldquo;Cowboy, I&rsquo;m shore glad Bodkin didn&rsquo;t read it.... Brazos, there ain&rsquo;t much I can say, except I&rsquo;m glad I trusted you. If I hadn&rsquo;t an&rsquo; you&rsquo;d sprung thet letter on me, I&rsquo;d shore been ashamed.... It&rsquo;s a wonderful letter, Brazos. I&rsquo;d like you to know I rode fer Colonel Ripple when I was yore age. I knew Cap Britt. We were Texas Rangers under McKelvy. An&rsquo; I shore have heahed of Frayne.&rdquo; &ldquo;Aw! Yu don&rsquo;t say?&rdquo; burst out Brazos, gladly. &ldquo;All my agonizin&rsquo; fer nothin&rsquo;! When I heahed the name Kiskadden&mdash;Las Animas sheriff&mdash;I reckoned yu might be one of them hangin&rsquo; sheriffs an&rsquo; I was shore sick.&rdquo; &ldquo;Don&rsquo;t blame you, cowboy.... Wal, we&rsquo;ll go oot now an&rsquo; I&rsquo;ll be settin&rsquo; you free pronto. What then, Brazos?... Tell me, on the square, cowboy, air Holly Ripple&rsquo;s love for you an&rsquo; her faith&mdash;an&rsquo; Frayne&rsquo;s an&rsquo; all of them&mdash;air they justified?&rdquo; &ldquo;Hell no!&rdquo; ejaculated Brazos, with a wrench. &ldquo;But, sheriff, I could look Holly in the eye an&rsquo; Frayne an&rsquo; Britt&mdash;all thet tough ootfit of pards&mdash;an&rsquo; swear to Gawd I&rsquo;ve never done a crooked thing since I left them.&rdquo; &ldquo;Wal, I ask no more,&rdquo; returned the Texan, feelingly. &ldquo;All the same, I&rsquo;m gonna tell yu more,&rdquo; flashed Brazos. &ldquo;This day marks a change in my life. It&rsquo;s been comin&rsquo; a long time. Somethin&rsquo;s busted inside me like a million sparks. I&rsquo;ll be what Holly Ripple thought I was or I&rsquo;ll die tryin&rsquo;.&rdquo; &ldquo;Wal, now, cowboy, I reckon thet&rsquo;s to say you&rsquo;ve wiped oot the five years. Good! You&rsquo;ve got much to live up to an&rsquo; I believe you&rsquo;re equal to it.&rdquo; &ldquo;Thanks, Kiskadden.... An&rsquo; heah we air forgettin&rsquo; the most important thing in thet letter. The hunch aboot Surface!&rdquo; &ldquo;No, Brazos. I&rsquo;m lettin&rsquo; thet ride fer the present. All aboot you was personal. Frayne&rsquo;s hunch is business. An&rsquo; believe me, damn serious.&rdquo; Brazos took a swift survey of these spectators, more to sense their attitude than to look for some one who knew him. That there would be old acquaintances present he felt sure. For the time being the feeling in general seemed one of curious hostility. &ldquo;Set there, Keene,&rdquo; said Kiskadden indicating one of two chairs back of his desk. Brazos saw his gun and belt, his watch and penknife, lying on some papers. The desk drawer was half open, showing the dark butts of several Colts. &ldquo;Let everybody in, if there&rsquo;s room,&rdquo; called the sheriff, to the guard at the door. Presently Kiskadden pounded on his desk to stop the talking. He stood erect. &ldquo;Fellow citizens,&rdquo; he said. &ldquo;My mind aboot this case is made up. But I&rsquo;ll hold a hearin&rsquo; so thet you-all can get the facts.&rdquo; Surface took a step out from the group of ranchmen evidently accompanying him. His mien was forceful, arrogant, suggestive of power. His bland face appeared to Brazos to be a mask. Not since Brazos had taken to the cattle trails had he trusted eyes like these. &ldquo;Sheriff, I move we try this man before twelve jurors. I will serve along with the members of the Cattlemen&rsquo;s Association. We can pick the others from businessmen here.&rdquo; &ldquo;What&rsquo;s the idee of thet?&rdquo; demanded Kiskadden. &ldquo;Your declaration that you had already come to a decision proves the consensus of opinion correct.&rdquo; &ldquo;An&rsquo; what&rsquo;s thet opinion, Mister Raine Surface?&rdquo; queried the sheriff, sarcastically. &ldquo;You wouldn&rsquo;t hang a Texas cowboy. This murderer would already have swung but for Inskip, who&rsquo;s another of your Texas breed.&rdquo; &ldquo;Wal, Surface, thet Texas breed opened up this cattle empire. An&rsquo; the strange fact is you seldom heah of one of them gettin&rsquo; hanged. Thet might come from their gun-throwin&rsquo; proclivity, an&rsquo; then again it might be thet few Texans deserve to swing. In this case, I&rsquo;m refusin&rsquo; your offer of a jury. The law of this county is invested in me.&rdquo; &ldquo;Kiskadden, you may rest assured your authority will not last long,&rdquo; rejoined Surface, heatedly. Brazos took in this byplay with a thrilled interest and keen observation. Surface certainly had no conception of Texas character. Evidently, he was rich, powerful, sure of himself. He seemed utterly blind to the fact that he himself was on trial there, before at least three cool Texans. &ldquo;I&rsquo;m as shore as you air of thet,&rdquo; drawled Kiskadden, his narrowed eyes like slits of gray on the rancher. &ldquo;An&rsquo; I&rsquo;m also shore of somethin&rsquo; else. It&rsquo;s goin&rsquo; to look damn queer presently, when I prove this cowboy innocent, thet you&rsquo;re so keen on hangin&rsquo; him.&rdquo; Surface turned a dark red. His collar appeared to be too tight for his bulging neck. &ldquo;You insulting Texan. I&rsquo;ll run you&mdash;out of office for that!&rdquo; he exclaimed, stridently. &ldquo;Run an&rsquo; be damned. Yore action an&rsquo; yore talk air not regular in this deal. They look fishy to this court. To be lousy with money an&rsquo; haid of this new Cattle Association shore doesn&rsquo;t entitle you to run me an&rsquo; my office.... Do I make myself clear, Mister Surface?&rdquo; If the rancher did not take the hint at that, his associates surely did, for they drew him back and shut his mouth. &ldquo;All right. The hearin&rsquo;s on,&rdquo; called out Kiskadden, loudly. &ldquo;Deputy Bodkin, step forward.&rdquo; &ldquo;Yes, sir,&rdquo; replied the burly officer, coming up to the desk. &ldquo;Take off yore hat when you testify to the court.... Place yore hand on this Bible an&rsquo; swear to tell the truth an&rsquo; nothin&rsquo; but the truth.&rdquo; Bodkin took the oath. &ldquo;Now proceed with yore testimony.&rdquo; &ldquo;Wal, sir, it was late after two o&rsquo;clock, night before last,&rdquo; began Bodkin, glib with importance. &ldquo;I&rsquo;d been playin&rsquo; cards an&rsquo; had hardly got asleep when I was woke by somebody at my winder. I seen two men. It was too dark to see their faces plain. They was strangers. One of them told me they&rsquo;d watched a cowboy shoot another off his horse, search him and drag him into the cabin. Thet was the old Hill cabin, long empty, six miles west of town. My informant told me the cowboy came out of the cabin, unsaddled the horses, an&rsquo; turned them loose. Then he went back. It was rainin&rsquo; an&rsquo; cold. He&rsquo;d likely stay in the cabin till daybreak. Then the two fellars rustled off in the dark. I heerd their horses.... Wal, I got up, dressed an&rsquo; rustled out fer a posse. At thet hour, it wasn&rsquo;t easy. I had to take who I could get. It was near dawn when I&rsquo;d collected ten men. Inskip come along on his own accord. I didn&rsquo;t want him. He heerd me wake his riders. He told them to saddle his hoss.... Wal, we rode out fast, an&rsquo; arrived at the cabin jest at daybreak. The prisoner thar had jest stepped out the door. We held him up, took his gun an&rsquo; what he had in his pockets. He was a cool one. I seen blood on his hand. I sent men inside to search the cabin. They found the dead man in the cabin an&rsquo; fetched him out. It was Allen Neece. Thet was sure a surprise to me. His pockets was turned inside out. I heerd today thet Neece won a hundred dollars at faro the afternoon before he rode out of town. He was goin&rsquo; to see some girl.... Wal, the prisoner hyar sure went white an&rsquo; sick when the dead boy was carried out an&rsquo; laid on the grass. A blind man could have seen thet he&rsquo;d murdered him. We found one hoss, the prisoner&rsquo;s. An&rsquo; Segel packed the dead boy in on his saddle.... All the way in I was debatin&rsquo; on hangin&rsquo; the murderer. An&rsquo; when I got to it, this side of Twin Sombreros Ranch, Inskip crowded in front of us an&rsquo; gave the cowboy a chance to grab his two guns.... We got held up pronto an&rsquo; drove into town. An&rsquo; I&rsquo;m fer arrestin&rsquo; Inskip&mdash;&mdash;&rdquo; &ldquo;When Surface called you back, what did he say?&rdquo; interrupted Kiskadden. &ldquo;What?&rdquo; queried Bodkin, disconcerted for the first time. &ldquo;Surface halted you at his ranch, then followed you an&rsquo; stopped you. He drew you out of hearin&rsquo; of yore men. This court is powerful interested in what Surface said.&rdquo; &ldquo;Wal&mdash;sir,&rdquo; exploded the deputy, his swarthy visage turning yellow. &ldquo;He advised hangin&rsquo; the cowboy right then an&rsquo; thar. Said he distrusted this office. Too much red tape an&rsquo; favor to Texans.&rdquo; &ldquo;Surface advised hangin&rsquo; the prisoner without trial?&rdquo; &ldquo;Yes, sir. An&rsquo; I was just set to do it. Barsh had the rope around his neck when Inskip broke up the game.&rdquo; &ldquo;Thet will do, Bodkin,&rdquo; said the sheriff. &ldquo;Doctor Williamson, will you please step forward an&rsquo; make yore report.&rdquo; A stout middle-aged man, with ruddy face, approached the desk. &ldquo;Mister Sheriff,&rdquo; he began, &ldquo;and gentlemen. My fellow practitioner and I find that young Neece came to his violent death not later than the middle of the afternoon of day before yesterday. We find that death was caused by a compound fracture of the skull with consequent concussion of the brain. The bullet hole in his back was made long after he was dead. He had been roped and jerked heavily to the ground, probably from a horse.&rdquo; &ldquo;Thank you, Doctor,&rdquo; replied the sheriff. &ldquo;Now, gentlemen, let me read you a telegram received heah this mawnin&rsquo;. It is dated Latimer, Colorado, an&rsquo; it reads: &lsquo;Sheriff Steve Kiskadden, Las Animas. The letter addressed to Brazos Keene was delivered to him in person at eight-ten o&rsquo;clock day before yesterday morning. May fifth. Signed Postmaster John Hilton.&rsquo;&rdquo; &ldquo;Brazos Keene!&rdquo; ejaculated Bodkin, as if the name stirred vague associations. A murmur ran through the standing crowd. But it was certain that Raine Surface had never heard the name. A stalwart man, long past sixty, yet erect and keen-eyed, stood out from the crowd. &ldquo;Mr. Sheriff an&rsquo; fellow citizens,&rdquo; he said. &ldquo;I have known Brazos Keene for years, in fact since his first trail trip up from Texas to Dodge City. He was a wild cowboy, as indeed all those boys had to be to survive those days. After that I gave him many an important job. Long after that time Brazos rode for the Ripple outfit at Don Carlos&rsquo; Rancho. He often rode into Las Animas. I am sure many of our merchants and cattlemen will remember him, and join me in saying no finer, straighter, squarer cowboy than Brazos Keene ever straddled a horse in the interest of the honest cattle business of the range.&rdquo; Next, without being called, a little wizen-faced, bright-eyed Mexican rushed to take Hutchinson&rsquo;s place. &ldquo;Mexican Joe&mdash;he know thees Señor Brazos Keene,&rdquo; he began, dramatically. &ldquo;Joe run leetle restaurants here many years. Joe lend the moneys many times to thees cowboy. He ees always pay. One time he save my girl from the drunk hombres. Señor Brazos es un caballero grande!&rdquo; This eloquent tribute elicited a smile from many of the sober-faced spectators. Then the crowd parted to let out a stooped, gray, hawk-faced, bowlegged man who had grown bent and stiff in the saddle. Brazos gave a violent start. &ldquo;Hank Bilyen!... Aw, sheriff, don&rsquo;t let thet son-of-a-gun talk aboot me!&rdquo; cried Brazos, appealingly. &ldquo;Howdy, Brazos,&rdquo; drawled Bilyen, as he hobbled up to offer his hand. Brazos wrung it heartily. &ldquo;Hank, you old geezer! I clean forgot aboot you.&rdquo; &ldquo;Wal, I ain&rsquo;t forgot you, cowboy, as you&rsquo;ll get a hunch pronto,&rdquo; replied Bilyen, stepping up to Kiskadden. &ldquo;Sheriff, I take pleasure in accommodatin&rsquo; you with some facts about this cowboy you&rsquo;ve had the nerve to arrest.&rdquo; &ldquo;No, Hank, you&rsquo;ve got it wrong,&rdquo; drawled Kiskadden. &ldquo;My bright deputy arrested him. Keene rode in heah with the whole posse in front of him. An&rsquo; he gave himself up for trial. I&rsquo;ll take it as a personal favor to me if you testify to yore estimate of him.&rdquo; &ldquo;Wal, gentlemen,&rdquo; said Bilyen, with a dry chuckle, as he faced the gaping crowd. &ldquo;It jest occurs to me how orful long five years is. Fer it&rsquo;s been thet long since Brazos Keene used to give us somethin&rsquo; to talk about round the campfires. An&rsquo; in thet time the railroad has come, with many new people, an&rsquo; most of the old-timers have passed on. So to most of you, Brazos Keene is a stranger. An&rsquo; to the newcomers, like Surface an&rsquo; Bodkin, an&rsquo; the rest of you, I&rsquo;d say you&rsquo;re lucky to see one of the real cowboys who made this range safe. Brazos Keene was one of Cap Britt&rsquo;s outfit. An&rsquo; if thet means little to you, let me add thet Britt was Colonel Ripple&rsquo;s right-hand man. An&rsquo; Ripple was one of the greatest ranchers the West ever knew. Some years back, the Colonel fetched his daughter Holly Ripple up from the South, an&rsquo; about thet time the bad men of the border concentrated in New Mexico from the Cimarron to Glorietta Pass. Britt had a hundred thousand steers. To hold them he got together the greatest outfit of riders ever runnin&rsquo; fer one brand. Chisum&rsquo;s bunch wasn&rsquo;t a marker to Britt&rsquo;s. An&rsquo; Brazos Keene was the leader of thet outfit.... It ill becomes a Westerner to speak of another&rsquo;s gun record. An&rsquo; Brazos Keene might be offended. But I&rsquo;ll risk a little. He was the swiftest man with a gun, the fiercest in spirit, the most relentless tracker any of us old-timers ever knew. It was Keene&rsquo;s cunnin&rsquo;, his hound-scent on a trail, an&rsquo; last, his skill with a gun thet broke up the associated bands of rustlers in New Mexico. Sewall McCoy&mdash;sure you&rsquo;ve all heard his name&mdash;he was one of these rich, smooth, respectable, office-seekin&rsquo;, social-prominent cattlemen who behind all thet was the blackest, craftiest, bloodiest rustler baron we ever had out heah. McCoy believed dead cowboys told no tales. If he could not corrupt honest boys he had them ambushed an&rsquo; shot. Brazos got this gentleman to rights&mdash;an&rsquo; he killed him.... Also he killed Williams, another cattleman followin&rsquo; in McCoy&rsquo;s tracks. An&rsquo; he fetched the boss rustler Slaughter before McCoy&mdash;packin&rsquo; him over a saddle, hanged before he was bored full of holes&mdash;an&rsquo; he confronted Mr. McCoy with the little book thet rustler kept, an&rsquo; he called McCoy all the turrible names the range ever heerd of. Exposed him before two outfits, dared him, cussed him to draw, an&rsquo; killed him!... Thet, gentlemen, was only one of the little deals Brazos Keene is known by.... An&rsquo; this is the Brazos Keene our clever deputy had the gall to arrest, an&rsquo; our new neighbor Mr. Raine Surface, had the nerve to want hanged, an&rsquo; our smart young citizen, one Barsh, had the&mdash;Gawd! I don&rsquo;t know what to call it, but for want of a better word I&rsquo;ll say insanity&mdash;this Barsh had the insanity to throw a noose around Brazos Keene&rsquo;s neck.... If I haven&rsquo;t lived all these last twenty years on the frontier in vain, if Hank Bilyen hasn&rsquo;t lost his sense of Western creed, an&rsquo; his memory for historical facts&mdash;then it&rsquo;s a thousand to one thet Brazos Keene will find the murderer of Allen Neece, an&rsquo; he&rsquo;ll get to the bottom of Mr. Surface&rsquo;s queer hankerin&rsquo; to hang him&mdash;an&rsquo; as for Bodkin an&rsquo; Barsh&mdash;Wal! I ain&rsquo;t predictin&rsquo; in these days of onsartin life, but I wouldn&rsquo;t be in their boots for a million dollars!&rdquo; Once in his life Brazos had stood for frank eulogy about himself and for blunt reference to his career. The crowd stood silent under Bilyen&rsquo;s harangue, and at the thundering end, it gave further proof of how the truth had sunk in, especially in the case of the cowering Barsh, the pallid sweating Bodkin, and the pale Surface. Brazos wrestled up out of his seat. &ldquo;Hank, old-timer, thet&rsquo;ll be aboot all,&rdquo; he drawled in his cool dry way. &ldquo;If you think so doggone much of me as thet, stake me to a sack full of pesos, will yu? I&rsquo;m flat broke an&rsquo; it&rsquo;s shore embarrassin&rsquo;.&rdquo; &ldquo;Come to the bank with me,&rdquo; replied Bilyen, grinning, shaken out of his passionate earnestness. Kiskadden pounded on the table to stop the talking, laughing and shuffling of boots. &ldquo;This hearin&rsquo; ain&rsquo;t quite over yet,&rdquo; he explained. &ldquo;Brazos, I&rsquo;m returnin&rsquo; yore gun.... There! An&rsquo; I&rsquo;m apologizin&rsquo; as a man if I cain&rsquo;t as a sheriff.&rdquo; &ldquo;Thanks, Kiskadden,&rdquo; replied Brazos, as he buckled on the heavy belt. &ldquo;Gosh, thet&rsquo;s good! You know I sorta feel undressed withoot it.... Ahuh! Wal, I had a hunch. Some slick law-abidin&rsquo; hombre took the shells oot. I&rsquo;ll just load up again&mdash;to be ready in case I meet any skunks in the street.&rdquo; The humorous speech of the cowboy contrasted strangely with the singular dexterity with which he reloaded the weapon, and then rolled it in a flashing steely wheel. One of the onlookers burst into a nervous laugh, but the majority seemed fascinated by that action of Brazos&rsquo;. On the frontier, the six-shooter was supreme, law or no law. &ldquo;Bodkin,&rdquo; called out the sheriff, drastically. &ldquo;I never did cotton to you. How I ever hired you to represent me in this community is hard to explain an&rsquo; impossible to swallow. But I reckon you was forced on my hands by Surface an&rsquo; some of his Cattle Association friends.... You&rsquo;re the blunderin&rsquo;est&mdash;not to say wuss&mdash;excuse for a deputy thet I ever seen. Why, down in Texas you&rsquo;d last aboot as long as it took you to walk oot from yore appointment. An&rsquo; my last official act as sheriff heah is to discharge you. Get oot! You&rsquo;re fired. It&rsquo;d be a good idee for you an&rsquo; Barsh to hide.... Open up there. Let &rsquo;em oot!... Now, gentlemen, the Brazos Keene hearin&rsquo; is ended, an&rsquo; I&rsquo;m resignin&rsquo; as sheriff.&rdquo; A response approaching uproar succeeded this dramatic climax of the trial. Brazos caught a general disapproval in the assembly. There appeared to be immediate discord in the Cattlemen&rsquo;s Association. Kiskadden would not hear those who approached him to disclaim against the turn of affairs. He forced these men to follow the others out to the street. Brazos and Bilyen were left alone with the sheriff. &ldquo;Wal, mebbe I wasn&rsquo;t glad of a chance like thet!&rdquo; he exclaimed, his face shining. &ldquo;Don&rsquo;t blame you. But the county will be hot over it,&rdquo; replied Bilyen. &ldquo;What yu got up yore sleeve, Texas Jack?&rdquo; queried Brazos, shrewdly. &ldquo;I&rsquo;ll tell you later. Brazos, you ain&rsquo;t slopin&rsquo; off, by any chance?... Haw! Haw! Excuse me.... Wal, I want to move oot.
