What you want done with thet fine bay hawse?&rdquo; &ldquo;Doggone if I know.&rdquo; &ldquo;Tell you what,&rdquo; interposed Bilyen. &ldquo;I&rsquo;ll send a boy over to fetch your hoss an&rsquo; saddle to where I keep mine. Later I&rsquo;ll be takin&rsquo; you out to meet my boss.&rdquo; &ldquo;An&rsquo; who&rsquo;s he?&rdquo; &ldquo;No less than Abe Neece&mdash;a grand old man, Brazos. I was his foreman at Twin Sombreros, an&rsquo; after the crash, I jest couldn&rsquo;t leave him. Brazos, you&rsquo;ll be so sorry fer him thet you&rsquo;ll go gunnin&rsquo; fer trouble.&rdquo; &ldquo;Will I? Doggone it! Oot of the fryin&rsquo; pan into the fire!... At thet, I&rsquo;m sorry enough now for these Neeces. Hank, it was like yu to stick. I kinda think yu&rsquo;re a real fellar.&rdquo; At the door they were accosted by a lithe young man in rider&rsquo;s garb much the worse for wear. He had a clean-cut, youthful face, tanned by exposure, and fine eyes. &ldquo;I&rsquo;d like to shake your hand, Keene,&rdquo; he said hesitatingly, but with a winning smile. &ldquo;Shore. An&rsquo; who&rsquo;re yu?&rdquo; returned Brazos, slowly, as he returned the smile. He was the easiest cowboy on the ranges to approach&mdash;when he happened to meet this type. &ldquo;Jack Sain. Hank knows me. I&rsquo;ve been pretty friendly with the Neeces. Allen was my pard. It plumb busted me all up&mdash;what happened to him.&rdquo; &ldquo;Ahuh. Wal, I&rsquo;m darned glad to meet yu, Jack.&rdquo; &ldquo;Brazos, it was Jack&rsquo;s friendship for the Neeces thet cost him his job. He rode fer Surface. You see there ain&rsquo;t any love lost between the Surfaces an&rsquo; the Neeces.&rdquo; &ldquo;Wal, Jack, I&rsquo;ll be wantin&rsquo; to hobnob with yu some,&rdquo; said Brazos, thoughtfully. &ldquo;Where yu workin&rsquo; now?&rdquo; &ldquo;Nowhere. I can&rsquo;t get a job. Surface is strong in the association an&rsquo; he&rsquo;s queered me.&rdquo; &ldquo;Doggone!&rdquo; mused Brazos. &ldquo;Thet&rsquo;s interestin&rsquo;. Surface &rsquo;pears to be playin&rsquo; a high hand around heah.... Jack, where can I find yu later in the afternoon?&rdquo; &ldquo;Meet me at the Twin Sombreros Restaurant, up by the railroad station. About suppertime.&rdquo; &ldquo;Thet the place run by the Neece girls? Won&rsquo;t they be kinda nervous&mdash;seein&rsquo; me?&rdquo; &ldquo;Janis was with me in the sheriff&rsquo;s office. She slipped out just after Hank&rsquo;s speech. Before she went she said: &lsquo;Jack, I&rsquo;d never believe that cowboy murdered Allen.&rsquo;... Both the girls are dead game, Brazos, an&rsquo; they&rsquo;ll be glad to see you.&rdquo; &ldquo;All right, Jack. I&rsquo;ll be there.&rdquo; They parted, and Bilyen led Brazos slowly up the wide street. &ldquo;Fine lad thet,&rdquo; Bilyen was saying. &ldquo;Down on his luck now. I reckon he didn&rsquo;t tell you everythin&rsquo;. Lura Surface was sweet on Jack. She throws herself at every fellar who strikes her fancy. But when Jack met June Neece, he went loco. You never seen a cowboy so deep in love. An&rsquo; June leans to him a lot, though she&rsquo;s not a hell of a flirt at all like Janis.&rdquo; &ldquo;My Gawd!... Hank, is this a story yu&rsquo;re readin&rsquo; me? The next thing yu&rsquo;ll tell me these sisters will be pretty an&rsquo; sweet an&rsquo;&mdash;wal, Jack said they were daid game.&rdquo; &ldquo;Cowboy, wait till you see them.&rdquo; &ldquo;What am I gonna wait for? Tell me, man. An&rsquo; then if it&rsquo;s bad news I can fork my hawse an&rsquo; ride.&rdquo; &ldquo;It&rsquo;s good news, Brazos,&rdquo; replied Bilyen, soberly, taking him seriously. &ldquo;June an&rsquo; Jan Neece are the wonderfullest girls this range ever saw. Pretty! Hell, thet ain&rsquo;t no word! What&rsquo;s more they&rsquo;re sweet an&rsquo; true&mdash;an&rsquo; game? Say!... Old Abe developed thet ranch for them&mdash;sent them east to school to be educated&mdash;to do him proud. Ten years ago! They came back with trunks of stylish clothes an&rsquo; crazy to make joy at Twin Sombreros. Only they never got there! Folks love these girls because they&rsquo;re unspoiled. An&rsquo; when their fortunes fell, they went plumb to work.&rdquo; &ldquo;Hank, I reckon I better climb Bay an&rsquo; race for Montana,&rdquo; declared Brazos, ruefully. &ldquo;Why, you darn fool?&rdquo; &ldquo;&rsquo;Cause I have a turrible weakness.&rdquo; &ldquo;Haw! Haw! You haven&rsquo;t outgrowed thet. Wal, Brazos, I reckon it&rsquo;s on the cairds fer you to stay here.&rdquo; &ldquo;On the cairds? Hell, yes! They always run thet way for me. Same old&mdash;deal! If I&rsquo;ve got any sense atall, I&rsquo;ll rustle.&rdquo; &ldquo;Since when did Brazos Keene grow selfish?&rdquo; queried Bilyen, with subtle scorn. &ldquo;Selfish? Me! What&rsquo;s eatin&rsquo; you, Hank Bilyen?&rdquo; &ldquo;Think of thet pore murdered boy&mdash;an&rsquo; his brokenhearted dad&mdash;an&rsquo; them fine girls workin&rsquo; from daylight to midnight.&rdquo; &ldquo;Thet&rsquo;s what I am thinkin&rsquo; aboot!&rdquo; protested Brazos. Brazos leaned back against the rough stone wall of the bank and drew a deep breath, that whistled at the intake. His narrowed gaze fastened down the wide street, with its wagons and horses and busy sidewalks, out to the gray rangeland and the purple mountains. There was no use for him to rail at destiny or to try to run away from the inevitable. He pressed a steel-like hand against his breast where his precious letter lay in his pocket. He remembered. &ldquo;Shore, Hank, I savvy yu,&rdquo; he answered, with the old cool drawl. &ldquo;Let&rsquo;s go in an&rsquo; rob the bank. Then yu can take me oot to meet Abe Neece. An&rsquo; after thet, I&rsquo;ll see the twins.... Doggone! Only yesterday or thereaboots, I was a friendless, grub-line ridin&rsquo; cowboy. Funny aboot life! But it&rsquo;s worth livin&rsquo;.&rdquo; A few minutes later, Brazos stood outside the bank again, feeling a compact bulge in his pocket not altogether made by his precious letter. &ldquo;Hank, I only wanted a little money,&rdquo; expostulated Brazos. &ldquo;How&rsquo;n hell will I ever pay it back?&rdquo; &ldquo;Holy mackerel, Brazos, ask me an easy one. But I know you will,&rdquo; rejoined Bilyen, with a laugh. &ldquo;I can spare thet. Before I went to work fer Neece, I sold my herd to him, an&rsquo; I&rsquo;ve saved my money an&rsquo; wages. Lucky I did. I&rsquo;m takin&rsquo; care of the old man now an&rsquo; I lent the twins enough to start their restaurant.&rdquo; &ldquo;Wal, yu always was a good friend, Hank. Yu deserve to be a big rancher.... Say, who&rsquo;s this gazabo comin&rsquo;?&rdquo; &ldquo;Thet&rsquo;s Sam Mannin&rsquo;. Still has his store down the street. Sure, you remember Sam.&rdquo; &ldquo;No, I shore wouldn&rsquo;t have known him,&rdquo; said Brazos. &ldquo;Gosh, what a few years can do!&rdquo; A spare gray Westerner of venerable and kindly aspect came up to them, his lined face breaking into a smile. &ldquo;Hello, Brazos,&rdquo; he said, heartily, extending his hand. &ldquo;I heard you were in town, but I didn&rsquo;t see any smoke. Glad to see you again. An&rsquo; just about the same!&rdquo; &ldquo;Howdy, Sam. It&rsquo;s just fine to shake yore hand. I&rsquo;m gonna run in pronto an&rsquo; buy oot yore store. Have you any of those red silk scarfs Louise used to sell me?&rdquo; &ldquo;Plenty, cowboy. My store an&rsquo; business have grown with the years.&rdquo; &ldquo;Thet&rsquo;s fine. An&rsquo; how&rsquo;s Louise?&rdquo; &ldquo;Married long ago, Brazos. She has two children.&rdquo; &ldquo;I&rsquo;ll be doggoned! You tell Louise Mannin&rsquo; I swore she&rsquo;d wait for me.&rdquo; &ldquo;I will. An&rsquo; if I remember Louise in your day, she&rsquo;ll be fussed. Be sure to drop over.... How are you, Hank?&rdquo; &ldquo;Wal, Sam, I was feelin&rsquo; low till Brazos rode to town. Things will pick up now.&rdquo; &ldquo;I shouldn&rsquo;t wonder,&rdquo; responded Manning, wagging his gray head, and he passed on into the bank. &ldquo;Hank, let&rsquo;s duck down an alley, or somethin&rsquo;. If I meet any more old friends I&rsquo;ll bust.&rdquo; &ldquo;Stand your ground, cowboy. I got to buy some grub.... Haw! Haw! Look who&rsquo;s spotted you. Has she got eyes? Aw no&mdash;&mdash;&rdquo; &ldquo;Save me, Hank. Who&rsquo;n hell? I&rsquo;ll bet it&rsquo;s thet Surface girl.&rdquo; &ldquo;Right, Brazos. I&rsquo;ll duck in the store. Hope some of you&rsquo;ll be left when I come out.&rdquo; Brazos had attention only for the stunningly handsome and strikingly attired young woman who bore down upon him, face flushed and eyes alight. She was taller than she had appeared astride a horse, beautifully proportioned, and several years beyond her teens. &ldquo;I congratulate you, Mr. Brazos Keene,&rdquo; she said, graciously offering her hand. &ldquo;I&rsquo;m very glad indeed. It was a stupid blunder.&rdquo; &ldquo;Wal, thet&rsquo;s shore nice of yu, Miss Surface,&rdquo; replied Brazos, as he bowed bareheaded to take her hand. &ldquo;Considerin&rsquo; how keen yore father was to see me hanged, I&rsquo;m more&rsquo;n grateful to see yu wasn&rsquo;t.&rdquo; &ldquo;Oh, Dad is impossible,&rdquo; she declared, impatiently. &ldquo;He seems to suspect every cowboy who rides in from the West. If one happens to come along from Kansas, he&rsquo;ll hire him.&rdquo; &ldquo;Shore does seem unreasonable an&rsquo; hard on us Western riders,&rdquo; drawled Brazos, his gaze strong on her. &ldquo;I was aboot to shake the dust of Las Animas. But now, I just reckon I&rsquo;ll hang around. Do you think I might get to see yu again?&rdquo; &ldquo;You might,&rdquo; she replied, blushing very becomingly. &ldquo;I&rsquo;d like nothing better.&rdquo; &ldquo;But Mr. Surface wouldn&rsquo;t like it.&rdquo; &ldquo;I&rsquo;m over twenty-one.&rdquo; &ldquo;Wal, you shore don&rsquo;t look it.... I wonder where I was all the time yu&rsquo;ve been growin&rsquo; up into such a lovely girl.&rdquo; &ldquo;I&rsquo;ve wasted a good deal of it on cowboys less appreciative than you,&rdquo; she replied, accepting his nonchalant challenge with a dark flash in her green-blue eyes. &ldquo;Most cowboys air dumb.... When an&rsquo; how can I see yu, Lura?&rdquo; &ldquo;When do you want to, Brazos?&rdquo; she returned, brightly, the red spots playing prettily in her cheeks. &ldquo;Wal, I want to right now. But I&rsquo;ve got to go with Hank. Would tomorrow be too soon? I reckon I can wait thet long.&rdquo; &ldquo;I imagine you will find that long time very trying,&rdquo; she said, quizzically, watching him with amused wonder. Yet she had the soft light in her eyes that usually shone in women&rsquo;s eyes for Brazos. &ldquo;I&rsquo;ll just aboot die.... I&rsquo;m afraid it happened to me oot at yore ranch the other day&mdash;when yu told yore father thet I never murdered Allen Neece.&rdquo; &ldquo;What happened?&rdquo; &ldquo;I don&rsquo;t know what yet. But I felt powerful strange around heah,&rdquo; replied Brazos, putting his hand to his heart. &ldquo;Wal, in thet case, I&rsquo;ll forgive yu.&rdquo; &ldquo;I&rsquo;ll bet it&rsquo;s true you&rsquo;ve been a perfect devil with women.&rdquo; &ldquo;If havin&rsquo; my heart broke a lot of times proves thet&mdash;wal, I&rsquo;m guilty.&rdquo; &ldquo;There&rsquo;s Dad down the street,&rdquo; she returned, coolly. &ldquo;Meet me tomorrow afternoon about three in the grove on the east bank of the brook that runs into the Purgatory about a mile out of town. Can you remember all that?&rdquo; &ldquo;I&rsquo;ll be there,&rdquo; promised Brazos. She rewarded him with a dazzling smile and swept on down the street. &ldquo;Brimstone an&rsquo; chain lightnin&rsquo;,&rdquo; soliloquized Brazos, watching the superb form depart. &ldquo;Turrible took with herself. Crazy aboot men. An&rsquo; I cain&rsquo;t savvy what else. But doggone it! I like her.&rdquo; Bilyen emerged from the store burdened with bags, of which he gave Brazos a generous share. &ldquo;You look kinda sheepish,&rdquo; he observed. &ldquo;I&rsquo;d be some worried if I didn&rsquo;t know you was goin&rsquo; to meet June Neece today.&rdquo; &ldquo;Yeah. An&rsquo; why June if she&rsquo;s got a twin sister you cain&rsquo;t tell from her?&rdquo; &ldquo;In thet case he&rsquo;d be in deep, believe me.&rdquo; They walked to a stable and corral on the outskirts of town, where Hank sent a boy after Brazos&rsquo; horse and saddle. In due time, the two were riding out over country that brought thrilling memories to Brazos. And what interesting historical facts Brazos did not remember or never knew, Hank glibly supplied. &ldquo;Brazos, you should remember Fort Lyon. You once had a fight there, I reckon about seven years ago. Wal, there&rsquo;s the old fort. It was abandoned by the army two years ago. Kit Carson stayed there a good deal in the sixties, as also did Buffalo Bill, supplyin&rsquo; buffalo meat to the fort. West of town about ten miles is the ruins of Fort William Bent, built by the Bent brothers in 1874, the same year I come to Las Animas. Them was the good old wild days, Brazos. When there was roundups of great cattle herds from the Panhandle, an&rsquo; huge shipments of buffalo meat an&rsquo; hides to the east. Wagon trains, prairie schooners, stage coaches. All gone&mdash;them days of romance!&rdquo; Bilyen had a little ten-acre ranch on the Purgatory. A gray shack faced the rocky, swift-running stream, and the splendid vista of plains to the south and the noble slopes of foothills rising to the Rockies on the west. &ldquo;I can set on my doorstep an&rsquo; ketch trout,&rdquo; boasted Hank. &ldquo;An&rsquo; look at thet!&rdquo; &ldquo;Wal, I reckon I&rsquo;ll buy this place from yu an&rsquo; settle down,&rdquo; drawled Brazos, dreamily. He was leaning over the rocky bank, still dreaming, when Hank came out of the shack accompanied by a man whose lean gray visage denoted the havoc of trouble if not of years. Brazos leaped erect, galvanized with an instinct in this meeting. It was to meet the penetrating gaze of tawny shadowed eyes. &ldquo;Howdy, Brazos Keene,&rdquo; was the man&rsquo;s greeting. &ldquo;Hank has told me about you. I&rsquo;m glad you were cleared of that trumped-up charge.&rdquo; &ldquo;Shore happy to meet you, Mr. Neece,&rdquo; responded Brazos, warmly. &ldquo;Cowboy, you&rsquo;ve got the cut of my son Allen.... Only you&rsquo;re older&mdash;an&rsquo; there&rsquo;s something proved about you. Allen was young, reckless, inexperienced.&rdquo; &ldquo;Let&rsquo;s set down on the bank heah. Nice view. I&rsquo;m gonna buy this place from Hank.&rdquo; &ldquo;Have you met my twin girls?&rdquo; &ldquo;Not yet. I&rsquo;ve heahed all aboot them, though. An&rsquo; I gotta hand it to them, Mr. Neece. I shore look forward to meetin&rsquo; them.&rdquo; Neece sighed and gazed out across the greening brakes and swales to the open range. He was not old, nor feeble, but it appeared plain that the shock of disaster had broken him. &ldquo;Brazos, is what Bilyen tells me true?&rdquo; he queried presently, with an effort. &ldquo;Gosh, I&rsquo;d trust Hank every way &rsquo;cept talkin&rsquo; aboot me.&rdquo; At this juncture Brazos fell from humor to earnestness, changed by the dark meaning fire in Hank&rsquo;s eye. &ldquo;Hank says you&rsquo;re goin&rsquo; to stay here an&rsquo; look into the deal we Neeces have had.&rdquo; &ldquo;I shore am. It looks queer to me,&rdquo; declared Brazos, realizing that he was not averse to being drawn into the Neece mystery. &ldquo;That&rsquo;s good of you, cowboy. But why do you interest yourself in our troubles? You never knew Allen. You have not met my girls. Surface, who ruined us, is at the head of the strong combine of cattlemen in east Colorado. You&rsquo;re takin&rsquo; a large order on yourself.&rdquo; &ldquo;Wal, thet&rsquo;s easy to answer,&rdquo; declared Brazos, coolly. &ldquo;Bodkin arrested me because he needed to hang the crime on somebody. He thought I was a stranger&mdash;a cowboy down on his luck. Surface wanted me hanged. For reasons I&rsquo;m gonna find oot. If thet wasn&rsquo;t enough to rile Brazos Keene&mdash;wal, this rotten deal handed to yu an&rsquo; yore three kids shore would be. Thet&rsquo;s all, Neece. I don&rsquo;t want to brag, but the ootfit chalked up some bad marks for themselves.&rdquo; &ldquo;You insinuate Surface is in some way connected with Bodkin?&rdquo; &ldquo;Insinuate nothin&rsquo;. I&rsquo;m tellin&rsquo; you, Mr. Neece. But did yu need to be told thet Surface doesn&rsquo;t ring true&mdash;thet Bodkin was a fourflush shady deputy sheriff? Wal, Kiskadden knew it an&rsquo; he was damn glad thet I rode along to blow this ootfit up. Kiskadden resigned pronto.&rdquo; &ldquo;He did? Well!&rdquo; ejaculated Neece, beginning to grow intense. &ldquo;I don&rsquo;t just savvy Kiskadden. Shore he&rsquo;s a Texan an&rsquo; would have backed me to the throwin&rsquo; of guns. I seen them in his desk. All easy to snatch!... But he&rsquo;s got somethin&rsquo; up his sleeve beside the deal given me. Shore I reckon my surrenderin&rsquo; to him&mdash;&mdash;&rdquo; &ldquo;Surrenderin&rsquo;? I thought you were arrested an&rsquo; jailed.&rdquo; &ldquo;Not exactly,&rdquo; drawled Brazos. &ldquo;Bodkin an&rsquo; his posse arrested me, shore. But when we rode into Las Animas an&rsquo; up to the sheriff&rsquo;s office, I had thet posse in front of me, sittin&rsquo; their saddles stiff.&rdquo; &ldquo;How, for heaven&rsquo;s sake?&rdquo; Brazos gave a brief account of the fray and how Inskip had helped him come out on top. &ldquo;Inskip? So he was in the posse. Brazos Keene, I note the real Westerners take to you.&rdquo; &ldquo;Wal, Kiskadden shore has somethin&rsquo; up his sleeve beside takin&rsquo; to me.... Neece, I have a letter thet cleared me of implication in the&mdash;the murder of yore son. It&rsquo;s from New Mexico. Now I read it to Kiskadden. An&rsquo; I&rsquo;m gonna read a little of it to yu an&rsquo; Hank heah. When I ran into Surface, I hadn&rsquo;t even read that part of it, an&rsquo; it came as a surprise to me. Yu air to keep mum aboot it, see?&rdquo; Brazos took out Holly&rsquo;s letter, carefully opened and smoothed and sorted the pages until he came to Renn Frayne&rsquo;s postscript. The passage that related to Surface he slowly and gravely read. Neece showed that he still had flint in him to strike fire from. Manifestly deeply stirred, he controlled himself admirably and very probably found his real self for the first time since disaster and grief had overcome him. &ldquo;No coincidence! That was my herd. It was last seen on the Canadian.&rdquo; &ldquo;Wal, I had thet hunch myself. What yu think, Hank?&rdquo; &ldquo;Brazos Keene! So you dropped out of the sky with thet letter? Same old Brazos!... By Gawd, I&rsquo;m riled. I can see light an&rsquo; it&rsquo;s red. Haw! Haw! There&rsquo;s some of us left.... Surface, the&mdash;&mdash;&rdquo; &ldquo;Cheese it, Hank,&rdquo; interposed Brazos. &ldquo;You&rsquo;re turrible profane. An&rsquo; after all it may be a pore steer. Only it cain&rsquo;t be! But we gotta be shore. My idee is thet Frayne has tipped me a hunch damn important to eastern Colorado.... Neece, I&rsquo;ve heahed yore story from Hank. Just now, I only want to put one question. How an&rsquo; when did yu lose thet money of Surface&rsquo;s yu got in Dodge?&rdquo; &ldquo;Simple as a, b, c. I wanted cash. Got it, an&rsquo; took it on the train in a satchel. The train was late. It didn&rsquo;t get into Las Animas till after midnight. Jerry, my stableboy, met me with the buckboard. We drove out toward the ranch. At the turn of the road, where the brook crosses an&rsquo; the cottonwoods grow thick, I was held up by three men an&rsquo; robbed.&rdquo; &ldquo;Ahuh. An&rsquo; they shore knew where yu&rsquo;d been an&rsquo; what you had.&rdquo; &ldquo;You bet they did.&rdquo; &ldquo;Was there anythin&rsquo; at all familiar aboot them?&rdquo; &ldquo;No. Strangers. They wore masks. But I never forget a voice, once I hear it. One of the three had a young nervous high-pitched voice almost womanish. He called the burly man what sounded like &lsquo;Brad,&rsquo; an&rsquo; got cussed for doin&rsquo; it. They were rough, tough range riders.&rdquo; &ldquo;Brad,&rdquo; echoed Brazos, with a wild leap of his pulse. &ldquo;Was thet all you heahed?&rdquo; &ldquo;Yes. One of them batted me on the head. Jerry is not well yet from the beatin&rsquo; they gave him.&rdquo; &ldquo;Did yu ever tell thet you heahed the name Brad&mdash;spoke by a young nervous high-pitched voice?&rdquo; &ldquo;Come to think of it, I don&rsquo;t believe I ever did except maybe to Allen. How about that, Hank?&rdquo; &ldquo;You never told me.&rdquo; &ldquo;It must have slipped my mind after I told Allen. You excite me so it all comes back clear.&rdquo; &ldquo;Wal, thet&rsquo;s all I want to heah this time. I&rsquo;ll walk aboot a bit an&rsquo; think. Then I&rsquo;ll ride back to town an&rsquo; keep my appointment with Jack Sain.... Neece, do you like thet cowboy?&rdquo; &ldquo;Jack Sain? Yes, I do, though I&rsquo;ve only known him since the girls came home. He and Allen were friends, and...&rdquo; Neece&rsquo;s voice broke. &ldquo;Ahuh. Wal, I liked him too.... Hank, I&rsquo;ll be heah in the mawnin&rsquo;. An&rsquo; Mr. Neece, don&rsquo;t get het up overly aboot this. I might be loco, but I swear we&rsquo;re on as black an&rsquo; bloody a trail as I ever took up. So it behooves us to use our haids. Adios.&rdquo; When he arrived at the railroad station it was near the supper hour. The restaurant he sought had more prominence than the station itself and had been remodeled from an old adobe building he had difficulty in remembering. A second story had been added and the whole given a coat of whitewash. The building, the location and the neat sign were all attractive. A hitching rail ran along in front. Brazos dismounted, still too soberly pondering his predicament to awake to the momentous meeting at hand. Tying Bay to the rail, he stalked with his clinking step into the restaurant, expecting to see the cowboy with whom he had an appointment. But Jack Sain was not there. So far as he could see at a moment&rsquo;s notice, the place was empty. Brazos slid a long leg over the bench and leaning upon the counter, looked about him. The restaurant had windows on three sides. Evidently the entrance into the kitchen was through the other side. There appeared to be a double counter in the center with benches all around, and small tables with chairs next to windows. The inside of this new restaurant only added to the spotless white attractiveness of the outside. &ldquo;Doggone! This is shore a hash joint. Cowboys shore will stuff themselves heah. Wonder who runs&mdash;&mdash;&rdquo; At this juncture, two things happened simultaneously&mdash;Brazos remembered the Neece twins, and a door opened to admit a girl. Brazos never figured out what gave him such a shock, but the fact was that never in his life before had any girl produced the effect this one had on him. She was slight and graceful of form, fair-haired, but not blonde, and her face was white, sweet, sad. She had seen him before she entered and it struck Brazos she did not act like a waitress. She approached him, and putting her hands on the counter, she leaned forward. Her eyes were a tawny light brown. They engulfed Brazos. They searched his very soul. They appeared to Brazos to be the loveliest, saddest, most accusing eyes that ever shook the heart out of a poor cowboy. A long unfamiliar trouble suddenly fermented in Brazos&rsquo; breast, but disturbing as it was, it was not so torturing as the fear her dark and troubled gaze aroused. Brazos was about to blurt out, to swear by heaven that he had not murdered Allen Neece. &ldquo;Brazos Keene,&rdquo; she affirmed. &ldquo;Wal, I&mdash;I was thet pore hombre when I come in heah,&rdquo; he said, fighting to smile, &ldquo;but I cain&rsquo;t say now for shore.&rdquo; &ldquo;I am&mdash;June Neece,&rdquo; she returned, her low voice breaking a little. &ldquo;Aw, I wish I could say,&rdquo; floundered Brazos. He did not know what he wanted to say. &ldquo;We are sorry you were arrested and locked up on&mdash;&mdash;&rdquo; &ldquo;Thet was nothin&rsquo; atall, Miss Neece,&rdquo; interrupted Brazos, with unconscious pathos. &ldquo;Shore I hardly ever ride into a town but somethin&rsquo; like thet happens. I&rsquo;m a marked man.... As for the cause this time, wal, I oughtn&rsquo;t to remind you aboot... but I swear to Gawd I&rsquo;m innocent...&rdquo; Then it appeared to the bewildered and thrilled cowboy that another June Neece walked into his heart. He was keen enough then to realize the nature of the malady that for once shunted aside his cool nonchalant self. &ldquo;Jan, this is he,&rdquo; said the first tawny-eyed vision to the second, and then to him, with a little smile: &ldquo;My sister, Janis.&rdquo; There was absolutely no telling these twin sisters apart. The one called Janis blushed and a bright glow suddenly burned out the shadow in her eyes. &ldquo;Brazos Keene? Oh, I am glad to meet you!&rdquo; she exclaimed, and repeating her sister&rsquo;s action, she put her hand in his other as it lay on the counter. &ldquo;Wal, I shore am happy to meet yu-all,&rdquo; responded Brazos, coming to himself. There they stood, these unfortunate twins, holding his hands. It was a beautiful wonderful fact. Two pairs of tawny eyes instead of one pair gazed up at him with a soft warm light of faith, of sympathy. That was the moment Brazos Keene had wanted; that was the spark which set him afire. Brazos enfolded both of these little white hands in his own hard ones and held them tight. &ldquo;June an&rsquo; Janis,&rdquo; he said, intensely. &ldquo;I didn&rsquo;t just happen to ride along heah. There&rsquo;s somethin&rsquo; back of it. I&rsquo;ve met yore friend Jack Sain. Hank Bilyen is an old pard of mine. He took me oot to meet yore Dad. An&rsquo; I&rsquo;ve heahed yore story.&rdquo; &ldquo;Oh, I hope Dad was not unjust to you,&rdquo; interposed June. &ldquo;He&rsquo;s so strange&mdash;so dazed&mdash;&mdash;&rdquo; &ldquo;Yore father is a fine man&mdash;a real Westerner. He&rsquo;s not old or broken. Only down, heartbroken under these blows.... Would it make yu glad, girls, to know he shore wasn&rsquo;t strange or dazed when I left him?&rdquo; &ldquo;Oh!&rdquo; cried June. &ldquo;Brazos Keene! We&rsquo;ve heard all about you,&rdquo; cried Janis. &ldquo;We wouldn&rsquo;t wonder at anything. But if you woke Dad out of his lethargy&mdash;if you roused his old fighting spirit&mdash;cheered him a little, as Allen tried so hard to do and couldn&rsquo;t do.... Oh, Brazos Keene, June and I will worship you!&rdquo; &ldquo;Wal, it looks like I done thet already&mdash;so yu may just begin thet worshipin&rsquo; right heah,&rdquo; drawled Brazos, with the slow smile that so transformed him. &ldquo;You have!&rdquo; they cried, in unison. &ldquo;I told yore Dad I was pretty shore aboot what had become of the herd of Texas longhorns he sold to Surface, but lost on the trail north.&rdquo; &ldquo;Oh, are you sure?&rdquo; entreated Janis, squeezing his hand. June gazed at him mutely, as if he were a phenomenon too good to be true. &ldquo;Wal, I&rsquo;d gamble on it.&rdquo; &ldquo;That is what Allen was trying to find out. He confided in me.&rdquo; &ldquo;Ahuh. Then yore brother was workin&rsquo; on this deal?&rdquo; rejoined Brazos ponderingly. &ldquo;Allen swore he&rsquo;d never rest until he&rsquo;d got our&mdash;our Twin Sombreros Ranch back again,&rdquo; answered Janis, with tears brimming her eyes. Brazos succumbed to the moment, realizing its inevitableness, and that perhaps he was swearing away his life. But the bewitching nearness of these girls, the sense of a great part he had been destined to play in their lives, magnified all his old spirit to do, his reckless disregard of self. He drew the girls closer over the counter, while he flashed a wary look around. &ldquo;Listen,&rdquo; he whispered. &ldquo;An&rsquo; keep this secret in yore pretty haids.... I&rsquo;m gonna track down the murderers of yore brother&mdash;an&rsquo; kill them! An&rsquo; what&rsquo;s more, I&rsquo;m gonna run Surface oot of the ranch he stole from yore Dad an&rsquo; put yu back there!&rdquo; Brazos, inspired by what he felt and much he could not understand, had exaggerated hope, daring, resolve. Once spoken aloud, the pledge seemed outrageous. But the girls gave him no reprieve, no chance to make provisions. They took him intensely, with deadly earnest, their faces paling to pearl hue and their eyes dilating. He might have been looking at just one girl, so incredibly similar were they. Neece&rsquo;s daughters were new to the West, but they were part of it. They had been east long enough to share the tenderfoot&rsquo;s glamorous regard for a cowboy desperado, but were Western enough not to doubt or fear it. The street door banged, interrupting whatever eloquent acceptance appeared to tremble on the sweet lips of the Neece twins. Jack Sain came tramping in, his sleepy eyes alight, his smile infectious. &ldquo;Howdy, Brazos. I see you&rsquo;ve got acquainted without my help,&rdquo; he remarked, as the girls withdrew their hands from Brazos&rsquo; grip. &ldquo;Jack, we introduced ourselves all around,&rdquo; replied Janis, gayly. June was silent, though she smiled at the young man. &ldquo;Wal, Sain, heah yu air, an&rsquo; I clean forgot yu,&rdquo; drawled Brazos. &ldquo;Yes, I&rsquo;ve met yore friends an&rsquo; am I glad? I&rsquo;ve been tellin&rsquo; them what tough luck I&rsquo;d been playin&rsquo; in not to be heah long ago. An&rsquo; thet I&rsquo;d shore make up for it.&rdquo; &ldquo;Jack, this Brazos cowboy is not so slow,&rdquo; said Janis, teasingly. &ldquo;Slow! Never in this world could you apply that word to Brazos Keene. I see he&rsquo;s perked you up already. An&rsquo; I&rsquo;m darned glad.&rdquo; &ldquo;Wal, I&rsquo;m kinda glad myself,&rdquo; said Brazos. &ldquo;Let&rsquo;s get our order in before the gang comes rollin&rsquo; along,&rdquo; suggested Sain. &ldquo;Order? Gang? Say, am I loco?&rdquo; ejaculated Brazos, mildly. &ldquo;You are, an&rsquo; it tickles me. I&rsquo;ll bet you couldn&rsquo;t ask June or Jan to serve you ham an&rsquo; eggs&mdash;not to save your life.&rdquo; &ldquo;Ham an&rsquo; eggs? Wal, I&rsquo;ll be doggoned! No, I cain&rsquo;t.&rdquo; &ldquo;Boys, what will you have?&rdquo; asked one of the twins. The other had turned to the vanguard of hungry visitors now flocking from all directions. &ldquo;Aw, air you June or Janis?&rdquo; queried Brazos, helplessly. &ldquo;Never mind. Either of us can serve you. But hurry. We will be swamped soon.&rdquo; &ldquo;I was starvin&rsquo; to death when I come in, but now I cain&rsquo;t eat,&rdquo; declared Brazos, eloquently. &ldquo;Ha! Ha! Didn&rsquo;t I have a hunch?&rdquo; laughed Sain, evidently delighted at something not apparent to Brazos. &ldquo;I&rsquo;ll order double. Steak medium, mashed potatoes an&rsquo; gravy, bread an&rsquo; butter, coffee.... An&rsquo; tell that Mexican cook Billy the Kid is out here.&rdquo; While the restaurant rapidly filled with a crowd of various types and the young waitresses flitted to and fro from customers to kitchen and back again Brazos listened to his voluble friend and eagerly watched for June without any hope whatever of being able to tell which of the twins really was June. When, however, Sain gave Brazos a dig in the ribs with his elbow, Brazos came out of his trance. &ldquo;Say, cowboy, go easy. I&rsquo;ve been pitched off hawses too often to have ribs of iron.&rdquo; &ldquo;Look behind you&mdash;at thet handsome dressed-up dude rancher,&rdquo; whispered Sain. &ldquo;At the table.&rdquo; &ldquo;Ahuh. Wal...&rdquo; replied Brazos, leisurely complying. &ldquo;Kinda spick an&rsquo; span, at thet. But he&rsquo;s got a nice face. Who is he?&rdquo; &ldquo;Henry Sisk, an&rsquo; he has a nice face I&rsquo;m bound to admit. Too damn nice! Women like him a heap.&rdquo; &ldquo;Don&rsquo;t blame them. How aboot June an&rsquo; Janis heah?&rdquo; &ldquo;June couldn&rsquo;t see him with a telescope. But I got a hunch Jan likes him. Anyway, it&rsquo;s Jan he &rsquo;pears to be courtin&rsquo;.&rdquo; &ldquo;How&rsquo;n hell does he know which one he&rsquo;s courtin&rsquo;?&rdquo; &ldquo;He doesn&rsquo;t, unless they tell him, you can bet your roll on thet.&rdquo; Sain reddened perceptibly, but was not ashamed of it. &ldquo;I don&rsquo;t. Only the girls are decent enough to give me a hunch.&rdquo; &ldquo;Gosh! What&rsquo;d yu do if they didn&rsquo;t steer yu?&rdquo; &ldquo;Brazos, I&rsquo;d be a plumb crazy cowboy, believe me. But don&rsquo;t get a wrong notion. Both June an&rsquo; Janis have been friendly to me. Thet&rsquo;s all. I never even had nerve enough to hold June&rsquo;s hand. They&rsquo;re not the flirtin&rsquo; kind, Brazos.&rdquo; &ldquo;So I see. Wal, how aboot this Henry Sisk? Is he a decent hombre?&rdquo; &ldquo;Yes. I&rsquo;m jealous, I reckon. Henry is young, good-lookin&rsquo;, rich, an&rsquo; a fine fellow.&rdquo; Brazos gave his heavy belt a hitch and took several slow strides over to the table where young Sisk sat, glowering at no one in particular. His frank face and dark eyes impressed Brazos favorably. &ldquo;Howdy, Sisk,&rdquo; said Brazos. &ldquo;My pard heah told me who yu air. I&rsquo;m Brazos Keene.&rdquo; &ldquo;How do. I saw you when I came in,&rdquo; returned the young rancher, not exactly incivilly though certainly awkwardly. He was surprised and aghast. But he put out a hand willingly enough. &ldquo;I&rsquo;m wonderin&rsquo; if yu need a rider,&rdquo; replied Brazos, after the grip. &ldquo;I always need a rider who can work.&rdquo; &ldquo;Doggone! Work isn&rsquo;t my long suit,&rdquo; drawled Brazos, with his captivating smile. &ldquo;I cain&rsquo;t rope very wal, an&rsquo; I&rsquo;m no good atall at most cowboy jobs, an&rsquo; I&rsquo;d just starve before I&rsquo;d dig fence-post holes.... But if I do say it myself, I&rsquo;m pretty fair with guns.&rdquo; &ldquo;Brazos, you&rsquo;re that thing impossible to find&mdash;a modest cowboy,&rdquo; said Sisk, laughing. &ldquo;If you&rsquo;re serious, ride out to see me.&rdquo; &ldquo;Thanks, I&rsquo;ll do thet some day,&rdquo; concluded Brazos, and returned to his seat beside young Sain. That worthy gave Brazos an inquiring look. At this moment one of the girls brought a generous golden slice of apple pie and a large glass of creamy milk. Brazos stared from these to the charming waitress. &ldquo;Do my pore eyes deceive me?&rdquo; &ldquo;Didn&rsquo;t you order apple pie and milk?&rdquo; she asked, regretfully. &ldquo;I shore did. But I never even dreamed of such pie an&rsquo; milk. Will yu duplicate the order, Miss Janis?&rdquo; Brazos found his favorite dessert and drink even more delicious than they looked. Both had disappeared as if by magic when the girl returned to set another plate of pie and brimming glass of milk before him. &ldquo;Miss Janis, all I want to know is can I come in heah as often as I want an&rsquo; get a gorgeous supper like what I&rsquo;ve had?&rdquo; asked Brazos, most impressively. &ldquo;Why, certainly&mdash;so long as you pay for it,&rdquo; she replied, trying to keep her face straight. &ldquo;But I&rsquo;m broke a good deal. Money slips right through my fingers.&rdquo; &ldquo;This is a strictly cash business, Mr. Keene,&rdquo; she said, demurely. &ldquo;Mexican Joe trusts me,&rdquo; importuned Brazos. &ldquo;Aw, Miss Janis, I shore wouldn&rsquo;t want to be exclooded from this heah lovely place just on account of bein&rsquo; financially embarrassed now an&rsquo; then.&rdquo; &ldquo;Have you any references as to&mdash;to good credit and character?&rdquo; she asked, mischievously. &ldquo;If you will bring these, we shall be glad to trust you.... And by the way, I am not Janis, but June.&rdquo; &ldquo;Help!&rdquo; prayed Brazos, fervently, throwing up his hands. Sain had heard this byplay, and he was all grin. &ldquo;There ain&rsquo;t any help,&rdquo; he said. &ldquo;What&rsquo;s a fellow gonna do?&rdquo; implored Brazos, honestly aghast at an unparalleled situation. &ldquo;I reckon the only thing a fellar can do is impossible,&rdquo; returned Sain, sagely, from which cryptic remark Brazos gathered no solace. They finished their dessert and had to wait to pay their bill. &ldquo;Wal, June,&rdquo; said Brazos, taking a chance on this sister and apparently hitting it right, &ldquo;how much do we owe for thet scrumptious supper&mdash;an&rsquo; do we have to stand around on the corner ootside till mawnin&rsquo; to see yu again?&rdquo; &ldquo;Dollar eighty,&rdquo; replied June, as she took the bill he extended. &ldquo;Twenty! Yes, you&rsquo;re broke.&rdquo; &ldquo;Gosh, is thet a twenty?&rdquo; While June went to get change, Brazos saw Janis carry a tray to Sisk&rsquo;s table. There was no trouble in ascertaining the state of that young man&rsquo;s mind. Janis might have been in the solitude of the outside prairie for all he cared. He raised a strong earnest appealing face to the girl. And when she had set the several dishes before him, Brazos&rsquo; sharp eye caught him plucking at her hand. Janis smiled down upon him, but shook her head and left him. &ldquo;Heah you air, Mister Brazos Keene,&rdquo; drawled June at Brazos&rsquo; elbow. She imitated his Texas accent perfectly, and did it with a straight face. Brazos held out his hand to take the change. &ldquo;Say, Lady, I&rsquo;ve handled girls turrible rough for less than thet,&rdquo; he said. &ldquo;Pooh!... There&rsquo;s your change. I hope you will become a good customer.&rdquo; &ldquo;June, I&rsquo;ll eat myself to death,&rdquo; he rejoined, softly. &ldquo;When can I see yu again?&rdquo; &ldquo;Breakfast time.&rdquo; &ldquo;But listen. There&rsquo;s nothin&rsquo; for me to do heah. Only the saloons an&rsquo; cairds. An&rsquo; yu know, I&rsquo;m Brazos Keene, doggone it! There&rsquo;s always some hombre layin&rsquo; to shoot me in the back.&rdquo; That was a happy lead for the cunning Brazos to take. It was no falsehood and he had worked it often. June&rsquo;s eyes dilated. She gazed up at him, unconscious that she had already taken possession of him. &ldquo;We are off at ten. I&rsquo;d like you to meet my aunt. She is Dad&rsquo;s sister, and lives with us upstairs.&rdquo; &ldquo;Thet&rsquo;s just fine. I&rsquo;ll come.&rdquo; &ldquo;Jack, you&rsquo;ll come, too?&rdquo; &ldquo;I&rsquo;m sorry, June. I can&rsquo;t tonight.&rdquo; When they got outside and Brazos went to the hitching rail to untie Bay, he glanced back into the restaurant. June still stood there, as if alone in the crowded room, her big eyes dark upon him. They made Brazos&rsquo; heart leap, and then they sobered him out of selfish exultation. Had he misled this girl and her sister to hope vainly? He cursed himself for a reckless cowboy. &ldquo;Jack, where&rsquo;s yore hawse?&rdquo; he asked, gruffly. &ldquo;I don&rsquo;t own one.&rdquo; &ldquo;Hell! A cowboy without a hawse!... We gotta remedy thet. I&rsquo;ll get one from Hank. Where yu live?&rdquo; &ldquo;Out of town. Not far,&rdquo; replied Sain, evasively. &ldquo;It was doggone good to meet yu, Jack. I kinda like yu,&rdquo; said Brazos, as he tightened his cinch. &ldquo;We gotta see a lot of each other.&rdquo; &ldquo;Well, cowboy, if you have ever been withoot a job or a hoss or a friend, you&rsquo;ll know how I feel,&rdquo; rejoined Sain, and bidding Brazos good night, he strode away up the dimly lighted street. &ldquo;Wal. Thet boy&rsquo;s in trouble more&rsquo;n aboot a girl,&rdquo; soliloquized Brazos, as he swung up on Bay. &ldquo;Doggone it, there&rsquo;s nothin&rsquo; but trouble in this world. Heah I am deep in again an&rsquo; lovin&rsquo; it!&rdquo; Brazos rode out to the stable where Bilyen kept his horse while in town, and turning Bay over to the boy there, he drifted slowly up the street. Brazos could never control his heart but he had long been absolute master over his mind. And it behooved him to relegate this sweet insidious emotion to the background and begin cudgeling his brains. The main street of Las Animas had always boasted of its numerous saloons, gambling dens and dance halls. These did not appear to be so numerous or so prosperous as during the cattle drives of 1874 and later, but there were plenty left, Brazos thought, and some as disreputable looking as they had ever been. Brazos strolled up one side of the long wide street and down on the other. It took him just the half-hour required for this walk to decide on his line of procedure. After all, it seemed to Brazos, vital things were decided for him. With how many thousand crooked men had he rubbed elbows? What was it that made him gravitate toward the crux of a situation? Brazos asked himself many questions that he never answered. He had a supreme faith in something that led him on. All he had to do was to ride the range, and walk this wide street of Las Animas, and hang around the drinking dives and gambling dens, and the stores and corners. Watching and listening had become second nature with Brazos. When he got a scent he would track it like a hound. Allen Neece had been murdered. His father had been robbed. These black deeds seemed bracketed together. There had been a plot against the Neeces, whether simple or complex Brazos had not clarified in his mind. On the moment it looked pretty simple to Brazos. All he had to do was to find a man named Brad or Bard and a companion with a high-pitched nervous voice. That might happen any time, but Brazos did not delude himself. A rotten deal like this would have many little clues, all leading to the main issue, which was the theft of cattle on a gigantic scale. At length, Brazos entered a saloon named Happy Days, which was a new one to him. But the garish furnishings, the noisy drinkers and the smell of rum were far from unfamiliar. The hour was too early for the gamesters. Brazos&rsquo; entrance did not appear to be noted, but though he held aloof from bar and guests, he was seen and recognized. He did not need to pretend that he was looking for someone. He actually was, though he did not know whom, and his presence had a quieting effect upon the inmates. From this place he passed along the street taking in the several saloons. Returning on the opposite side, he entered the Call You gambling den, a house that had once been pretentious and patronized by the range elect, but which now was the most sordid he had seen. He found the bar crowded and the gaming tables mostly occupied. At one sat a gambler who would have attracted a less keen eye than Brazos&rsquo;. His pale cold face, enhanced by his dark frock coat, drew attention to himself in a saloon full of range-garbed men. He espied Brazos not many seconds after Brazos singled him out. His glance held more than the cold curiosity of the gambler. Brazos accosted a cowboy on the way out. &ldquo;Say, bud, who&rsquo;s the cairdsharp?&rdquo; he asked. &ldquo;Reckon they&rsquo;re all sharps in this camp.... Thet&rsquo;s Howard.&rdquo; &ldquo;Where does he hail from?&rdquo; &ldquo;Denver, they say.&rdquo; &ldquo;Howard.... Is he the only flash gambler hereaboots?&rdquo; &ldquo;You don&rsquo;t see many like him. An&rsquo; he drops in from the train pretty often.&rdquo; &ldquo;I&rsquo;m a stranger heah,&rdquo; explained Brazos. &ldquo;Wal, if you wasn&rsquo;t you wouldn&rsquo;t be askin&rsquo; questions about Howard.&rdquo; &ldquo;Yeah? An&rsquo; why not?&rdquo; &ldquo;He ain&rsquo;t partial to curiosity.&rdquo; &ldquo;Aw, I see. Ladies&rsquo; man, huh?&rdquo; &ldquo;Cowboy, he&rsquo;s a killer in more ways than one. Haw! Haw!&rdquo; Brazos strolled unobtrusively around until he reached a point behind Howard. The gambler sat at table with three cowboys, playing poker, and he appeared to be ahead of the game. &ldquo;Excuse me, but I object to any one standing behind me,&rdquo; presently spoke up the gambler, politely. &ldquo;Wal, thet&rsquo;s no wonder,&rdquo; drawled Brazos. &ldquo;I&rsquo;ll go around on this side.&rdquo; &ldquo;What you hintin&rsquo; at, cowboy?&rdquo; growled one of the three youths at the table, gazing irritably up at Brazos. &ldquo;Gosh, I wasn&rsquo;t hintin&rsquo;.&rdquo; &ldquo;Hell you wasn&rsquo;t. You better&mdash;&mdash;&rdquo; &ldquo;Shut up, before you say somethin&rsquo;,&rdquo; fiercely interrupted the player next to the disgruntled one. &ldquo;Don&rsquo;t you have no idee who you&rsquo;re talkin&rsquo; to?&rdquo; After that the three cowboys were apparently blind to Brazos&rsquo; presence, but the gambler was not, though he did not look up directly. Brazos watched him steadily, studied his features, his frock coat, his white, wonderfully dexterous hands. At length the gambler queried sarcastically: &ldquo;Don&rsquo;t you want to sit in?&rdquo; &ldquo;Game&rsquo;s too slow for me.&rdquo; &ldquo;You appear mighty interested in watching it. I must ask you to sit in or get out.&rdquo; &ldquo;Wal, I&rsquo;ll tell you, mister,&rdquo; replied Brazos. &ldquo;I&rsquo;d like to join yu, but it sorta riles me to play cairds with a cairdsharp when he&rsquo;s got a little gun hid high up under his coat on the left side.&rdquo; The gambler dropped his cards, and his hand quivered while his eyes blazed cold fury upon Brazos. &ldquo;Careful, Howard,&rdquo; interposed the eldest of the three cowboys. &ldquo;Don&rsquo;t be crazy enough to draw on thet fellar.&rdquo; &ldquo;Who is he?&rdquo; snapped the gambler, perceptibly relaxing. &ldquo;Wal, I ain&rsquo;t sayin&rsquo;. He might be Billy the Kid.&rdquo; &ldquo;Brazos Keene&rsquo;s my name, if you want to know,&rdquo; said Brazos. At this juncture, the losing member of the cowboy triangle leaped up, red of face, to slam his cards down on the table. &ldquo;Whoever he is, he&rsquo;s busted up this game,&rdquo; he said. &ldquo;An&rsquo; a damn good thing,&rdquo; agreed the third. They abruptly left the gambler sitting alone. He raked in the few chips and little silver that his fellow players had abandoned. It was obvious that the presence of Brazos and the attention thus attracted to himself were distinctly unwelcome. &ldquo;Brazos Keene, eh?&rdquo; he queried at length, sitting back to flash brilliant icy eyes upon Brazos. &ldquo;Yeah. An&rsquo; I heahed one of yore victims call you Howard.&rdquo; &ldquo;That&rsquo;s my name,&rdquo; replied the gambler, curtly. &ldquo;From up Denver way, I heah?&rdquo; &ldquo;Where I hail from and what I do doesn&rsquo;t concern you, cowboy.&rdquo; &ldquo;Wal, I&rsquo;m not so damn shore aboot the last.&rdquo; &ldquo;Were those fellows friends of yours?&rdquo; &ldquo;Never saw them before. It was the way yu hide the little derringer gun thet riled me. I just naturally get cussed when I see thet kind of gun-packin&rsquo;.&rdquo; &ldquo;So you broke up my game.&rdquo; &ldquo;I didn&rsquo;t intend to do thet. Yu talked powerful sarcastic, so I thought I&rsquo;d call yu an&rsquo; see if yu&rsquo;d throw yore toy pistol,&rdquo; drawled Brazos, with glinting eyes that did not match his soft voice. The gambler&rsquo;s face turned a shade grayer, either from anger or realization of what he had escaped. &ldquo;Curiosity has cost many men their lives.&rdquo; &ldquo;Shore. But not men like me. An&rsquo; now I&rsquo;ll tell yu thet I had another reason for tryin&rsquo; yu oot.&rdquo; &ldquo;I had a hunch you did. And what is it, Mister Keene?&rdquo; Brazos leaned close to the thin-lipped cold face and answered low: &ldquo;Ask Lura Surface.&rdquo; It was a random shot that Brazos hazarded, but it went home. Howard growled his surprise and wrath, and turning on his heel strode by the watching line of curious men to the bar, where he ordered whisky. Brazos backed against the wall and leaned there. He kept a narrowed gaze upon the gambler until he left the saloon. From sundry remarks which came to Brazos&rsquo; ears he gathered that he had not hurt his status there by offending Howard. Brazos pondered over the amaze and rancor Howard had evidenced. Indeed there must be something between Lura Surface and this handsome cardsharp who secreted a small gun in his breast pocket where he could snatch it swiftly. Something that probably was to Miss Surface&rsquo;s discredit. Brazos had nothing against her. He really liked her for her defense of him. Nevertheless, in his cunning speculative mind she was being relegated to a place where she could not be considered with feeling. Brazos decided, however, to give her the benefit of a doubt. A little before ten o&rsquo;clock, Brazos wended a reluctant and yet impelled way toward the Twin Sombreros Restaurant. He could not have resisted the urge if he had wanted to. And he fought off the presage of calamity. When he arrived at the corner, he espied one of the twins talking to Henry Sisk. Indeed the two were arguing if not actually quarreling, from which fact Brazos deduced that this was Janis. There appeared to be several customers who were being waited upon by a Mexican girl. Brazos mounted the side stairway leading up to the second story and knocked on the door, sure of the trepidation and another nameless sensation obsessing him. The door opened as if someone had heard his step outside. June stood there, in a white dress that had never been made in Las Animas. This apparition smiled upon him and Brazos dated his abject enthrallment from that moment. As always with him, when a thing was settled, inevitable, he found his cool easy poise. &ldquo;Evenin&rsquo;, Miss June. I reckon I&rsquo;m ahaid of time,&rdquo; he said. &ldquo;No. You are late. Come in.&rdquo; She ushered Brazos into a cosy bright little sitting room. &ldquo;Auntie, this is our new-found friend, Mr. Brazos Keene,&rdquo; she said to a gray-haired woman who sat beside the lamp table. &ldquo;My Aunt Mattie, Miss Neece&mdash;Daddy&rsquo;s sister.&rdquo; Brazos made the lady a recipient of his most gallant bow and pleasantest smile. &ldquo;I shore am glad to meet yu, Miss Neece,&rdquo; he drawled, as he bent to take the hand she hesitatingly offered. &ldquo;Yu favor yore brother an&rsquo; I&rsquo;d have known yu.&rdquo; &ldquo;For the land&rsquo;s sake!... June, this nice-looking boy can&rsquo;t be your terrible Brazos Keene,&rdquo; exclaimed the aunt. &ldquo;Yes, he is, Auntie.&rdquo; &ldquo;Aw, Miss Neece, don&rsquo;t believe everythin&rsquo; yu heah,&rdquo; implored Brazos, honestly. &ldquo;I&rsquo;m not turrible atall.&rdquo; &ldquo;I don&rsquo;t believe you are. I&rsquo;m glad to meet you. Janis filled my old head with nonsense. Said you were a black-browed giant&mdash;very fierce to see.&rdquo; &ldquo;Air yu shore it was Janis?&rdquo; inquired Brazos. &ldquo;Yes, indeed. June has been telling me the... well, I&rsquo;ll not give her away. But your ears must have burned.... Take his hat, June.... And hadn&rsquo;t you better lay aside that cumbersome gun?&rdquo; &ldquo;Wal, Lady, I wouldn&rsquo;t feel dressed proper if I did thet.... There, I&rsquo;ll slip it around so you cain&rsquo;t see it.&rdquo; &ldquo;Thank you, I&mdash;I guess that&rsquo;s better,&rdquo; she replied, rising. &ldquo;Mr. Keene, you met my brother Abraham?&rdquo; &ldquo;I did, an&rsquo; I shore like him.&rdquo; &ldquo;June tells me you cheered him up,&rdquo; she went on, in hurried earnestness. &ldquo;And Dave Wesley called on me this afternoon. He had just ridden by Bilyen&rsquo;s ranch. He said he had not seen my brother so near like his old self. Oh, if you are responsible for that, I thank you.&rdquo; &ldquo;Lady, I&rsquo;m afraid I am responsible,&rdquo; rejoined Brazos, seriously. &ldquo;An&rsquo; I shore hope I didn&rsquo;t overdo it.&rdquo; &ldquo;Have you any ground to believe Abraham&rsquo;s loss can be retrieved?&rdquo; she asked, beseechingly. &ldquo;I cain&rsquo;t explain. It&rsquo;s what a cowboy calls a hunch. I&rsquo;ve trailed up a good many of my hunches an&rsquo; never lost oot on one yet.&rdquo; &ldquo;Only a hunch! Oh, I had prayed you might have really learned something,&rdquo; she returned, sadly. &ldquo;Miss Neece, I cain&rsquo;t talk aboot it now. All I can say is for yu to go on hopin&rsquo; and prayin&rsquo;, too.&rdquo; &ldquo;Perhaps Abraham will tell me. I&rsquo;ll see him tomorrow. Good night, Mr. Brazos Keene. Somehow you inspire me strangely.... June, I&rsquo;ll leave you young folks alone. Good night, dear.&rdquo; Brazos found himself alone with June Neece; and his five endless years of wandering for he knew not what were as if they had never been. &ldquo;You must understand Auntie, and Jan and me,&rdquo; said June, gravely. &ldquo;It is not the loss of Dad&rsquo;s fortune, and Twin Sombreros Ranch that hurts so terribly. It was Dad&rsquo;s broken heart. All these years he had worked for us. The blow crushed him. And he was sinking under it.... Then Allen&rsquo;s sudden ghastly death...&rdquo; Her face was white and her big eyes shone darkly tragic up at him. &ldquo;Never mind, June,&rdquo; interposed Brazos, feelingly. &ldquo;I reckon I understand. It&rsquo;s hard. But you must bear up.... I&rsquo;ve had my grief. An&rsquo; I&rsquo;m a livin&rsquo; proof thet grief passes&mdash;an&rsquo; thet joy an&rsquo; hope come back.&rdquo; &ldquo;You have had trouble?&rdquo; she asked, softly. &ldquo;For five years I&rsquo;ve been a driven cowboy... an&rsquo; my trouble came to an end today&mdash;when I met yu.&rdquo; &ldquo;Me?... Oh!&rdquo; She warmed wonderingly to that. &ldquo;Tell me your story.&rdquo; &ldquo;Some day, when I reckon I dare.&rdquo; They stood by the table, with glances locked, and constraint overcoming the simplicity of that meeting. June turned away with a blush, only to be drawn again to look at him, as if to make sure the situation was real. &ldquo;Wal, I hope it&rsquo;s goin&rsquo; to be good for yu thet I am Brazos Keene,&rdquo; returned he, mournfully. &ldquo;But maybe if I was Henry Sisk or Jack Sain I would have more chance for you to like me.&rdquo; &ldquo;Wal, what do I look, then?&rdquo; he queried, a little gruffly, for once weakening to permit discussion of this delicate subject. &ldquo;I haven&rsquo;t really dared to look at you&mdash;close,&rdquo; she rejoined, shyly. &ldquo;Come here, your back is to the light.... There. Brazos, at the risk of seeming a flatterer like Lura Surface, I must say you&rsquo;re a stunning-looking cowboy. You&rsquo;ve a clean tanned, boyish, handsome face&mdash;nice curly hair almost blond, the kind any girl would like to run her fingers through.... Oh, Brazos! It&rsquo;s a little gray over your temples!... And your eyes take something from your winning smile and soft Southern drawl.&rdquo; &ldquo;My Gawd, June, you must have kissed the blarney stone. Now, what&rsquo;s wrong with my eyes?&rdquo; &ldquo;Nothing. Janis said they were gray. Now I see they&rsquo;re blue. You&rsquo;re making sheep&rsquo;s eyes at me now, Brazos Keene. But you can&rsquo;t fool me. Those eyes could be terrible.&rdquo; &ldquo;Could they? But I&rsquo;m comin&rsquo; back at yu, June Neece. There&rsquo;s nothin&rsquo; wrong with yu atall. An&rsquo; to say yu&rsquo;re the prettiest girl I ever saw in my life isn&rsquo;t sayin&rsquo; what I mean. I reckon it&rsquo;s the class yu was born with an&rsquo; somethin&rsquo; yu got away at school.&rdquo; &ldquo;Yu mean we&rsquo;re gettin&rsquo; some place where I&rsquo;ve no right to be?&rdquo; &ldquo;Come sit here,&rdquo; she returned, and led him to a little sofa in the corner. They gazed at each other again, questioningly, yet with no hint of doubt or uncertainty. There was something vital, compelling, drawing, that made no allowance for short acquaintance. &ldquo;June, I&rsquo;m gonna be honest. Meetin&rsquo; yu has thrown me plumb oot of my saddle.&rdquo; &ldquo;It means much to me, Brazos&mdash;I don&rsquo;t know what.&rdquo; &ldquo;Yu&rsquo;re countin&rsquo; on yore hopes of what I can do for yore Dad?&rdquo; &ldquo;Yes. But if we were out home&mdash;at Twin Sombreros&mdash;and if we had no trouble&mdash;I&mdash;think I would feel the same.&rdquo; &ldquo;Girl, yu cain&rsquo;t be in love with Jack Sain?&rdquo; &ldquo;Who said I was?&rdquo; she answered, smiling. &ldquo;I like Jack. We played together when we were kids.&rdquo; &ldquo;Wal, I was afraid&mdash;I reckon I thought yu might care more&rsquo;n thet. Jack is crazy aboot yu, which is no wonder.&rdquo; &ldquo;I&rsquo;m sorry, Brazos. But I didn&rsquo;t flirt with him as Jan did with Henry Sisk. I&rsquo;m sorry for Jack in more ways than one. He has had one misfortune after another. And the last is too bad. He had just found a good job after being idle for months, then lost it.&rdquo; &ldquo;How&rsquo;d he lose it?&rdquo; &ldquo;Al said he was running after Lura Surface. Her father caught them meeting on the road one night. He raised Cain and had Jack discharged.&rdquo; &ldquo;Ahuh. The Surfaces don&rsquo;t mix up in this thing&mdash;Aw, no, not atall.... June, I&rsquo;m gonna meet thet young lady oot along the road, tomorrow afternoon. But I&rsquo;m not runnin&rsquo; after her.&rdquo; &ldquo;Already! You? I thought you might be one man proof&mdash;&mdash;&rdquo; Brazos took June&rsquo;s hands in his and bent to interrupt her. &ldquo;Listen, June. If I hadn&rsquo;t met yu, I&rsquo;d have flirted with Lura Surface. But I have met yu&mdash;an&rsquo; thet&rsquo;s all the difference in the world.... Raine Surface is one of these respectable crooked cattlemen. I&rsquo;ve met a few like him&mdash;to their sorrow, an&rsquo; my idee in meetin&rsquo; this girl is to get at things. Thet&rsquo;s my way, June. I cain&rsquo;t explain. But I follow my hunches. Please believe me, June.&rdquo; &ldquo;Never mind what I was. It&rsquo;s what I am now thet should count with yu. Trust me, June.&rdquo; &ldquo;It&rsquo;s none of my business,&rdquo; said June, distantly, her face averted. Brazos gave her a little shake and pulled her closer, scarcely master of himself under the sweet and bewildering feelings she had betrayed. She had made no attempt to withdraw her hands, and when he drew her so close that her cheek brushed his shoulder still she did not repulse him. &ldquo;June, it&rsquo;s all yore business. If yu won&rsquo;t trust me aboot this Surface girl I&rsquo;ll never go near her. An&rsquo; by thet we might lose time. For Lura Surface, withoot knowin&rsquo;, will give me hunches.&rdquo; &ldquo;But she hates Jan an&rsquo; me,&rdquo; declared June, with heat. &ldquo;Jan hates her&mdash;and I&rsquo;m going to hate her.&rdquo; &ldquo;Why does Janis hate her?&rdquo; &ldquo;She made a fool out of Allen. That happened before we came home.&rdquo; &ldquo;Was Allen in love with her?&rdquo; &ldquo;Yes.&rdquo; &ldquo;Wal, thet lady &rsquo;pears to have a trade rat&rsquo;s instinct to collect&mdash;only she doesn&rsquo;t trade.&rdquo; &ldquo;There you&rsquo;re wrong, Brazos. She does trade&mdash;if you mean kiss for kiss, and all that.&rdquo; &ldquo;How do yu know?&rdquo; &ldquo;Women don&rsquo;t have to be told such things. We just know.&rdquo; &ldquo;Ahuh,&rdquo; rejoined Brazos. He was thrillingly aware of June resting her cheek ever so slightly against his shoulder, and yearned to prolong the disturbing contact. &ldquo;Jan and I tried to be decent to Lura Surface,&rdquo; went on June. &ldquo;We didn&rsquo;t hold anything against her because she lived in our home. And she is pretty and fascinating, even to girls. But she stuck up her nose at us. One day she came into the restaurant with Henry Sisk. Jan and I ignored them. Would not wait upon them. Then Henry came to the counter and wanted to know why he could not get any service. It was Jan he approached. And did Jan tell him? Right then and there Lura Surface lost him.&rdquo; &ldquo;A handsome cat,&rdquo; muttered Brazos, to himself. &ldquo;June, yu&rsquo;d hate me, I reckon. An&rsquo; shore with reason. But isn&rsquo;t this because of the new hope thet I may be able to help yore Dad?&rdquo; &ldquo;Yes. Yet it&rsquo;s only fair to tell you that&rsquo;s not the only reason.&rdquo; She had paled again and her eyes were eloquent. Brazos dared not ask that reason. The sweeter she grew, the more restraint he put upon himself. Then June withdrew her hands. &ldquo;Wal, I wouldn&rsquo;t,&rdquo; interposed Brazos, smiling. &ldquo;Shore yu&rsquo;ve been sweet an&rsquo; gracious. Yu make me proud, June. An&rsquo; I&rsquo;m askin&rsquo; yu. Yore brother Allen was a cowboy. Now if he&rsquo;d swore on his honor, wouldn&rsquo;t yu have trusted him?&rdquo; &ldquo;Indeed I would have, Brazos. Allen didn&rsquo;t make many promises, but you could rely on him.&rdquo; &ldquo;Wal, I&rsquo;m like Allen, June. I give yu my word. An&rsquo; if yu won&rsquo;t take it, I&rsquo;ll be hurt powerful deep.&rdquo; &ldquo;Your word for what?&rdquo; she flashed, dark eyes bright and solemn on his. &ldquo;Oh, I wouldn&rsquo;t want Dad to think&mdash;&mdash;&rdquo; &ldquo;I couldn&rsquo;t fool Hank Bilyen, an&rsquo; he&rsquo;ll explain to Neece. I reckon Kiskadden will savvy, too. An&rsquo; thet Texan will help me, June.... Give me a free rein, girl.&rdquo; &ldquo;Is it so&mdash;so personal, Brazos?&rdquo; she inquired, wistfully. &ldquo;Wouldn&rsquo;t you help Dad to get his property back even if there were no June Neece?&rdquo; &ldquo;How could I help worry? Suppose by some miracle you reinstated Dad at Twin Sombreros Ranch. What would you ask of me?&rdquo; &ldquo;Nothin&rsquo;, June. Yu&rsquo;ve already paid me for thet.&rdquo; &ldquo;How?&rdquo; &ldquo;Why&mdash;by bein&rsquo; so sweet an&rsquo;&mdash;an&rsquo; all. I feel what I mean, but cain&rsquo;t explain.... An&rsquo; June, yu forget I&rsquo;m Brazos Keene&mdash;gunman an&rsquo; killer.&rdquo; &ldquo;Oh, you&rsquo;re either the most wonderful fellow in the world&mdash;or the most brazen devil,&rdquo; she exclaimed, in a quandary. &ldquo;June, I&rsquo;m shore not the first.&rdquo; &ldquo;Yes, you are, Brazos, I don&rsquo;t care what you&rsquo;ve been,&rdquo; she declared suddenly glowing. &ldquo;I don&rsquo;t do things by halves. I&rsquo;ll believe in you, Brazos Keene.&rdquo; Brazos put his hand inside his vest and took out his precious letter. &ldquo;June, shore thet&rsquo;ll be aboot all.&rdquo; Brazos&rsquo; drawl could not entirely subdue the emotion in his voice. &ldquo;Another girl believed in me once. I helped her all I could, but I failed her in thet I never lived up to what she thought me.... I want yu to read this letter from her. Kiskadden had me read it to him. An&rsquo; thet&rsquo;s why he set me free. It&rsquo;ll prove some things to yu, June&mdash;the things yu believed true of me. Maybe yu&rsquo;re the girl Holly speaks of in this letter. I hope to Gawd yu air an&rsquo; thet I can be worthy. But for the job in hand, I&rsquo;m yore man.&rdquo; &ldquo;Holly who?&rdquo; questioned June, wondering, as she received the letter. &ldquo;Did yu ever heah of Holly Ripple?&rdquo; &ldquo;Indeed I did. I remember hearing about her years ago before Jan and I went away to school. She lived out in New Mexico&mdash;on a vast Spanish grant.... Oh, Allen wrote me five or six years ago&mdash;all about Holly Ripple&rsquo;s ranch and her terrible riders. Brazos, could you have been one of them?&rdquo; &ldquo;Wal, I reckon.&rdquo; &ldquo;Holly Ripple!&rdquo; June beheld him with searching woman&rsquo;s eyes. &ldquo;Yes... I rode with that ootfit, June. She called us her Knights of the Range.... Wal&mdash;wal&mdash;we were all in love with her, yu savvy. An&rsquo; the best of us, Renn Frayne, won her love an&rsquo; married her. They have a boy they call Brazos, after me.... But read the letter some time, an&rsquo; keep it safe till yu see me again.... An&rsquo; now let&rsquo;s get down to practical things.&rdquo; &ldquo;Oh, Brazos, can we ever be practical?&rdquo; &ldquo;I&rsquo;ve got to be if yu cain&rsquo;t.... I want to ask some questions aboot Allen. Were yu in his confidence, June?&rdquo; &ldquo;Yes. Allen was afraid to tell Dad what he was doing. And he didn&rsquo;t even tell Jan.&rdquo; &ldquo;Ahuh. Wal, if I figure Allen correct, he was trackin&rsquo; the ootfit thet ruined yore father.&rdquo; &ldquo;He was on the trail of the three men who held Dad up that night and robbed him.&rdquo; &ldquo;Did he tell yu anythin&rsquo;?&rdquo; &ldquo;Not much. Oh, let me recall it,&rdquo; she went on, excitedly. &ldquo;They did not belong around Las Animas. But they rode here often. He had nothing to go by except&mdash;except the night they robbed Dad, one of them&mdash;a boy with a girl&rsquo;s voice called another of the three by the name Brad. Allen said Dad told him that.&rdquo; &ldquo;Yes, June, your Dad told me. And here&rsquo;s the funny part of it. One of the three hombres who held me up thet night called his pard Brad, or a name thet sounded like thet. By Gawd! Those men murdered Allen. He was on their trail.... Did any one else but yu know Allen was workin&rsquo; on yore father&rsquo;s case?&rdquo; &ldquo;Yes. It was found out. I remember Allen was sore because the loungers around town called him the cowboy detective. Allen kept the secret of what he suspected and had learned&mdash;except from me&mdash;but any one could have guessed what he was doing. He was so dark and grim and determined.&rdquo; &ldquo;June, do yu reckon Raine Surface heahed what Allen was up to?&rdquo; &ldquo;Lura would have heard it, surely.&rdquo; &ldquo;Shore, she&rsquo;d tell her father.... June, can yu remember any more Allen told yu?&rdquo; &ldquo;Let me think. Yes... The night before Allen was killed he had supper with me downstairs. It was late, almost time for Jan and me to come off duty. Allen asked me if I&rsquo;d seen a handsome hard-faced cowgirl, small and slim with eyes like black diamonds. She looked the real thing in riders, he said. I told Allen no girl of that description had come into the restaurant. Then he said she had made up to him in the Happy Days saloon. He seemed curious, yet distrustful. But he didn&rsquo;t tell me any more.&rdquo; &ldquo;A cowgirl!... Wal, now I wonder.... An&rsquo; thet&rsquo;s all, June?&rdquo; &ldquo;I wouldn&rsquo;t say all by any means. Only it&rsquo;s all I can remember now. Perhaps when I see you again&mdash;&mdash;&rdquo; &ldquo;Thet&rsquo;ll be in the mawnin&rsquo;, I reckon. But don&rsquo;t worry aboot me. I&rsquo;m takin&rsquo; over Allen&rsquo;s job of huntin&rsquo; for the three hombres who robbed yore Dad&mdash;an&rsquo; murdered Allen&mdash;an&rsquo; held me up.... An&rsquo; shore as death, one of thet three was a girl with a high-keyed voice!&rdquo; &ldquo;Good afternoon, Miss Surface. I shore am sorry to be late,&rdquo; he drawled, and throwing aside his sombrero he sat down and slid to his elbow beside her. The green eyes devoured him, for nothing except her effect upon him. Yet her quick breath, and the quick rise of her breast, betrayed a little shock at his nearness. She was used to men, but not of his stripe, Brazos thought. His gun had bumped her knee. &ldquo;Howdy, Brazos Keene,&rdquo; she said, with a smile that enhanced her hard bright charm. &ldquo;Wal, I do pretty good, considerin&rsquo;,&rdquo; returned Brazos. &ldquo;Cowboys don&rsquo;t often fall into such luck as this.&rdquo; &ldquo;I came early. Had a quarrel with father. But I thought yu&rsquo;d never get here.&rdquo; &ldquo;Wild hawses couldn&rsquo;t have kept me away from yu, Lura.&rdquo; &ldquo;Same old cowboy blarney.&rdquo; &ldquo;Ump-umm. If yu take me for any other cowboy, wal, we won&rsquo;t get nowhere atall.&rdquo; &ldquo;Where will we get if I take you as I did yesterday?&rdquo; &ldquo;An&rsquo; how was thet?&rdquo; &ldquo;A lonely cowboy, down on his luck, unjustly jailed, suspicious of men&mdash;an&rsquo; needing a friend.&rdquo; &ldquo;Thet&rsquo;s takin&rsquo; me true, Lura. But I cain&rsquo;t say I&rsquo;m without friends altogether.&rdquo; &ldquo;You could always get women friends, Brazos.&rdquo; &ldquo;Shore. Thet&rsquo;s my trouble. I almost didn&rsquo;t come today.&rdquo; &ldquo;Why?&rdquo; &ldquo;Wal, yu shore took my eye. An&rsquo; I knew if I saw yu again I&rsquo;d go loco.&rdquo; &ldquo;Loco? What&rsquo;s that? I&rsquo;ve heard the word.&rdquo; &ldquo;Loco is a weed hawses eat sometimes an&rsquo; go oot of their haids.&rdquo; &ldquo;Wal, suppose at thet I did go loco?&rdquo; &ldquo;I&rsquo;d be delighted. You&rsquo;re different, Brazos. Oh, I was sorry when I thought they&rsquo;d hang you! And what a thrill I had yesterday! Scared too? Brazos Keene&mdash;the notorious Brazos Keene!... But I&rsquo;m not so scared now.&rdquo; &ldquo;Gosh, Lady, I&rsquo;m as harmless as a kitten.... So yu&rsquo;d be delighted if I went plumb loco aboot yu?&rdquo; &ldquo;I&rsquo;d love it,&rdquo; she said, slyly, yet with a catch in her voice. Her brazen coquetry had not heretofore been used to subjugate gunmen. &ldquo;Lura Surface, I&rsquo;m not gonna do it. I reckon I&rsquo;ll get over this meetin&rsquo; with yu. But I won&rsquo;t risk another.&rdquo; &ldquo;Oh,&rdquo; she pouted, blushing becomingly. &ldquo;And why not?&rdquo; &ldquo;Yu&rsquo;re just my dish of a woman an&rsquo; if I tasted yu once I&rsquo;d have nothin&rsquo; to look forward to but starvation.&rdquo; &ldquo;I never satisfied any man&rsquo;s hunger yet. But I might&mdash;yours.&rdquo; Brazos sat up, and with swift strong arms he drew her back so that she lay almost flat with her head on his breast. Then he held her, gazing down upon a suddenly paling face and eyes that dilated strangely between fear and desire. Her red lips parted. But she was thoroughbred in that, as she had invited this onslaught, she would not show the white feather. &ldquo;Lura, yu shore oughtn&rsquo;t play at love with a hombre like me.&rdquo; &ldquo;Who says I&rsquo;m playing?&rdquo; &ldquo;Shore yu air. An&rsquo; I&rsquo;ve got sense enough to see it an&rsquo; decency enough to spare yu what many a cowboy I&rsquo;ve known would take.&rdquo; &ldquo;You think I&rsquo;m a flirt?&rdquo; &ldquo;Wal, I never call women names, onless they&rsquo;re nice names.... Yu&rsquo;re powerful seductive, Lura, turrible appealin&rsquo;, an&rsquo; pretty isn&rsquo;t the word. Yu&rsquo;ve got a devastatin&rsquo; kind of beauty.... If I let go of myself now, an&rsquo; fell to kissin&rsquo; yu, as I reckon I might do by force, I&rsquo;d be a gone goslin&rsquo;. I might fall stark ravin&rsquo; mad in love with yu. An&rsquo; where&rsquo;d thet get me, Lura? I&rsquo;m Brazos Keene, only a notch or two behind Billy the Kid in range standin&rsquo;. Yu&rsquo;re daughter of Raine Surface, rich rancher, an&rsquo; yu&rsquo;re the belle of this corner of Colorado. Suppose such a wild thing as yore fallin&rsquo; in love with me. Yu couldn&rsquo;t never marry me.&rdquo; &ldquo;I could run away with you,&rdquo; she panted, her eyes like green stars. &ldquo;Wal, thet&rsquo;s too wild for even me to reckon with an&rsquo; yu wouldn&rsquo;t.... So when it comes to Brazos Keene just yu figure thet he&rsquo;s not gonna dream such dreams. At the same time, I&rsquo;d like yu to know it&rsquo;d be damn sweet an&rsquo; wonderful to tear yu to pieces.&rdquo; &ldquo;Well, since you&rsquo;re not going to try&mdash;please let me up,&rdquo; she said, serious over what seemed a greater conquest than she had hoped for. If he resisted her, at least he paid tribute to her charm. And perhaps he grew all the more desirable for that. She sat up, scarlet of face. &ldquo;I wouldn&rsquo;t let yu off thet easy next time,&rdquo; he warned. &ldquo;You are a queer one. What&rsquo;d you meet me for, if not to make love? Who ever heard of a cowboy who didn&rsquo;t?&rdquo; &ldquo;Wal, heah&rsquo;s one. Lura, could yu get me a job ridin&rsquo; for yore Dad?&rdquo; &ldquo;Oh, I&rsquo;d like that. In fact I thought of it. I said to father: &lsquo;Why not get this Keene cowboy to ride for us?&rsquo; He flouted the idea. &lsquo;That gun-throwing desperado from New Mexico! I guess not!&rsquo; And I said: &lsquo;But, father, you never care how tough cowboys are coming from Dodge or Abilene.&rsquo; And he shut me up.&rdquo; &ldquo;Ahuh. He&rsquo;s got a grudge against Western riders, I reckon. Wal, thet&rsquo;s tough on us from over the divide.&rdquo; &ldquo;I can&rsquo;t understand it, Brazos,&rdquo; she replied, as she straightened her disheveled hair. &ldquo;Riders like you are not tough or low-down. You may be wild, dangerous, and all that. But father&rsquo;s excuse is queer. Why, he has hired rustlers and even outlaws when we ranched outside of Abilene. He had some bad outfits&mdash;bad in another sense. That&rsquo;s why he sold out and came to Colorado.&rdquo; &ldquo;Reckon I savvy thet. Bad ootfits sometimes hurt a cattleman&rsquo;s reputation,&rdquo; replied Brazos, casually. &ldquo;Indeed they do. Father lost friends in Kansas. He had one serious lawsuit during which some pretty raw things were hinted against him. He shot a cattleman named Stearns.&rdquo; &ldquo;Kill him?&rdquo; queried Brazos, as if shocked. &ldquo;No. Stearns recovered, I&rsquo;m glad to say.&rdquo; &ldquo;Wal, yore Dad didn&rsquo;t strike me as the shootin&rsquo; kind.&rdquo; &ldquo;He&rsquo;s not,&rdquo; the girl returned, with some note akin to contempt. &ldquo;Unless he&rsquo;s got the edge on the other man. Why, he was scared to go into town for fear he&rsquo;d run into Allen Neece.&rdquo; &ldquo;Neece? Thet was the cowboy I was accused of killin&rsquo;. Did yu know him, Lura?&rdquo; &ldquo;Yes. I liked him better than any boy I knew. I was terribly shocked at his death.&rdquo; &ldquo;Reckon yu would be. From all I heah, Neece was a nice chap. Did he ever ride for yu?&rdquo; &ldquo;No. Father not only wouldn&rsquo;t have Allen but ran him out of the job he had.&rdquo; &ldquo;What for?&rdquo; &ldquo;Allen was in love with me.&rdquo; &ldquo;Aw, I see. Shore tough for Allen&mdash;an&rsquo; then gettin&rsquo; murdered in the bargain. Who could have done thet job, Lura? Some cowboy jealous of yore likin&rsquo; for Allen?&rdquo; &ldquo;When did yu see Allen last?&rdquo; asked Brazos, apparently growing interested. &ldquo;The very night he was murdered. I was in town. I met him coming out of the Show Down Saloon. He was half drunk. Allen took to drink after the Neeces lost Twin Sombreros. He didn&rsquo;t see me. And I didn&rsquo;t stop him for the good reason that he was with a little black-eyed wench in boy&rsquo;s pants. She was hanging on to Allen as if she&rsquo;d lose him. I had seen her once before somewhere. I think on the street in Dodge. Not the dance-hall type, but a pretty hard-faced hussy. I&rsquo;ll always remember her and think she had something to do with Allen&rsquo;s murder.&rdquo; &ldquo;Shore thet might be. She scraped acquaintance with Allen, got him to drinkin&rsquo;, an&rsquo; had a couple of hombres ootside, maybe, waitin&rsquo; to rob him. I&rsquo;ve known thet to happen to many a cowboy.&rdquo; &ldquo;It&rsquo;ll never happen to Allen Neece again, poor devil. If it hadn&rsquo;t been for that black-eyed girl my conscience would hurt me.&rdquo; &ldquo;Yore what?&rdquo; drawled Brazos, with his slow smile. &ldquo;I daresay you think I have no conscience&mdash;or any womanly virtues.&rdquo; &ldquo;Nope. But yu don&rsquo;t need anythin&rsquo; with yore good looks.... Lura, yu&rsquo;ve made me doggone interested in young Neece&rsquo;s case.&rdquo; &ldquo;Have you seen his doll-faced sisters&mdash;the twins?&rdquo; she asked, quickly, and the green eyes showed her true nature. &ldquo;Did he have sisters? I didn&rsquo;t know. Tough on them, I reckon.&rdquo; &ldquo;Humph! They didn&rsquo;t seem to take it so hard. They didn&rsquo;t even stop slinging hash&mdash;not for a day.&rdquo; &ldquo;Wal, yu cain&rsquo;t never tell,&rdquo; said Brazos, dropping his head. What little regard he had felt for Lura Surface went into eclipse. &ldquo;I mustn&rsquo;t stay longer, Brazos,&rdquo; said the girl, consulting her watch. &ldquo;Father watches me closely and times my rides. I told him I wanted to ride into town, but he wouldn&rsquo;t hear of it.... Here we have spent an hour&mdash;the last of which didn&rsquo;t keep the promise of the first.... When shall we meet again?&rdquo; &ldquo;I reckon never, Lura. But thanks for this once. When I&rsquo;m far away, I&rsquo;ll think I might have kissed yu, an&rsquo; kick myself.&rdquo; &ldquo;Oh, don&rsquo;t go away. Why, I&rsquo;ve only met you!... Tell me, when?&rdquo; &ldquo;Wal, maybe some day I&rsquo;ll meet yu in town an&rsquo; weaken. But, Lady, yu&rsquo;ve been warned.&rdquo; &ldquo;I&rsquo;ll take the risk.... It has been different from&mdash;well, other times I&rsquo;ve met boys here.&rdquo; She mounted gracefully to her saddle, aware of his appreciation, and said with dark-green provocative eyes on him: &ldquo;Adios, Brazos Keene.&rdquo; Brazos watched her ride away with only one regret&mdash;that he liked her well enough to be sorry she was innocently involved in a sinister plot that dimly shadowed her father, and which she had unwittingly made clearer. Brazos rode back to town, for once not seeing the afternoon sun flooding the range with golden glory. Long past was the day when he had distrusted his intuition. A pending tragedy of oncoming events vied in his mind with the consideration of the callousness of the acts which he had sworn to reveal. Despite his spirit of flame, and in this case the irresistible incentive, he felt as never before the reluctance of the intelligent and normal gunman to force issues. In times past, wild youth, sometimes inflamed by the bottle, had needed little spurring on. But he was a man now; he was through with rum; and he hated the trail that led inevitably to bloodspilling. Yet here he was, unalterably committed; he did not want to draw back; and still the passion needed held aloof. Brazos longed for the incident or the revelation that would make him a tiger. When he dismounted at the corral where Bilyen had first taken him, and turned Bay over to the stableboy, he suddenly had an inspiration: &ldquo;Pedro, did yu know Allen Neece?&rdquo; &ldquo;Si, señor,&rdquo; replied the Mexican. Further queries rewarded Brazos with some significant facts. Allen Neece had come to the stable on the night he was murdered. He was on foot and under the influence of drink though not by any means drunk. He had to wake Pedro to get his horse. It was not until Neece had mounted and ridden off that Pedro had noted a companion&mdash;a boy on a black horse, waiting. This last information seemed of tremendous importance to Brazos. That boy was the cowgirl June Neece had mentioned and the girl in rider&rsquo;s garb that Lura Surface had seen with Allen. Brazos strode uptown to the restaurant. The hour was early and only a few customers were at supper. One of the twins came to Brazos and though he thought he could trust her smile and bright eye, he would not take a chance. &ldquo;Wal, which one air yu?&rdquo; &ldquo;Brazos, what has happened?&rdquo; she whispered, leaning over the counter. He answered low: &ldquo;June, I&rsquo;ve been oot to meet Lura Surface an&rsquo; the hunch I got from her riled me.&rdquo; &ldquo;Oh&mdash;what&mdash;&mdash;&rdquo; &ldquo;Never mind now. But don&rsquo;t worry aboot me or yore Dad. Please fetch me some supper.&rdquo; That night Brazos haunted the main street and the saloons. The cowboys and cattlemen on the street, coming in or going home, the drinkers and bartenders in the saloons, the gamblers at the tables and the loungers around, and more than one dark-garbed group who drank by themselves&mdash;all heard that Brazos Keene was hunting for some one.
