It was spoken first by a man who had seen Keene enter Las Animas&rsquo; saloons in years gone by. Then it passed from lip to lip. Brazos&rsquo; stalk was no pose, yet he did it deliberately. Nevertheless he had little hope that he would encounter the trio who now loomed large in the mystery of Allen Neece&rsquo;s murder. They would be out on the range, hidden in the hills, or back east in the gambling dens of Dodge or Abilene. They would be in close touch with the man or men who were back of this crime. It might well be that they would be summoned presently to do away with Brazos Keene. In the Happy Days saloon Brazos came unexpectedly upon Bodkin, whom he had not seen since the day of his release by Kiskadden. The ex-deputy had just set down his glass on the bar. Sight of Brazos cut short words he was speaking to a companion. &ldquo;Hey, Bodkin, heah yu air,&rdquo; called Brazos, so ringingly that the inmates of the crowded saloon went silent. &ldquo;Where yu been?&rdquo; &ldquo;I&rsquo;ve been around town as usual,&rdquo; replied Bodkin, turning a dirty white under his swarthy skin. &ldquo;Like hell yu have. I been lookin&rsquo; for yu. Have yu been put back as deputy sheriff of this heah town?&rdquo; &ldquo;No. Kiskadden fired me, you know, an&rsquo; then he resigned. The Cattlemen&rsquo;s Association haven&rsquo;t made no appointment yet. But I&rsquo;m expectin&rsquo; it.&rdquo; &ldquo;Yu&rsquo;re expectin&rsquo; what?&rdquo; drawled Brazos, with scathing insolence. &ldquo;To be elected sheriff.&rdquo; &ldquo;Aw, hell! Elected? Who&rsquo;s electin&rsquo; yu? Not the citizens of this heah town. They won&rsquo;t be asked. If they would be, yu&rsquo;d never get a vote, onless from some of yore hired hands.... An&rsquo; who&rsquo;s yore Cattlemen&rsquo;s Association ootside of Raine Surface?&rdquo; &ldquo;Miller, Henderson, Sprague&mdash;all big cattlemen,&rdquo; returned Bodkin, hurriedly. &ldquo;Inskip was one&mdash;but he quit.&rdquo; &ldquo;Ahuh. An&rsquo; when does this ootfit aim to settle yore appointment&mdash;an&rsquo; also yore hash?&rdquo; &ldquo;They meet tomorrow night.&rdquo; &ldquo;Wal, tell them I&rsquo;ll call an&rsquo; cast one vote against yu.&rdquo; &ldquo;I&rsquo;ll do thet, Keene.&rdquo; &ldquo;An&rsquo; while yu&rsquo;re carryin&rsquo; messages from me, take this for yoreself. If yu&rsquo;re appointed sheriff I&rsquo;m gonna see red.... An&rsquo; this for yore hired hand, Barsh. He better keep oot of my way.&rdquo; Thus Brazos talked himself to a verge of the passion he required of himself. Seldom was he a talker until the cumulation of events propelled disaster. &ldquo;I reckon Joel won&rsquo;t invite any meetin&rsquo; with you, Keene. You oughtn&rsquo;t to have it in fer him &rsquo;cause he was only actin&rsquo; on orders.&rdquo; &ldquo;But he threw thet noose round my neck,&rdquo; flashed Brazos, as if the mere fact brooked no pardon. Backing through the swinging doors, Brazos left that saloon to break its silence with a subdued sound of excited voices, and then an angry protesting roar from Bodkin. Brazos had scarcely turned up the street when the doors banged behind him. &ldquo;Hold on, Brazos. It&rsquo;s Hank.&rdquo; And Bilyen, keen and glinting of eye, joined him. &ldquo;My Gawd, cowboy, but you burned Bodkin up! What&rsquo;s the deal?&rdquo; &ldquo;Howdy, Hank. Aw, I was only bluffin&rsquo; Bodkin, an&rsquo; takin&rsquo; thet chance to set the town talkin&rsquo; aboot Surface an&rsquo; his Cattlemen&rsquo;s Association.&rdquo; &ldquo;Brazos, you&rsquo;ve got goin&rsquo;,&rdquo; rejoined Bilyen, shrewdly. &ldquo;You shore are. You never was one to talk wild. Mebbe you were throwin&rsquo; a bluff, but you had somethin&rsquo; behind it.&rdquo; &ldquo;Wal, enough to want to rile myself up.... Hank, I was wantin&rsquo; to see yu. If yu cain&rsquo;t give me the low-down on rustlin&rsquo; in eastern Colorado, find oot for me pronto.&rdquo; &ldquo;I know, Brazos. Got thet this very day. Kiskadden an&rsquo; Inskip told me. They&rsquo;re shore interested. As a matter of fact, I was surprised. I reckon you won&rsquo;t be though.... Brazos, there &rsquo;pears to be considerable cattle stealin&rsquo; in small numbers, takin&rsquo; in all the big brands on this range. Too slick an&rsquo; bold to be the work of any gang but real rustlers under a smart leader. Kiskadden an&rsquo; Inskip lost three hundred haid last month. The Star Brand not so many. Small ootfits down the Purgatory none at all. Henderson&rsquo;s ootfit rarin&rsquo; aboot a big drive on their Circle Dot Brand. Miller has lost considerable haid. Sprague an&rsquo; the big cattlemen up on the slopes hard hit for these times. All this last month, an&rsquo; the herds driven over into Kansas an&rsquo; shipped east.&rdquo; &ldquo;Ha! Ha!&rdquo; laughed Brazos, mirthlessly. &ldquo;Say, what&rsquo;s so funny about thet?&rdquo; demanded Bilyen, affronted. &ldquo;Struck me funny.&rdquo; &ldquo;What did?&rdquo; &ldquo;The way my hunches work oot,&rdquo; returned Brazos, grimly. &ldquo;But for some men it&rsquo;s aboot as funny as death.... Hank, will yu meet me oot west of town at sunup in the mawnin&rsquo;?&rdquo; &ldquo;Yes, I will, cowboy. Where?&rdquo; answered Bilyen, soberly. &ldquo;At thet old cabin on the hill&mdash;where Allen Neece was murdered,&rdquo; said Brazos, tersely, and abruptly strode away toward Mexican Joe&rsquo;s place, where he had a room. Next morning Brazos rode out of Las Animas at the break of day. Nine out of ten mornings for fifteen years he had been in a saddle at this hour. But he was not trail driving today, or guarding the herd, or riding a grub line from one camp to another. He felt a keen cold exhilaration that was not entirely engendered by the morning crispness from the mountain heights. When he rode by Twin Sombreros Ranch the sun was just rising red and glorious over the range. Two cowboys espied him from the lane that led down to the big barns, and they halted to watch him. It never occurred to Brazos that they did not recognize him. Their dark intent gaze seemed prophetic of the shadow which hovered over that beautiful ranch. What a property to lose! No wonder Abe Neece, at his age, had seen the fruit of a lifetime pass from him with inconsolable grief! But then, Brazos reflected, Neece had worked and planned for those lovely twin daughters. It was the kind of ranch Brazos would have liked to own, and, failing that, ride for. The ideal location on the talking river, the groves and lines and clumps of trees, the peeled-log fences, and the cabins, and the low-roofed, rambling ranch house, white amid the green&mdash;they left a picture in Brazos&rsquo; mind. And they created another&mdash;of the chestnut-haired girls waving to him from the porch. &ldquo;Doggone! Thet was a funny idee,&rdquo; soliloquized Brazos, puzzled. &ldquo;Was I ridin&rsquo; in or ridin&rsquo; oot?... But it&rsquo;s nothin&rsquo; like Don Carlos&rsquo; Rancho. Thet was un grande hacienda española.... I can see Holly now, ridin&rsquo; down from the hill. My Gawd, wasn&rsquo;t she lovely?... I just gotta see thet little boy they call Brazos.... An&rsquo; Cap Britt, the old Texas son of a gun! An&rsquo; thet wild ootfit of mine!&rdquo; But the past was gone irrevocably, and for the first time since Brazos had ridden away from the Ripple Ranch he could remember without pain. Some magic had charmed away that old sadness. Never would what he had done for Holly Ripple be forgotten by her or her husband or her men! He had had help on that hard job&mdash;a bunch of the wildest and keenest riders who ever forked a horse. Some of them had died by his side, fighting for the señorita of Don Carlos&rsquo; Rancho. But in this Neece job he had started practically alone. He thought he had better go it alone, unless the trail led into the stronghold of a gang of plains rustlers. That Brazos believed was possible and probable, in which case he would need some riders. Trotting west, Brazos&rsquo; mind was crowded and active. Yet as he cleared the timber and began the long ascent that led up to the hill, he had an eye for the country. The whole eastern range was flooded with crimson light&mdash;a rolling undulating land of shining grass, bisected by a winding green-bordered river. He could see tens of thousands of cattle, black acreage near at hand and twin dots in the distance. With cattle at forty dollars a head and a boundless range, what a harvest for a bold well-led band of rustlers! That harvest would come. It always did come. It would be a quick short one, and then the gang would ride north or east or south. Not west! That led into New Mexico, and the ranges there dealt summarily with wholesale cattle thieves. Yet even in New Mexico, just so long as cattle black-spotted the valleys and plateaus, just that long would the rustler flourish. Brazos reached the top of the hill and halted. The cabin and grove of trees he sought lay off the road to the left and a little farther on. What halted Brazos was the prospect to the west. He looked across the Colorado line into New Mexico, and next to Texas he loved that country of black-belted, snow-capped mountains, of silver-grassed valleys, of wide shining ranges encompassed by protecting walls. Spring had almost ended, and the warm sun had made inroads upon the snowy south slopes of the Rockies. But the peaks were still white, glistening rose, still impervious to the lord of summer. Pikes Peak in the north rose dim and grand; the closer Spanish Peaks dominated the scene; and the great red and black wall, leading south, hid the base of the range beyond. If there were any cattle along this last heave of the Great Plains into the Continental Divide they were lost in gullies and swales between the foothills, or the valley of the Purgatory. The rough, gray, rocky foothills harbored little grass and they led into the fastnesses of the mountains. Southwest from where Brazos sat astride his saddle, gazing raptly at old familiar landmarks, yawned the gap in the range, where the Old Trail led up and over into New Mexico. It had a tremendous fascination for Brazos, not all of which came from the fact that he had ridden it and fought on it all the way from Dodge to Lincoln. Three hundred years before, the French fur traders had walked that trail, and the Spanish padres and explorers after them, and then the American fur men, down to the time of Kit Carson, and then the freighters in their covered wagons with their guards of soldiers, and the tide of gold hunters and pioneers, and lastly those other empire builders of whom Brazos counted himself one&mdash;the cowboys with their herds of longhorn cattle from Texas. It was a grand scene to Brazos, knowing as he did know so well what had taken place there down the long years. He had a melancholy feeling that he was a part of the West which had vanished. The noble wilderness appeared the same, despite the iron track he could see curving between the hills; the sun rose as always to gild those unassailable ramparts, and to shine on the rugged red walls, the black belts, the canyons of white, and the beckoning dim range. But the glory and the dream&mdash;that was to say the wildness and the romance&mdash;seemed to be passing away. Then as a clip-clop of a trotting horse disrupted Brazos&rsquo; thoughts and he turned to see Hank Bilyen coming, he remembered his errand and he laughed outright. The robbery of Neece and the murder of his son, the mysterious workings of a hard outfit, and the recent raids on cattle herds&mdash;these were far from tame and bereft of romance. Brazos realized that June and Janis Neece could furnish enough of that disturbing element to satisfy a whole range of cowboys. &ldquo;Mawnin&rsquo;, Hank,&rdquo; was Brazos&rsquo; greeting. &ldquo;Howdy, Texas. Seen you a long way back. What&rsquo;s the idee, Brazos?&rdquo; &ldquo;Wal, I want yu to help me go over this ground with a fine tooth comb.&rdquo; &ldquo;Tracks, eh? I ain&rsquo;t takin&rsquo; any back seat for you, Brazos Keene. Never was one to hobble hawses.&rdquo; They rode into the clump of trees and tied their horses. &ldquo;Allen Neece was found in thet cabin. But accordin&rsquo; to the doctors he wasn&rsquo;t killed there. What I want powerful to find an&rsquo; shore expect to find is a small boot track.&rdquo; &ldquo;Ahuh. Wal, let&rsquo;s get goin&rsquo;.&rdquo; They entered the cabin, with riders&rsquo; eyes searching the ground for tracks. Hank got down on his knees to scrutinize those just inside the door. He was slow. &ldquo;Reckon them was made the mawnin&rsquo; Bodkin found you heah,&rdquo; he said, at length. &ldquo;An&rsquo; heah&rsquo;s my track, goin&rsquo; an&rsquo; comin&rsquo; back. I slept on thet bunk.&rdquo; They searched the musty dry cabin as hunters of treasure might have. &ldquo;Well, nothin&rsquo; heah,&rdquo; said Brazos. &ldquo;If thet little boot marked the dust heah it&rsquo;s been tracked over. Let&rsquo;s go up in the loft.&rdquo; The loft covered half the space under the roof and had been built of peeled poles laid close together. It shook under their weight. The light was dull up there, but they could distinguish objects. Bilyen concluded that the murderers had climbed the ladder up to a point level with the loft and had shoved the body headfirst back upon the poles. A dark smear of blood ran along one of them. &ldquo;What&rsquo;s thet in the corner?&rdquo; asked Brazos, and crawled carefully back. He found a rope, a lasso, that had evidently been hurriedly flung there without being coiled. He crawled back to Bilyen with it. &ldquo;Look, Hank.... Must have been the rope which made the marks on his arms,&rdquo; said Brazos. &ldquo;Take it down, Bilyen, while I investigate some more.&rdquo; Brazos went over every inch of that loft without further discovery. When he got down he found Hank sitting in the door, studying the rope. Brazos knelt to scrutinize with him. They were tense and silent. Next to a gun and a horse, the lasso was a cowboy&rsquo;s most treasured possession. &ldquo;Wal, what you make oot?&rdquo; queried the Texan gruffly of Brazos. &ldquo;Lasso all right. Manila, wal made. Same as any one of a hundred.&rdquo; &ldquo;Yes, an&rsquo; what else, cowboy?&rdquo; &ldquo;It shore never was used on a calf or a cow or a steer.&rdquo; &ldquo;Hell no. Brazos, it ain&rsquo;t new. It&rsquo;s been tied on a saddle fer a long time. A cowboy riata never used by a cowboy!... Does thet say any thin&rsquo; to you?&rdquo; &ldquo;Ump-umm. Don&rsquo;t talk so much, Hank. Let&rsquo;s go all over the ground heah.&rdquo; Every path and bare spot and thin-grassed bit of ground out to the road and along the road to east and west they carefully inspected. &ldquo;Now thet clump of trees,&rdquo; said Brazos. &ldquo;Hank, heah&rsquo;s where I run into the three hombres.&rdquo; He related the incident, laying stress on the name he had heard and the nervous young high-pitched voice. Bilyen&rsquo;s silence attested to the impression Brazos&rsquo; story made on him. They proceeded to the patch of timber. &ldquo;Aboot heah is where their hawses stood.&rdquo; It appeared to be a scraggy bit of dead and dying timber, extending back a considerable distance. Brazos directed Hank to search there, while he began at the farther end. Brazos had something in his mind. At the farthest point, under the largest and thickest-foliaged of the trees, he found a bare spot of ground. At sight of hoof tracks and tiny boot tracks his blood leaped. Down he knelt and almost smelt at the boot tracks. Of all the innumerable imprints by riders&rsquo; boots that Brazos had ever studied these were the smallest. No cowboy ever had small enough feet to take a boot that made these tracks. Ten-year-old boys did not wear boots made to order. These had been left by a girl. Brazos knelt there as if he were reading a dark page of the book of life. &ldquo;What&rsquo;n hell air you porin&rsquo; over, cowboy?&rdquo; asked Bilyen, curiously, as he came upon Brazos. &ldquo;There! My hunch was true.&rdquo; Bilyen did not utter a word but made a careful inspection of the spot, and then faced Brazos with a curious fire in his eye. &ldquo;Cowboy, there&rsquo;s a girl mixed in this deal.&rdquo; &ldquo;Shore.&rdquo; &ldquo;So will I, Hank,&rdquo; rejoined Brazos, ponderingly. &ldquo;Get me some little sticks so I can measure this track.&rdquo; &ldquo;Brazos, I reckon you won&rsquo;t be lookin&rsquo; for a cowgirl answerin&rsquo; to this boot track?... Not much!&rdquo; &ldquo;Not any at all,&rdquo; returned Brazos, with a cold steely note in his voice. &ldquo;She was the one of them three hombres with the young nervous high-pitched voice.... She was afraid of the risk.... She was goin&rsquo; to bore you, Brazos!&rdquo; &ldquo;I shore gotta meet thet sweet little lady.&rdquo; &ldquo;How you goin&rsquo; aboot thet, Brazos?&rdquo; asked Hank, scratching his scrubby beard. &ldquo;Ride the line an&rsquo; take a look at the gamblin&rsquo; halls.&rdquo; &ldquo;They got cash an&rsquo; they ain&rsquo;t cowmen. Wal, when you find out where their money comes from you&rsquo;ll be gettin&rsquo; hot.&rdquo; &ldquo;Hot? Say, old-timer, I&rsquo;m burnin&rsquo; right now.... But I&rsquo;ll cool off. I don&rsquo;t like the idee of a hard nut of a cowgirl in this deal. I might have to wing her. I did thet once to a woman who was drawin&rsquo; on me an&rsquo; I&rsquo;ll never forget her scream.&rdquo; &ldquo;How you figure her part in this?&rdquo; &ldquo;How do yu?&rdquo; &ldquo;Plain as print. She an&rsquo; her two pards air from oot of town&mdash;she&rsquo;s a good-looker an&rsquo; likely enticin&rsquo; to cowboys. Allen Neece was easy took in by girls. He liked a drink, too, same as all the boys. Wal, this gang of three was after him, for reasons that bear strong in this deal. She got to Allen&mdash;an&rsquo; the rest was easy.&rdquo; &ldquo;I figure aboot like thet, Hank,&rdquo; returned Brazos, thoughtfully. &ldquo;Beside I know more&rsquo;n yu. The night Allen was killed he walked down to the barns to get his hawse. Pedro said there was a boy with him&mdash;a boy on a black hawse&mdash;an&rsquo; they hung ootside. They rode away. Now what happened is this. If I remember correct thet night was nice an&rsquo; warm, with a moon an&rsquo; the frogs peepin&rsquo;&mdash;just the night for a rendezvous oot heah. But they never got heah. Thet Brad an&rsquo; his other pard roped Allen an&rsquo; dragged him off his hawse. The fall killed Allen, but they didn&rsquo;t know it. They packed him up heah, shot him&mdash;an&rsquo; left him in the cabin.&rdquo; &ldquo;While the girl waited heah under this tree nervous an&rsquo; sick,&rdquo; added Hank. &ldquo;Nervous, anyhow. Wal, she had&mdash;good reason to be nervous,&rdquo; declared Brazos, darkly. &ldquo;Just aboot then I rode into the deal.&rdquo; &ldquo;Funny how things work oot. It shore don&rsquo;t pay to be crooked.... Brazos, who&rsquo;s behind all this?&rdquo; &ldquo;Hank, yu&rsquo;re a curious cuss,&rdquo; drawled Brazos, carefully depositing in his pocket the little sticks with which he had measured the foot track. &ldquo;Let&rsquo;s go back to town an&rsquo; have breakfast.&rdquo; On the return they did not exchange another word until they got by Twin Sombreros Ranch, when Bilyen curtly asked Brazos: &ldquo;Did you see Surface standin&rsquo; in the door?&rdquo; &ldquo;Shore. He looked big as a hill.&rdquo; &ldquo;Must be one of these early birds, eh?&rdquo; rejoined Bilyen, sarcastically. From there on into town Brazos found his tongue and asked his comrade many questions regarding the possibility of one of the big cattlemen of the range being the genius and backer of a bold and clever gang of rustlers. &ldquo;Kinda hipped on thet idee, ain&rsquo;t you?&rdquo; rejoined Bilyen, who seemed nettled that Brazos did not tell all he knew. &ldquo;Reckon I am. It&rsquo;s a great idee.&rdquo; &ldquo;Wal, it wasn&rsquo;t born with you, Brazos. I had thet long ago.&rdquo; &ldquo;Hank, yu&rsquo;re from Texas an&rsquo; no fool,&rdquo; mused Brazos, as if apologizing for Bilyen. &ldquo;Will yu swear to it?&rdquo; &ldquo;I shore will&mdash;to you. But I wouldn&rsquo;t want to yell it oot loud in the Happy Days or the post office.&rdquo; &ldquo;Why wouldn&rsquo;t yu, old-timer?&rdquo; &ldquo;I want to live long enough to see Abe an&rsquo; the twins back at their ranch.&rdquo; &ldquo;Ahuh. Wal, I&rsquo;ll yell it&mdash;tonight at thet Cattlemen&rsquo;s Association meetin&rsquo;.&rdquo; &ldquo;By Gawd, you&rsquo;ve a nerve, Brazos! An&rsquo; you&rsquo;re cunnin&rsquo;, too. Wonder I didn&rsquo;t think you&rsquo;d do just thet.... Shore we figger the same. You&rsquo;d egg one of them cattlemen an&rsquo; whoever&rsquo;s in with him to sic the cowgirl an&rsquo; her hombres on to you, eh?&rdquo; &ldquo;Eggs-ack-ly, Hank,&rdquo; drawled Brazos. &ldquo;Hell! It&rsquo;s no good. If they wasn&rsquo;t too smart to trail you they&rsquo;d be too scairt.&rdquo; &ldquo;Wal, maybe they never heahed of me.&rdquo; &ldquo;They&rsquo;re livin&rsquo; west of the Mississippi, ain&rsquo;t they?&rdquo; &ldquo;It&rsquo;s a good idee anyhow, Hank. An&rsquo; heah&rsquo;s where we eat.&rdquo; &ldquo;I&rsquo;ll ride oot home an&rsquo; get breakfast for Abe. See you pronto.&rdquo; In a few more moments Brazos was gazing across the counter into a pair of lovely hazel-brown eyes that shone upon him. &ldquo;Mawnin&rsquo; June&mdash;if yu&rsquo;re June,&rdquo; he said, with a smile. &ldquo;Good mawnin&rsquo;, Brazos,&rdquo; she replied, mimicking his accent. A perverse devil in Brazos led him to whisper: &ldquo;What would I get&mdash;if I&rsquo;d found oot somethin&rsquo; important for yu?&rdquo; Her eyes dilated and the rose of her cheek faded. &ldquo;What would you like&mdash;flapjacks or lamb chops?&rdquo; she queried, meeting him in spirit. &ldquo;Aw, no!... Would I get a kiss?&rdquo; She blushed scarlet and the hint of humor fled. &ldquo;Certainly, you would.&rdquo; The surprise of that quick assent shocked Brazos into a realization of his bold levity. &ldquo;Forgive thet. I&rsquo;m loco this mawnin&rsquo;,&rdquo; he added, hurriedly, lowering his eyes. He knew that his remorse was not going to live long in the light of her consent. Presently under his lowered gaze he saw both flapjacks and lamb chops set before him, and mumbling his thanks he began to eat. Presently his ears were assailed by a sweet voice: &ldquo;No coffee and biscuits this morning?&rdquo; &ldquo;Aw, I forgot. Shore I do,&rdquo; replied Brazos, nonplussed at he did not know what. Until he looked up! &ldquo;So that is the secret of your heroics?&rdquo; &ldquo;Wha-at?&rdquo; blurted Brazos, dumbfounded. &ldquo;You would extract a kiss from Janis for some little service to the Neeces?&rdquo; &ldquo;June!... So help me heaven I swear I thought she was yu.&rdquo; &ldquo;Cowboy!&rdquo; At that Brazos flushed deeply. To be relegated to the rank and file of ordinary cowboys was something he could not tolerate. &ldquo;Look heah, Lady,&rdquo; he said, coolly, &ldquo;I thought she was yu. Thet&rsquo;s a mistake I cain&rsquo;t help. It just popped oot&mdash;thet aboot askin&rsquo; for a kiss. I shore didn&rsquo;t mean it an&rsquo; I apologized to her. An&rsquo; I do to yu. I was loco.&rdquo; &ldquo;Why were you so&mdash;so loco this morning?&rdquo; she asked, dubiously, a little less aloof. Brazos cautiously made certain no one could hear his reply. &ldquo;June, this mawnin&rsquo; I found a real clue to Allen&rsquo;s murderers. I cain&rsquo;t tell yu now, but I will tonight, if yu&rsquo;ll see me.&rdquo; &ldquo;Come after ten,&rdquo; she replied, in earnest eagerness. &ldquo;Oh, I can hardly wait.... Brazos, I&rsquo;m ashamed. I&mdash;I doubted you again.&rdquo; &ldquo;Again? I didn&rsquo;t know yu&rsquo;d already done thet.&rdquo; &ldquo;Yes, again. I may as well confess it. I&mdash;I&rsquo;m horribly jealous of Jan. She gets all the boys and men, too.&rdquo; &ldquo;How did I say it, Brazos?&rdquo; &ldquo;I am sorry. It&rsquo;s my turn to beg pardon.... Then I&rsquo;m to tell Jan you took her for me&mdash;and you were only teasing about the kiss?&rdquo; Heightened color and rather abashed eyes accompanied this query. &ldquo;Please do, June. An&rsquo; tell her I know she was teasin&rsquo; too&mdash;aboot givin&rsquo; me the kiss.&rdquo; &ldquo;Not Jan! She meant that.&rdquo; &ldquo;O Lord, what am I missin&rsquo;!&rdquo; burst out Brazos, frankly. There were times when he had to be deadly honest. &ldquo;If you really took Jan for me&mdash;you will miss nothing,&rdquo; replied June, tilting her chin. &ldquo;I really did. An&rsquo; what yu mean, June Neece&mdash;thet I&rsquo;ll miss nothin?&rdquo; &ldquo;Because I will give you two!&rdquo; she flashed, merrily, and fled. Brazos rode twenty miles that day and visited a spring roundup, two cow camps, and a ranch with a shadowy reputation. At each place he introduced himself with a question. &ldquo;Have yu-all seen a bald-faced thoroughbred hawse with three white feet? I&rsquo;m Brazos Keene an&rsquo; my hawse was stole. Last seen goin&rsquo; north with two men an&rsquo; a kid&mdash;maybe a girl in boy&rsquo;s clothes&mdash;on a black hawse.&rdquo; His inquiry elicited sympathy everywhere except at a ranch where the burly dark-browed rancher, Sneed, betrayed a glint of curiosity that the keen Brazos did not fail to record. &ldquo;No, Keene, I hain&rsquo;t seen any hoss marked like that,&rdquo; he replied, slowly. &ldquo;But yu did see the two men an&rsquo; the kid&mdash;who&rsquo;s a cowgirl?&rdquo; swiftly followed Brazos. &ldquo;What you givin&rsquo; me? I didn&rsquo;t say so.&rdquo; &ldquo;Aw, the hell yu didn&rsquo;t. Sneed, if yu want to tell me when an&rsquo; where yu seen thet ootfit, wal an&rsquo; good. But if yu don&rsquo;t yu&rsquo;re shore gonna be watched.&rdquo; &ldquo;Watched! An&rsquo; who by, an&rsquo; what fer, Mister Keene?&rdquo; &ldquo;Ask yoreself. Air yu tellin&rsquo; me, Sneed?&rdquo; &ldquo;I hain&rsquo;t seen no two men an&rsquo; a kid,&rdquo; repeated Sneed, stoutly. &ldquo;How aboot cattle?&rdquo; hazarded Brazos, like a hound on the scent. &ldquo;Seen any herds of mixed brands drove along heah toward the Kansas line?&rdquo; &ldquo;No,&rdquo; replied Sneed, harshly. &ldquo;Then yu must have been wearin&rsquo; blinders... or maybe yu couldn&rsquo;t see for dust.... Good day, Sneed. I&rsquo;ll be seein&rsquo; yu again.&rdquo; Brazos would never have ventured such bold and scarcely veiled remarks as these, had he not intuitively caught this cattleman over on the wrong side of the fence. Brazos had found out that Sneed had a bad name in the first place, and secondly, he had not been equal to Brazos&rsquo; sharp interrogation. There might have been only a little wrong with Sneed and then again there might have been a great deal. Brazos added another tiny link to his chain. &ldquo;Wal, in the next month or so,&rdquo; he soliloquized, as he rode back toward Las Animas, &ldquo;if somebody takes a pot shot at me or I find myself trailed&mdash;I&rsquo;ll be doggoned shore I&rsquo;m on a hot scent. An&rsquo; it behooves little Brazos to move careful.&rdquo; Brazos avoided the restaurant that night and had his supper at Mexican Joe&rsquo;s. Afterward he began the gamut of the saloons, where he pretended to drink. And at nine o&rsquo;clock, when he mounted the steps up to the Odd Fellows Hall, he pounded on the door with the butt of his gun. &ldquo;Open up heah!&rdquo; he shouted. The door was promptly unlocked, allowing Brazos to enter, a little unsteady on his feet. But seldom had Brazos Keene been any more sober and cool than at this moment. He needed the sharpness of vision and wit of a dozen cowboys. Much might depend on this venture. &ldquo;Excuse me, gennelmen, for intrudin&rsquo; heah. I&rsquo;ll leave it to yu whether what I say is important or not.&rdquo; A dozen or perhaps fifteen men sat around a long table, upon which stood bottles and glasses and a box of cigars. Brazos swept the group in one glance, to recognize Henderson and Surface. He had never seen Sprague, but identified him from Bilyen&rsquo;s description. Other faces were familiar. And lastly, to his surprise, he saw Inskip. &ldquo;It&rsquo;s that cowboy, Brazos Keene,&rdquo; spoke up one of the men. &ldquo;Drunk! Put him out,&rdquo; called Surface, rising from his seat. &ldquo;Go slow, Mister Surface, aboot puttin&rsquo; me oot,&rdquo; drawled Brazos, his eyes on the rancher like slits of fire. &ldquo;I&rsquo;m not drunk. Shore I&rsquo;ve had a few nips just to make me talky. But yu&rsquo;ll find me level-haided enough.&rdquo; &ldquo;Let him have his say, Surface,&rdquo; advised Henderson, intensely interested. &ldquo;Go ahaid, Brazos,&rdquo; interposed Inskip, dryly. &ldquo;But the intrusion of a drunken cowboy! Intolerable,&rdquo; protested Surface, and as he sank back into his seat it was not anger alone that marked his sallow visage. &ldquo;Speak up, Keene,&rdquo; ordered Henderson. &ldquo;Be brief and to the point.&rdquo; Brazos, having gained his point of entrance, changed visibly, and sheathed his gun, though he left his hand on the butt. &ldquo;Gentlemen, I picked oot this meetin&rsquo; as the proper place an&rsquo; time to make a statement shore to be interestin&rsquo; to all Colorado cattlemen,&rdquo; began Brazos, swiftly, with his glance roving as swiftly. &ldquo;It so happens thet events kinda gravitate to me. An honor I never cared for but was thrust on me! The cattle situation heah on this range from the Spanish Peaks down to the Old Trail is nothin&rsquo; new to me. I recall five situations like it. Yu all know what caused the Lincoln County War in New Mexico. Yu all shore have heahed of the Sewall McCoy combine with Russ Slaughter. On the one hand there was the educated rich smooth cunnin&rsquo; gentleman-rancher, an&rsquo; on the other the dyed-in-the-wool rustler, hard as flint, an&rsquo; leader of as bloody an ootfit of cattle thieves as ever forked hawses. Yu-all may have heahed too, what I had to do with the trailin&rsquo; an&rsquo; breakin&rsquo; thet double ootfit. I mention it heah, not to brag, but to give some importance to what I&rsquo;m aboot to tell yu.&rdquo; Brazos let that sink in. He did not rest his gaze upon any one of the fascinated faces of his listeners, but he missed nothing of the effect of his words. &ldquo;Yu cattlemen face the same situation heah on this range,&rdquo; he went on impressively. &ldquo;An&rsquo; if yu don&rsquo;t break it up there&rsquo;s no tellin&rsquo; how powerful an&rsquo; all-embracin&rsquo; it&rsquo;ll grow.... Short an&rsquo; sweet then, gentlemen, there&rsquo;s a cattleman on this range who&rsquo;s workin&rsquo; like Sewall McCoy. He&rsquo;s yore friend an&rsquo; maybe pardner. I&rsquo;m not insultin&rsquo; any of yu heah or any citizen of Las Animas. &rsquo;Cause what I know cain&rsquo;t be proved at this tellin&rsquo;. But it&rsquo;s the truth yu can gamble on.... Thet&rsquo;s all gentlemen. Take it for what it&rsquo;s worth.&rdquo; Slowly Brazos, while ending this biting speech, backed to the door, limning on his mind&rsquo;s eye the strangely contrasting visages there. Then with a leap he was out of the door, to bound down the stairs. &ldquo;That nice curly-haired cowboy!&rdquo; whispered one, a girl in her teens. &ldquo;Who would have thought it!&rdquo; ejaculated her mother. &ldquo;Cowboy, it&rsquo;s two days yet till July Fourth,&rdquo; remarked the conductor. &ldquo;Wal, heah at Las Animas I don&rsquo;t feel right withoot my hardware on,&rdquo; drawled Brazos, smiling at the girl. &ldquo;Who are you?&rdquo; she asked, eagerly. &ldquo;Sorry to confess, Lady. But I been sailin&rsquo; under false colors. I&rsquo;m Curly Keene, bandit, an&rsquo; all around desperado.... Adios.&rdquo; Brazos picked up his bag and made for the platform. As the train slowed to a halt he espied Bilyen foremost of the waiting bystanders. Before Brazos stepped down he swept the platform with searching gaze. Bilyen beckoned for him to come off. &ldquo;Howdy, Hank,&rdquo; drawled Brazos. &ldquo;Kinda like old times to see yu packin&rsquo; thet gun.&rdquo; &ldquo;Wal, you dressed-up son of a gun,&rdquo; ejaculated Hank, delighted. &ldquo;Brazos, you shore look fine.&rdquo; &ldquo;How about things heah?&rdquo; &ldquo;Not so good. But no hurry tellin&rsquo;. I hope you had better luck than me.&rdquo; &ldquo;Hank, I shore learned a heap. But what good it&rsquo;d do I cain&rsquo;t say.... Come with me. I&rsquo;ve got somethin&rsquo; for the twins.&rdquo; They entered the restaurant to find June and Janis excited and glowing, for once forgetting their duties. Brazos made them a gallant bow, and then gazed from one to the other in exceeding great earnestness, and back again. &ldquo;Aw! Three weeks makes no difference. I cain&rsquo;t tell yu apart. I cain&rsquo;t tell nothin&rsquo; &rsquo;cept yu air lovelier,&rdquo; he said. &ldquo;Heah, I fetched yu somethin&rsquo; from Kansas City.&rdquo; And opening his bag he drew forth two large beribboned boxes of candy and presented them. &ldquo;Oh, Brazos&mdash;you look grand!&rdquo; exclaimed one, taking him in with rapt eyes. If she were June she neglected the little identifying mannerism she had agreed to adopt for Brazos&rsquo; sake. &ldquo;Thanks, cowboy. We thought you&rsquo;d never, never get back,&rdquo; murmured the other. &ldquo;And almost prayed you wouldn&rsquo;t!&rdquo; That would be June, Brazos thought, and thrilled to the soft dark shadow in her tawny eyes. &ldquo;Aw, thet wasn&rsquo;t kind.... Has anyone been lookin&rsquo; for me?&rdquo; &ldquo;Brazos, we try not to hear talk across the counter&mdash;but we did... and Jack is worried.&rdquo; &ldquo;Wal, girls, don&rsquo;t worry none, now I&rsquo;m back. See yu later. I&rsquo;m goin&rsquo; oot with Hank. Got some news for yore Dad.&rdquo; Outside Brazos turned to his faithful comrade and voiced a sharp query: &ldquo;What&rsquo;s come off? June an&rsquo; Janis look a little strained.&rdquo; &ldquo;Worry about you, I reckon. The damn burg has been full of talk.&rdquo; &ldquo;Ahuh. Wal, let&rsquo;s walk down to Joe&rsquo;s. I&rsquo;ll leave my bag. Did yu fetch my hawse in?&rdquo; &ldquo;No. I drove in for supplies. You can ride oot on the wagon with me.&rdquo; Brazos gave attention to the street and pedestrians ahead. Las Animas showed the stimulus due to the near approach of the Fourth. It was scarcely likely that any one on the street saw Brazos first. Mexican Joe, at sight of him, beamed all over his seamed and swarthy face. &ldquo;Ah, Señor Brazos. Now thees good old time she come back!&rdquo; Hank turned a corner with Brazos and led him to a side street. &ldquo;Did yu savvy thet damn greaser?&rdquo; growled Brazos. &ldquo;Shore. Joe reckons yu&rsquo;ll find oot pronto who&rsquo;s lookin&rsquo; for yu. Nobody seems to know.&rdquo; &ldquo;Did Bodkin get put in as sheriff?&rdquo; &ldquo;Not yet. The Cattlemen&rsquo;s Association split. Henderson an&rsquo; Surface had a hell of a row. Henderson resigned.&rdquo; &ldquo;Ahuh. Henderson? I reckoned thet night when I busted up the meetin&rsquo; he was gettin&rsquo; leary.&rdquo; &ldquo;Brazos, he shore had reason. Not long after thet row Henderson lost a thousand haid of steers an&rsquo; his best hawses. The two cowboys on guard were shot. An&rsquo; the rest of the ootfit didn&rsquo;t know till mornin&rsquo;.&rdquo; &ldquo;Hell yu say!&rdquo; ejaculated Brazos. &ldquo;Pretty bold an&rsquo; raw. It&rsquo;s gettin&rsquo; hot, Hank.&rdquo; &ldquo;Kinda queer, don&rsquo;t you reckon, right after thet row with Surface?&rdquo; &ldquo;Wal, damn if I see it,&rdquo; rejoined Hank, stoutly. &ldquo;You&rsquo;re onsartin&rsquo; then of Surface&rsquo;s guilt?&rdquo; &ldquo;Hell no! An&rsquo; thet&rsquo;s what&rsquo;s eatin&rsquo; me. I&rsquo;m shore aboot Surface, but cain&rsquo;t prove it.&rdquo; &ldquo;Boy, you cain&rsquo;t get proof in a minnit.&rdquo; &ldquo;Not to be thought of.&rdquo; &ldquo;I&rsquo;m gettin&rsquo; sore. An&rsquo; thet&rsquo;s good. It&rsquo;ll do what a bottle of red likker used to do.... Wal, what&rsquo;d yu find oot?&rdquo; &ldquo;Nary a thing. I took train to Hebron an&rsquo; talked with the railroad men there. Got no satisfaction. They&rsquo;d shipped cattle train after cattle train east. An&rsquo; one bunch was no different from another. &lsquo;It&rsquo;s no mix of ours if some of them cattle was rustled,&rsquo; said the station agent. Billy the Kid&rsquo;s ootfit is sellin&rsquo; cattle to the beef buyers for the Indian reservations. Nobody talkin&rsquo;.&rdquo; Brazos had no more to say until he and Hank met Neece at the cabin. It pleased Brazos to see that Neece was a changed man. He had pulled out of hopelessness. He had gained. &ldquo;I reckon it&rsquo;s significant,&rdquo; declared Neece, soberly. &ldquo;My idee is it strengthens our case,&rdquo; added Bilyen. &ldquo;Wal, I agree,&rdquo; said Brazos. &ldquo;But what we air convinced of might not be worth a whoop in court. Surface has money an&rsquo; influence. He&rsquo;d beat us. An&rsquo; we don&rsquo;t want any court decision against us. This deal will never go before a Denver court.&rdquo; &ldquo;You are daid right, Brazos,&rdquo; returned Bilyen. &ldquo;Yes, an&rsquo; what I want is to get Surface daid to rights. Let me get thet in my own way. All the same I won&rsquo;t overlook the littlest hunch yu can give me. Anythin&rsquo; could be a hunch.&rdquo; &ldquo;Lura Surface has left Twin Sombreros, so I heah,&rdquo; put in Hank. &ldquo;She&rsquo;s stayin&rsquo; with a friend, Delia Ross. An&rsquo; lettin&rsquo; thet gambler Howard run around with her.&rdquo; &ldquo;Yu don&rsquo;t say? Wal!&rdquo; &ldquo;Brazos, did Hank tell you Henderson called on me?&rdquo; queried Neece. &ldquo;Well, he did. An&rsquo; though he didn&rsquo;t mention Surface I took it as an expression of regret an&rsquo; sympathy. Henderson is head of the bank that wouldn&rsquo;t lend me the money to save my ranch.&rdquo; &ldquo;Ahuh. Wal, thet is a hunch. Rustle my hawse, Hank. I&rsquo;m ridin&rsquo; to town.&rdquo; Henderson received Brazos with a veiled surprise not unmixed with interest. &ldquo;I called to ask a couple of questions, Mr. Henderson, an&rsquo; maybe one is in the nature of a favor,&rdquo; said Brazos, frankly. &ldquo;Well, shoot, cowboy,&rdquo; replied the banker, with an encouraging smile. &ldquo;Do yu know Jack Sain?&rdquo; &ldquo;By sight, only.&rdquo; &ldquo;Could yu give him a job ridin&rsquo;? From all I heahed since I came back yu need some riders. I&rsquo;ll stand for Jack.&rdquo; &ldquo;Very well. That is recommendation enough. Send him in.&rdquo; &ldquo;Thet&rsquo;s fine of yu, Mr. Henderson. Jack&rsquo;s down in the mouth, he&rsquo;s had such bad luck. He took the Neeces&rsquo; trouble to heart.... My other question is kinda personal an&rsquo; I hope yu excuse it.&rdquo; &ldquo;What is it, Keene?&rdquo; &ldquo;Air yu for or against Raine Surface?&rdquo; asked Brazos, deliberately. &ldquo;Is that any business of yours?&rdquo; &ldquo;Not onless yu make it mine. But I&rsquo;m against him. I&rsquo;m on Abe Neece&rsquo;s side in this deal.&rdquo; &ldquo;Keene! So that is what Inskip meant?&rdquo; &ldquo;Mr. Henderson, yu know me an&rsquo; I make bold enough to think yu have confidence in me. If Cap Britt was heah in yore place he&rsquo;d put me on this job.&rdquo; &ldquo;What job?&rdquo; &ldquo;Why, yu&rsquo;re a Westerner, Mr. Henderson.&rdquo; &ldquo;Yes. And you&rsquo;re a clever cowboy, Keene.... Did you know that Raine Surface killed a cattleman named Stokes for insinuating things?&rdquo; &ldquo;Shore, I heahed thet.&rdquo; &ldquo;Keene, I won&rsquo;t say anything. But you can make your own conclusions.&rdquo; &ldquo;Will yu respect my confidence?&rdquo; &ldquo;Absolutely.&rdquo; &ldquo;Wal, I reckon Raine Surface is another Sewall McCoy.&rdquo; &ldquo;Aha! That was behind your little address to the Cattlemen&rsquo;s Association some weeks ago? Inskip told me that very thing.&rdquo; &ldquo;Yes, it was an&rsquo; is.&rdquo; &ldquo;Ticklish business, even for a Brazos Keene. Surface has many interests, riders galore, and according to range gossip a tough outfit somewhere up in the hills.&rdquo; &ldquo;All powerful interestin&rsquo; to me, Mr. Henderson. If Surface didn&rsquo;t have them, he wouldn&rsquo;t class with Sewall McCoy. At thet I reckon McCoy had what Surface doesn&rsquo;t show to me. An&rsquo; thet&rsquo;s brains. McCoy lasted for years in New Mexico. An&rsquo; if it hadn&rsquo;t been for my suspicion aboot a cowboy rider in my ootfit, why McCoy might be playin&rsquo; a high hand yet. But Surface won&rsquo;t last the month oot. He just doesn&rsquo;t savvy us.&rdquo; &ldquo;Us? And who are us?&rdquo; queried Henderson, tersely. &ldquo;See here, Keene, I didn&rsquo;t say&mdash;I didn&rsquo;t intimate&mdash;&rdquo; stammered the banker-cattleman, much perturbed. &ldquo;All I needed was to talk to yu a little. I know what yu think. But yu didn&rsquo;t tell me an&rsquo; yu can rest safe in thet assurance. Keep oot of Surface&rsquo;s way. He might try to bore yu to strengthen his stand.&rdquo; Brazos strode out of the bank, glad to be in the open air, where he wanted to whoop and swear. But all he did do was to stand with apparent carelessness watching the passers-by. Presently he turned the corner, down the side street and went around to Mexican Joe&rsquo;s. From the little window of his room upstairs he bent a keen and penetrating eye upon the men on the street. Henderson knew Surface was crooked and yet he dared not betray him. That was one of the strange angles peculiar to the West. He swallowed his loss and waited for some one else to risk calling the cattle baron a rustler. Brazos&rsquo; original hunch, almost at sight of Surface, had been true as a compass needle pointed to the north. Raine Surface was a thief, a leader of rustlers, a counterfeit rancher, an instigator of murder if not actually a murderer, an ingratiating man of important affairs and movements, a man who would show yellow in the face of death. He was not a master rustler. For that matter neither had been Sewall McCoy. But Russ Slaughter had been as game and desperate as the frontier bred them. He had to be shot full of holes before a rope had twanged around his neck. &ldquo;Wal, I&rsquo;ve got the cairds an&rsquo; I can shore play them,&rdquo; soliloquized Brazos, as broodingly he watched the passers-by and grew dark in the mood that held him aloof. &ldquo;All I gotta do is lay for some of these hombres who&rsquo;re oot to cash me in.... An&rsquo; Bodkin is one of them.... An&rsquo; thet trio of hombres includin&rsquo; the cowgirl with the little feet.... An&rsquo; maybe some others I gotta savvy.... An&rsquo; by Gawd, I&rsquo;ll bore some of them&mdash;an&rsquo; cripple one who&rsquo;ll squeal.... Failin&rsquo; all thet I&rsquo;ll corner Surface himself&mdash;make him crawl or kill him!&rdquo; It was a harsh decision, but relentlessly welcome. And Brazos now had something more authentic to justify his personal suspicion&mdash;the moral backing of a man whose loss at Surface&rsquo;s hands had not rendered him unsound. Brazos stayed by that little window all afternoon until dusk. Of the many strangers he watched there were a few to whom he would ever turn his back. When Brazos ventured forth again he felt that he had eyes in the back of his head&mdash;that he could see through doors and walls&mdash;into the very brains where skulked the villainous purposes to his undoing. When he entered the restaurant he chose a seat where he could watch the door. &ldquo;Wal, air yu shore yu&rsquo;re Janis?&rdquo; he asked, in dubious admiration, as he looked up into the pretty face above him. &ldquo;I don&rsquo;t want to offend June,&rdquo; he replied, truthfully, not dreaming of the interpretation the girl might put upon that. The quick brightening flash of her did not alarm him. &ldquo;Have you offended her?&rdquo; &ldquo;I shore have,&rdquo; admitted Brazos, remorsefully. &ldquo;Brazos, you went away without collecting something I owed you,&rdquo; she said, with subtle charm. &ldquo;Aw Janis, I was only foolin&rsquo;.&rdquo; While she went to fetch his supper Brazos pondered that satiric statement. He failed to find anything of January in Janis Neece. She seemed warm, responsive, not in the least shy like June, and altogether alluring and provocative. All at once Brazos saw her in the same light wherein he saw June, which was to admit that they were as like as two peas. What was the matter with this Janis Neece? Did she not know that he was in love with June? It was most disturbingly sweet to see and hear Janis as she had been then. What a quandary to be in! To feel the same tumultuous wave go over him for either when the other charmer was absent! Brazos vowed he would dare an understanding with June, or if that did not seem politic, to postpone his courting until the grave issue with Surface and his minions had been settled. And the next moment Brazos cursed himself for a sentimental fool, realizing that even with death on his trail he could no more stop making love to June Neece than he could stop breathing. He might, however, be able to stay away from the restaurant, which course would enable him to give all his thought and nerve to the purpose he had set himself. That night after supper Brazos began his stalk, as stealthily as if he were deer hunting, though with the wary intensity which accompanied the blood pursuit of man. He kept to the shadow close to the sides of the buildings and he proceeded slowly. He was seen, but never by any pedestrian or lounger whom he had not seen first. One of Brazos&rsquo; uncanny faculties&mdash;that which had always been a part of any gunman who survived long&mdash;was to sense in any person the mood which now gripped him. Wherever possible, Brazos had a long look into a saloon before he entered. When he went in, it was with sudden stride, to stand facing the whole assembly, with a keen and menacing front. On these occasions there were indeed few occupants whom he did not see. And always, after a few moments, when the significance of his stand had permeated to the farthest corner, he would back out. This bold maneuver had its telling effect. It made the crowd aware Brazos Keene was on the rampage, all the more dangerous because he was sober. It told his enemies, if any were present, that it was not possible to shoot him in the back, that a false move on the part of any man would precipitate a flashing gunplay, that he was ready for an even break. This procedure was a wall of advantage for any gunman. Buck Duane, King Fisher, Wild Bill&mdash;most of the celebrated gunmen lived and dominated by that sole act of defiant hardihood. Billy the Kid, youthful desperado of the period, walked coolly before a crowd of men all itching to kill him; and he lived to be assassinated by a sheriff who feared to meet him in the open. The secret of this strength lay in its strange effect upon men who revered nerve above all else in an opponent&mdash;upon the obvious fact that the first who attempted to draw upon the gunman would almost certainly perish for his brazen pains. Brazos left that impression behind him in all the saloons and gambling dens of Las Animas. He left more&mdash;an intense curiosity as to whom he was so boldly seeking. Those who had seen him had not the least idea that Brazos himself did not know. He guessed they would whisper the names of Bodkin, Barsh, possibly Surface, though hardly the last named, for the rancher did not frequent these disreputable halls. Lastly, the act itself affected Brazos deeply, almost equivalently to the stimulus of hard liquor, from which fire and violence of spirit he would not recover until this bloody business had run its course. Contrary to Brazos&rsquo; earlier consideration of what he thought he had better do, he presented himself at the door of the Neeces&rsquo; apartment over the restaurant and knocked solidly. The door opened quickly, to disclose one of the twins in a dressing gown, most bewitching in the dim lamplight. &ldquo;Sorry&mdash;but I gotta see June,&rdquo; announced Brazos, with a deep breath. &ldquo;Come in. I&rsquo;ve been waiting. I knew you&rsquo;d come. Janis and Auntie have gone to bed,&rdquo; she replied, in a low voice a little hurried. Brazos strode in with his clinking tread and dropped his sombrero on the floor. Almost, the sweetness of June&rsquo;s presence, the intimacy she granted, burned away that dark mood. He flinched at a thought of his brazenness&mdash;at his stubborn need to end misunderstanding. But a second thought reassured Brazos. It was only fair to him that she should hear his honest intentions. He might not be able to come back again. June stood before him, turning up the lamp ever so little. She looked at him with dark wide eyes. &ldquo;Had I not better run to my room and slip on a dress? I&rsquo;m quite&mdash;quite&mdash;&mdash;&rdquo; &ldquo;Quite distractin&rsquo;&mdash;an&rsquo; thet&rsquo;s what I need,&rdquo; he interrupted her gloomily. &ldquo;Brazos!&rdquo; She came close to catch the lapels of his coat and look up anxiously. &ldquo;What has happened? I never saw you look like this.&rdquo; &ldquo;Nothin&rsquo; happened yet, June. But it&rsquo;s gonna happen&mdash;an&rsquo; pronto. There air men in town&mdash;I don&rsquo;t know how many&mdash;come to kill me. An&rsquo; I just been goin&rsquo; the rounds to let them see I won&rsquo;t be so easy to kill.&rdquo; Flattered by her anxiety, Brazos had reacted to the desire to carry it still farther. Though his statement was not in the least exaggerated, it was yet one he would not have made except under the stimulus of those troubled eyes. &ldquo;Oh, mercy! I feared&mdash;this,&rdquo; whispered June, unsteadily, and leaned shaking against him. &ldquo;June, I reckoned yu&rsquo;d better heah it from me,&rdquo; he said, earnestly. &ldquo;&rsquo;Cause no matter if I am Brazos Keene&mdash;somethin&rsquo; might happen. But I&rsquo;ve been in a heap tighter place&mdash;to come oot safe. An&rsquo; so it&rsquo;ll be this time.&rdquo; &ldquo;And it&rsquo;s all because you want to help us,&rdquo; she said, eloquently. &ldquo;Never mind thet,&rdquo; he rejoined hastily. &ldquo;June, it&rsquo;s shore hard to say the rest. My chest&rsquo;s cavin&rsquo; in.... Yu remember the night I left for Kansas City&mdash;how I was mad enough to take them&mdash;them two kisses yu was mad enough to say yu owed me?&rdquo; &ldquo;I&rsquo;ll never forget, Brazos!&rdquo; &ldquo;Wal, I was so scairt thet I ran off withoot declarin&rsquo; myself. An&rsquo; it&rsquo;s kinda haunted me since.... June, I cain&rsquo;t have yu misunderstandin&rsquo; me. It wasn&rsquo;t thet I was askin&rsquo; anythin&rsquo; of yu then. I just couldn&rsquo;t go withoot them kisses.... An&rsquo; the reason is yu&mdash;I&mdash;I&mdash;Aw! I love yu turrible, June. Thet&rsquo;s all.... An&rsquo; if I come oot of this mess alive I&rsquo;ll shore ask yu to marry me.... Somethin&rsquo; I&rsquo;d better ask now&mdash;&rsquo;cause when yu go home to live at Twin Sombreros an&rsquo; be an heiress&mdash;why I just couldn&rsquo;t have the nerve.&rdquo; She lifted her face flushed and radiant. &ldquo;Brazos,&rdquo; she whispered shyly, &ldquo;I&rsquo;ve loved you from the very first minute you looked at me.&rdquo; &ldquo;Aw, June&mdash;thet cain&rsquo;t be so!&rdquo; he implored, and took her into his arms. &ldquo;It is so,&rdquo; she whispered, hiding her face on his shoulder. &ldquo;It has nearly&mdash;driven me crazy.... I was afraid&mdash;I thought you&mdash;you loved Janis best.&rdquo; &ldquo;Good Lord!&rdquo; breathed Brazos, over her clustering hair. But the nearness of her, the surrender of her soft palpitating person to him, the maddening fact that her white arms were slipping round his neck&mdash;these drove away the appalling thought of Janis. &ldquo;Brazos&mdash;did you ask me&mdash;anything?&rdquo; she murmured. &ldquo;I said I would&mdash;if&mdash;when I come oot of this mess alive.&rdquo; &ldquo;Better ask me&mdash;now.&rdquo; He was overcome and happier than he had ever hoped to be in his life. &ldquo;Darlin&rsquo; June. I&rsquo;m turrible unworthy of yu. But I love yu.... An&rsquo; I ask yu to&mdash;to be my wife.&rdquo; &ldquo;You have my promise,&rdquo; she said, simply, and lifted her face from his shoulder, and then blushing scarlet&mdash;her lips to his. At breakfast in Mexican Joe&rsquo;s, Brazos met a rough cattleman whom he engaged in conversation. &ldquo;Howdy. How&rsquo;s tricks oot yore way?&rdquo; &ldquo;Too many tricks,&rdquo; returned the other, gruffly. &ldquo;Which way is thet?&rdquo; &ldquo;Over toward Spanish Peaks.&rdquo; &ldquo;Losin&rsquo; cattle?&rdquo; &ldquo;Didn&rsquo;t have many an&rsquo; wouldn&rsquo;t have minded the loss, but I got a note to meet at the bank.&rdquo; &ldquo;Cain&rsquo;t yu get more time?&rdquo; &ldquo;I haven&rsquo;t tried, stranger.&rdquo; &ldquo;Wal, tell me who rustled yu oot an&rsquo; I&rsquo;ll get yore time extended.&rdquo; &ldquo;Who might you happen to be?&rdquo; deliberately asked the cattleman. &ldquo;I might happen to be anybody to yu, but to these rustlin&rsquo;s&mdash;I&rsquo;m plain Brazos Keene.&rdquo; &ldquo;I&rsquo;m Jim Blake, an&rsquo; glad to meet you, Keene.&rdquo; &ldquo;Do yu know who&rsquo;s stealin&rsquo; yore stock?&rdquo; &ldquo;No.&rdquo; &ldquo;Have any ideas?&rdquo; &ldquo;I used to have. They&rsquo;ve been routed lately. Rustlers hadn&rsquo;t bothered me much this last year, up till lately. There are a couple of two-bit outfits workin&rsquo; back under the Peaks. But they never raided me. My cattle was runnin&rsquo; with the Arrow Brand, most of which was rustled last month.&rdquo; &ldquo;Arrow Brand. Whose is thet?&rdquo; &ldquo;Belongs to Patterson who recently throwed in with Surface.&rdquo; &ldquo;Ahuh. Now we&rsquo;re gettin&rsquo; somewhere. Pretty slick. Rustlin&rsquo; their own stock.&rdquo; The cattleman stared mutely at Brazos, amazed and perturbed. &ldquo;Every man can&rsquo;t say what he thinks. Reckon you can.&rdquo; &ldquo;Suppose yu could?&rdquo; &ldquo;You&rsquo;re a cool hand, Keene. I don&rsquo;t wonder no more.&rdquo; &ldquo;Wal, I&rsquo;m gamblin&rsquo; I&rsquo;ve guessed what you think.... Yu go to Henderson, the banker, an&rsquo; ask him to renew yore note. Yu can mention me.&rdquo; &ldquo;Thanks, Keene. I&rsquo;ll do it.... An&rsquo; listen&mdash;if I didn&rsquo;t have a wife an&rsquo; kids I&rsquo;d talk freer. Savvy thet?&rdquo; &ldquo;Jim, have yu got any cowboys?&rdquo; &ldquo;Two. One&rsquo;s my son.&rdquo; &ldquo;Tell them I&rsquo;ll ride oot to see them some day soon. Much obliged for tellin&rsquo; me so much. Adios.&rdquo; That morning Brazos began patrolling Las Animas much in the same mood as that in which he had accosted Blake. It was Saturday, and the influx of cowboys and other ranch folk had noticeably begun. Brazos stalked by the Twin Sombreros restaurant looking out of the corner of his eye. Across the railroad tracks the station platform showed the usual crowd and bustle incident to the arrival of a train. Inside the station Brazos encountered Lura Surface just turning away from the ticket window. She was dressed for a journey and her appearance was striking. She carried a satchel and evidently the larger bag at her feet belonged to her. &ldquo;Mawnin&rsquo;, Lura Surface. Air yu runnin&rsquo; away on me?&rdquo; drawled Brazos, doffing his sombrero. &ldquo;Yu don&rsquo;t say. Aw, I&rsquo;m sorry. I been wantin&rsquo; to see yu powerful bad.&rdquo; &ldquo;Yes, you have,&rdquo; she rejoined, with scorn. &ldquo;Honest Injun, Lura.&rdquo; &ldquo;Why didn&rsquo;t you then? I wrote you. I wanted to ask you to&mdash;to help me. I went to the place where we met before. But you didn&rsquo;t come. And you never wrote.&rdquo; &ldquo;Lura, thet&rsquo;s too bad. I&rsquo;m sorry. I never got yore letter. Fact is I haven&rsquo;t been to the post office. Don&rsquo;t never want to get another letter! An&rsquo; I&rsquo;ve been away for weeks.&rdquo; &ldquo;I heard you had&mdash;only yesterday. Too late to save my hard feelings toward you.&rdquo; &ldquo;Lura, yu make me feel bad. What&rsquo;d I do to hurt yu?&rdquo; &ldquo;The nerve of you! To ask that.&rdquo; &ldquo;Wal, I&rsquo;m askin&rsquo;,&rdquo; he returned, with his frank smile. As a matter of fact Brazos remembered very well, and also the regret he had felt at the time. &ldquo;It&rsquo;s too late, Brazos,&rdquo; she said, a little bitterly. &ldquo;I&rsquo;m going to Denver to marry Hal Howard.&rdquo; &ldquo;Aw!... yu don&rsquo;t say? Wal, I&rsquo;m shore congratulatin&rsquo; thet hombre.&rdquo; &ldquo;But you don&rsquo;t congratulate me?&rdquo; she flashed. &ldquo;Hardly. I just cain&rsquo;t see yu throwin&rsquo; yoreself away on a cairdsharp. Why, Lura, yu got all the girls oot heah skinned to a frazzle.&rdquo; &ldquo;For what? Playing at love?&rdquo; &ldquo;For downright good looks. Yu&rsquo;re shore the handsomest girl I know, ootside of bein&rsquo; an heiress.&rdquo; &ldquo;If you thought so&mdash;so much of me why did you...&rdquo; she asked, softening under his warm praise, and faltering to a close. Her hard green eyes misted over. Then she went on, &ldquo;Was it because you&rsquo;d heard things about my love affairs?&rdquo; &ldquo;No, it shore wasn&rsquo;t,&rdquo; he replied, bluntly, realizing that he had met her at a singularly opportune moment. &ldquo;Lura, I reckon I don&rsquo;t know how sweet I started, but I shore know I fell flat,&rdquo; said Brazos, earnestly. It was only a half truth, but it did not lack sincerity. &ldquo;An&rsquo; if I&rsquo;d gone on, meetin&rsquo; yu, I&rsquo;d fall&rsquo;n so turrible in love with yu thet I&rsquo;d half died. But I swear, Lura, it wasn&rsquo;t thet which made me back oot.&rdquo; A perfect blaze of glow and color suffused her face and warmed it to genuine beauty. That betrayed the insatiate need of her soul&mdash;the food and drink of love without which it would starve; and her eyes shone with glad comprehension, and forgiveness. Thus she faced Brazos a moment, the truest of her womanhood possessing her, while impulsively she squeezed his hand. Then a dark intelligence succeeded all this feeling in her glance, with a swift reversion to the fact that they were not alone in a railroad station. &ldquo;Brazos! You were afraid of Dad?&rdquo; she whispered. She met his piercing gaze with understanding, and a visible shudder. &ldquo;I can forgive you now, Brazos. And I can return your confidence.... Dad wanted you hanged. And you didn&rsquo;t want me to break my heart over a cowboy who knew why Dad wanted him out of the way.... Howard figured it all out. He had that hold on father&mdash;so we played it for all it was worth&mdash;while I was still an heiress.&rdquo; The train whistled for the stop. Lura designated her bag, which Brazos took up. They went out on the platform, where Brazos reverted to the man who was not going to be surprised by an enemy. The engine rumbled past; the train halted with squeak and jar; and there followed the bustle incident to its arrival. Brazos helped Lura on, found a seat for her, and depositing her bag he held out his hand, finding speech difficult. &ldquo;Good-by an&rsquo; good luck,&rdquo; he said. &ldquo;Yu&rsquo;re game, Lura... I&rsquo;m gonna risk a word of advice. Stop Howard&rsquo;s caird playin&rsquo;.&rdquo; &ldquo;He will not need to gamble,&rdquo; she flashed, with a smile. &ldquo;Ahuh. Thet&rsquo;s fine.... Doggone it, Lura, I wish now I hadn&rsquo;t been so&mdash;so cold thet day.&rdquo; Brazos could find no answer. He clasped her hand hard, bent over it and then let go to stand erect. The train was moving. One last glance he took at her eyes, brimming with tears, and dark with pain. Then he wheeled to run back to the platform, and jump off. He stood till the train passed by, and then, absorbed in thought, he moved to stand back against the high wooden platform of the freight house. And whirling thoughts occupied his mind. The crowd cleared away; the station platform grew vacant except for a blanketed Mexican; the boy with the mail bags went off toward the post office; horses and wagons moved down the main street of Las Animas. Out of the welter of his thoughts Brazos attained a clear thread of fact and reasoning. It came only after regret and admiration for Lura Surface had had their sway. Despite his first conception of her and the succeeding estimates to which he had listened, Brazos preferred to believe in her genuine interest in him. Perhaps he flattered his masculine vanity. He knew that many women had an instinct to collect men as an Indian brave had to collect scalps. For his own duplicity he suffered a modicum of shame, but he excused it on the score of necessity, and felt glad that he had left her the impression that not personality but circumstances had disrupted her love affair with him. That had done no harm; it had pleased and mollified her, and had moved her to a startling confession and plea. Lura Surface had learned what a great scoundrel her father was. She had then visualized or had been told of his inevitable downfall. She and Howard had frightened him or forced him to give them a considerable sum of money, and they were eloping. And Lura, in reward to Brazos for his sentimental assurance, had verified his suspicions about her father, though not as a betrayal. She had wanted Brazos to know that she knew that he knew. And lastly, with a true Western girl&rsquo;s knowledge of what her father deserved, she had implored Brazos to spare him the deepest degradation of a cattleman. Brazos wended a pondering watchful way down the street. At the corner where the bank stood an idea struck him. He went in to see Henderson. Without any greeting or other preliminary Brazos flung a query at the keen-eyed banker. &ldquo;Did this heah bank get held up yesterday or maybe day before?&rdquo; &ldquo;By a bandit?&rdquo; replied Henderson, laughing despite his surprise. &ldquo;I reckon one man might think thet.... A bandit with green eyes an&rsquo; red hair.&rdquo; &ldquo;Keene, you&rsquo;re a wizard. You beat me all hollow.&rdquo; &ldquo;Wal, come oot with it then. Didn&rsquo;t Raine Surface draw a big sum of money?&rdquo; &ldquo;All he had in cash.&rdquo; &ldquo;How much was thet?&rdquo; &ldquo;Close to forty thousand dollars.&rdquo; &ldquo;Doggone!... An&rsquo; wasn&rsquo;t Howard with him?&rdquo; &ldquo;Yes. Surface claimed it was a gambling debt. He didn&rsquo;t strike me as good a loser as usual.&rdquo; &ldquo;Gamblin&rsquo; debt yore eye!&rdquo; retorted Brazos, scornfully. &ldquo;Henderson, thet was the price of Howard&rsquo;s silence. The gambler sold oot cheap. But still he got the girl.&rdquo; &ldquo;Lura!&rdquo; &ldquo;Who else?&rdquo; &ldquo;Good heavens!&rdquo; ejaculated the banker, intensely astonished. &ldquo;I begin to see light.&rdquo; &ldquo;Yu been wearin&rsquo; blinders long enough, Henderson. Keep this under yore hat for the present.&rdquo; &ldquo;Wait, Keene,&rdquo; said the other, as Brazos turned to go. &ldquo;That little matter of putting Bodkin in as sheriff has come up. What&rsquo;ll I do about it?&rdquo; &ldquo;Air yu still in Surface&rsquo;s Cattle Association?&rdquo; &ldquo;I resigned.&rdquo; &ldquo;Wal, if I was yu, Mr. Henderson, I&rsquo;d say pretty pert thet I was for savin&rsquo; the town Bodkin&rsquo;s burial expenses by not electin&rsquo; him sheriff.&rdquo; &ldquo;That&rsquo;s certainly pert. I&rsquo;ll do it, Brazos. But let me give you a hunch. They&rsquo;ll make Bodkin sheriff.&rdquo; &ldquo;Shore they will&mdash;if he&rsquo;s crazy enough to accept it. I guess I better throw a scare into him.&rdquo; Brazos left the bank to stalk down the street. The business of this important day was in full swing. All available space along the sidewalks was occupied by wagons, buckboards and saddle horses. It was dusty and hot. Shirt-sleeved Westerners of every class moved along the walks or stood at doors or corners. It was noticeable to Brazos that as he approached, some of them froze and most of them attempted to conceal restraint. None of them met his glance. But when he passed they turned to watch him. Passing the open door of the largest merchandise store Las Animas could boast of, Brazos had a glimpse of Bodkin holding forth to a group of men, some of them dusty yellow-booted visitors from the range. Brazos passed on and halted. What could he make out of an encounter with Bodkin? The man would not draw. But he could be made a target for speech that would sweep over town like fire in prairie grass. Brazos turned back to enter the store. He assumed a swinging forward crouch and the sullen mien of a cowboy who had been tilting the bottle. The little group spread from a circle to a line, leaving Bodkin in the center and apart. The action was like clockwork. Bodkin showed no marked effect at sight of Brazos. As the cowboy had let him off before, he would again. This time, however, the ex-deputy packed a gun at his hip. &ldquo;Bodkin, I been lookin&rsquo; all over this heah town for yu,&rdquo; declared Brazos, in a surly voice. &ldquo;Keene, I haven&rsquo;t been hidin&rsquo;,&rdquo; complained Bodkin. &ldquo;Wal, yu&rsquo;re damn hard to find, an&rsquo; yu shore got thet Barsh hombre hid somewhere.&rdquo; &ldquo;He&rsquo;s out of town.&rdquo; &ldquo;When&rsquo;s he comin&rsquo; back?&rdquo; &ldquo;I don&rsquo;t know. Probably soon.&rdquo; &ldquo;Can yu get word to him?&rdquo; &ldquo;I could if I wanted to.&rdquo; &ldquo;Ahuh. Wal, yu better want to. Yu tell yore ropin&rsquo; hombre thet he&rsquo;d be wise to stay away from heah or else do some tall figgerin&rsquo; how he&rsquo;s gonna keep me from borin&rsquo; him.&rdquo; &ldquo;Keene, Barsh wouldn&rsquo;t dare meet you in an even break. He&rsquo;s only a boy. He never shot at a man. An&rsquo; you wouldn&rsquo;t shoot him in cold blood.&rdquo; &ldquo;Hell I wouldn&rsquo;t! Hasn&rsquo;t there been a lot of shootin&rsquo; in cold blood goin&rsquo; on aboot heah?... I&rsquo;m sore, Bodkin. I&rsquo;m spittin&rsquo; fire.&rdquo; &ldquo;So I see. That&rsquo;s your game. It&rsquo;s none of my business. Everybody knows you&rsquo;re rarin&rsquo; to fight, but you don&rsquo;t shoot men who&rsquo;re tryin&rsquo; to keep out of your way.&rdquo; &ldquo;What&rsquo;s to keep me from shootin&rsquo; Barsh&rsquo;s laig off?&rdquo; demanded Brazos, swaggering a little. He had his sombrero pulled down over his eyes so that they were in shadow. &ldquo;Your bein&rsquo; Brazos Keene, I reckon.&rdquo; &ldquo;I&rsquo;m not worryin&rsquo; none,&rdquo; returned Bodkin, but the fading of his healthy tan attested to another state of mind. The interview had begun to be painful to him. He seemed to catch a point in it. &ldquo;Ahuh. I reckon yu got me figgered good.... Wal, then, yu&rsquo;re so damn smart what&rsquo;s to keep me from shootin&rsquo; Raine Surface&rsquo;s laig off?&rdquo; Bodkin&rsquo;s start and expression were peculiar, and he did not reply. All the other men stood spellbound. The business of the big store ceased, clerks and customers standing amazed in their tracks. &ldquo;Answer thet, Bodkin. Talk, damn yu! Wasn&rsquo;t yu loud-mouthed when I dropped in on yu?&rdquo; shouted Brazos, in a loud sullen voice that halted pedestrians outside. &ldquo;What&rsquo;s to keep me from shootin&rsquo; Raine Surface&rsquo;s laig off?&rdquo; &ldquo;Nothin&rsquo;, Keene&mdash;nothin&rsquo;,&rdquo; ejaculated the other, harassed and impotent. He knew what was coming and he could not ward it off. Only a gun could do that! &ldquo;But you couldn&rsquo;t do it&mdash;any more than to Barsh.... Mr. Surface is out of your reach. He&rsquo;s a big man on this range. You&rsquo;re loco, Keene. You&rsquo;re drunk.&rdquo; &ldquo;Not so drunk as yu reckon, Bodkin.... An&rsquo; yu&rsquo;re defendin&rsquo; Surface from a likker-soakin&rsquo;, fire-spittin&rsquo;, gun-throwin&rsquo; cowboy?&rdquo; &ldquo;I&rsquo;m tryin&rsquo; to talk sense. You might as well bust in on Henderson in the bank, or Mr. Jones here at his desk&mdash;as Raine Surface. Why, it&rsquo;s outlandish.... Mr. Surface is a generous, big-hearted gentleman, a power in this town, a fine citizen who has the best interests of the community&mdash;&mdash;&rdquo; &ldquo;Sure he&mdash;never did,&rdquo; panted Bodkin, loyally, beginning to sweat. He was caught in a trap of his own setting. &ldquo;He didn&rsquo;t order me. I was actin&rsquo; under orders from Kiskadden.&rdquo; &ldquo;My Gawd&mdash;no!&rdquo; gasped Bodkin, huskily. &ldquo;Keene&mdash;all I can say is&mdash;you&rsquo;re drunk&mdash;or crazy.&rdquo; &ldquo;Yu&rsquo;re the crazy one, Bodkin,&rdquo; rasped Brazos, in a voice no drunken man could have used. &ldquo;An&rsquo; yore boss, Surface, is wuss than crazy. He&rsquo;s new heah. An&rsquo; yu&rsquo;re not so damn old. Why, man, this corner of Colorado is close to New Mexico. There air Texans on this range.... Yu&rsquo;re lookin&rsquo; at one now.&rdquo; &ldquo;Brazos Keene&mdash;Texan or no&mdash;you&rsquo;ll be run out of Las Animas,&rdquo; blustered Bodkin, haggardly, fighting for wit and courage. &ldquo;An&rsquo; who&rsquo;s goin&rsquo; to do thet little job?&rdquo; queried Brazos, scornfully. &ldquo;Surface will. The Cattlemen&rsquo;s Association.... The business men of this town.... They can&rsquo;t stand for such ravin&rsquo;&mdash;&mdash;&rdquo; &ldquo;Cheese it, Bodkin,&rdquo; cut in Brazos, piercingly. &ldquo;Yu heahed me. An&rsquo; yore little audience heahed me.... Just what yu air ootside of a monumental liar I haven&rsquo;t figgered yet. But yu&rsquo;re crooked. Yu heah thet?... Yu&rsquo;re crooked. An&rsquo; if yore crooked boss puts yu in as sheriff of Las Animas I&rsquo;ll kill yu!&rdquo; Brazos ended that ringing denunciation in a silence which could be felt. Bodkin&rsquo;s terrified visage satisfied Brazos that he had driven his point home. The spectators equally satisfied Brazos that his incredible affront would fly swiftly as the wind on a thousand tongues to every corner of the range. His New Mexico friends, Holly Ripple, Renn Frayne and Cap Britt would hear it before the week was out. Raine Surface would be a marked if not a ruined man. One of the spectators outside the store was Kiskadden. &ldquo;Howdy, Texas. Where&rsquo;n hell have yu been for so long? Did yu heah our little session in there?&rdquo; &ldquo;Heah yu! Say, cowboy, yu could have been heahed over in the next county. Thet was good, even for Brazos Keene. I reckon they got yu desperate.&rdquo; &ldquo;Riled, anyway. What&rsquo;ll they do, Kiskadden?&rdquo; &ldquo;I&rsquo;ll be damned if I can reckon thet. Import some ootfit to get rid of yu, I reckon.&rdquo; &ldquo;I&rsquo;ve been lookin&rsquo; for the two men an&rsquo; a girl who killed Allen Neece. Cain&rsquo;t find a trace of them.&rdquo; &ldquo;Wal, yu will now. Surface will have to kill yu to save his name. It shore was a clever trick, Brazos. Let&rsquo;s drop in somewhere an&rsquo; talk.&rdquo; For three days Brazos watched Bodkin unobtrusively. Brazos found nothing tedious in hiding in every conceivable kind of place to get track of Bodkin&rsquo;s movements. The ex-deputy went about with a bold front, but it was evident to Brazos that the man was wearing to extreme perturbation. He never went near Twin Sombreros Ranch. Bodkin was waiting for the terrible news to reach Surface&rsquo;s ears. On the third night Brazos frightened the proprietor of the hotel where Bodkin stayed into giving him the room next to Bodkin&rsquo;s. Brazos made sure Bodkin was out, then carefully cut a hole through the partition in a corner where it would not soon be discovered. This done, Brazos sat down to wait. Some time Bodkin would be cornered in that room by Surface, or would confer with one or more of the rancher&rsquo;s men. Brazos meant to hide there, going out only after nightfall, until the developments he expected reached their climax. But as Brazos&rsquo; luck would always have it, his marvelous patience did not need to be exercised. At midnight, just after the eastbound train had arrived, Bodkin entered his room with two men. Brazos, moving about in his stocking feet, glued his ear to the little hole in the corner. &ldquo;Talk low, fellars,&rdquo; Bodkin said, &ldquo;I&rsquo;m scared even of the walls in this town.... Keene hasn&rsquo;t been seen for three days.&rdquo; &ldquo;Sure as God made little apples he&rsquo;s trailin&rsquo; you,&rdquo; whispered one of them. &ldquo;I feel it, Brad.... Set down. Hyar&rsquo;s likker an&rsquo; cigars. I sure got a lot on my chest.&rdquo; Brazos&rsquo; frame leaped as if galvanized by a vital current at the name Brad. That night on the road, had he mistaken the name Bard for Brad? He was going to find out, and he stiffened with eagerness. He heard the gurgle of liquor out of a bottle and the striking of matches. &ldquo;Panhandle Ruckfall showed yellow clear to his gizzard,&rdquo; spoke up another voice, thin and low, somehow sibilant. &ldquo;He turned the job down. I raised the ante to two thousand dollars. Ruckfall gave me the laugh. &lsquo;A hell of a lot of fun I&rsquo;d get out of ten thousand after meeting Brazos Keene!&rsquo; is what he said.... He had too much sense to tackle such a deal. He might have killed Keene, but it&rsquo;s an even bet that Keene would kill him.... There was not another gunman in Dodge or Abilene who would have any chance against Keene. I advised against that... and there we are. Wild Bill was in Hays City&mdash;sheriff there. But we couldn&rsquo;t hire him for a dirty job like this. And if we tried Billy the Kid he&rsquo;d be liable to bore us.&rdquo; &ldquo;We&rsquo;re stuck,&rdquo; whispered Bodkin, thickly. &ldquo;I&rsquo;ve been keepin&rsquo; out of the boss&rsquo;s way. But he corraled me today.... Gawd Almighty! I reckoned he was goin&rsquo; to shoot me.&rdquo; An eloquent silence gave Brazos time to grasp this new connection. So there was a Bard as well as a Brad! &ldquo;Did you fetch them?&rdquo; queried Bodkin. &ldquo;Yes. And Orcutt with them. They went to Hailey&rsquo;s.&rdquo; &ldquo;Now what?&rdquo; asked the third man. &ldquo;We&rsquo;ll lay low till it&rsquo;s over, Brad.&rdquo; &ldquo;Listen,&rdquo; whispered this member of the trio. &ldquo;It&rsquo;ll be over pronto. Brazos Keene will see through thet dodge. Bard&rsquo;s black-eyed wench is a slick one. But I&rsquo;ll bet she fails hyar.&rdquo; &ldquo;The hell&rsquo;s fire he can&rsquo;t,&rdquo; retorted Brad. &ldquo;You don&rsquo;t savvy that hombre. Now here&rsquo;s what I think of your deal. I&rsquo;m not beholdin&rsquo; to any of you. An&rsquo; tomorrow I&rsquo;m lightin&rsquo; out of this town an&rsquo; I&rsquo;m ridin&rsquo; far. If you&rsquo;ve got an ounce of sense you&rsquo;ll do the same.&rdquo; &ldquo;Brad, I can&rsquo;t pull up stakes hyar. I&rsquo;m goin&rsquo; to be sheriff of this county.&rdquo; &ldquo;You&rsquo;re goin&rsquo; to be a stiff,&rdquo; snorted Brad. &ldquo;Not so loud,&rdquo; put in the third man, with his cool voice. &ldquo;Bodkin, I&rsquo;m afraid Brad has it figured. I&rsquo;d say if we had plenty of time we&rsquo;d have a sure thing with Bess Syvertsen on the job. She&rsquo;s the most fascinating girl I ever met. But the hell of it is, can we take time? It&rsquo;s got to be done right now.&rdquo; &ldquo;We&rsquo;ll have to give her time.&rdquo; &ldquo;Every hour adds to the doubt and suspicion already working.&rdquo; &ldquo;Even with Brazos Keene dead&mdash;which is sure a far-fetched conclusion, gentlemen&mdash;this town is going to think on. Henderson, Kiskadden, Inskip, Moore, Hadley, Stevens&mdash;all these men are getting their heads together. They are going to buck the Cattlemen&rsquo;s Association. They&rsquo;ll split it wide open. Most of them are honest cattlemen, you know. They&rsquo;ve just been fooled. Cattlemen are the easiest of men to fool because they take a little irregularity for granted, even among themselves, and they don&rsquo;t want to think. But when it comes to being robbed by rustlers&mdash;they wake up.... Look at the Lincoln County War&mdash;the Nebraska range feud, the Wyoming Jasper deal, or any of the famous examples, especially that Sewall McCoy-Russ Slaughter combine a few years ago over here in New Mexico. And Brazos Keene is the cowboy who ferreted that out, confronted those men with the fact of their guilt&mdash;and killed them both!&rdquo; Another pregnant little silence ensued. One of the men got up to move about and breathe hard. A second poured out a drink. That interview was wearing to a close. And Brazos grew tense and stiff with a fast approaching problem of what his next move should be. &ldquo;Fellars,&rdquo; said Brad, at length, &ldquo;I&rsquo;m pullin&rsquo; up stakes. An&rsquo; I don&rsquo;t mind tellin&rsquo; you I&rsquo;d take that bag of gold with me, if I could find it.&rdquo; &ldquo;Ha! Ha!&rdquo; laughed Bodkin low and sarcastically. Brad was not the only one who had had that ingenious idea. &ldquo;Where did he put it?&rdquo; queried the unknown man. &ldquo;He must have banked such a large sum.&rdquo; &ldquo;Not much. He hid it,&rdquo; declared Bodkin. &ldquo;What was the motive in that?&rdquo; &ldquo;He couldn&rsquo;t bank it. An&rsquo; it&rsquo;s too soon yet after Neece&rsquo;s holdup. But it runs in my mind that he&rsquo;ll keep it close so he&rsquo;ll be well heeled when he slopes.&rdquo; &ldquo;Does Bard know where that money is?&rdquo; &ldquo;No more than do I.... It&rsquo;s always stuck in his craw&mdash;that bag of gold. He an&rsquo; Orcutt held Neece up. An&rsquo; once I heard Orcutt say, &lsquo;Why did we let that gold get out of our hands?&rsquo;&rdquo; &ldquo;Same reason that applies to all of us. The stronger will of a crookeder man!... Well, he&rsquo;s run his race. It&rsquo;s not in the nature of things for all the men he has used to stand around now, waiting to be hanged or shot. How about you, Bod?&rdquo; &ldquo;I&rsquo;ll stick around,&rdquo; replied Bodkin, evasively. &ldquo;Every man for himself from now on, eh?&rdquo; &ldquo;Let&rsquo;s drink to thet.&rdquo; Brazos had only a moment more to decide his course of action. All the tiger in him leaped at the thought of confronting these conspirators before they left that room. He had heard the facts. But the strong heady impulse to kill could not hold against his intelligence, his judgment, his genius for thinking the right thing at the right moment. There would be little to gain in a fight and very much to risk. Wherefore Brazos relaxed from that passionate blood lust. This Brad, and the unnamed man, would go their separate ways, and probably never cross Brazos&rsquo; trail again. But Bodkin, coward though he was, had some powerful motive for remaining in Las Animas. Brazos heard the two men depart, treading softly, and he heard Bodkin curse his relief and satisfaction. Something had ended and it was more than that interview. Brazos went to bed and for a long while was aware of Bodkin pacing the floor. Then all sounds ceased. The hour was late. Brazos could address himself to considering the exciting possibilities for the morrow. So he was to be made game of by this Bess Syvertsen&mdash;this handsome black-eyed clever little siren whom no cowboy could resist? Brazos confessed to a weakness for meeting Bess and he began to revolve in mind a cunning to match her own. But before he perfected it, he went over the situation as it stood.
