He had no immediate gunplay to consider. The old range custom of getting rid of an enemy by the common method of provoking a gun fight was going to fail in the case of Brazos Keene. As Brazos&rsquo; tension of mind eased to that fact he had a melancholy exultation in his reputation. This Surface combine could not find a man to meet him face to face. Wild Bill Hickok and Billy the Kid could not be approached for the simple reason that the great frontier sheriff on one hand and the great frontier desperado on the other would both take Brazos Keene&rsquo;s side. It was something of which to be proud, something to tell June, when he saw her. He had sent word to her by Jack that he might not be able to see her for a day or two. June would understand. The warmth and the softness of the thought of her was an emotion he sternly banished from his mind. This Bard Syvertsen, with his allies, would proceed with caution and secrecy. They would not risk an open encounter with Brazos, nor would they dare shoot him in the back on the street or in a gambling den. That would bring to a head the already aroused public suspicion. The girl Bess would work on Brazos as she had done upon poor Allen Neece. Brazos thought over every possible phase of such a plot and how to meet it and what invention of his own might not only defeat them but gain the objective he had set himself&mdash;a betrayal of Surface. Brazos occupied his old stand against the wall of the station building. His presence, for those who noticed him, served to inhibit somewhat the leisurely pleasure of the hour. Bess Syvertsen was there with some country folk about whom Brazos was almost as curious as he was about her. He needed only one look to convince himself that no two of the four men could be Bard Syvertsen or Orcutt. The fifth was a woman of rather bold and flashy appearance. Brazos studied them with interest. He was surprised at this incident. He had not expected the stellar member of Surface&rsquo;s crooked trio to show any interest in people of the community. He had expected them to be strangers. Moreover Bess Syvertsen should be looking for him, which she assuredly did not happen to be doing. The train arrived, and the woman, accompanied by the best-dressed of the men, boarded it. Bess, with the other two, turned away to stroll along the station platform, following the crowd up street. Brazos, from under his sombrero brim, looked that trio over as if his eyes were magnifying glasses. The two men were hangers about town; he had seen them somewhere. Brazos paid little attention to Bess Syvertsen&rsquo;s cowgirl garb, except to note that she packed a gun somewhat too heavy for her slim build. He looked at her face. From a distance it appeared oval, of pale olive hue, lighted by piercing dark eyes. As she came nearer he had opportunity to observe more closely. A small face, framed in dark hair which showed well under the small black sombrero, it would have been strikingly pretty but for a hard ruthless hawklike cast that Brazos did not miss. He would have been most thoughtful about it but for the conviction, as she passed, that without doubt she knew him and had all the while been aware of his presence. She was a consummate actress then, which discovery occupied his thoughts to the exclusion of her singular physical attraction. Brazos wended a thoughtful way up street, and though he lingered here and there until nightfall he did not see her again. Monday brought back the bustle and rising dust and moving color to the cattle town. Brazos felt that this day he would meet Bess Syvertsen and he was on edge for the event. He would give her all the opportunity to approach him, if that was to be her first step. Would she make that move on the street, in one of the stores, at the post office or railroad station, or in the seclusion of a saloon or gambling hall? During the day he saw her twice on the street and once in the lobby of Hailey&rsquo;s hotel, and each time she passed him with different persons and apparently did not see him. Brazos decided that she was making as many chance acquaintances as she could, so that eventually her attachment of him would not appear marked. &ldquo;Doggone!&rdquo; drawled Brazos, to himself. &ldquo;I&rsquo;m shore gettin&rsquo; powerful keen aboot this heah bad girl in cowboy pants. Darn good thing for me I&rsquo;m in love with June Neece!&rdquo; Wherefore Brazos was all primed and set for the momentous meeting when it came about at the post office. Bess had dropped out of the sky, apparently, to follow him up to the window where Brazos was asking for mail. She pressed close to Brazos and asked the clerk for a stamp. What a hot gush ran along Brazos&rsquo; veins at the sound of that young high-pitched voice! He recognized it, except for the sweet quality, which had taken the place of the nervous shrill note he remembered. For the stamp she tendered a hundred-dollar bill, which the clerk pushed back with a laugh. &ldquo;What will I do?&rdquo; she complained. &ldquo;I&rsquo;ll trust you. Go to the bank and get change.&rdquo; Brazos promptly produced some coins. &ldquo;Heah, Lady, I&rsquo;ll oblige yu,&rdquo; he drawled. &ldquo;Oh, thank you,&rdquo; she replied, suddenly becoming aware of his presence. She took the two cents and paid for her stamp, but she had no letter upon which to put it. Then she turned to Brazos, who had dropped back a few steps. That was the moment and it certainly gave Brazos one of the great thrills of his life. Here was the girl who was the prime instrument in the plot to murder him. Nevertheless, Brazos felt her charm. Brazos took off his sombrero and stood uncovered before her, with his habitual cool courtesy in the presence of the opposite sex. &ldquo;I am Bess Syvertsen,&rdquo; she said, deliberately. Brazos made her a gallant bow. &ldquo;I shore am happy to meet yu,&rdquo; he replied, but he did not mention his name. While these remarks were exchanged she led Brazos aside from the doorway to the window. They stood there then, looking at each other. She was as sincere as a woman could be. Brazos&rsquo; interest, a cowboy&rsquo;s sudden flare-up at meeting a new girl, was only a smiling dissimulation. Surprise seemed to be her dominant feeling of the moment, behind which she betrayed a profoundly deep interest. Brazos thought ironically that a girl who meant to murder a man in cold blood would be likely to have some interest in him. &ldquo;Put on your sombrero. I&rsquo;m not used to men standing bareheaded before me,&rdquo; she said, a little irritated by something she had not anticipated. &ldquo;Thet&rsquo;s just one of my habits,&rdquo; rejoined Brazos, frankly. &ldquo;Where I come from men bare their haids to women.&rdquo; &ldquo;I like it. But it struck me&mdash;sort of&mdash;of odd.... You&rsquo;re from Texas?&rdquo; &ldquo;I reckon yu guessed right.&rdquo; &ldquo;Who are you?&rdquo; &ldquo;Aw, I hate to tell yu.&rdquo; &ldquo;You needn&rsquo;t be afraid,&rdquo; she said, with a smile that changed the flinty beauty of her face. &ldquo;I can stand a shock.&rdquo; &ldquo;But if I tell yu&mdash;why yu&rsquo;ll dodge an&rsquo; run oot thet door, leavin&rsquo; me unhappy.&rdquo; &ldquo;No, I won&rsquo;t.&rdquo; &ldquo;Wal, then, doggone it&mdash;I&rsquo;m thet poor hombre, Brazos Keene.&rdquo; &ldquo;No!&rdquo; she exclaimed. Despite her deceit she betrayed sincerity as well. &ldquo;Not that hard-riding, hard-drinking, hard-shooting cowboy?&rdquo; &ldquo;I shore am ashamed to confess it, Lady.&rdquo; &ldquo;But he&rsquo;s a grown-up man&mdash;and a gunman to boot.... Not you!&rdquo; &ldquo;Yes&mdash;me.&rdquo; &ldquo;Oh, no. Not you with your nice boy&rsquo;s face, your sleepy eyes, your curly hair, your wonderful smile!&rdquo; &ldquo;Air yu complimentin&rsquo; me, Miss Syvertsen?&rdquo; &ldquo;I just can&rsquo;t believe you&rsquo;re Brazos Keene,&rdquo; she declared, seriously, and she flashed those glittering black eyes over Brazos&rsquo; silver spurs and high boots, his dark garb against which the heavy black gun belt and gun did not show conspicuously, his vest and scarf, to his face where it fixed with as compelling a scrutiny as Brazos cared to meet. &ldquo;You forget yore West, my girl,&rdquo; he drawled. &ldquo;Range talk blames me for a lot thet I&rsquo;m innocent of.&rdquo; &ldquo;Range talk is always true, Brazos Keene.&rdquo; &ldquo;Wal, I&rsquo;m gonna return yore compliment, so far as lookin&rsquo; yu over is concerned,&rdquo; said Brazos, with his disarming smile, and proceeded to treat her to an examination as keen as hers had been. The spurs she wore had been used to ride with and not for ornament, though they were of finest Spanish design. Her elegant custom-made boots adorned the smallest and most shapely feet Brazos had ever seen. He felt the creeping fire under his skin. Those little boots had made the tracks in the dust under the tree out at the Hill cabin. Brazos had to keep his gaze lowered so that she might not read the sudden passion that flamed over him. But her slender legs and rounded hips, the grace of which the trousers accentuated rather than hid, were excuse enough to hold the gaze of a woman-mad cowboy such as he was said to be. The gun she packed had too businesslike a look to please Brazos. She wore a gray blouse and a red scarf and a dark leather vest with pearl buttons. It hung open. Brazos noted that it could not have been buttoned over her swelling breasts. And as he permitted his scrutiny to end on her face, he saw that to be beautiful on the moment, warm and radiant, almost sweet with the light of woman&rsquo;s pleased vanity. And smiling into her eyes Brazos could no more believe that she was a hardened frontier dance-hall girl, a lure used to entice men, than she could believe he was the frontier&rsquo;s wildest and bloodiest cowboy. &ldquo;Well, how do you like me?&rdquo; she queried, archly. &ldquo;You took long enough about it. I felt as if you were undressing me.&rdquo; &ldquo;Wal, I like yu too darn powerful much,&rdquo; rejoined Brazos, which outburst was a true statement. &ldquo;Why too much? It couldn&rsquo;t be too much to suit me.&rdquo; &ldquo;Aw, yu&rsquo;re same as all the rest of them, even if yu have got on pants.... Gosh, I&rsquo;d love to see yu in the clothes yu belong in.&rdquo; &ldquo;And what are they?&rdquo; &ldquo;Wal, I reckon somethin&rsquo; white, with a touch of color&mdash;red, like them spots in yore cheeks&mdash;an&rsquo; with yore arms an&rsquo; neck bare.... Yu&rsquo;d shore slay the cowboys, Bess.&rdquo; &ldquo;It&rsquo;s not impossible to see me that way, Brazos,&rdquo; she replied, dreamily. &ldquo;But the occasion is wanting.... When have I been to a decent dance&mdash;with a&mdash;&mdash;&rdquo; She caught herself before romance, the poor jewel of her soul, betrayed her, and she gazed thoughtfully out of the window at the passing procession. Brazos had struck the right note. No matter what she was&mdash;no matter what any woman was&mdash;there still abided deep within that something eternally, ineradicably feminine. This girl might be old in border experience, but she could not have been more than a year or two along in her twenties. &ldquo;How come I never saw yu before?&rdquo; Brazos repeated his earlier query. &ldquo;I&rsquo;m sure I don&rsquo;t know. You&rsquo;ve got a pair of eyes, cowboy,&rdquo; she replied. &ldquo;Wal, fact is I been lookin&rsquo; for men.&rdquo; &ldquo;What men?&rdquo; &ldquo;I don&rsquo;t know. Reckon the hombres who&rsquo;re lookin&rsquo; for me,&rdquo; rejoined Brazos, gloomily. &ldquo;Oh, I see. Have you enemies here? But of course you have. I forget who you are.&rdquo; &ldquo;I made some when I hit this burg a few weeks ago. Yu probably heahed aboot thet.&rdquo; &ldquo;Why don&rsquo;t you fork your horse and ride back to Texas?&rdquo; &ldquo;I shore been wantin&rsquo; to an&rsquo; maybe I will yet. But I was kinda riled. At thet I cain&rsquo;t go, now I&rsquo;ve met yu.&rdquo; &ldquo;Some things I heard about you are true,&rdquo; she said, brightly. &ldquo;Ahuh. Who told yu?&rdquo; &ldquo;It might have been Lura Surface and the Neece twins. Women often help each other to self-preservation, you know.&rdquo; &ldquo;Wal, do yu hold it against me thet I tried oot those girls?&rdquo; &ldquo;No. I admire your taste.&rdquo; &ldquo;Thet Surface girl was shore provokin&rsquo;. But she&rsquo;s a destroyin&rsquo; angel. An&rsquo; the Neeces air too soft an&rsquo; sweet. An&rsquo; besides yu cain&rsquo;t tell them apart.&rdquo; Even though he was playing a game, Brazos had a sense of disloyalty at this speech. &ldquo;I would have thought one of them just your kind.&rdquo; &ldquo;Yu think a lot wrong aboot me, Bess.... For Gawd&rsquo;s sake don&rsquo;t tell me yu&rsquo;re some lucky cattleman&rsquo;s wife?&rdquo; &ldquo;No, I&rsquo;m not, worse luck. I&rsquo;m single and fancy free. At least I was until I came into this post office.&rdquo; &ldquo;We air gettin&rsquo; somewhere.&rdquo; That drawling cool assertion seemed to acquaint Bess Syvertsen with the fact that she was reacting honestly to an intrigue the intent of which had been entirely different. &ldquo;Where are we getting, Brazos?&rdquo; she inquired, curtly. &ldquo;I leave it to yu, Lady,&rdquo; replied Brazos, just as curtly. &ldquo;You just met me, cowboy!&rdquo; she expostulated. &ldquo;Wal, what do yu want? Yu struck me deep. But don&rsquo;t misunderstand me. No Texan ever insulted a girl.&rdquo; &ldquo;Insult! How could you insult me?&rdquo; &ldquo;You like me, Brazos Keene?&rdquo; &ldquo;Like! Why, I think yu&rsquo;re just wonderful. An&rsquo; I don&rsquo;t care who yu air, where yu come from, or who yu&rsquo;re with.&rdquo; &ldquo;You might be taking a risk. My father has no use for cowboys.&rdquo; &ldquo;Is he heah?&rdquo; &ldquo;Yes. Bard Syvertsen. He&rsquo;s a cattle buyer. We travel all over from Kansas City to Denver. Father has a deal on with Surface and Miller.&rdquo; &ldquo;Who else with yu, Lady?&rdquo; &ldquo;Orcutt, a partner of father&rsquo;s. He&rsquo;s not so young, but he&rsquo;s sweet on me. And he won&rsquo;t take to you.&rdquo; &ldquo;Jealous, eh? But air yu sweet on him?&rdquo; &ldquo;Sweet on Hen Orcutt?&rdquo; she ejaculated, as if the idea was unutterably new and somehow belittling. &ldquo;Hell no!&rdquo; Brazos stared hard at her. &ldquo;Lady, yore lips air too sweet an&rsquo; red for cuss words.&rdquo; &ldquo;Can&rsquo;t I swear in front of you, Brazos Keene?&rdquo; &ldquo;Wal, yu can, I reckon, but only once.&rdquo; &ldquo;You&rsquo;d turn your back on me?&rdquo; she queried, in amaze. &ldquo;I shore would&mdash;an&rsquo; be most doggone sorry.&rdquo; &ldquo;Are you trying to string me? Whoever heard of a cowboy who hadn&rsquo;t played up to dance-hall girls?&rdquo; &ldquo;Yu&rsquo;re lookin&rsquo; at thet cowboy now, Lady&mdash;if by playin&rsquo; up yu mean more than havin&rsquo; a drink an&rsquo; a dance with one.&rdquo; &ldquo;I meant more, Brazos Keene.&rdquo; &ldquo;Then yu got me wrong, Miss Syvertsen,&rdquo; returned Brazos, coldly. &ldquo;Good day.&rdquo; &ldquo;Wait,&rdquo; she said, and detained him with a hand on his arm. &ldquo;I apologize. I take your word. But don&rsquo;t hold it against me that naturally I thought you to be like most men. I see you&rsquo;re not.... Neither am I a dance-hall girl, though I can swear.&rdquo; &ldquo;Wal then&mdash;where air we?&rdquo; drawled Brazos, his smile coming slowly. &ldquo;I don&rsquo;t know,&rdquo; she admitted. &ldquo;I reckon talkin&rsquo; aboot yore menfolks.&rdquo; &ldquo;At this particular moment I wish they were in Hades,&rdquo; she said, with sudden passion that transformed her. &ldquo;Bess, does thet mean if it wasn&rsquo;t for them yu&rsquo;d like to try oot this new acquaintance?&rdquo; &ldquo;Yes&mdash;and more.&rdquo; &ldquo;Wal, it&rsquo;s just too bad. Always my luck! I ride the ranges an&rsquo; I meet girls. Reckon I&rsquo;m hard to please. My mother taught me to respect her sex. I don&rsquo;t care for town hussies or camp trulls. I cain&rsquo;t stand these nice goody-goody spooney little girls, neither. Lura Surface was one to make a cowboy ride high an&rsquo; handsome. But she was a flirt. An&rsquo; heah I meet yu!&rdquo; &ldquo;Brazos, I might be a flirt&mdash;or worse,&rdquo; she said, as he ended eloquently. &ldquo;Shore. I&rsquo;m only confessin&rsquo; how yu strike me. An&rsquo; yu&rsquo;ll have to tell me if yu want to change thet.&rdquo; &ldquo;Brazos! The hell of it is I&mdash;I don&rsquo;t want to change it,&rdquo; she said, with emotion. &ldquo;It&rsquo;s got me by the throat.... Now doesn&rsquo;t that flatter your cowboy vanity?&rdquo; &ldquo;I don&rsquo;t savvy yu, Bess. I never could savvy a girl who was deep. All the same I feel as if yu were fightin&rsquo; somethin&rsquo; yu didn&rsquo;t want me to know. Tell me or not, as yu like. But if I strike yu pretty pronto, yu know, an&rsquo; bold&mdash;it&rsquo;s because I see no sense in holdin&rsquo; back things. I&rsquo;ve a bad reputation an&rsquo; I&rsquo;m liable to be shot any time. Life is too short for my kind not to live from day to day.&rdquo; &ldquo;Thet&rsquo;ll make no difference. It&rsquo;s what yu air to me thet&rsquo;d count. Take it or leave it. No harm&rsquo;s been done. An&rsquo; if there&rsquo;s a regret it&rsquo;ll be mine. I&rsquo;ll shore be glad I met yu an&rsquo; had this little talk&mdash;an&rsquo; I&rsquo;ll ride on always rememberin&rsquo; what a turrible lot I missed.&rdquo; &ldquo;Meaning friendship&mdash;or more&mdash;with me... kisses and all that?&rdquo; &ldquo;I reckon.&rdquo; &ldquo;Take you or leave you?&rdquo; she mused, darkly. &ldquo;For a notorious gunman you certainly are a strange fellow. You put me in a hard place.... I won&rsquo;t let you go.... I&rsquo;d rather you didn&rsquo;t think me as&mdash;as good as gold&mdash;such a wonderful girl. Yet it&rsquo;s so&mdash;so&mdash;&mdash;&rdquo; &ldquo;Wal, Bess, if yu&rsquo;re not sendin&rsquo; me aboot my business let me dream on,&rdquo; interrupted Brazos. &ldquo;Yu couldn&rsquo;t change yore looks nor thet somethin&rsquo; like sweet fire thet I feel in yu.&rdquo; &ldquo;But, romantic cowboy, you don&rsquo;t understand a woman.&rdquo; &ldquo;Wal, am I the only romantic cowboy yu ever met?&rdquo; They stood at that post-office window for two hours more. Brazos had the advantage over her, inasmuch as he knew beforehand just what she was, while all the characteristics she had conjured up about him were wrong, as well as the ugly, vicious and hard, and matured attributes supposed to mark gunmen. Brazos could not help but be aware that he did not have to put anything on to be fascinating to women. There was a glamour about even his name. If Bess Syvertsen had expected to find him like Panhandle Ruckfall she was suffering for it now. It seemed possible that Brazos&rsquo; physical person had had the same effect he had known it to have on other girls. He had been swift to find her vulnerable point. If he had taken a cowboy&rsquo;s fancy for her and had chucked her under the chin it would have been diamond cut diamond. But whatever Bess Syvertsen&rsquo;s life had been&mdash;and Brazos knew that it had been dishonest and bitter&mdash;she was helpless before the terrible fact of being taken for good when she was evil, for fine when she was base, for being loved when she should have been despised. She never got anywhere near telling Brazos this lamentable truth. Yet in her woman&rsquo;s heart she wanted to. If he had fallen in love with her at first sight, that was really more than she had planned. As she saw it, the game was already won. And she found herself in a dilemma. As they talked on, apparently at cross-purposes, little by little this became evident to Brazos. The cruel truth was that she had plotted to lend her beauty and her person to the enticement of this cowboy for the sole purpose of luring him somewhere so that her accomplices could murder him. But now, having met him, possibly fallen in love with him, at first sight, she did not want him murdered. That was how Brazos interpreted her contradictory and ambiguous and strange remarks. The more she talked, the more she listened to Brazos and felt his slow smile warm upon her, the more she became divided against herself. He saw her predicament and augmented it by every word and glance possible. He was relentless, and yet as he had liked her at once, he grew to pity her at the last. &ldquo;It&rsquo;s growing late. I&rsquo;m hungry,&rdquo; she said, finally. &ldquo;Take me some place to eat.&rdquo; &ldquo;I reckon the safest place will be the Twin Sombreros restaurant,&rdquo; he replied, as he followed her out upon the street. &ldquo;Did yu ever walk with a gunman&mdash;when he was kinda nervous aboot the men he met?&rdquo; &ldquo;No. It&rsquo;ll be thrilling,&rdquo; she laughed. &ldquo;And perhaps make history for Las Animas.&rdquo; They walked down the street, which at that hour was pretty numerously populated. She was a superb actress and walked and talked as naturally as any ordinary girl accompanied by a masculine friend. But Brazos, out of the corner of his eye, saw the unobtrusive piercing glances she shot ahead. She was pale, too. He could not read her mind then. Nevertheless on the walk from the post office to Twin Sombreros restaurant he made certain they did not meet Bard Syvertsen and Orcutt. They went into the restaurant, which, fortunately for Brazos, appeared half full of customers. They found seats at a corner table where he could watch the door. On the way to it Brazos had flashed a significant glance to one of the twins, whom he took to be June. Her face had not changed markedly at sight of him, but he read her eyes. This entrance of his with Bess Syvertsen would be an ordeal for her. Brazos regretted that he could not avoid it. On the other hand he wanted to see how the cowgirl would react to the proximity of the Neece girls. If Bess had any qualms of conscience, if she had any compunction whatsoever she certainly hid them perfectly. She was tense, in the grip of strong feeling, but it had nothing to do with memory of the boy she had betrayed to his death. Brazos marveled at her cool command of herself and his heart became flint to her. His plot to deceive her and wring a confession from her, one way or another, seemed to grow immeasurably easier. That she had vanity, and could succumb to it, and to love, did not soften him. To Brazos&rsquo; relief June sent the Mexican girl to wait upon them; and after that he attended only to the door and to the strange creature opposite him. &ldquo;Do you know these Neece girls?&rdquo; she asked, curiously. &ldquo;Yes, I&rsquo;ve met them,&rdquo; replied Brazos, easily. &ldquo;Reckon they didn&rsquo;t cotton to me.... Yu see I was arrested by Bodkin for murderin&rsquo; their brother. But I was innocent an&rsquo; Kiskadden let me off.&rdquo; &ldquo;Bard told me about it,&rdquo; said the girl, dropping her eyes for a second. &ldquo;About your holding up the posse and driving them to the jail. That was a Brazos Keene trick, he said.... You must be sore at Bodkin.&rdquo; &ldquo;Tolerable. I&rsquo;ll bore him sooner or later, when I&rsquo;ve nothing else to do,&rdquo; drawled Brazos, with an air of detached deadliness that had its effect on her. &ldquo;It&rsquo;s thet Joel Barsh I&rsquo;m sore at. The young hombre who put the noose aboot my neck.&rdquo; &ldquo;And how about these&mdash;these gunmen, or whoever they are&mdash;who are on your trail?&rdquo; &ldquo;Bess, when men like them don&rsquo;t come right oot in the open to meet yu, then yu know they&rsquo;re hardly worth considerin&rsquo;.&rdquo; &ldquo;You&rsquo;re not afraid?&rdquo; she asked, her dark gaze in brooding wonder and admiration upon him. &ldquo;Nope.... But, Bess, I never talk aboot thet sort of thing. Yu flatter me if yu&rsquo;re worried. An&rsquo; I kinda like thet. Only don&rsquo;t yu worry none.&rdquo; &ldquo;Worry!&rdquo; she uttered an incredulous laugh, as if suddenly realizing she was concerned about the life of a cowboy whom she had been hired to lure to his death. &ldquo;It seems to me I&rsquo;d take a precarious chance with my heart&mdash;to fix it upon an uncertain hombre like Brazos Keene.&rdquo; &ldquo;Yu shore would, Bess.... Wal, heah comes our supper. Air yu hungry?&rdquo; &ldquo;I forgot we came here to eat,&rdquo; she said. During the meal she spoke but little and seemed pondering her growing problem. Once more out on the street she recovered something of her vivacity. When Brazos remarked that he had better be saying good night she took his arm and held it. They walked up and down the main street of Las Animas. She made no move to entice Brazos down a dark side street or into any place. &ldquo;Wal, Bess, I hate to be makin&rsquo; excuses,&rdquo; drawled Brazos, &ldquo;but I&rsquo;m a cowboy. I&rsquo;m used to forkin&rsquo; a hawse. An&rsquo; I&rsquo;m near daid walkin&rsquo;.&rdquo; &ldquo;We might try the railroad station an&rsquo; set down for a while.&rdquo; So they went there. Bess appeared less strained when they were among people, though she did not lose her watchfulness. After the train left she rose and said: &ldquo;Come, I must go now. I&rsquo;m so tired I can hardly wag.... Do you affect all girls this way, Brazos Keene?&rdquo; &ldquo;What way?&rdquo; She clung to his arm during the walk to Hailey&rsquo;s where she released it. &ldquo;Brazos, I thought I was glad to meet you&mdash;at first. But I&rsquo;m not so sure now.&rdquo; &ldquo;Aw, thet&rsquo;s not kind. Is it good-by, then?&rdquo; &ldquo;Where will you meet me tomorrow?&rdquo; &ldquo;Heah. Anywhere&mdash;any time.&rdquo; &ldquo;Anywhere?&rdquo; she asked, with her unfathomable eyes piercing him. &ldquo;How about out of town?&rdquo; &ldquo;Wal, I reckon it&rsquo;d better be heah,&rdquo; returned Brazos. And when he said that it seemed a passion wrenched her. &ldquo;Tomorrow then. Here at two o&rsquo;clock. Adios.&rdquo; Next day Bess Syvertsen was late. Brazos walked to and fro in front of the hotel. She came at length betraying signs of anger and she was all the more handsome for that. She vouchsafed no explanation. But Brazos did not need one. They spent the afternoon together, walking, sitting in the station, standing in the post office. Brazos made violent love to Bess and she drank it up thirstily. She begged off going to supper with him or seeing him that night. When she left him she was apparently again dominated by the attraction he had for her. This state of affairs continued next day and on the following, with Bess betraying to Brazos&rsquo; keen eye varying augmenting emotions. Despite Brazos&rsquo; knowledge and his ruthlessness, it was quite impossible not to be receptive to her fascination. No doubt her predicament accounted for some of this. She had not done one single thing, since their meeting at the post office, that Brazos could construe to be intent on her part to lure him to his death. That, of course, had been the original incentive; that was what her father, Bard Syvertsen, and his man Orcutt, were waiting for. No doubt when Bess faced them after another day with Brazos she had to lie and plead for more time. These hours together must have been sweet to the unfortunate girl. She prolonged them. Brazos had begun by dissimulation. But as their affair wore on there seemed to be less and less need for this. The respect, the gallantry, the deference Brazos paid her were things she thirsted for almost as much as love. And as daily, almost hourly, she responded to these, Brazos grew more toward sincerely feeling them. He divined, too, that she was so bound to her father, and perhaps to Orcutt, that she could not accept an honest man&rsquo;s offer of marriage even if she dared risk his love. On the fourth day of this strange relation Bess came an hour late. Her face was colorless and showed other signs of havoc. For once Brazos failed to read the expression of her piercing eyes. Behind her stalked two men, one tall, the other short. That they were without vests and gun belts Brazos&rsquo; sharp eye recorded before he paid attention to their features. The little man had a visage that was a map of frontier crime. This should be Orcutt. The tall man then was Bard Syvertsen and he was a splendid specimen of Norwegian manhood, lofty of stature, fair-haired, with eyes like blue ice, and a handsome craggy face. He was all of forty years old. &ldquo;Brazos,&rdquo; said Bess, hurriedly, as she advanced, &ldquo;meet my father, Bard Syvertsen, and Hen Orcutt.&rdquo; &ldquo;Howdy, gentlemen,&rdquo; drawled Brazos, in his cool voice, and he made no effort to be other than Brazos Keene. At the moment he knew he risked no peril from them, and they had confronted him significantly unarmed. What their idea was, Brazos could not conjecture. Perhaps it was an overwhelming curiosity to see the cowboy at close hand. &ldquo;Howdy, Keene. Glad to meet you,&rdquo; said Orcutt, curtly. Syvertsen eyed Brazos with a curious intentness that was not all due to the fact of Brazos&rsquo; status on the frontier. Brazos read that cold blue eye as if it had been an open printed page. The Norwegian did not know fear; he was callous, yet he burned inwardly. He returned Brazos&rsquo; greeting in a voice Brazos would have recognized among a thousand voices. If Bard Syvertsen had been armed at the moment he would have been some time closer to the death Brazos meant to mete out to him. &ldquo;My girl has been spending a good deal of time with you, cowboy,&rdquo; he said. &ldquo;Wal, I reckon I know thet, an&rsquo; how lucky I am,&rdquo; replied Brazos. &ldquo;I object to it.&rdquo; &ldquo;Yeah.... An&rsquo; on what grounds, Mister Syvertsen?&rdquo; &ldquo;No insult intended. But it&rsquo;s common talk about town&mdash;you&rsquo;re a trifler with women. I told Bess, and that she must stop your attentions. She said I could tell you myself.&rdquo; &ldquo;Ahuh. Wal, I&rsquo;m sorry to say I cain&rsquo;t take offense. But in this heah case I&rsquo;m in daid earnest.&rdquo; Both these allies of Bess Syvertsen evinced expectation of any other reply save this&mdash;evasiveness on Brazos&rsquo; part or curt assurance that it was none of their business, or a cool denial. Orcutt blazed under his swarthy seamed skin and if Syvertsen&rsquo;s eyes did not glare with jealousy, Brazos was wrong. He loved the girl with a passion Brazos did not think paternal. &ldquo;Keene, I did not believe Bess,&rdquo; returned Syvertsen, as if forced. &ldquo;That accounts for this intrusion. You&rsquo;ll excuse us.&rdquo; They turned into the hotel and Brazos&rsquo; keen ear caught a remnant of a curse Orcutt was bestowing upon the other. Brazos could not gauge the significance of this encounter. &ldquo;Bess, what&rsquo;n hell was all thet aboot?&rdquo; queried Brazos, turning to the girl in apparent bewilderment. She seemed to be in distress. &ldquo;Come. People are staring,&rdquo; she answered hurriedly, and drew him away. The ensuing hours of the afternoon grew to be something of a nightmare to Brazos. They walked all over town and when too tired to walk more they sat down on whatever there was available. If Bess Syvertsen had been a fascinating creature on former days, she was on this occasion vastly more. Only late in the day did Brazos gather that the climax had come&mdash;that Bess Syvertsen had been driven by her accomplices to end the farce&mdash;or that she was a woman being torn apart by love and an evil power too strong for her. After supper, which they ate at Mexican Joe&rsquo;s, she leaned her elbows on the table, her face on her hands and gazed at Brazos with eyes that hid much and expressed more. &ldquo;Air yu in love with me, Bess?&rdquo; asked Brazos, for the hundredth time. &ldquo;Terribly. But what&rsquo;s the use?... If I give in to it I&rsquo;ll be killed.... If I don&rsquo;t you&rsquo;ll be killed.&rdquo; Brazos noted that she did not grasp it as strange when he asked no explanation of her enigmatic reply. She was beyond cunning. Evil was losing its hold over her. It struck him that she was evading the issue or postponing it, trying to cheat time, to extract some bitter sweetness from the present. Brazos had no doubt that when she had left the hotel with him some hours previous she had bowed to Syvertsen&rsquo;s bidding. But all afternoon she had struggled against that. Brazos knew what she did not guess&mdash;that he had seen Syvertsen and Orcutt ride out a side street toward the open country where they expected Bess to make a rendezvous with Brazos. She was a tortured woman. Up to nightfall Brazos expected some importunity of hers&mdash;some subterfuge to entice him out of town. But it never materialized, and for that proof of womanliness on her part he swore he would spare her when the worst befell. &ldquo;Let&rsquo;s go,&rdquo; she said, suddenly, her eyes alight with new impulse too soft to be crafty. They went out upon the street. It was the supper hour and the street was deserted. There was no one in the lobby of Hailey&rsquo;s hotel. &ldquo;Come!&rdquo; And she drew him with steel hands and will as steely, up the stairs to the floor above. The lamp had not been lit and the corridor was shadowy. Brazos grew wary. Still he could not sense any relation to Syvertsen and Orcutt in Bess&rsquo;s tense mood and action. She unlocked a door and opened it. &ldquo;Wal, sayin&rsquo; good night early, eh, honey?&rdquo; he drawled. &ldquo;It&rsquo;s been a hard day at thet. See yu tomorrow same time.&rdquo; &ldquo;Yes&mdash;but come in&mdash;now,&rdquo; she panted. &ldquo;Bess! Air yu loco&mdash;askin&rsquo; me into yore bedroom?&rdquo; &ldquo;Loco, indeed! Come... don&rsquo;t be a fool.&rdquo; &ldquo;I&rsquo;m only human, Bess&mdash;an&rsquo; I reckon I&rsquo;d weaken if we was goin&rsquo; to marry. But with all yore love talk I cain&rsquo;t see yu&rsquo;d marry me.&rdquo; &ldquo;My Gawd! What yu take me for? I told yu I was a Texan an&rsquo; had respect for a woman I loved.&rdquo; She threw her arms around his neck and clung to him quivering, appearing to stifle speech as well as sobs upon his breast. It was as if a new emotion had consumed a lesser fire within her. The paroxysm ended in a passionate embrace, in sudden wild despairing kisses upon his cheek and lips. And she tore at his hair. &ldquo;Go... go&mdash;before I...&rdquo; She broke off huskily, and releasing him shut the door in his face. Brazos heard her fling herself on a bed with smothered sobs. He went swiftly downstairs and out upon the street, where in a shadowed doorway he halted to watch and think. The street appeared less deserted; a wagon rolled by; the clip-clop of a saddle horse sounded at a distance; pedestrians were chugging by with heavy boot tread. &ldquo;Before I&mdash;before I&mdash;&rdquo; mused Brazos, thinking of Bess&rsquo;s last choked words. Before she what? Before she succumbed to that frenzy which had possessed her and dragged him into her room? No!... Before she betrayed herself to be other than the good woman she believed he thought her? No! Before she told him the truth! That was what Brazos felt drummed into his temples. That was what love had done for Bess Syvertsen. She might not betray Surface and she would not betray Syvertsen and Orcutt, but she could no longer deceive Brazos as to herself. Brazos&rsquo; morning habit of whipping and rolling his guns&mdash;at rare intervals he packed two guns&mdash;had infinitely more next morning than the perfunctory practice indulged in by all gunmen. His instinct told him the day had come&mdash;the meeting with the murderers of Allen Neece, was not far away. His dark favorite gun made a shining wheel as he rolled it on his finger. And like magic it leaped at his bidding from the gun sheath. He went down to breakfast with his right hand burning, with the thin skin on his thumb feeling almost raw. He was late for this meal, yet he lingered over it, brooding while he watched the street. When he saw Surface drive by in a buckboard he muttered. &ldquo;Ah-huh. I reckon my hunch was aboot correct.&rdquo; At length Brazos stalked out of Mexican Joe&rsquo;s tense for the climax, choosing as always to let the moment demand its decision and action of him. He met Kiskadden and Inskip on the street. &ldquo;What&rsquo;s Surface doin&rsquo; in town?&rdquo; he queried, bluntly. &ldquo;Meetin&rsquo; of the Cattlemen&rsquo;s Association,&rdquo; replied Inskip. &ldquo;Surface looked black as a thundercloud.&rdquo; &ldquo;Either of yu know Syvertsen an&rsquo; Orcutt when yu see them?&rdquo; &ldquo;I do,&rdquo; returned Kiskadden. &ldquo;They ducked in Hall&rsquo;s to avoid meetin&rsquo; me. Somethin&rsquo; on their minds, Brazos.&rdquo; &ldquo;Will yu fellars do me a favor? Cross the street heah an&rsquo; walk up thet side an&rsquo; down on this side. Don&rsquo;t miss seein&rsquo; anybody, but be particular to locate Syvertsen an&rsquo; Orcutt. I&rsquo;ll wait heah. Take yore time. Those hombres shore won&rsquo;t be paradin&rsquo; the street.&rdquo; Brazos leaned against the wall and watched, while his friends reconnoitred. They seemed to take a long while. It wanted a quarter of an hour to his appointment with Bess Syvertsen. Hank Bilyen came along, apparently casually, but he stepped aside to join Brazos. &ldquo;Kiskadden told me you was heah. What&rsquo;s comin&rsquo; off, Brazos?&rdquo; he queried, sharply. &ldquo;Go into Hall&rsquo;s an&rsquo; line up at the bar so yu won&rsquo;t look nosey. But if Syvertsen an&rsquo; Orcutt come oot be shore where they go.&rdquo; Bilyen&rsquo;s uncertainty ceased. Without another word he walked on to enter Hall&rsquo;s saloon. Inskip was the first of the other two men to get back. He breathed hard, his gray eye glinted. &ldquo;Brazos, I got a hunch there&rsquo;ll be hell a&rsquo;poppin&rsquo; pronto,&rdquo; he announced, excitedly. &ldquo;I seen Surface an&rsquo; Bodkin in the doorway of the stairs leadin&rsquo; up to the Odd Fellows. Surface was poundin&rsquo; his fist in his hand, purple in the face. An&rsquo; Bodkin was the color of sheep-skin.&rdquo; &ldquo;Ahuh... Aboot what time will thet cattlemen&rsquo;s meetin&rsquo; be comin&rsquo; off?&rdquo; &ldquo;At two. But I reckon with Surface on the rampage it&rsquo;ll be late.&rdquo; &ldquo;Wal, yu an&rsquo; Kiskadden make it yore business to be there so in case I run in yu won&rsquo;t miss nothin&rsquo;.&rdquo; &ldquo;Brazos, are you goin&rsquo; to brace Surface?&rdquo; &ldquo;Don&rsquo;t stand heah. Go back across the street. Watch Hall&rsquo;s. An&rsquo; when I go in yu come pronto.&rdquo; Kiskadden reached Brazos at exactly two o&rsquo;clock, the time of Brazos&rsquo; appointment with Bess. The Texan showed no exterior fire, but Brazos felt him burn. &ldquo;Surface just went into Hailey&rsquo;s. He stopped Bess Syvertsen, who was comin&rsquo; oot. I took time to light a smoke. I couldn&rsquo;t heah what Surface said to the girl, but I shore heahed her answer.&rdquo; &ldquo;An&rsquo; what was thet?&rdquo; &ldquo;&lsquo;No &mdash;&mdash; you, Surface! I won&rsquo;t! Get some one else to do yore dirty work!&rsquo;&rdquo; &ldquo;Ahuh. Short an&rsquo; sweet. I had Bess figured.... Anythin&rsquo; more?&rdquo; &ldquo;Surface hissed like a snake an&rsquo; dragged the girl into the lobby. He&rsquo;s there now, ridin&rsquo; her, I&rsquo;ll bet.&rdquo; &ldquo;Wal, he&rsquo;s ridin&rsquo; for a fall. What else, Kis? I&rsquo;m rustlin&rsquo;.&rdquo; &ldquo;I peeped into Hall&rsquo;s. Yore men air still there.&rdquo; &ldquo;Drinkin&rsquo;?&rdquo; &ldquo;Not them. Watchin&rsquo; oot the window.&rdquo; &ldquo;Wal, thet&rsquo;ll be aboot all. Yu stay heah. An&rsquo; when I go into Hall&rsquo;s yu follow pronto.&rdquo; &ldquo;Brazos, let me go with yu?&rdquo; &ldquo;Nope. The cairds air all oot, but they don&rsquo;t savvy them.&rdquo; Brazos strode swiftly into the first store, traversed its length, hurried out into the alley and ran to the side street. Here he slowed up, caught his breath, and went on to Hailey&rsquo;s hotel, which occupied the corner at its junction with the main street. Brazos stepped into the side entrance and on to the lobby. Surface stood near the door of the hall, his tall form bent over the girl who was in the act of wrenching free from his clutch. His back was toward Brazos. Bess leaned against the wall as if for support. She looked a defiant hounded creature, game to the finish. &ldquo;You can&rsquo;t scare me, Raine Surface,&rdquo; she said, low and hard. &ldquo;I tell you I wouldn&rsquo;t be in your boots for all your money.&rdquo; Brazos entered the lobby to confront them. &ldquo;Oh&mdash;Brazos!&rdquo; gasped the girl. Surface&rsquo;s visage changed instantly, markedly in color, monstrously in expression. The surprise was so complete that had Brazos sought more proof of the man&rsquo;s perfidy he would have seen it with the mask off. Unquestionably for an instant Surface thought his death was imminent. &ldquo;What yu raggin&rsquo; my girl for?&rdquo; asked Brazos, with a pretense of jealousy. &ldquo;Your&mdash;girl!&rdquo; ejaculated Surface, huskily, his jaw ceasing to wobble. &ldquo;She&rsquo;s deceived you, Keene&mdash;same as all of us.... She&rsquo;s Syvertsen&rsquo;s&mdash;&mdash;&rdquo; &ldquo;Daughter, yu mean?&rdquo; interposed Brazos. The rancher swerved. As his first shocking fear subsided he began to recover his nerve. &ldquo;Daughter&mdash;hell! She&rsquo;s no more Syvertsen&rsquo;s daughter than mine.&rdquo; &ldquo;So yu say? Wal, what is she, then?&rdquo; &ldquo;What could she be, Keene? For a cowboy who&rsquo;s supposed to be so damned smart you&rsquo;re sure a fool....&rdquo; Brazos jerked as if stung. That was a liberation of his pent-up force. But the imperative need of pretense still persisted. &ldquo;What the hell!&rdquo; he flashed. &ldquo;Bess, I don&rsquo;t like this talk between yu an&rsquo; him. But I trust yu.... Surface, I always thought there was somethin&rsquo; queer aboot yu.&rdquo; Dealing Surface a powerful left-handed blow, Brazos knocked him flat. The rancher, scrambling up, stuttering maledictions, lifted a bloody, distorted visage. &ldquo;You&rsquo;ll pay for this&mdash;outrage&mdash;you &mdash;&mdash;&rdquo; &ldquo;Aw, go for yore gun,&rdquo; snorted Brazos, contemptuously. But if Surface had a gun on his person he made no move to get at it. Surface now concerned himself about the several witnesses to this scene. &ldquo;This cowboy is drunk,&rdquo; he said, thickly, as he faced them, and began to brush dust from his black coat. &ldquo;Another example for citizens of Las Animas. We&rsquo;ve got to have law and order here.&rdquo; &ldquo;Bah! Who told yu yu were a citizen of Las Animas?&rdquo; retorted Brazos. &ldquo;Yu&rsquo;re not even Western, Surface. Yu don&rsquo;t belong oot heah. An&rsquo; if yu&rsquo;re gonna stay I advise yu to get law an&rsquo; order&mdash;a hell of a lot.&rdquo; Surface controlled a malignant rage. He had sense enough to see that he was impotent in the Western creed of man to man. But he could not control his expression, which flamed demonically upon Brazos and Bess, as he lunged away, colliding with the door in his hurry. Brazos watched him a moment. The man was not big or strong in any sense. Brazos marveled that he had lasted as long as he had. To compare Raine Surface with Sewall McCoy, or any of the great cattle thieves, would have been to insult them. &ldquo;Come&mdash;Brazos,&rdquo; said Bess, low-voiced, and she touched his arm. &ldquo;Doggone it, Bess!&rdquo; complained Brazos, going with her into the street. &ldquo;I come pretty near gettin&rsquo; sore.&rdquo; &ldquo;Ump-umm, Bess. But yu don&rsquo;t have to tell me nothin&rsquo;.&rdquo; &ldquo;I must... if it&rsquo;s the last honest thing I ever do.&rdquo; &ldquo;All right, if yu put it thet way.&rdquo; &ldquo;Will you believe me, Brazos?&rdquo; she entreated. &ldquo;Believe me&mdash;when I&rsquo;ve been such&mdash;a&mdash;a&mdash;cheat and liar?&rdquo; &ldquo;Wal, Bess, yu air upset,&rdquo; he replied, soothingly. But after a swift glance he did not want to look at her again. He had to see every man who approached them, as they walked on down the street. &ldquo;An&rsquo; I can make allowance. If it&rsquo;ll do any good to tell me what&rsquo;s on yore mind go ahaid&mdash;an&rsquo; I&rsquo;ll believe yu.&rdquo; &ldquo;Brazos Keene, you are the only man I ever honestly loved,&rdquo; she said earnestly. &ldquo;Wal, I&rsquo;m glad to heah thet, but I don&rsquo;t savvy &lsquo;honestly&rsquo;.&rdquo; &ldquo;I am proving it right now. If I hadn&rsquo;t loved you&mdash;you&rsquo;d be a dead man right now.&rdquo; &ldquo;Yeah? Bess, thet kind of talk has a familiar ring. I&rsquo;ve heahed it before.&rdquo; &ldquo;Ump-umm, sweetheart,&rdquo; returned Brazos, enigmatically, and he felt rather than saw her sudden start. They had almost reached Hall&rsquo;s saloon. Inskip stood at his post across the street; Kiskadden remained where Brazos had left him; Bilyen had not come out. Brazos laid hold of Bess&rsquo;s arm with his left hand, so that she could not break away from him. But she appeared unresisting, bewildered. &ldquo;Girl, when yu confessed all thet yu proved a lot.... Yu won my respect.... An&rsquo; yu saved yoreself a term in prison if not yore life!&rdquo; With that he swung her with him into the door of Hall&rsquo;s saloon, and sent her whirling, almost falling toward Syvertsen and Orcutt, who were backing away from the window. Brazos leaped back in front of the door, so that he could face them and all the big room. An instant silence contrasted strangely with the former clink and rattle and hum of the saloon. On the moment Kiskadden came sliding in behind Brazos, closely followed by Inskip. Then they backed slowly to Brazos&rsquo; left step by step until the tables halted them. On the other side Bess sagged against the wall, ashen of face, her piercing eyes on Brazos in a terrible comprehension. She knew that Syvertsen and Orcutt were trapped wolves. The staring crowd at the bar, at the gaming tables divined the same, though they did not understand why. But the Norwegian and his swarthy ally divined nothing except the monstrous possibility that they might have been betrayed. Passion, not fear, transfixed them. &ldquo;Yu hellcat!&rdquo; burst out Syvertsen. &ldquo;What does this mean?&rdquo; His base epithet, his cold query, acted upon the girl like a goad. She stiffened as her head swept up and back to the wall, knocking off her sombrero. Then she appeared a white-faced woman at bay. &ldquo;I told him!&rdquo; she cried. &ldquo;You told him that... you told him who...&rdquo; gasped Syvertsen, suddenly shaken from his icy fury. &ldquo;No&mdash;not who... but if Brazos Keene has half the sense he&rsquo;s credited with he knows.&rdquo; &ldquo;You&rsquo;ve been in love with him&mdash;all this time?&rdquo; demanded Orcutt, incredulously, jealousy mastering all else. &ldquo;All this time,&rdquo; she said, tauntingly. &ldquo;You love this towheaded, girl-chasin&rsquo; cowboy... this snoopin&rsquo;, sneakin&rsquo;, watchin&rsquo; gunman!&rdquo; shouted Orcutt, furiously, his leather face no longer swarthy. &ldquo;Love Brazos Keene? Yes&mdash;yes! Thank God, I had honesty enough left in me to love him!&rdquo; she returned, with a passion that matched Orcutt&rsquo;s. &ldquo;I&rsquo;ll tear out your lyin&rsquo; &mdash;&mdash;&rdquo; Both Bess&rsquo;s antagonists had actually forgotten the presence of Brazos Keene. They were rudely reminded of it and that the stiffness of the spectators, the silence, the strange position of the cowboy, bent a little, both brown powerful hands extended a little low, and quivering&mdash;that all these constituted a tremendous menace. Then the significance of Brazos Keene dawned appallingly upon them. He confronted them. There was no escape. No matter by what incredible means this encounter had worked out, it was a reality. And the reputation of this fire-eyed cowboy might as well have been blazoned on the walls. &ldquo;Ask me, yu yellow dawgs,&rdquo; rang out Brazos. But neither of these trapped men voiced a query regarding what was dawning upon them. Engrossed in their own greeds and lusts, slow-witted and weak-willed, they had been falsely led into a mortal predicament, into an encounter with the very man they had plotted to murder. &ldquo;Wal, if yu haven&rsquo;t nerve to ask I&rsquo;ll tell yu,&rdquo; went on Brazos. &ldquo;Bess told me, but she didn&rsquo;t need to. All the time I knew it.&rdquo; &ldquo;That&rsquo;s not true,&rdquo; flashed Bess, a dark scarlet staining her white cheek. &ldquo;He never kissed me.... And I don&rsquo;t believed he made a&mdash;a fool of me.&rdquo; &ldquo;Ask him. Look at him&mdash;an&rsquo; ask him,&rdquo; shouted Orcutt, beside himself. Bess swerved her fathomless gaze to the cowboy. &ldquo;Brazos, is that true?&rdquo; Brazos did not answer, nor shift his narrowed gaze from the two men. &ldquo;Let her alone,&rdquo; he called, bitingly. &ldquo;What difference does it make to yu now?&rdquo; &ldquo;Keene, if she squealed&mdash;I&rsquo;ll kill her!&rdquo; choked out Syvertsen. &ldquo;Squealed?... Yu mean aboot young Neece an&rsquo; the dirty job yu hung on me!&rdquo; queried Brazos, once more in his slow tantalizing drawl with its note of mockery. &ldquo;By Gawd!&rdquo; ejaculated Syvertsen, hoarsely, his eyes rolling. And the content of his words, if not their audible sound, was echoed by Orcutt. &ldquo;No! No!&rdquo; cried Bess, suddenly desperate. &ldquo;Bard, I swear I didn&rsquo;t.... I told him no more.... Hen, believe me.... I didn&rsquo;t&mdash;I couldn&rsquo;t be that rotten!&rdquo; Orcutt&rsquo;s white thin lips framed a vile name he could not utter. &ldquo;Ah! I see it all now,&rdquo; burst out Syvertsen, tragically. &ldquo;&mdash;your false soul!&rdquo; &ldquo;You!&rdquo; echoed the two pale men, in unison. They had. It struck them like a bludgeon. Orcutt&rsquo;s lips tightened. Syvertsen began to bluster. By these signs Brazos read that Orcutt was the more dangerous of the two. Then Brazos let loose the dammed-up flood that for weeks had been waiting for this moment. &ldquo;I&rsquo;ve run into some low-down hombres in my day. But yu two take the bacon. I wouldn&rsquo;t waste my breath callin&rsquo; yu the names thet&rsquo;d fit yu, if I could think of any dirty enough.&rdquo; Brazos paused with that. Even in liberating passion he did not forget his cunning. He knew how to work on such men&mdash;to destroy what effectiveness they might have had. And the fact that he could rail at them proved his estimate of their nerve and prowess. &ldquo;So what am I callin&rsquo; yu for?... Remember thet little deal of yore&rsquo;s oot at the Hill cabin? Aha! Yu haven&rsquo;t forgot thet. Wal, neither have I&mdash;an&rsquo; thet&rsquo;s what I&rsquo;ve been workin&rsquo; on all these weeks.... Orcutt&mdash;Syvertsen, if yu ever got oot of heah alive yu&rsquo;d swing. But then maybe yu might have too many powerful friends who&rsquo;d clear yu of the rope. Miller, for instance, an&rsquo; Bodkin, who&rsquo;s runnin&rsquo; for sheriff&mdash;an&rsquo; Raine Surface.... Ahuh. Thet makes yu kinda pale aboot the gills.... Wal, I reckon yu won&rsquo;t get oot of heah alive. I&rsquo;m not trustin&rsquo; the justice of Las Animas&mdash;nor yore cattle combine.&rdquo; &ldquo;Keene! You&rsquo;re clean&mdash;mad,&rdquo; broke out Syvertsen. &ldquo;You hombres murdered Allen Neece an&rsquo; blamed thet job on me,&rdquo; went on Brazos, relentlessly. &ldquo;Yu murdered him because Surface wanted it done. An&rsquo; yu schemed to put me oot of the way because Surface was afraid I&rsquo;d take Allen Neece&rsquo;s trail. Wal, yu bet yore life I took it an&rsquo; it ends right heah.... Surface beat Abe Neece oot of Twin Sombreros Ranch. Yu men held up Neece thet night an&rsquo; robbed him of the money he had to pay Surface for his cattle. An&rsquo; yu-all sicked this girl on me &rsquo;cause none of yu had the nerve to meet me face to face.... Wal, thet&rsquo;s my say. An&rsquo; after all yu&rsquo;re meetin&rsquo; me face to face!&rdquo; As Brazos ended he read the desperate intent in Orcutt&rsquo;s eyes and beat him to a gun. Orcutt&rsquo;s heart was split even as he pulled trigger and his bullet hissed hotly by Brazos&rsquo; ear. Syvertsen, slow to realize and act, scarcely had his gun free when Brazos shot him through. The ball thudded into the wall. Syvertsen&rsquo;s vitality equaled his terrible fury. He did not fall. He did not lose sight or intent. But his muscular co-ordination had been destroyed. Fire and smoke belched from his wavering gun. His frown of immense surprise, his pale lighted eyes, his incoherent ejaculations of hate were all appalling to see. Brazos had to end them all, though the man was mortally struck, by blowing out his brains. Syvertsen swayed from his lofty stature, to fall across a table, to slide from that into another, and to crash down. The smoke cleared away disclosing Bess, back against the wall, her arms wide spread, with her gaze fixed terribly upon the fallen men. Suddenly she sprang out from the wall, an incarnate fury, formidable as a tigress. &ldquo;Bess,&rdquo; called Brazos, who had feared her reaction to the tragedy. &ldquo;You fooled me&mdash;to kill them!&rdquo; &ldquo;I&rsquo;ll kill you!&rdquo; As she whipped out her gun Brazos had to be quick to save his life. He took a shot at her arm, high up. The heavy bullet spun her around like a top and sent the little gun flying. Shrieking wildly she collided with the wall, bounced out to fall beyond the two dead men, where her boots pattered on the floor. As Brazos sheathed his gun and knelt to lift her head she ceased the cry of agony. She gazed up at Brazos, fascinated, suddenly bereft of all hate and passion. &ldquo;Brazos&mdash;you shot me,&rdquo; she whispered accusingly. &ldquo;My Gawd, I did, girl! But why did you draw on me? Why did yu, Bess?&rdquo; &ldquo;You made a fool of me.&rdquo; &ldquo;No. I swear I didn&rsquo;t. At least I didn&rsquo;t intend to. Yu did all the foolin&rsquo;, Bess.&rdquo; &ldquo;You&rsquo;ve killed me&mdash;Brazos?&rdquo; &ldquo;I&rsquo;m terrible scared, Bess,&rdquo; replied Brazos, and he did not lie. He saw that he had hit her in the breast or shoulder, instead of in the arm. Blood was pouring out. He was afraid to open her blouse. &ldquo;It&rsquo;s better so. I deserve it.... But to be killed by you, Brazos Keene&mdash;for loving you! Oh, what irony!... Oh, my wasted life!... the pity of it!&rdquo; Bilyen knelt beside Brazos. Kiskadden, Inskip&mdash;the others crowded around, shocked and silent. &ldquo;Bess, if yu have to go&mdash;make it a clean job,&rdquo; said Brazos, earnestly. &ldquo;Confess. Tell the truth aboot this deal.&rdquo; &ldquo;The truth?&rdquo; she whispered. &ldquo;Yes. Of Allen Neece&rsquo;s murder.&rdquo; &ldquo;Oh, I will, Brazos.&rdquo; &ldquo;Hank, Kiskadden&mdash;somebody get paper an&rsquo; pencil. Take down what she says.... An&rsquo; all of yu listen. Yu&rsquo;ll be called to prove some things important to this range.&rdquo; &ldquo;I&rsquo;m fainting.... Whisky!&rdquo; called the girl, almost inaudibly. Somebody fetched a glass, and Brazos, with bloody hand, held it to her ashen lips. She drank. &ldquo;All right,&rdquo; she said, smiling up at him. &ldquo;My right name is Bess Moore. I am not Syvertsen&rsquo;s wife.... We belonged to Raine Surface&rsquo;s crooked outfit at Abilene. Surface is a man of two sides. One of them is black as hell.... We were called here to put Allen Neece out of the way. I got him to drink&mdash;coaxed him to ride out of town with me.... Orcutt roped him from behind bushes on the road&mdash;jerked him off his horse.... As he lay on the ground Bard shot him&mdash;in the back.... They carried him to the Hill cabin&mdash;left him in the loft.... Then Brazos Keene rode up. Bard had a few words with Brazos&mdash;thought he deceived him. He rode back to town and fastened the crime upon Brazos.... But our own plot miscarried... and lately&mdash;Surface called us again&mdash;to do the same job&mdash;over...&rdquo; &ldquo;Thet&rsquo;ll do, Bess. Give me the paper, Kiskadden. Bess, can you sign yore name&mdash;heah?&rdquo; importuned Brazos, with strong feeling. Bess signed her name and then fell back fainting. Brazos, with shaking hands, tore open her blouse, shivering at the white swelling breast. He pulled the blouse down over the blood-stained shoulder to feel for the wound, frantic in fear that it would be too low. But it was not low. He found it high up, just where the arm met the shoulder, a bad painful wound, but not in any sense dangerous to life. &ldquo;Aw!&rdquo; Brazos burst out. &ldquo;She&rsquo;s not bad hurt at all. She&rsquo;s only fainted.... Hank, get somebody to help carry her to Hailey&rsquo;s. Call the doctor. An&rsquo; when she comes to, tell her she&rsquo;s not gonna die an&rsquo; I&rsquo;ll be back pronto.&rdquo; Brazos snatched the paper from Bilyen and relinquished the girl to him. Then he stood up, tense and eager. &ldquo;It&rsquo;s aboot all, men, but not quite,&rdquo; he said, as he carefully folded the confession. &ldquo;Come with me. Yu too, Kiskadden, an&rsquo; fetch somebody with yu.&rdquo; At the foot of the Odd Fellows stairway Brazos halted to load his gun and wait for the followers he had out-stripped. &ldquo;Brazos, is yore haid cool?&rdquo; asked Kiskadden, breathing hard. &ldquo;I ain&rsquo;t presumin&rsquo; to advise yu. I&rsquo;m just askin&rsquo;.&rdquo; &ldquo;Speak oot, old-timer.&rdquo; &ldquo;It might look better to hold yore hand at Surface. Yu know the range&mdash;an&rsquo; he has friends. Don&rsquo;t let them call this a gunman&rsquo;s spree.&rdquo; &ldquo;Wal, onless he goes for his gun&mdash;which he won&rsquo;t. Only I hope to Gawd he does! Come on an&rsquo; step easy.&rdquo; Inskip arrived with thumping strides, followed by men in twos and threes. &ldquo;Did yu search them?&rdquo; asked Brazos, facing around from the stairway. &ldquo;Yes. Both well heeled. Bilyen took charge of money, papers, guns.&rdquo; Brazos went up the stairs three steps at a time, and his followers strung after him, trying to step softly on heavy boots. The door of the hall stood open. Surface was holding forth with resonant voice. &ldquo;Gentlemen, all our fellow citizens were invited to participate here. Evidently those who stayed away were satisfied to leave important matters to us.... We have all voted and the result assures Bodkin&rsquo;s election as sheriff of Las Animas. Formerly he was appointed by the Cattlemen&rsquo;s Association. That is a distinction with a difference.&rdquo; Surface halted impressively for a moment, then resumed in strong voice: &ldquo;There remains to invite undesirable loafers, gamblers, dissolute women, suspected cowmen, and at least one notorious cowboy to leave Las Animas.&rdquo; Brazos drew his gun and stepped into the hall. &ldquo;Wal, Surface,&rdquo; he called, ringingly, &ldquo;heah&rsquo;s yore last named undesirable&mdash;to talk for himself.&rdquo; Surface stood on a platform facing a room full of men, sitting in rows. A stiffening jerk appeared to run through them, but all of them turned to look. &ldquo;Set tight, everybody,&rdquo; ordered Brazos. &ldquo;Surface, the jig&rsquo;s up!&rdquo; No noticeable change showed in the rancher&rsquo;s pale face. He had begun to weigh this intrusion. Kiskadden, Inskip, and others filed in with grave grim visages. They must have meant as much, or more to him, as the advent of Brazos. &ldquo;Gentlemen, you come too late to participate in this election,&rdquo; he rolled out, sonorously. &ldquo;Ump-umm!&rdquo; retorted Brazos. &ldquo;Surface, did yu heah me? I said yore jig was up.&rdquo; &ldquo;What do you mean?&rdquo; shouted Surface, harshly. &ldquo;I just shot yore ootfit.&rdquo; &ldquo;Wha-at!... Who?&rdquo; &ldquo;Bard Syvertsen... Hen Orcutt... an&rsquo; Bess!&rdquo; &ldquo;Wal, the girl lived to sign her confession.&rdquo; Then a startling transformation made Surface another man. &ldquo;Yu&rsquo;re gonna heah thet confession read.&rdquo; With left hand, watching the cattleman like a hawk, Brazos extracted the paper from his vest and held it back. &ldquo;Somebody read this.&rdquo; Kiskadden took the paper and with slow deliberate voice, somehow more telling and inflexible for the cool Texas accent, he read it solemnly. When he had finished, Surface seemed actually to have shrunken in stature. He opened his mouth several times as if to speak, but no words issued forth. &ldquo;Surface, I shore hope yu got the guts to throw yore gun, but I&rsquo;m gamblin&rsquo; yu&rsquo;ve not,&rdquo; called Brazos, in cold scorn. The rancher flunked that challenge, and as that fact became manifest the stiff occupants of the seats began to scrape their boots nervously, to squirm and mutter, and at last to gaze at each other for angry confirmation. &ldquo;All right, Surface. I cain&rsquo;t waste time waitin&rsquo;,&rdquo; went on Brazos. &ldquo;March down heah.&rdquo; Without protest Surface obeyed and when he reached the open space behind the chairs Brazos ordered him to halt and had him searched. &ldquo;Wal, so yu was packin&rsquo; a gun!&rdquo; drawled Brazos, in derisive exclamation. &ldquo;I wonder what&rsquo;n hell for.... Surface, yu&rsquo;re aboot as low-down as they come. If we was in New Mexico yu&rsquo;d be strung up an&rsquo; bored while yu was kickin&rsquo;.&rdquo; This speech from Brazos precipitated expression of the pent-up astonishment and wrath of the men whom Surface had addressed. Then he punched Surface in the back with his gun. &ldquo;Mosey along, yu! An&rsquo; don&rsquo;t forget I&rsquo;d jump at the chance to try oot yore specialty of shootin&rsquo; men in the back.&rdquo; Brazos marched Surface down the stairway to the street, and into the rancher&rsquo;s buckboard. Brazos climbed into the back seat. &ldquo;Drive oot to Neece&rsquo;s ranch,&rdquo; he called, loud enough for the gathering bystanders to hear. &ldquo;Neece&rsquo;s ranch!... Where&rsquo;s that?&rdquo; choked out Surface. &ldquo;Where do yu reckon, yu&mdash;robber?... Twin Sombreros Ranch!&rdquo; The crowded sidewalks of Las Animas were then treated to another of Brazos Keene&rsquo;s peculiar actions. And it was of the most prominent citizen of that frontier town driving his team of black horses down the middle of the street with a gun at his back and behind that gun the cold-faced cowboy. Brazos did not look to right or left, and he was too grimly concerned to enjoy that ride, or the gathering whoop which rang along the street and out of town with him. In short order the spirited team arrived at the ranch. &ldquo;Surface, I want thet bag of gold.&rdquo; &ldquo;What&mdash;bag&mdash;of gold?&rdquo; &ldquo;Yu know. Syvertsen held Neece up an&rsquo; robbed him of it.&rdquo; &ldquo;That! I haven&rsquo;t got it,&rdquo; replied Surface. &ldquo;Wal, thet&rsquo;ll be too bad. Think again an&rsquo; maybe yu&rsquo;ll remember. I heahed Bodkin an&rsquo; Brad an&rsquo; another man talk aboot thet bag of gold. Yu got it. Find it&mdash;or I&rsquo;ll bore yu pronto an&rsquo; find it myself.&rdquo; &ldquo;All right. I&mdash;I&rsquo;ll get it,&rdquo; rejoined Surface, thickly. At the point of Brazos&rsquo; gun the rancher led the way into the ranch house, and into his room, where from under the floor of a closet he dragged up an extremely heavy satchel. &ldquo;Open it,&rdquo; ordered Brazos, eagerly. Surface complied to expose packs of greenbacks and bags that gave forth a musical clink of precious metal. &ldquo;All right. Carry it oot.&rdquo; Brazos had Surface drive him back as they had come. Groups of men stood on corners and in front of saloons. There was a crowd in front of Hailey&rsquo;s. &ldquo;Drive to the station, Surface. It&rsquo;s aboot time for the afternoon train.&rdquo; With gun in hand Brazos saw that the deposed rancher bought a ticket to Abilene&mdash;saw him stand on the platform a target for all eyes&mdash;saw him mount the platform of the passenger coach of the train. Then he delivered himself of a final word. &ldquo;Surface, yu&rsquo;re gettin&rsquo; off turrible lucky. Maybe it&rsquo;ll make yu think when I tell yu thet it&rsquo;s due to yore daughter.... Get oot of Colorado an&rsquo; stay oot.... If I ever run into yu again I&rsquo;ll kill yu.&rdquo; Brazos stalked back to the buckboard as the train pulled out. Through a window he saw Surface, white and haggard, stare out with unseeing eyes. Bilyen came hurrying across the tracks carrying heavy gun belts. &ldquo;My Gawd&mdash;cowboy but yu&rsquo;re hard to keep track of,&rdquo; he panted. &ldquo;Hank, I let him off,&rdquo; said Brazos, as if the fact was incomprehensible. &ldquo;First low-down rustler I ever weakened on... all &rsquo;cause of thet green-eyed, red-haided girl of his!&rdquo; &ldquo;Best thing you could have done,&rdquo; rejoined Bilyen, heartily. &ldquo;Brazos, it may have been yore weakness for women, but it&rsquo;ll look different to hardheaded men of this range.... But where&rsquo;d yu go&mdash;what&rsquo;d yu do?&rdquo; &ldquo;Hank, I got thet bag of gold an&rsquo; bills Syvertsen stole from Neece,&rdquo; declared Brazos, triumphantly. &ldquo;Heah, under the seat.&rdquo; &ldquo;You&mdash;dod-blasted amazin&rsquo; son of a gun of a Texas cowboy!&rdquo; ejaculated Bilyen, utterly confounded. &ldquo;Listen. Take this bag oot to Neece. An&rsquo; yu drive him right in this heah buckboard oot to Twin Sombreros. Today!... Tell June an&rsquo; Janis their home is ready for them&mdash;an&rsquo; no blood spilled oot there to spoil their homecomin&rsquo;. Tell them they won&rsquo;t have to sling hash over the counter any more.... An&rsquo;&mdash;an&rsquo; tell them I&rsquo;ll be goin&rsquo; oot in the country for a spell, but I&rsquo;ll come back shore.&rdquo; &ldquo;Brazos, no man on earth but yu should tell Neece thet an&rsquo; the twins. Cowboy, think what yu&rsquo;ll miss!&rdquo; &ldquo;Hank, for a Texan yu&rsquo;re plumb thick. I just got through some bloody gunplay, didn&rsquo;t I&mdash;an&rsquo; damn near killed a woman?&rdquo; &ldquo;I savvy. Mebbe yu&rsquo;re right. But the girl Bess ain&rsquo;t bad hurt.... We carried her to Hailey&rsquo;s an&rsquo; Doc Williamson fixed her up pronto.&rdquo; &ldquo;Ahuh. Any bones broke?&rdquo; &ldquo;Wal, she won&rsquo;t try to throw any more guns.... Gee, wasn&rsquo;t she wild, Brazos? Funny how a woman can love you an&rsquo; kill you almost in the same breath.... Heah&rsquo;s her little gun&mdash;an&rsquo; the hardware we found on them hombres.... Money, too, Brazos. Both of them had stacks of bills. Here, you better take charge of that. You sure earned it, ha-ha!&rdquo; Brazos slung the gun belts on his left arm, and gazed with a grim smile at the little gun Bess had drawn on him. &ldquo;Doggone! Yu gotta hand it to her! She would have bored me, Hank.... Right in thet weak spot I had for her! All right, I&rsquo;ll take charge of these an&rsquo; the money if yu think I&rsquo;d better.... Gosh! what a wallet!&rdquo; &ldquo;Thet was Syvertsen&rsquo;s,&rdquo; said Hank, &ldquo;an&rsquo; heah&rsquo;s Orcutt&rsquo;s.&rdquo; &ldquo;Ahuh. Money was easy for those hombres. Wal, they won&rsquo;t need any of it where they&rsquo;re goin&rsquo;. Bess will.&rdquo; &ldquo;An&rsquo; so will you, Brazos. You should keep Orcutt&rsquo;s wallet yourself, and if I know you, everyone but yourself will be gettin&rsquo; the good out of it.&rdquo; &ldquo;Well, we&rsquo;ll see.... So long, Hank.&rdquo; &ldquo;Look us up soon, Brazos,&rdquo; called Bilyen after him. &ldquo;Oot at Twin Sombreros!&rdquo; * * * * * There were two windows in Bess&rsquo;s room, letting the sunlight flood in, to show her white, strained face on the pillow. But the fire, the hate, the passion were gone. Brazos advanced to the bed as he spoke to the woman in attendance. &ldquo;Leave us alone a little, nurse.&rdquo; &ldquo;Howdy, Brazos Keene,&rdquo; said the girl, looking up with her unfathomable eyes. &ldquo;Howdy yoreself, girl,&rdquo; he replied, and carefully sat down on the bed. &ldquo;Air yu in pain?&rdquo; &ldquo;Not so bad now. It did hurt like hell, though.&rdquo; &ldquo;Close shave, Bess.... Gosh, I was scared.&rdquo; &ldquo;You didn&rsquo;t mean to kill me?&rdquo; &ldquo;Heavens no! I had to do somethin&rsquo; pronto an&rsquo; tried to wing yu.&rdquo; &ldquo;I wish you had killed me.&rdquo; &ldquo;Shore, yu mad girl. But I didn&rsquo;t. An&rsquo; yu&rsquo;re gonna get over this, an&rsquo; somewhere far from heah, live yore&mdash;yore trouble down, an&rsquo; turn oot fine.&rdquo; &ldquo;Brazos! You believe that&rsquo;s in me?&rdquo; &ldquo;Yes, I shore do.&rdquo; &ldquo;But won&rsquo;t I have to go to prison?&rdquo; &ldquo;I should smile not.... Bess, have yu any relatives or friends yu could go to?&rdquo; &ldquo;Some friends back in Illinois. No kin, Brazos.... Oh, how good you are! Back of all your deviltry.... Brazos, if I can go I should go at once. This town will hum.&rdquo; &ldquo;I reckon. Yore mix-up with cattle rustlers was not so bad. But thet deal of Surface&rsquo;s&mdash;hirin&rsquo; yu an&rsquo; yore pards to do away with young Allen Neece.... Pretty raw an&rsquo; low-down, Bess! Yu&rsquo;ll do wal to get oot of Colorado pronto.&rdquo; &ldquo;I&rsquo;ll leave on the night train,&rdquo; she returned, hurriedly. &ldquo;I can be carried. This woman will take me&mdash;across the line, anyway.... But, I&rsquo;ll need money, Brazos. I have none. Bard had a wallet full of big bills. Did they find that?&rdquo; &ldquo;Heah it is, Bess,&rdquo; answered Brazos, and slipped the wallet under her pillow. &ldquo;An&rsquo; I reckon it&rsquo;d be wise for yu to go pronto. Nothin&rsquo; might come of yore stayin&rsquo;, but when yu recover&mdash;wal, men air queer, Bess. An&rsquo; whether yu were forced or not, yu had somethin&rsquo; to do with Allen Neece&rsquo;s murder. Thet&rsquo;ll come oot.&rdquo; &ldquo;Oh, yes, I know. It&rsquo;s horrible,&rdquo; she returned. &ldquo;I was forced. But that&rsquo;s no excuse. They tried harder to force me in your case. It didn&rsquo;t work.&rdquo; &ldquo;Wal, it damn near worked, at thet,&rdquo; said Brazos. &ldquo;You wonderful, terrible cowboy!... You&rsquo;re lovable, too. I&rsquo;m going to remember you that way&mdash;instead of... God! it all came to me like a flash&mdash;when you dragged me into that saloon to spin me before Syvertsen and Orcutt. There was death in your face. But I was thick&mdash;I couldn&rsquo;t figure your dodge.... I know now. Orcutt had you figured. Too late! I&rsquo;m glad it&rsquo;s over. I think&mdash;if I was sure of one thing&mdash;I could go straight now.&rdquo; &ldquo;Shore of what one thing, Bess?&rdquo; &ldquo;I could stand your&mdash;your fooling me&mdash;if you really cared,&rdquo; she said, unaware of her strange inconsistency. Brazos took her little tight cold hand in his and held it close. He did not need to perjure himself to help this girl, but he would have done so and thought it justified. &ldquo;Bess, I knew yu was in with a bad ootfit,&rdquo; he said. &ldquo;But I thought yu was Syvertsen&rsquo;s daughter, an&rsquo; I was plumb loco aboot yu until I found yu wasn&rsquo;t. Thet hurt like hell, Bess. Yu&rsquo;re not atall like one of these dance-hall girls. Yu&rsquo;ve got class, Bess&mdash;an&rsquo; I mean thet as I&rsquo;d mean it when I called a hawse thoroughbred. Yu&rsquo;re the prettiest darn thing I ever saw. The other night&mdash;heah at yore door&mdash;when yu got yore arms around my neck I was scared plumb stiff.&rdquo; &ldquo;Ump-umm, sweetheart, I didn&rsquo;t&mdash;&mdash;&rdquo; &ldquo;Brazos, say that again.&rdquo; &ldquo;You loved me then, Brazos?&rdquo; &ldquo;Wal, what would yu call it?&rdquo; &ldquo;It&rsquo;s different, Bess. Somethin&rsquo; to despise yu aboot&mdash;yet sweet an&rsquo; regretful.... I&rsquo;m gonna remember yu, Bess. I&rsquo;ll forget, after a while, yore&mdash;wal, yu know, an&rsquo; think of yu as yu made yoreself for me. Thet&rsquo;s all.&rdquo; &ldquo;Kiss me, Brazos.&rdquo; He bent over and kissed her as he might have if she were indeed what she had tried to deceive him into believing. &ldquo;Oh, Brazos! What have you&mdash;done to me!&rdquo; she cried, brokenly, clinging to him. &ldquo;Wal, wearin&rsquo; yu oot, for one thing,&rdquo; he replied, gently disengaging himself and rising. &ldquo;I&rsquo;ll go now, sweetheart. Yu look most as turrible as when yu lay on the floor at Hall&rsquo;s an&rsquo; I reckoned yu was dyin&rsquo;... I&rsquo;ve excited yu too much.&rdquo; &ldquo;You&rsquo;ve broken&mdash;my heart... and made me bless you&mdash;for it&mdash;and want to&mdash;to live.&rdquo; &ldquo;Wal, think of breakin&rsquo; a girl&rsquo;s heart an&rsquo; makin&rsquo; her the better for it!&rdquo; drawled Brazos and he bent to kiss her again. &ldquo;Thet&rsquo;s somethin&rsquo; for a hombre like me to remember. I&rsquo;ll come down to the train an&rsquo; see yu off.&rdquo; She whispered something too faint for him to hear and her dark eyes followed him to the door. Though only early September, the altitude provided for the frost that had begun to gild the aspens and fire the oaks. Brazos had been there years before. Time did not make much difference with nature if man did not come to despoil it. He could see no change. He remembered the lone dead pine that stood like a sentinel along the trail which crossed over into New Mexico. The hour was near sunset, with thunder rumbling among the heavy cumulus clouds along the black horizon. Through rifts, bars of gold shone down into the green valley, with its squares of alfalfa, its gray pastures, dotted with horses and cattle, its meandering stream of shining water. &ldquo;Doggone!&rdquo; soliloquized Brazos, pensively. &ldquo;Thet was the kind of a nook I was gonna homestead some day. An&rsquo; now I reckon I gotta ride for a big ranch.&rdquo; This valley was forty miles up in the foothills from Las Animas, a secluded spot once inhabited by Ute Indians, who still came down from the mountains occasionally. The tribe had moved on into a more inaccessible spot, driven farther by the advance of their unscrupulous foe&mdash;the white man. They were friendly to Coglan, Brazos remembered. &ldquo;Wal, I reckon it was aboot time for me to hole-up a spell,&rdquo; went on Brazos. &ldquo;&rsquo;Cause I&rsquo;d shore got in a wuss fight somewhere.... Them hombres with Bodkin thet night&mdash;Brad an&rsquo; the other fellow&mdash;they kinda worry me. The job wasn&rsquo;t finished. I&rsquo;ve a hunch I&rsquo;ll look up Bodkin someday, anyway.... Heah I am, an&rsquo; it&rsquo;s aboot time&mdash;or I&rsquo;d been lookin&rsquo; at red likker. Already I feel sorta loosenin&rsquo; up around my gizzard. I&rsquo;ll chop wood &rsquo;till I drop, an&rsquo; I&rsquo;ll pack a rifle up on those slopes&mdash;an&rsquo; after a while maybe I&rsquo;ll let myself think&mdash;&mdash;&rdquo; It was over&mdash;the long strain, the uncertainty, the continual need of watchfulness and sleeplessness, the drain on his reserve force, the hard cold expectancy of a fight&mdash;the blood lust. He was sick now, with that ice in his bowels&mdash;the gnawing remorse of the man who was not a killer by instinct. And his gladness was that of freedom to work and rest and dream, and presently to think of the splendid girls he had served and the love he had won. Brazos rode on down into the valley and up to the log cabin among the firs. Two little girls were playing about the door. They ran like Indians. Presently a buxom, rosy-cheeked young woman looked out. The sight both startled and pleased Brazos. Coglan had gotten himself a wife. &ldquo;Evenin&rsquo;, lady,&rdquo; said Brazos, taking off his sombrero, &ldquo;is Coglan anywhere aboot?&rdquo; &ldquo;He was. Get down an&rsquo; come in, stranger.&rdquo; Brazos had scarcely dismounted when Coglan appeared, ax in hand. He was a strapping man, still young, half hunter and half trapper, brown as an Indian. &ldquo;Howdy, Coglan,&rdquo; drawled Brazos, &ldquo;I shore am glad to see yu.&rdquo; &ldquo;Brazos Keene, by Gawd!&rdquo; ejaculated the mountaineer, with a whoop. &ldquo;You pestiferous, long-legged cowpuncher! Put her thar!&rdquo; And he nearly crushed Brazos&rsquo; hand. &ldquo;Hey, man, be careful of thet paw,&rdquo; yelled Brazos, trying to extricate it. &ldquo;I just had to use it an&rsquo; I might be needin&rsquo; it bad.&rdquo; &ldquo;Haw! Haw! I figgered thet. Nothin&rsquo; else would fetch you up hyar to see me. But you&rsquo;re welcome, cowboy, as the flowers in spring.... Rose, this is an old pard of mine. Brazos Keene! We rode together in the Panhandle... an&rsquo; but for him you wouldn&rsquo;t have me for a husband. Brazos, hyar&rsquo;s the little wife you always told me to get.&rdquo; They made Brazos welcome and the little girls, owl-eyed and shy, came forth to capitulate. Later Brazos and Coglan walked down to the corrals leading Brazos&rsquo; horse. &ldquo;Coglan, I want to hang aboot heah for a month or so,&rdquo; Brazos was saying. &ldquo;Chop wood an&rsquo; hunt an&rsquo; loaf. An&rsquo; be alone. Yu know!&rdquo; &ldquo;I savvy. Tell me when you feel like it or not at all.&rdquo; &ldquo;Wal, I&rsquo;ll get it off my chest,&rdquo; replied Brazos, and briefly related the Las Animas tragedy. &ldquo;So thet was it,&rdquo; said Coglan, soberly. &ldquo;I thought you looked kind of pale an&rsquo; peaked. Another McCoy-Slaughter deal, eh?... I&rsquo;ve heerd of Surface. An&rsquo; I&rsquo;ve lost cattle this summer. I had about a thousand head.&rdquo; &ldquo;Wal, I reckon rustlin&rsquo; will slow up for a spell,&rdquo; said Brazos, thoughtfully. &ldquo;Hope you winter up hyar with me, cowboy,&rdquo; returned Coglan, warmly. &ldquo;Aboot a month will be all. I&rsquo;ll sweat oot this poison. Gosh, I haven&rsquo;t had enough to eat lately to keep a grub-line rider alive. Yu&rsquo;ll have to feed me up, Coglan. An&rsquo; I want yu to ride in town once a week an&rsquo; fetch me the news. Yu can make some excuse to call on Neece. Bilyen knows I&rsquo;m gonna be heah. Yu can talk to him. I&rsquo;ll be powerful interested in all thet&rsquo;s goin&rsquo; on. But don&rsquo;t tell anybody, especially the Neeces, thet I&rsquo;m up heah.&rdquo; Brazos erected a bough shack for himself under the pines on the bank of the brook. He made a mattress of fir boughs and spread his blankets upon that. Later he returned to Coglan&rsquo;s cabin and ate a bountiful supper, among the good things of which were venison and wild turkey. He did not linger long with the hospitable Coglans. To the children he said: &ldquo;We&rsquo;re gonna be friends after a bit.&rdquo; Then he sought his bed in the darkness of the pines and stretched out on it as if he wished never to move again. The mountain air was cold and rare; the brook rushed murmuringly over the stones; the wind moaned through the pine tops; and the old familiar lonely wail of coyotes came thrillingly at long intervals. He had it out then with the dark forces that had actuated him. This time Brazos did not have a drunken spree to bring oblivion and to dull memory. That ruthless side of him was only a part of his nature. Like a demon in the night it passed out, leaving him free to sleep. Next day he awoke to new life, but he could not let himself revel in perfect solitude, in the colorful beauty of the autumn morning, in the songs of bird-travelers that had halted in the valley on their way south.
