He must drive himself physically to make the hours and days string out behind him with their softening influence. As a boy in Texas he had been a great hand with an ax. Here at Coglan&rsquo;s woodpile, where logs of dead aspen and oak lay heap on heap, Brazos went to work. He wore gloves because it was one of his melancholy cares to keep his hands, especially the right one, soft and flexible. He heaved and he chopped and split wood until, as if by magic, that day was gone and he was spent. Days passed swiftly then until the evening of one came with Coglan returning from Las Animas. Brazos saw that he was bursting with news which he did not care to impart before his wife. &ldquo;Brazos,&rdquo; he said, after supper, &ldquo;I got a couple two-bit cigars thet was give to me. Let&rsquo;s go out an&rsquo; smoke them.&rdquo; They went out in the mellow gloaming. The red was fading off the ramparts; cool air was blowing down from the heights; the late crickets were at their autumn requiem. &ldquo;Brazos, I heerd so damn much thet I&rsquo;ll never remember it all,&rdquo; began Coglan, enthusiastically. &ldquo;Talk, man, or I&rsquo;ll bounce somethin&rsquo; off yore haid,&rdquo; retorted Brazos, impatiently. &ldquo;Listen to this. The very day you rode out hyar Raine Surface was killed on the street in Dodge City.&rdquo; &ldquo;No!&rdquo; ejaculated Brazos, amazed. &ldquo;Fact. An&rsquo; it caused a heap of talk from Dodge to Denver.&rdquo; &ldquo;Who?&rdquo; flashed Brazos, in sudden sharpness. &ldquo;Nobody in Las Animas could say just who killed him. But talk laid it to a tall man with a queer voice. He was heerd to cuss Surface an&rsquo; after the shootin&rsquo; he left Dodge pronto.&rdquo; &ldquo;By thunder! I&rsquo;ll be doggoned. It was thet third party I heahed the night I spied on Bodkin with his two cronies. The other was called Brad. He an&rsquo; this unknown hombre was nursin&rsquo; a grievance.... Wal! So Raine Surface got his just deserts pronto.&rdquo; &ldquo;He sure did. I wonder if they don&rsquo;t all get it, sooner or later.&rdquo; &ldquo;Nope. They shore don&rsquo;t. I&rsquo;ve known more than one cattle baron who was respected by most an&rsquo; known only by a few to be a bloody thievin&rsquo; rascal. I know one now over heah in New Mexico who&rsquo;ll die in his bed surrounded by family an&rsquo; friends. But there air only a few great cattlemen of thet stripe.... Go on, Coglan. Talk! You haven&rsquo;t told me nothin&rsquo; yet.&rdquo; &ldquo;Wal, there&rsquo;s Bodkin,&rdquo; resumed Coglan. &ldquo;He&rsquo;s taken office as sheriff. No one in town cared to dispute his claim. An&rsquo; some of the Cattlemen&rsquo;s Association appeared to be back of him.&rdquo; &ldquo;Yu don&rsquo;t tell me!&rdquo; ejaculated Brazos. &ldquo;Wal, thet&rsquo;s a sticker. Coglan, shore as yu&rsquo;re smokin&rsquo; thet punk cigar, Surface was stronger than I guessed. An&rsquo; his combine was stronger.... I&rsquo;m a son of a gun.&rdquo; &ldquo;Brazos, is Bodkin crooked?&rdquo; &ldquo;Crooked! Say, man, a Missouri rail fence would be straight alongside Bodkin.... But come to think aboot it I didn&rsquo;t implicate Bodkin. Neither did Bess in her confession.... Wal, of all the nerve! I wonder what Kiskadden an&rsquo; Inskip said aboot thet.&rdquo; &ldquo;I&rsquo;ll tell you, Brazos. I know them both well. An&rsquo; you bet this trip to town I was meetin&rsquo; everybody, buyin&rsquo; drinks an&rsquo; smokes, an&rsquo; thirstin&rsquo; for news.... Kiskadden said the situation was by no means cleared up. An&rsquo; Inskip said it never would be until the cattle range was all plowed up. Neither of them came out blunt about Bodkin. But Bilyen did. He said Miller had stepped into Surface&rsquo;s boots an&rsquo; the new Cattlemen&rsquo;s Association was as strong as ever, with fewer dissentin&rsquo; voices. What did Bilyen mean by thet, Brazos?&rdquo; &ldquo;I don&rsquo;t savvy. It&rsquo;s got me buffaloed.&rdquo; &ldquo;Wal, the town people are inclined to lay hands off. An&rsquo; the cattlemen are waitin&rsquo; to see what Bodkin makes out of his job.&rdquo; &ldquo;Ahuh. Wal, I&rsquo;m not predictin&rsquo;,&rdquo; said Brazos. &ldquo;Let thet pass.... How aboot the Neeces?&rdquo; &ldquo;I rode out to Twin Sombreros,&rdquo; returned Coglan. &ldquo;Thet&rsquo;s where I seen Bilyen. Neece remembered me. He was sure a new man&mdash;if what I heerd was true. Same keen-eyed cattleman I knew, busy as a bee. Asked me if I had any stock to sell, an&rsquo; I said yes&mdash;what the rustlers left me. An&rsquo; he said, &lsquo;Drive it down before the snow flies.&rsquo;... Bilyen took me all over the ranch. Surface had spent a pile of money on improvements. It sure is a wonderful ranch. I met the twins, June an&rsquo; Janis. Couldn&rsquo;t tell them apart! But who&rsquo;d want to? They &rsquo;peared to me like girls in a trance.&rdquo; &ldquo;Trance! Wal, no wonder.... What&rsquo;d they do with their restaurant?&rdquo; &ldquo;Kept it, by golly. It&rsquo;s a money-maker. They put in a manager&mdash;a woman whose name I forget&mdash;an&rsquo; they&rsquo;ll run it as before.&rdquo; &ldquo;Good idee. But I reckon all the young buckaroos will stop eatin&rsquo; there.... What&rsquo;d Neece do with Surface&rsquo;s ootfit?&rdquo; &ldquo;Let &rsquo;em go pronto. He&rsquo;s hired a new bunch of riders an&rsquo; he&rsquo;s waitin&rsquo; for you to come back an&rsquo; be his foreman.&rdquo; &ldquo;Aw!&rdquo; gasped Brazos, and in his collapse he bit his cigar almost into two pieces. &ldquo;What ails you, cowboy?&rdquo; queried Coglan. &ldquo;Don&rsquo;t be a damn fool now, an&rsquo; go ridin&rsquo; away! Yu should have seen the eyes of them twins when their Dad said thet.&rdquo; &ldquo;Aw!&rdquo; repeated Brazos, in greater volume. &ldquo;Cowboy, I&rsquo;m givin&rsquo; you a pointer,&rdquo; replied the puzzled homesteader. &ldquo;Some of the young bucks around Las Animas would like to be in your boots. You might marry one of them twins. It wouldn&rsquo;t make any difference which one. You&rsquo;re a hero down thar, an&rsquo; you ought to make hay while the sun shines. Young Henry Sisk is throwin&rsquo; in with Neece on some pardnership deal. An&rsquo; Jack Sain is temporary foreman of the outfit. Them two boys ain&rsquo;t lettin&rsquo; any grass grow under their feet. Bilyen says they make life miserable for the twins.&rdquo; &ldquo;Aw, hell!&rdquo; burst out Brazos, and unable to endure more he flung his cigar away and strode to the solitude of his camp. Brazos sawed and split all the wood Coglan had hauled in for winter use. When he surveyed the cords of neatly stacked billets he could scarcely believe his own eyes. He belonged essentially to that class of cowboy who rode and stood guard, and handled ropes and guns. To have set Brazos Keene to the task of digging fence-post holes would have been to invite disaster. Yet there stood the evidence of his toil&mdash;a long winter&rsquo;s firewood. After that job was completed Brazos took to the slopes with a rifle. He climbed. He hunted. Deer, wild turkey and elk fell to his unerring aim; and it took strenuous work to pack the meat down to the valley. When to kill more would have been wanton slaughter, Brazos still climbed and hunted, but did not shoot again. Brazos had the prairie range rider&rsquo;s love for slopes and hills and peaks&mdash;all high places. Not since he left Don Carlos&rsquo; Rancho had he indulged in mountain climbing. Here he satisfied a long-felt want&mdash;a climbing and a hunting for he knew not what. It was perhaps the epitome of solitude. As September wore on toward October he unconsciously grew to returning to certain places that fascinated him. One was a sunny open swale thickly carpeted with high silver grass and yellow daisies, and marked by clusters of aspens burning dead gold against the blue sky. Pines and firs walled this swale about. Deer and elk browsed there paying little attention to the lonely watcher. Brazos went there every day, to cast himself on the thick grass just at the edge of the pines where sun and shade met. And here he would rest and dream for hours. Another favored spot was a rocky glen, heavily timbered, and full of huge moss-covered boulders. At the bottom rushed a mountain brook, clear as crystal, falling white here and eddying there, filling the glen with melody. Such experiences were rare in a cowboy&rsquo;s life. Brazos idled there the sleepy noon hours, when only a low moan of wind stirred the lofty tree tips, and the brook seemed to grow drowsy and tarry in its descent. At length Brazos came to frequent more and more a lofty promontory and to linger there longer and longer. It looked down upon the fastnesses of the mountain gorges, the gray rock ridges, the green thickets and yellow patches, out through a grand gateway to the vast plateau to the south. Somewhere across that dim purple stretch Brazos had ridden the Old Trail from Dodge to Cimarron. Coglan had been twice again to Las Animas to return with pleasant, disturbing, thought-provoking news and gossip. Brazos grew increasingly eager to ride down to Twin Sombreros. Yet he was loath to leave this high country. He would stay on into October. It seemed long since he had first arrived in Coglan&rsquo;s, and though rest and change had come in full measure, the transformation he had hoped for had not materialized. Perhaps Brazos had longed for a miracle. He found that unless a man-killer retrograded toward the beast he would never be free from remorse, from doubt, from the consciousness that fate must await him with another encounter, that his past inevitably militated against his chances of making a wife and her children happy. But Brazos had to face that gamble. He got back one October night to the valley to find Coglan home a day earlier than he had expected him. The rancher lacked his usual geniality. &ldquo;Bilyen says you&rsquo;re stayin&rsquo; away too long. Bodkin is braggin&rsquo; he will arrest you if you ever come back.&rdquo; &ldquo;Good Lord!&rdquo; ejaculated Brazos, incredulously. &ldquo;Wal, thet oughtn&rsquo;t to surprise you, Brazos,&rdquo; said Coglan, tersely. &ldquo;Sure we know his breed. Bodkin ain&rsquo;t very bright. He&rsquo;s like an animal. He soon forgets when the danger is gone.... But I reckon Bilyen is keen about your reputation. He&rsquo;s sore. He knows soon as you come back to Las Animas thet Bodkin will shut his loud mouth.&rdquo; &ldquo;Gosh, I hope he does,&rdquo; replied Brazos, ponderingly. &ldquo;There&rsquo;s a stranger lately dropped into town. Calls himself Knight an&rsquo; says he&rsquo;s a cattle buyer for a big Kansas City firm. He an&rsquo; Bodkin got thick pronto. Bilyen remembers seein&rsquo; this man with Bodkin once last August.&rdquo; &ldquo;Wal, I&rsquo;ll ride down soon. Gosh, I hate to leave this valley.... What else did yu heah, Coglan?&rdquo; &ldquo;Not much. I didn&rsquo;t go out to Twin Sombreros. But I met Neece in town. No one would think he&rsquo;d ever been down an&rsquo; out. The Neece-Sisk-Henderson cattle deal went through. They&rsquo;re runnin&rsquo; eighty thousand head.&rdquo; &ldquo;Thet&rsquo;s a solid combine. Reckon they&rsquo;re gonna buck the Miller ootfit. Neece is not goin&rsquo; to get caught again. I reckon Bilyen is behind thet deal.&rdquo; &ldquo;They&rsquo;re buildin&rsquo; a big barn at the ranch. Hauled in a sawmill. Hank says it&rsquo;ll be the biggest in Colorado. They got the roof up an&rsquo; the floor down when the twins stopped work with an idee. To give a grand dance!&rdquo; &ldquo;Twins&mdash;idee&mdash;dance?&rdquo; echoed Brazos, suddenly intensely curious. &ldquo;So Hank said. An&rsquo; thet if you didn&rsquo;t rustle down you&rsquo;d miss a hell of a time.&rdquo; &ldquo;Aw, June wouldn&rsquo;t give a dance withoot me!&rdquo; exclaimed Brazos, vaguely disbelieving. &ldquo;Girls are queer critters. You&rsquo;d better rustle, Brazos.&rdquo; Nevertheless Brazos had to have one more climb up the scarlet and gold slopes to the heights. The morning was frosty. The meadows and fields shone white. Over the valley hung a gray fog curtain through which faint blue patches and streaks appeared. Soon Brazos was lost in the cloud. At length when he emerged above it into the open sunlight the roof of this canopy resembled a soft pearly sea of rounded ripples with island crags lifting their tufted heads. And it was as if the sea circled the black base of a huge mountain with its peak rosy with the flush of sunrise. Brazos climbed on into the forest and lost himself in the great windfalls and the jumbles of avalanche, where to cover a short distance he had to travel a meandering course through a labyrinth of ruined walls and fallen timber, until he came out upon the promontory which had been his favorite retreat. The grassy mossy ledge with its swaying columbines lay warm and dry and fragrant under the sun, its background a forest and its foreground a leap into the abyss. Far below hung the silver fog curtain, dissolving in places, moving slowly, split by spear-pointed firs and shining-faced rocks. Brazos had not yet found out why he came so often to this almost inaccessible spot. And now he divined that probably he never would know. He reveled in its color and beauty and wild grandeur, but these were only a part of what he felt. It was the nearest he could come to being an eagle, which boyish wish had persisted in him. The secret, perhaps, abided mostly in the solitude that seemed never to have been broken. He sat down in his accustomed place and gave himself over to the influence of these lonely heights, feeling it magnified by the fact of this last visit and the need to carry it away with him, back to the haunts of men. Nature was lavish here with its magnificent ruggedness of cliff and peak, its ghastly bones of bared rock and bleached dead pines, its hundreds of perches that only an eagle could reach, its belts of scarlet, its patches of gold, its black fringe of fir high up and its purple clefts of canyon low down. As Brazos gazed, a golden eagle crossed his vision, sailing on broad still wings across the void, dominant in his command of his kingdom of the heights. Suddenly he bowed his wings and shot like a thunderbolt into the depths. The eastern wall of that colossal amphitheater was shrouded by shade, a blank mountain page, mysterious with its dim letters. Brazos followed the western fall, blazing with sunlight, to where it stepped down and down, in endless broken line, to the foothills below. These, black-domed and high, billowed away and down, losing height and color, until they sloped out into the world of plateau range. Hours afterward Brazos wended a thoughtful way back to Coglan&rsquo;s valley. He gathered from all his vigils up there that he had found a strength to lose himself in solitude and be at peace should his dream of love fail him. There was just one little ineradicable drop of bitterness in his cup and that was a strange vague doubt of the enchantment which burned like a rainbow in its promise of love, happiness, fortune. Then he put it out of his mind and next morning at dawn he left Coglan&rsquo;s and rode the downhill journey to Las Animas in eight hours. Brazos installed himself again at Mexican Joe&rsquo;s and sallied forth to visit the barber and to make purchases. &ldquo;Howdy,&rdquo; he drawled to everybody. &ldquo;I shore am glad to see yu-all again. Been holed-up in the hills, choppin&rsquo; wood an&rsquo; huntin&rsquo; deer. Looks like the old burg got along withoot me.&rdquo; Brazos walked into the big general store and said: &ldquo;I want the doggonedest best ootfit yu have in this heah store.&rdquo; Despite the most various and ample stock of cowboy garb short of Denver, Brazos seemed inordinately hard to please. &ldquo;I gotta look handsome, which is shore a turrible job for me.&rdquo; At last he chose a soft gray blouse and red scarf, a huge light sombrero, and the finest of high-top boots, soft as a glove and as snug. In addition to this principal outfit he bought low shoes and black socks, shirts, scarfs, underwear, a shaving kit, and altogether so many things that he forgot what they were. By the time his purchases had been brought to his room, darkness had set in. Brazos had supper with Joe and then went out to let the saloons and the gambling halls know he had returned. He went everywhere, even into Hall&rsquo;s saloon, where they had printed &ldquo;Brazos Keene&rdquo; under bullet holes in the wall, and he was the same cool, easy, drawling cowboy as of old. Next morning Brazos paid vastly more attention to his appearance than was usual with him. His clean-shaven face, tanned with a hint of red, did not show a line nor a shadow. &ldquo;Doggone it! I could look better,&rdquo; he soliloquized, dissatisfied. &ldquo;But at thet I&rsquo;m not so pore.&rdquo; When he buttoned up his new gray coat he found that only the tip of his gun sheath, belted high, showed beneath it. That afforded him great satisfaction, but when he went out to ride to Twin Sombreros he left that coat open and hitched the gun sheath to its old place. &ldquo;Doggone it! Where women air concerned no man is any good,&rdquo; he soliloquized. &ldquo;Heah I am burstin&rsquo; with love for the girl I&rsquo;m goin&rsquo; to see&mdash;an&rsquo; yet regrettin&rsquo; I didn&rsquo;t kiss thet red-haided cat!&rdquo; He forded the brook and rode through the pines down to the lane that came up from the pastures to the corrals and barns at the back of the ranch house. Presently he came upon the skeleton structure of a new barn, huge in dimensions. The floor was clean and shining. Benches had been built and set all around the wide square floor. &ldquo;By golly, I&rsquo;m in time for thet dance.&rdquo; Another new structure, probably of Surface&rsquo;s engineering, was a bunkhouse that almost could have rivaled Holly Ripple&rsquo;s at Don Carlos&rsquo; Rancho. Saddle horses stood bridles down, and cowboys watched Brazos&rsquo; slow approach. He reined in before them. How many times in his range life had Brazos surveyed such a group with narrowed gaze! On this occasion it left him favorably impressed. &ldquo;Howdy, cowboys. Is this heah Twin Sombreros Ranch?&rdquo; he drawled. &ldquo;It sure is, cowboy. Get off an&rsquo; be at home,&rdquo; answered one young fellow. &ldquo;Where&rsquo;s them twins? I want to hit them for a job ridin&rsquo; heah.&rdquo; &ldquo;Fact is, stranger, we got so many bosses thet we don&rsquo;t know who&rsquo;s boss,&rdquo; said another clear-eyed youth, with a laugh. &ldquo;How many bosses?&rdquo; queried Brazos, in pretended alarm. &ldquo;Mr. Neece, Henderson an&rsquo; Sisk, Hank Bilyen an&rsquo; Jack Sain.&rdquo; &ldquo;Doggone! Thet&rsquo;s an ootfit of bosses. I&rsquo;ll take my chance askin&rsquo; the twins.&rdquo; &ldquo;Say, cowboy, you can&rsquo;t fool us. You&rsquo;re Brazos Keene,&rdquo; spoke up another. &ldquo;Who&rsquo;n hell said I wasn&rsquo;t?&rdquo; inquired Brazos, mildly. &ldquo;Hey, Jack, come here,&rdquo; called Brazos&rsquo; first interrogator sticking his head into the door of the bunkhouse. &ldquo;You&rsquo;re wanted.&rdquo; Whereupon Jack Sain emerged to look, to stare, to give a whoop and thump clinking off the porch. No doubt as to his gladness! It shone in his eyes. &ldquo;Brazos! What you doin&rsquo; on that hoss? Git down!&rdquo; he yelled, leaping to meet Brazos&rsquo; outstretched hand. &ldquo;Aw, thet&rsquo;s too bad. I&rsquo;m doggone sorry.&rdquo; &ldquo;Where you been? You look great&mdash;young an&rsquo; pert&mdash;somehow different.&rdquo; &ldquo;I been workin&rsquo; oot.... Jack, introduce me to these heah boys.&rdquo; &ldquo;Damn! Excuse my manners, Brazos, I clean forgot. This is Neece&rsquo;s outfit, picked by Bilyen.... Fellars, walk forward an&rsquo; meet Brazos Keene.&rdquo; &ldquo;Shore glad to meet yu-all,&rdquo; replied Brazos, and shook the hand of each in turn. They were the youngest, cleanest bunch of cowboys Brazos had seen for years. Then Hank Bilyen appeared on the scene. At sight of Brazos he swore lustily. But the cloud left his tanned face. He beamed. &ldquo;Yu Texas ghost!... I was scared stiff. Reckoned yu&rsquo;d pulled yore old trick of ridin&rsquo; away. Pile off so I can hug yu!&rdquo; Brazos warmed to this welcome, yet it gave birth to an incalculable regret. Was he not going to ride for Twin Sombreros Ranch? &ldquo;Come on to the house,&rdquo; said Hank, eagerly. &ldquo;Neece was just hollerin&rsquo; aboot yu. He wants to go on puttin&rsquo; up thet barn, so to get it done before the snow flies. An&rsquo; he cain&rsquo;t go on with it because June and Janis wouldn&rsquo;t give the dance &rsquo;till yu come.&rdquo; &ldquo;Wal now, thet&rsquo;s doggone nice of them.... Hank, do I look all right to yu? I&rsquo;m kinda nervous.&rdquo; &ldquo;Nice? My Gawd! Yu look like Brazos Keene ten years ago&mdash;a pink-cheeked, curly-haided cowboy of sixteen, which you was when I met yu first at Doan&rsquo;s Post.&rdquo; &ldquo;Only ten years? I feel turrible old, Hank. But if I don&rsquo;t look it, what the hell?... Say, isn&rsquo;t thet Henry Sisk on the porch?&rdquo; &ldquo;Yep. One of Abe&rsquo;s pardners. Fine chap, but so lovesick he cain&rsquo;t be himself.&rdquo; &ldquo;Aw! Lovesick? Who with?&rdquo; ejaculated Brazos, in alarm. &ldquo;Janis. An&rsquo; is she leadin&rsquo; him a merry chase!&rdquo; Brazos&rsquo; keen perception never had been any stronger than when he met Henry Sisk again. The young rancher was a gentleman but his courtesy did not deceive Brazos. He was not glad for the return of the cowboy. Then a resonant voice, dry and crisp, gave Brazos a thrill. &ldquo;By the Lord Harry! It&rsquo;s Brazos Keene.&rdquo; Brazos turned on his heel to meet Neece, a transformed man he scarcely recognized. &ldquo;Howdy, old-timer,&rdquo; drawled Brazos, feeling his heart swell. Not since Cap Britt had bade him good-by had any man looked at him like that. &ldquo;Son, there ain&rsquo;t any use to try&mdash;tellin&rsquo; you...&rdquo; began the rancher, with strong emotion. &ldquo;Wal, then, don&rsquo;t try,&rdquo; interrupted Brazos, with his old slow smile. &ldquo;I&rsquo;ll take yore word for it.... Neece, yu shore look&mdash;wal, like what yore twins&rsquo; Dad ought to look.&rdquo; The call of an excited girl cut Neece short. It came from the front of the ranch house, no doubt through the open door of the sitting room. As young Sisk hurried across the porch to enter the room, Brazos had a glimpse of a pale sweet face with wild dark eyes drawing back from the door. &ldquo;Girls, don&rsquo;t be bashful. Come out,&rdquo; called Neece, gaily. Henry reappeared precipitously, as if vigorous arms had given him impetus. &ldquo;Keene, you&rsquo;re wanted inside,&rdquo; he said, gruffly. Apparently the ordeal was painful for him. &ldquo;Who wants me?&rdquo; queried Brazos, both to gain time and to see what further puzzling effect this circumstance had on Sisk. &ldquo;June&mdash;and Janis. They want you alone.&rdquo; &ldquo;Aw!&rdquo; exclaimed Brazos, and his heart leaped. Yet he had leaden feet as he crossed the porch. Sombrero off, Brazos crossed the threshold. One of the twins stood in the center of the room; the other closed the door behind him. Then they both met in front of him, pale, tremendously excited and inhibited, amber eyes darkly dilated. Brazos could not tell one from the other. &ldquo;Howdy&mdash;girls,&rdquo; he said, huskily. &ldquo;It&rsquo;s shore turrible good to see yu&mdash;heah.&rdquo; &ldquo;Oh&mdash;Brazos!&rdquo; gasped one. &ldquo;Cowboy!&rdquo; cried the other. Manifestly they knew him, yet it was obvious he appeared strangely at variance with what they had expected. Brazos bore little semblance to a wild and ruthless cowboy; none at all to a bloody gunman. Even his gun did not show. His tanned face wore the old guileless smile. The month of labor and solitude, with their weaning him from the dark and deadly mood, had removed years from his face. Youth shone upon him. He had every reason to be happy that he was Brazos Keene. &ldquo;Wal, I reckon I feel a little queer, too,&rdquo; he drawled, and laying his sombrero upon the table he made pretense of interest in the big sitting room. &ldquo;Gosh, what a fine place! Most as nice as Don Carlos&rsquo; Rancho. I shore&mdash;&mdash;&rdquo; Then they both talked at once, with identical voices that spoke different meanings, not any of which gave Brazos a clue to which was his fiancée. But he gathered that their great and measureless gratitude had to do with the salvation and rejuvenation of a dearly beloved father. For the rest, the ranch had not meant so much at first but had grown to be home&mdash;the relinquishment of actual work in their restaurant had been strangely regretted&mdash;the pleasure of fixing up rooms and changing the ranch house&mdash;the fun, at first, of seeing innumerable cowboys who applied to their father for jobs&mdash;the troops of horses and mustangs and colts&mdash;the building of the huge barn and the idea of a dance destined to make history on the range&mdash;all these and more poured out upon Brazos until the twins exhausted breath. &ldquo;Ahuh. Grand&mdash;just grand!&rdquo; ejaculated Brazos, bewildered. &ldquo;But will yu tell me which of yu is June an&rsquo; which is Jan?&rdquo; &ldquo;Guess,&rdquo; cried one, with radiant smile. &ldquo;Does it make so much difference who is who?&rdquo; asked the other, wistfully, with a devil in her eyes. Brazos suffered a flash of that old recurrent havoc. He sensed, if he did not realize, that only a hair&rsquo;s breadth separated him from something appallingly nameless and incredible. &ldquo;Doggone if I&rsquo;ll guess!&rdquo; he replied, stubbornly. &ldquo;Foolish boy! Don&rsquo;t you wish we were just one girl?... I am June.&rdquo; At that juncture the girls&rsquo; aunt entered to welcome Brazos; and she was so sincere and kind, so apparently oblivious of his status and the violence which had reinstated them at Twin Sobreros, that Brazos found himself at last. &ldquo;Wal, it was aboot time somebody rescued me,&rdquo; he drawled. &ldquo;Miss Neece, yu just couldn&rsquo;t guess how glad I am to see yu.&rdquo; &ldquo;Thank you. I&rsquo;d scarcely have known you, Brazos Keene.&rdquo; &ldquo;Well, little Jan was around when it happened,&rdquo; said her sister, subtly. &ldquo;Come, Brazos, let me show you my room.&rdquo; &ldquo;And mine, too,&rdquo; added June. One at each arm they dragged the bedazzled cowboy from one beautiful room to another, then all over the house, and out into the yard, out toward the corrals, past the group of gaping cowboys, at last to the barn. June held forth on what a marvelous place it would be for her horses when winter came and Janis dilated on its desirability for their dance. &ldquo;Now you&rsquo;re here we can have it! When?&rdquo; exclaimed June, delightedly. &ldquo;June Neece, were yu gonna have thet dance withoot me?&rdquo; demanded Brazos. &ldquo;You bet she was,&rdquo; declared Janis. &ldquo;She and Jack Sain were keen on it.&rdquo; &ldquo;Why, Jan Neece!&rdquo; contradicted June, red as a rose. &ldquo;I wasn&rsquo;t.... It was you and Henry who got Jack to nag me.&rdquo; &ldquo;Wal, never mind thet,&rdquo; interposed Brazos, seeing he had struck a discordant note. &ldquo;I&rsquo;m heah&mdash;an&rsquo; ararin&rsquo; to dance.... Doggone! I&rsquo;ll bet I&rsquo;ve forgot how.&rdquo; &ldquo;Everybody is waiting,&rdquo; concluded Janis, who was the dominating one. &ldquo;Let&rsquo;s say Friday night. That&rsquo;ll give us two days to decorate the barn with autumn leaves and flowers. And get the supper ready. Dad has a surprise for us&mdash;I don&rsquo;t know what. This will be the welcome home he had planned for us, June.&rdquo; &ldquo;Friday night&mdash;two days?&rdquo; queried June, dreamily, her eyes on Brazos. &ldquo;It will be full moon.&rdquo; They marched Brazos back to the house to announce with gay acclaim the date for the dance. Henry Sisk reluctantly obeyed their command to take the cowboys and go into the forest to fetch an abundance of autumn leaves and pine cones and ferns for ornament. The girls rushed in to confer with their aunt. Janis poked her head out to call: &ldquo;Cowboy, don&rsquo;t you go riding away!&rdquo; &ldquo;Son, when will you take charge?&rdquo; asked Neece. &ldquo;Yu mean of yore ootfits? Gosh!&rdquo; &ldquo;I mean of mine. Henderson has his own foreman. An&rsquo; Sisk his. They&rsquo;ve got pretty good outfits, in my judgment. But I depend a lot on Bilyen.&rdquo; &ldquo;What&rsquo;s Hank&rsquo;s job gonna be?&rdquo; &ldquo;Hank will buy an&rsquo; sell cattle.&rdquo; &ldquo;Fine. He&rsquo;s a shrewd hombre an&rsquo; honest as noonday. Coglan told me yu was runnin&rsquo; eighty thousand haid. Is thet so, boss?&rdquo; &ldquo;More by a few thousand.&rdquo; &ldquo;Ahuh. I don&rsquo;t know as thet is so good,&rdquo; rejoined Brazos, thoughtfully. &ldquo;Hank wasn&rsquo;t so keen about it, either. But I am. I&rsquo;d rather have Henderson in with me with all his money an&rsquo; bankin&rsquo; interests, an&rsquo; young Sisk, than tackle it alone with only the ten thousand head Surface left me.&rdquo; &ldquo;Neece, yu&rsquo;re an old-timer. It&rsquo;ll mean drawin&rsquo; rustlers like molasses draws flies.&rdquo; &ldquo;There won&rsquo;t be any more wholesale rustlin&rsquo;. I&rsquo;ve been thirty years on the frontier. An&rsquo; I&rsquo;ve seen the cattle business grow. It&rsquo;s about at its peak now. An&rsquo; I&rsquo;ve never seen big raids on any range but once. Did you?&rdquo; &ldquo;Wal, come to think aboot it&mdash;I reckon no. But all the same a steady stealin&rsquo; of stock in small bunches cain&rsquo;t be sneezed at.&rdquo; &ldquo;Brazos, I&rsquo;ll lose less throwin&rsquo; in with my pardners, an&rsquo; runnin&rsquo; a hundred thousand head, than if I stay out an&rsquo; run one-tenth of that number.&rdquo; &ldquo;Sounds sensible. Why isn&rsquo;t Hank keen aboot it?&rdquo; &ldquo;Bilyen is not against it, but he&rsquo;s not crazy about it. Says such big herds invite all kinds of range trouble from stealin&rsquo; by rival combines an&rsquo; out an&rsquo; out rustlers to corruptin&rsquo; cowboys.&rdquo; &ldquo;Wal, Hank is shore right.&rdquo; &ldquo;Brazos, we&rsquo;re goin&rsquo; to find out before the snow flies.&rdquo; &ldquo;Heah comes Hank now, in a buckboard.... Gosh, them blacks look kinda familiar!&rdquo; &ldquo;Where you goin&rsquo;, Hank?&rdquo; queried the rancher, as Bilyen drove to a halt. &ldquo;Town. Got a list longer&rsquo;n yore laig&mdash;all for thet darn dance.&rdquo; &ldquo;Hank, I&rsquo;ve been talkin&rsquo; with Neece heah. He says yu&rsquo;re not keen on his combine an&rsquo; the big herd.&rdquo; &ldquo;Wal, air yu, Brazos?&rdquo; parried Bilyen. &ldquo;Shore I am. The more the merrier.&rdquo; Brazos deliberately contradicted his opinion to Neece for reasons of his own. &ldquo;To be honest I feel all right aboot it now, &rsquo;cause yu&rsquo;re heah, Brazos.&rdquo; &ldquo;Ahuh. Wal, what was on yore chest before I got heah?&rdquo; &ldquo;Mebbe I&rsquo;m a little personal. I got a grudge against Bodkin. An&rsquo; I ain&rsquo;t so damn friendly toward this new cattleman Knight.&rdquo; &ldquo;Bodkin kinda rubs me the wrong way, too, boss,&rdquo; returned Brazos broodingly, &ldquo;He was crooked. I know. I heahed him talk with two men I didn&rsquo;t know. But all of them was in thet deal, an&rsquo; all of them was ready to double-cross Surface. It was from them I heahed aboot the bag of gold I got back for yu. They were all huntin&rsquo; for thet.... An&rsquo; Coglan told me the talk had it thet this man Knight is the hombre who shot Surface.&rdquo; &ldquo;Wal, they&rsquo;ve side-tracked thet talk.... Brazos, do yu reckon Bodkin&rsquo;s bein&rsquo; elected sheriff will make him go straight?&rdquo; &ldquo;Not in a million years!&rdquo; &ldquo;Thet will simplify the problem for Neece,&rdquo; declared Bilyen, gathering up his reins. And he drove off without another word. &ldquo;What&rsquo;d thet sore-haided Texan mean, anyhow?&rdquo; queried Brazos, irritably, when he knew perfectly well what Bilyen meant. &ldquo;Drive Bodkin out of Colorado,&rdquo; replied Neece, grimly. &ldquo;Thet&rsquo;d break up this range ring.&rdquo; &ldquo;Yu cain&rsquo;t drive thet hombre oot of Las Animas.&rdquo; &ldquo;Ahuh. I was thinkin&rsquo; aboot thet.&rdquo; &ldquo;Bodkin is not goin&rsquo; to be caught brandin&rsquo; calves or dealin&rsquo; with rustlers. He&rsquo;s going to play safe from now on.&rdquo; &ldquo;If!... For land&rsquo;s sake, Keene, don&rsquo;t say you might not. Why, I&rsquo;m relyin&rsquo; on you. Thet&rsquo;s what got Henderson to throw in with me.... Besides you&rsquo;re goin&rsquo; to be a son to me, aren&rsquo;t you?&rdquo; &ldquo;My Gawd&mdash;I&rsquo;d like to be,&rdquo; gulped Brazos. &ldquo;Help!&rdquo; cried Brazos, in a weak voice. Then after hanging his head a moment he looked up to speak feelingly. &ldquo;Neece, I&rsquo;m thankin&rsquo; yu for yore trust an&rsquo; likin&rsquo;. An&rsquo; I&rsquo;m shore proud thet June&mdash;an&rsquo; Janis, too&mdash;wants me heah.... I&rsquo;ll come, boss, an&rsquo; ride my damndest for yu.&rdquo; &ldquo;Good! Thet relieves me. An&rsquo; it&rsquo;ll make the girls happy,&rdquo; replied the rancher, with great satisfaction. &ldquo;I reckon it&rsquo;s a delicate situation for you, Brazos. Wild cowboy an&rsquo; gunman thet you are&mdash;thank God!&mdash;you still have pride an&rsquo; honor. You might feel compunctions against askin&rsquo; me for June or Janis, because you are who you are. But I&rsquo;ll declare myself before you ask me&mdash;I&rsquo;d like you to take Allen&rsquo;s place.... Don&rsquo;t worry about decidin&rsquo; between the twins. They&rsquo;ll settle your hash pronto, an&rsquo; it doesn&rsquo;t make any difference to me.... Now thet&rsquo;s off my mind. Let&rsquo;s go in an&rsquo; drink to it.&rdquo; &ldquo;Wal, I&rsquo;m kinda in need of a bracer myself,&rdquo; replied Brazos, rapt and troubled, following his host indoors. Evidently Neece considered the moment propitious for more than one drink. The result was, that when Brazos came out again, his natural reserve was somewhat lessened. And his worry about telling the twins apart did not seem so monumental. Fortunately for Brazos, however, there was a sort of general conference about the dance, in which he and Neece were consulted. After that came dinner, which was a festal board for Brazos. Everybody except Henry Sisk, who sulked over some real or imagined grievance, appeared wonderfully happy. Brazos was lost in the brightness of two pairs of eyes. He was asked, presently, what he thought about their having a huge bowl of punch in the living room the night of the dance. &ldquo;My Gawd, thet&rsquo;s a turrible idee,&rdquo; quoth Brazos. After the merry howl had subsided one of the twins asked him gravely: &ldquo;Good or bad?&rdquo; &ldquo;Bad.&rdquo; &ldquo;Aw, no, not any cowboy,&rdquo; returned Brazos, rising to the occasion, &ldquo;but yore Dad will be heah, an&rsquo; Hank, an&rsquo; Henry&mdash;&mdash;&rdquo; &ldquo;I don&rsquo;t drink,&rdquo; snapped Sisk. &ldquo;We&rsquo;ll take a chance on everybody,&rdquo; concluded Neece, rubbing his hands. &ldquo;It won&rsquo;t be an ordinary dance.&rdquo; &ldquo;I should smile it won&rsquo;t,&rdquo; agreed Bilyen. &ldquo;Holly Ripple gave a dance an&rsquo; dinner once years ago,&rdquo; said Brazos, reminiscently. &ldquo;Her Dad had kept open house like the old Don, Holly&rsquo;s grandfather. Thet was a party, folks, believe me. The whole range was invited. Why yu couldn&rsquo;t find any cowboys for cattlemen, trappers, Injuns, soldiers, freighters, bandits, rustlers, desperadoes. An&rsquo; the wonderfullest thing aboot thet party was there wasn&rsquo;t one single fight.&rdquo; &ldquo;We could not hope to emulate Miss Ripple,&rdquo; said one of the twins. And the other fixed Brazos with inscrutable eyes. &ldquo;One range celebrity will be quite all June and I can take care of.&rdquo; Amid the laughter Brazos subsided, as much from this retort as from the conviction that he had for the hundredth time taken Janis for June. After dinner they dragged Brazos, Neece, Henry, Bilyen out to the barn, and collected Sain and the cowboys on the way. Already heaps of evergreen, boughs of pine with the cones intact, and branches of different colored autumn leaves lay upon the floor. &ldquo;Not half nor a quarter enough,&rdquo; declared Janis. &ldquo;Henry, take the wagon and the boys out for more.&rdquo; Henry demurred and made a weak suggestion to the effect that Brazos go. &ldquo;Brazos can stay here and show us how to balance on a ladder,&rdquo; replied Janis, demurely. All that wonderful afternoon Brazos climbed and balanced and nailed, pounded his thumb, made mistakes, fell off the ladder, and proved according to Hank that he might be Brazos Keene but he was the locoedest decorator who ever came over the range. For once Brazos was meek, so far as taking criticism was concerned. Brazos scarcely heard his old comrade nor any of Neece&rsquo;s dry remarks. He tried to do the bidding of the twins, blandly oblivious of his limitations for the job, and thought only of the delicious and bewildering intimacy to which they admitted him. Half the time he was hidden with Janis or June, or both of them, behind great flaming boughs of maple leaves or golden aspens or russet oaks. And Brazos daringly took advantage of his opportunity to make love to June when he was sure he was with her. But, as the afternoon grew apace, in the excitement of fleeting moments, he would not always have staked his life upon a certainty that he was right. Then he was in too deep to get out and he did not care. And they did not help him to discriminate between them. That was the strange fiendish weakness of the twins. They had perpetrated this innocent mischievous prank since childhood. Nevertheless, the work progressed rapidly, especially as Neece and Bilyen lent a hand to close the open ends of the barns with brilliant foliage. Then the two kindly men took it upon themselves to attempt more without consulting Janis, which omission brought dire disaster upon their heads, and the patient cowboys from their other tasks. It also left Brazos alone with June in one of the foliage-screened stalls. &ldquo;June, this shore has been a thrillin&rsquo; day,&rdquo; said Brazos. &ldquo;June! I&rsquo;ve been as good as&mdash;as I know how to be,&rdquo; protested Brazos, aghast. &ldquo;Perhaps that&rsquo;s not very good.&rdquo; &ldquo;What have I done?&rdquo; &ldquo;Led poor Jan on&mdash;for one thing.&rdquo; &ldquo;Led pore Jan on!&rdquo; burst out Brazos, suddenly on fire. &ldquo;Brazos, I&mdash;I haven&rsquo;t told Jan or anybody we&mdash;we&rsquo;re engaged.... I&rsquo;m afraid Jan&mdash;l-likes you.&rdquo; &ldquo;Good Lord!&mdash;&rsquo;course she liked me. Why not? I&rsquo;ve been nice to her&mdash;an&rsquo; I shore&mdash;&mdash;&rdquo; &ldquo;June, I hope to die&mdash;&mdash;&rdquo; &ldquo;Do you swear that&mdash;Brazos?&rdquo; asked the girl, emotionally. &ldquo;On a stack of Bibles a mile high,&rdquo; declared Brazos, again possessing himself of her hands, and drawing her a little closer. &ldquo;Aw, she&rsquo;s in love with Sisk.&rdquo; &ldquo;Like hob she is! She likes Henry, better than any of her beaux, and she might have&mdash;&mdash;&rdquo; &ldquo;June, don&rsquo;t waste so much time,&rdquo; expostulated Brazos. &ldquo;Heah we&rsquo;ve been alone only a minute&mdash;the first time&mdash;an&rsquo; yu waste it.&rdquo; &ldquo;But Brazos! We can&rsquo;t spoon right under the noses of Dad&mdash;Jan&mdash;Jack&mdash;&mdash;&rdquo; &ldquo;Shore we can. An&rsquo; why Jack? June, I&rsquo;m scared yu&mdash;yu like thet cowboy.&rdquo; &ldquo;Darlin&rsquo;, yu gotta do two things or I&rsquo;ll go clean loco,&rdquo; importuned Brazos. &ldquo;And what are they?&rdquo; &ldquo;Wal, be more of a sweetheart&mdash;an&rsquo; wear somethin&rsquo; or give me some kind of a hunch yu&rsquo;re June.&rdquo; &ldquo;Brazos, I&rsquo;ve been the&mdash;the faithfulest sweetheart you ever had. If you only knew!&rdquo; &ldquo;June, I mean a lovin&rsquo; sweetheart. I&rsquo;m the kind of a hombre thet&rsquo;s got to have kisses&mdash;millions of kisses.&rdquo; But she yielded easily to his tender clasp and shyly held up her lips. Her kiss was cool&mdash;sweet&mdash;quick, gathering something as it ended. &ldquo;There! one on account.... Wait! they&rsquo;re calling me. I&rsquo;ll be back soon.&rdquo; &ldquo;June!&rdquo; came the clamoring cry from outside the leafy bower. She slipped away from Brazos, with a touch, a look that convinced him she wanted to stay there in his arms. Brazos stood staring after her in the gathering rosy gloom of the great barn. He did not attend particularly to the argument going on at the other end. He was still under the spell of June&rsquo;s shy surrender, her half promise, and he waited with strong impatience for her return. There would surely be a moment more. Dusk was falling. The cows were lowing in the fields. Then the supper bell put an end to the animated discussion that had disrupted Brazos&rsquo; moment of bliss. &ldquo;Come on, Brazos,&rdquo; yelled the merry voice of Jack Sain. &ldquo;Doggone!&rdquo; muttered Brazos. &ldquo;Hey, Texas, we&rsquo;re knockin&rsquo; off for supper,&rdquo; called Bilyen. Brazos stood there with a sense of blank disappointment, a letting down of his expectant mood, listening to the voices and footsteps receding. Then his heart leaped at a quick light patter of feet that slowed, halted significantly outside the booth. A slender form in white stood framed in the darkening doorway of foliage. &ldquo;Aw, heah yu air!&rdquo; whispered Brazos, with a passionate regurgitation of feeling. &ldquo;Oh, I couldn&rsquo;t see you.... They sent me back....&rdquo; Perhaps it was his sudden looming right over her that checked her gay voice. He saw her face, pale against the background of leaves, her eyes unnaturally large and dark. &ldquo;Yu near found a daid man,&rdquo; said Brazos, snatching her off her feet. &ldquo;There! Thet shore was&mdash;comin&rsquo; to yu&mdash;Lady,&rdquo; panted Brazos, as her head drooped back, her eyes closed under mystic lids. They opened. &ldquo;Cowboy devil!&rdquo; she whispered, both tone and look impossible to read. Nevertheless Brazos translated them in his own behalf. &ldquo;No&mdash;more,&rdquo; she cried, frantically, and surprising Brazos with sudden strength she freed herself and fled. Brazos followed, still in a transport. But as he got out of the gloom of the barn into the open where it was light he sustained a return of rationality. One of the boys had waited and in a moment more Brazos recognized Henry Sisk. &ldquo;What&rsquo;d you go back for?&rdquo; he asked, in low voice. &ldquo;What&rsquo;d you&mdash;drag June&mdash;away for?&rdquo; panted the girl, as she reached him. &ldquo;Ha! Ha!&rdquo; Janis&rsquo; sweet laugh not only silenced Sisk but also made a stone image of Brazos. The couple hurried on to catch up with the others down the lane. Brazos stood there in the summer twilight as suddenly stiff and cold as if he had been turned to stone, his consciousness capable of only one thought: &ldquo;My Gawd, if it wasn&rsquo;t Jan!&rdquo; Brazos had faced bad men, rustlers, wild beasts, outlaw mustangs, fire and flood with far less fear than it took to walk into the Neece dining room. But he had to go. To flee would have been a fatal blunder. Like a man going to his execution Brazos faced that merry table. &ldquo;Brazos, what made you so late? I called and called,&rdquo; said one of the twins, her dark eyes full of laughter. That had to be June. &ldquo;I went back after him,&rdquo; said the other twin, with her dark eyes lowered. &ldquo;He&rsquo;s no plains cowboy. He&rsquo;s a woodland faun.&rdquo; &ldquo;Wal, I reckon I don&rsquo;t know what thet is,&rdquo; Brazos drawled, in his old cool lazy voice. &ldquo;But in the fall when the leaves air all gold an&rsquo; red I get kinda loco!&rdquo; &ldquo;Wal, folks,&rdquo; he said at an opportune moment, &ldquo;I&rsquo;m gonna say good night an&rsquo; ride back to town.&rdquo; &ldquo;What&rsquo;s the sense in thet?&rdquo; spoke up Neece, quickly. &ldquo;This is your home. An&rsquo; Hank can fetch your pack tomorrow.&rdquo; &ldquo;Please don&rsquo;t go, Brazos,&rdquo; added June, suddenly anxious. &ldquo;Brazos Keene, you&rsquo;ve got a job,&rdquo; piled on Janis, with a merry subtlety impossible to interpret. &ldquo;This Neece outfit is getting obstreperous. You can&rsquo;t run it gallivanting to town.&rdquo; &ldquo;Wal, I reckon I cain&rsquo;t run it if I stay,&rdquo; replied Brazos, for whom the humor of the situation had ceased. &ldquo;I was only fooling,&rdquo; said Janis, hurriedly. &ldquo;Why must you go?&rdquo; pouted June. &ldquo;Wal, since yu call my hand,&rdquo; drawled Brazos, exaggerating his cool habit of speech, &ldquo;the fact is there&rsquo;s a couple of hombres in town thet I forgot aboot shootin&rsquo;.&rdquo; Blank surprise and silence ensued upon Brazos&rsquo; reply. As Brazos had intended they could not tell whether he was in jest or earnest. &ldquo;So long. See yu-all in the mawnin&rsquo;,&rdquo; he concluded, and left the room. Hank Bilyen followed him out on the porch, and one of the twins caught up with them. &ldquo;Brazos&mdash;wait,&rdquo; she faltered. &ldquo;Cowboy, I&rsquo;ll have yore hawse heah in a jiffy,&rdquo; said Hank, and thumped off the porch. Brazos had taken a step down, but turned to look at the girl, whose face was on a level with his. &ldquo;You are&mdash;angry?&rdquo; she asked. &ldquo;Not atall.... An&rsquo; which one of these heah Neece girls air yu?&rdquo; &ldquo;Brazos! I&rsquo;m June. Don&rsquo;t look at me like that. We were only in fun&mdash;and they coaxed&mdash;nagged me into it.&rdquo; &ldquo;Into what?&rdquo; queried Brazos, bluntly. June&rsquo;s distress told him there was something in the wind of which he had had no inkling. &ldquo;You&rsquo;ve guessed it&mdash;haven&rsquo;t you?&rdquo; &ldquo;Ump-umm. I&rsquo;m a pore guesser. An&rsquo; I&rsquo;m a pore social cuss, too. I reckon I don&rsquo;t belong heah.&rdquo; This was so astounding and delicious that Brazos could not resist prolonging it. &ldquo;Wal, June, I&rsquo;m not a forgiving cuss, either&mdash;when I see I&rsquo;m bein&rsquo; made fun of.&rdquo; &ldquo;Now they&rsquo;re making fun of me, too,&rdquo; protested June. &ldquo;They put up a job on us, Brazos.&rdquo; &ldquo;Ahuh. Suppose yu tell me aboot it.&rdquo; &ldquo;Darling!&rdquo; &ldquo;Awful sweet talk, June, but not gettin&rsquo; us anywhere.&rdquo; &ldquo;But don&rsquo;t be such a stranger to me,&rdquo; she wailed. &ldquo;Brazos, would&mdash;would you be terribly angry if I&mdash;I confessed something I said about&mdash;about you and me?&rdquo; &ldquo;No. If it was true I&rsquo;d like yu the better.&rdquo; &ldquo;You remember tonight before supper&mdash;when you and I were alone in the stall?&rdquo; &ldquo;Wal, I&rsquo;m not liable to forget.&rdquo; &ldquo;Wal, I shore took Jan for yu, all right,&rdquo; declared Brazos, with a grim satisfaction. &ldquo;Oh, Brazos&mdash;you&mdash;you didn&rsquo;t&mdash;&mdash;&rdquo; &ldquo;Let yore imagination run high, wide, an&rsquo; handsome, June, an&rsquo; maybe yu&rsquo;ll get somewhere.&rdquo; &ldquo;Brazos! I&rsquo;ll bet you were too smart for them. You knew Jan!&rdquo; exclaimed June, hopefully. &ldquo;You played up to them. Poor Jan! No wonder she was so strange&mdash;so tense. Served her right! Oh, Brazos, I&rsquo;m horribly jealous, but if you guessed the trick I&mdash;I can stand it.... Only Jan worries me.... Do you forgive me, Brazos?&rdquo; &ldquo;Shore, sweetheart, I&rsquo;d forgive yu any thin&rsquo;. But I&rsquo;m not so shore aboot Jan an&rsquo; Jack an&rsquo; yore Dad.&rdquo; &ldquo;Now you&rsquo;re my old Brazos again,&rdquo; murmured June. &ldquo;I&rsquo;ll be a match for them next time.... Brazos, let&rsquo;s play a terrible joke on them.&rdquo; &ldquo;I should smile. How aboot elopin&rsquo;?&rdquo; &ldquo;Oh!... Brazos, you&rsquo;re not serious?&rdquo; cried June, aghast yet intrigued at the idea. &ldquo;Shore am. We could slope off in the mawnin&rsquo;&mdash;get to Dodge City long enough to slip thet bridle on yu&mdash;then come back to the dance.&rdquo; &ldquo;Glorious! But&mdash;but&mdash;&mdash;&rdquo; &ldquo;Then it wouldn&rsquo;t make so much difference whether or not I took yu for Jan,&rdquo; drawled Brazos, dryly. &ldquo;Wouldn&rsquo;t it though?&rdquo; flashed June. &ldquo;Brazos Keene, I agree with Jan. Nobody can be quite sure of you.&rdquo; &ldquo;If yu were my wife, wouldn&rsquo;t yu feel tolerable safe?&rdquo; &ldquo;Don&rsquo;t tempt me, Brazos. If we eloped it&rsquo;d hurt Dad. And there&rsquo;s no need of hurry. I&mdash;I&rsquo;d like it! But I mustn&rsquo;t. Another thing&mdash;Jan would never forgive me.&rdquo; &ldquo;For marryin&rsquo; me!&rdquo; ejaculated Brazos. &ldquo;No. For not telling her. We&rsquo;ll wait, Brazos dear&mdash;if you can be true to me.&rdquo; &ldquo;I shore can if yu&rsquo;ll only wear somethin&rsquo; or do somethin&rsquo; so I cain&rsquo;t mistake yu for Jan again.&rdquo; &ldquo;I guess you don&rsquo;t really want to,&rdquo; she said, reproachfully. &ldquo;It must be great fun for a cowboy, especially when his fiancée&rsquo;s sister doesn&rsquo;t run away from him.&rdquo; &ldquo;About as funny as bein&rsquo; piled off yore hawse. June, will you promise to give me a hunch, so I&rsquo;ll know yu?&rdquo; &ldquo;Yes. I promise, Brazos. I&rsquo;ll think up something that no one else can tell.&rdquo; &ldquo;June, do yu reckon they-all took yu to mean we air engaged?&rdquo; asked Brazos, wistfully. &ldquo;No, they didn&rsquo;t. Dad never mentioned it. And Jan laughed in my face. Then she tried to pump me. Oh, she worries me, Brazos.&rdquo; &ldquo;Wal, if yu had my state of mind aboot thet girl yu&rsquo;d be loco.... Have yu got any nerve, darlin&rsquo;?&rdquo; &ldquo;Nerve? Yes. For what?&rdquo; &ldquo;Jan has backed into the room. Gosh, her eyes look like burnt holes in a blanket. They&rsquo;re all peekin&rsquo; oot at us. Makes me kinda sore. How aboot kissin&rsquo; me good night? Thet&rsquo;ll fix them.&rdquo; &ldquo;Oh, I haven&rsquo;t that&mdash;much nerve,&rdquo; faltered June. &ldquo;I might&mdash;&mdash;&rdquo; &ldquo;Jan would have in yore place,&rdquo; interrupted Brazos, a little bitterly. &ldquo;Aw, heah comes Hank with my hawse.&rdquo; &ldquo;Pooh! Who cares for Hank.... Brazos, Brazos, you may grab me&mdash;hug me like a bear&mdash;kiss me good night, then run.&rdquo; &ldquo;June!&rdquo; &ldquo;Yes&mdash;and explain to Dad tomorrow.&rdquo; Dared thus and spurred by June&rsquo;s provocative smile Brazos brazenly availed himself of the sweet privilege. It made his head whirl so that he nearly fell down the steps. &ldquo;Say, cowboy, what&rsquo;n hell&rsquo;s got into yu?&rdquo; queried Hank, in mild concern, as Brazos swung into his saddle. &ldquo;Gawd only knows, Hank,&rdquo; drawled Brazos, with his cool laugh. &ldquo;I might be ridin&rsquo; away from Don Carlos&rsquo; Rancho. So long, Texas.&rdquo; Brazos had a fleeting glimpse of the disheveled June standing slim and lovely in the light with her hands over her face. Riding furiously away down the dark road Brazos&rsquo; sheer joy crowded out his other emotions. June, the little minx, had challenged that sweet madness. Janis had seen it, her father, Sain, and all of them. They would tease June into a confession of their plighted troth. Brazos thought himself not only the happiest of men but the most fortunate. When he neared the town he slowed the spirited horse to a walk and his rushing mind slowed proportionately. Any cattle town had a quieting effect upon Brazos. And suddenly it struck him that he had never gone back to any town where he had engaged in a serious shooting fray. The cold stars, the breeze off the range, the dark avenues into the hills, the pale light that located Las Animas, these told him that love and happiness could not change the merciless truth that he was still Brazos Keene. He was hated by men he had frustrated. He would ever be marked by them and their allies. He could never enter that town without reverting to the hard steely vigilant gunman. The creed of border rustlers was that dead men did not find or trail their tracks. Brazos left his horse at the accustomed corral and walked up the side-street into the main thoroughfare. That element of Las Animas which did not sleep at night, and the cowboys and cattlemen upon whom they preyed, were out in force. Saloons, dance halls and gambling dens hummed with their peculiar low ominous roar. Las Animas was growing. The hour was midnight and Brazos met many men, some accompanied by women. He peeped into the dives. He stood back in dark doorways and watched. His conclusion was that Las Animas would never compare with Dodge, or Abilene, or Lincoln in the viciousness of those cattle towns in their hey-day, but it was a hard town, and a center for rustling activities that no one man could clean up. Brazos at last went to his room at Mexican Joe&rsquo;s, and after lighting the lamp he broodingly unbuckled his gun belt and sat down on the bed. &ldquo;Better men than I am have been in the same kind of fix,&rdquo; he mused, darkly. &ldquo;I&rsquo;m supposed to marry and settle down to quiet home life. An&rsquo; forget the men I&rsquo;ve killed an&rsquo; thet there&rsquo;s some left I ought to kill.... Ha! Thet&rsquo;s the idee. Thet for Brazos Keene!... Wal, I&rsquo;ll do even thet for June if they only let me.... Pore kid! It&rsquo;s tough on her. She really never ought to have fallen in love with me. If I&rsquo;d been half a man I&rsquo;d never let her do it. But I did an&rsquo; she did!... Bless her lovin&rsquo; heart!... An&rsquo; I gotta hide this secret fear an&rsquo; watch as I never did in my life, hopin&rsquo; time will change things.&rdquo; Brazos slept late, a luxury he seldom indulged in. And after he awakened he lay in bed, realizing that in broad daylight, with the sun pouring golden in at his window, he could not feel the same as he did in the black midnight hours. He was wonderfully happy. And that exalted mood lasted until, booted, spurred and gun-belted, he walked out up the street of Las Animas. He had not taken a dozen steps from Mexican Joe&rsquo;s when a cowboy, a lean hard-faced youth, sauntered out of a doorway. &ldquo;Howdy, Keene,&rdquo; he said, as if in casual greeting. &ldquo;I been walkin&rsquo; the street for an hour watchin&rsquo; for you.&rdquo; &ldquo;Howdy, cowboy,&rdquo; returned Brazos, slowly. &ldquo;Gimme a match. Make this look natural,&rdquo; returned the other. &ldquo;Ahuh. Heah yu air. Talk fast, stranger.&rdquo; The cowboy took the match and lighted his cigarette leisurely, but he spoke rapidly. &ldquo;Last night at Hall&rsquo;s&mdash;heerd two men talkin&rsquo;&mdash;Brazos Keene in town&mdash;Knight swears we&rsquo;re to git him at any cost&mdash;Bodkin&rsquo; rarin&rsquo;.&rdquo; The cowboy raised his young hard visage, puffed a cloud of smoke and turned away. &ldquo;Thanks, pard,&rdquo; said Brazos, and went on as before. &ldquo;Doggone! Thet&rsquo;s hot. I reckon thet cowboy risked his life an&rsquo; knew it.... They&rsquo;re gonna try to shoot me in the back. Bodkin again!&rdquo; Brazos went in the first store and out the back way, where he proceeded along the alley to a side street from which he reached the corral where he had left his horse. The Mexican boy, Pedro, had fed and watered Bay. Brazos lost no time riding out of town. The conclusion he reached was that Bodkin had taken up with this Knight&mdash;that they were going to proceed on the lines Surface had developed&mdash;and that such operations were retarded because of the unwelcome existence of one Brazos Keene. The approach of winter might have necessitated renewed activity on the part of cattle rustlers. Cattle had gone up in price to forty-three dollars on the hoof with increasing demand. Neece had seen this coming and he had bought wholesale, getting some small herds for as low as thirty dollars a head. When Brazos arrived at Twin Sombreros he rode in with little thought of the distracting sisters and the gala supper and dance to be held that evening. As luck would have it June or Janis called gaily to him from the house, but Brazos only waved without stopping. He found a merry bustling crowd of cowboys working to enhance the autumn effect desired by the girls. &ldquo;Heah, one of yu rollickin&rsquo; gazabos,&rdquo; he called. &ldquo;Tell Neece an&rsquo; Bilyen I want them pronto.&rdquo; The rancher was the first to reach Brazos and he wore the warm smile he had acquired for this cowboy. &ldquo;Mornin&rsquo;, son. You look pretty serious. Scared of bracin&rsquo; Dad after last night, eh?&rdquo; Brazos had to grin. No fear of this father&rsquo;s censure or criticism! Neece was on his side. &ldquo;Scared as hell, Dad, now yu make me think of last night. But thet wasn&rsquo;t on my mind atall.&rdquo; Hank Bilyen joined them at this juncture. &ldquo;Mawnin&rsquo;, Brazos,&rdquo; said the Texan, his gray gaze studying Brazos&rsquo; face. &ldquo;Come heah,&rdquo; returned Brazos, and drew the two aside. &ldquo;Aw, yu&rsquo;re gonna bust up the party,&rdquo; complained Hank, a sense of calamity evidently striking him. &ldquo;Ump-umm. Not atall. I&rsquo;ve got a tip, though.... Cattle gone to forty-three dollars. It&rsquo;ll be forty-five in less&rsquo;n a week, an&rsquo; goin&rsquo; up.&rdquo; &ldquo;You don&rsquo;t say!&rdquo; ejaculated the rancher, eagerly. &ldquo;Hank, my hunch was correct.&rdquo; &ldquo;Wal, I was holdin&rsquo; at forty for this fall. But forty-five! Say, Neece, we&rsquo;re settin&rsquo; with a powerful good hand.&rdquo; &ldquo;How many haid can yu drive in an&rsquo; ship pronto, inside the week?&rdquo; queried Brazos, thoughtfully. &ldquo;Close to twenty thousand if the railroad can handle them,&rdquo; replied Neece, promptly. &ldquo;I saw hundreds of empty stock cars as I rode oot. Neece, yu can beat the other cattlemen to it, an&rsquo; save, I reckon, half thet twenty thousand&mdash;if yu rush the ootfit.&rdquo; &ldquo;Save! What yu mean, cowboy?&rdquo; asked Bilyen, darkly suspicious of his young friend. &ldquo;Wal, there&rsquo;s a lull in rustlin&rsquo; just now, but it won&rsquo;t last long onless this ootfit Surface left behind gets ripped up the back. Neece, it&rsquo;d be a good bet to sell all the railroad can handle.&rdquo; &ldquo;I&rsquo;ll grab it pronto, Brazos, thanks to you.&rdquo; &ldquo;How aboot Henderson an&rsquo; Sisk? Can they grab it pronto, too?&rdquo; &ldquo;No. Henderson&rsquo;s stock is scattered all over the range. Sisk&rsquo;s is on his ranch forty miles out. Rough country. It&rsquo;d take a month to round up a big bunch.&rdquo; &ldquo;Too late for the top price&mdash;an&rsquo; too late to beat the rustlers.&rdquo; &ldquo;Hank, I tried to get Sisk an&rsquo; Henderson to see this very thing&mdash;that is, the price of beef goin&rsquo; up. I didn&rsquo;t figure on rustlers.... Brazos, how can they get away with any large numbers of cattle?&rdquo; &ldquo;Wal, it does seem ootlandish, with law an&rsquo; order in Las Animas,&rdquo; rejoined Brazos, with a grim laugh. &ldquo;Boss, ten hard-ridin&rsquo; rustlers can move a heap of stock. None of thet stock would come heah. They&rsquo;d shove it along the line, an&rsquo; have it shipped the day it got to the railroad, long before the owners found oot. Some of it would be throwed in with the shipment of cattlemen we reckon air crooked. Aw, it&rsquo;s easy enough. Too damn easy!&rdquo; &ldquo;I&rsquo;ll go write telegrams to my buyers an&rsquo; order all the stock cars available. Hank, you can ride in with these at once,&rdquo; said Neece, decisively, and hurried away. &ldquo;Come oot with it, darn yore pictoors,&rdquo; demanded Hank, gruffly. &ldquo;Wal, it&rsquo;s nothin new, but kinda worrisome, considerin&rsquo; the mix-up I&rsquo;m in heah,&rdquo; answered Brazos, and he gave Hank the information he had received from the strange cowboy in town. The Texan swore mightily and to no purpose, which verbiage Brazos received in silence. &ldquo;I knowed somethin&rsquo; was in the wind, else yu&rsquo;d never advise Neece to sell stock so pronto. Doggone lucky for Neece! We can ship thet number of cattle before the price drops or a hoof of it can be rustled.... But what&rsquo;n the hell air yu gonna do?&rdquo; &ldquo;Me? Aw, I better lay low.&rdquo; &ldquo;He&rsquo;s just aboot smart enough to figure it,&rdquo; admitted Brazos. &ldquo;Who ever heahed of such a &mdash;&mdash; muss!&rdquo; growled Hank. &ldquo;If this was only Texas!&rdquo; &ldquo;But it&rsquo;s not Texas. It&rsquo;s Colorado, where they got law an&rsquo; order,&rdquo; declared Brazos, bitterly. &ldquo;Where&rsquo;s layin&rsquo; low gonna get yu, cowboy?&rdquo; &ldquo;I don&rsquo;t know. It&rsquo;s all I can think of.&rdquo; &ldquo;They&rsquo;d reckon yu was scared an&rsquo; rustle the hair off this range.&rdquo; &ldquo;Shore. But they&rsquo;d hang themselves sooner or later. Las Animas won&rsquo;t stand it forever.&rdquo; &ldquo;No, I reckon not. All the same they&rsquo;re daid slow.&rdquo; &ldquo;Slow? Say, Texas, they&rsquo;re not alive. The trouble is each cattleman heah suspects his neighbor. Yu know, Hank, thet kind of deal is hard to handle. It just might last until this range has seen its best days.&rdquo; &ldquo;Nope, it won&rsquo;t, &rsquo;cause the haids of this combine, Bodkin an&rsquo; Knight, Miller mebbe, whoever they air, don&rsquo;t stock up enough gray matter.&rdquo; &ldquo;Hank, I just oughta ride away,&rdquo; said Brazos, tragically. &ldquo;Shore. Thet old dodge of yores would fit in heah,&rdquo; rejoined Bilyen, with sarcasm that made Brazos flinch. &ldquo;Only I cain&rsquo;t.&rdquo; &ldquo;An&rsquo; why cain&rsquo;t yu?&rdquo; &ldquo;It&rsquo;s not humanly possible for me to leave this girl.&rdquo; &ldquo;Brazos, I&rsquo;d be damned if I would, either.&rdquo; &ldquo;If only June would run off with me!... We could come back after these hombres peter oot.&rdquo; &ldquo;June? Say, cowboy, we reckoned it was Janis.&rdquo; &ldquo;Why, me, an&rsquo; Neece&mdash;an&rsquo; some of the boys. Leastways Jack.&rdquo; &ldquo;Jack Sain? The two-faced son of a gun! He&rsquo;s crazy aboot June himself. Thet&rsquo;s why he&mdash;&mdash;&rdquo; &ldquo;Jack is honest,&rdquo; interposed Bilyen. &ldquo;An&rsquo; Neece reckons yu&rsquo;re sweetest on Janis.&rdquo; &ldquo;Neece? Aw, my Gawd!&rdquo; ejaculated Brazos, throwing up his hands. &ldquo;Mixed up? I&rsquo;m standin&rsquo; on my haid right this heah minute.... Hank, I love June, honest an&rsquo; true&mdash;but&mdash;aw! it&rsquo;s orful.... I cain&rsquo;t tell her from Jan.&rdquo; The Texan laughed so hard that Brazos wanted to punch him. &ldquo;Haw! Haw! Haw!... doggone&mdash;me,&rdquo; he choked, trying to talk. &ldquo;Yu&rsquo;re not in any mix-up. Oh&mdash;no!... Yu reckon it&rsquo;s June, but yu cain&rsquo;t tell her from Jan!... One&rsquo;s the same as the other.... Thet settles yore hash, Brazos Keene.&rdquo; &ldquo;Hank, I could get mad at yu&mdash;yu &mdash;&mdash; ole muddle-haid,&rdquo; declared Brazos, red in the face. &ldquo;Now what&rsquo;n hell do yu mean by thet last crack? Yu saw me kiss June last night.&rdquo; &ldquo;Shore Brazos, but thet only proved yu&rsquo;re in love with them both.&rdquo; &ldquo;Yu&rsquo;re a damn liar.&rdquo; &ldquo;An&rsquo; yu&rsquo;re plumb lucky,&rdquo; returned Bilyen, curtly. &ldquo;There&rsquo;s safety in numbers. Yu been playin&rsquo; fast an&rsquo; loose with two fine girls. Don&rsquo;t forgit yu&rsquo;re from Texas.&rdquo; &ldquo;Wal, I&rsquo;ll be dod-blasted,&rdquo; muttered Brazos, as his friend strode away, plainly offended. &ldquo;What the devil did he mean by lucky&mdash;an&rsquo; safety in numbers?&rdquo; Brazos sat there trying to puzzle it out, but the more he thought the deeper in he got until he grew hopelessly bewildered. Presently Bilyen reappeared leading his horse. &ldquo;Doggone it, Brazos, I cain&rsquo;t be mad at yu,&rdquo; he complained. &ldquo;Wal, I can be at yu, old-timer, an&rsquo; don&rsquo;t yu overlook thet.&rdquo; &ldquo;Have yu got anythin&rsquo; in town yu want fetched oot?&rdquo; queried Hank, ignoring Brazos&rsquo; rudeness. &ldquo;Yes. Go to Mexican Joe&rsquo;s an&rsquo; pack my ootfit. An&rsquo; don&rsquo;t yu muss thet new gray suit thet I got to wear to the dance.&rdquo; &ldquo;So yu&rsquo;re gonna be there,&rdquo; said Hank, pretending surprise. &ldquo;I should smile I am.... Hey, who yu wavin&rsquo; to?&rdquo; &ldquo;Heah come June an&rsquo; Jan,&rdquo; observed Bilyen. &ldquo;Air they lookin&rsquo; for one Brazos Keene? They air!... Gosh, look at them! Two sweeter, purtier, nicer, finer, gooder girls never before graced this range.... Brazos, I&rsquo;d be only too glad to be in yore boots.&rdquo; &ldquo;Yes, yu would, yu brute!... Where air they? I&rsquo;m gonna slope.&rdquo; &ldquo;They see yu. Take yore medicine, Brazos.&rdquo; &ldquo;Fork thet hawse, yu grinnin&rsquo; ape,&rdquo; yelled Brazos, and then, at the sight of the twins, he sank back with a groan. &ldquo;Aw! Was there ever such a miserable cuss as Brazos Keene?... But this is never gonna do. I gotta make a bluff as big as Pikes Peak, or bust.&rdquo; He assumed a lolling lazy pose, and leisurely began to roll a cigarette, well and tinglingly aware when the girls stopped before him. &ldquo;Mawnin&rsquo;, Brazos,&rdquo; mimicked one. &ldquo;Air yu heah again?&rdquo; mimicked the other. Brazos slid off his sombrero, and sprang erect, his old cool smiling courteous self. &ldquo;Good mawnin&rsquo;, twins,&rdquo; he drawled. &ldquo;I shore am glad to see yu-all this mawnin&rsquo;.&rdquo; They stood hand in hand before Brazos, demure and arch, shy and provocative, dressed exactly alike as always and looking hopelessly more alike than ever, surely the sweetest pair that had ever distracted a poor cowboy. Brazos viewed them with a grim smile, vainly searching for some tiny bit of distinctive apparel on one different from the other&rsquo;s, waiting vainly for a wink or sly glance that would proclaim one to be his sweetheart June. &ldquo;Come, Brazos.&rdquo; &ldquo;You&rsquo;re going to help us.&rdquo; &ldquo;Shore, if yu stay together,&rdquo; said Brazos, doggedly. &ldquo;But I&rsquo;m not gonna ever be alone with one of yu again.&rdquo; Laughing merrily they separated and one at each arm they led him across the grassy plot to the barn. The rest of that day flew as if by magic. Late afternoon found all available ground space at Twin Sombreros Ranch occupied by saddle horses, and vehicles from fine buckboards to muddy-wheeled wagons. Two hours before sunset the girls had run off to dress and Brazos had had all that time to satisfy himself with his appearance. But he just could not arrive at that. All the same he had a sneaking idea that he had never looked better. The new gray coat seemed better buttoned, which was very desirable, as it hid his gun belt and all but the extreme tip of his gun sheath. His feet, in the new low shoes, felt as light as feathers, only Brazos imagined they looked too small for a hard-riding cowboy. Lastly he put a wildflower in his coat, and strode forth to do or die. Brazos ran into Jack Sain, slim, dark-garbed, shining of face. &ldquo;Hello, Brazos. Have you forgotten anything?&rdquo; &ldquo;Gosh, I reckon my haid,&rdquo; replied Brazos, but he clapped his hand to his hip to feel if his gun was there. &ldquo;It&rsquo;s time for us to light the pine cones. Henry an&rsquo; his gang have to take care of the fires. We get off easy.... Gee, what a night, Brazos! Look at the moon, peepin&rsquo; over the mountains.... The orchestra is here. From Denver! Think of that, cowboy. An&rsquo; the dancin&rsquo; begins at eight sharp.&rdquo; &ldquo;Wal, it ought to be aboot thet now,&rdquo; drawled Brazos, affecting a calm he did not feel. They lit the rustic pine-cone lanterns up and down the lane leading from the barn to the ranch house; then the oil lanterns in the colorful barn and the big locomotive lamp that had been fastened high on a post. The huge bonfires added to the cheerful night scene. And the moon soared above the black range, full and white and radiant. Then the crowd of girls, some in white, and most in bright hues that matched the autumn leaves, flocked down the lane with gay voices and merry laughter, to meet the eager young men waiting at the barn. Last came the older folk, less brilliantly garbed, but quite as merry and as happy. Voice and mirth of all were drowned in a burst of rhythmic music. Brazos was surrounded by the glad throng, although none appeared to notice him, and he drew to one side, with a feeling of detachment. He was beginning to lose something of the thrill and uncommon pleasure that possessed him, when a soft little hand slipped inside his. Brazos turned to find a vision in white beside him, with lovely face uplifted to his and dark eyes the dullest of men could have read aright. &ldquo;It&rsquo;s June,&rdquo; she said, simply. &ldquo;How do you like my New York gown?&rdquo; &ldquo;Girl&mdash;I never knew yu were so beautiful,&rdquo; replied Brazos, rapturously. &ldquo;Cowboy, you look stunning yourself. Jan, the old hawk, saw you first. She was making for you when Henry grabbed her.&rdquo; All at once he felt her other hand moving lightly down his coat on the right side, to stop on the hump that was his gun. &ldquo;Brazos, you would pack a gun at my party,&rdquo; she concluded, reproachfully. &ldquo;Darlin&rsquo;, I cain&rsquo;t go withoot it,&rdquo; explained Brazos, poignantly. &ldquo;Only last night I heahed there were men lookin&rsquo; for me.&rdquo; &ldquo;Oh, how terrible to be in love with a desperado!&rdquo; she exclaimed, her warm cheeks blanching. &ldquo;June! Thet hurts.&rdquo; &ldquo;But how terribly more sweet to be in love with a handsome cowboy whose very name makes girls&rsquo; hearts beat fast!&rdquo; she added quickly, voice and look making amends. &ldquo;Come. This first dance is yours. I chose a long waltz, because you told me you liked waltzing.&rdquo; They had whirled scarcely more than a quarter way round the big barn when June looked up to whisper: &ldquo;You said you were a clodhopper on your feet.&rdquo; &ldquo;Wal, I am&mdash;in my boots.&rdquo; &ldquo;Brazos, you can dance... but you hold me a little too tight&mdash;for public&mdash;&mdash;&rdquo; &ldquo;Aw, I don&rsquo;t know whether I&rsquo;m dancin&rsquo; or ridin&rsquo; or sailin&rsquo;.... An&rsquo; June, if I didn&rsquo;t hold yu like this&mdash;I&rsquo;d not know I had yu in my arms&mdash;yu&rsquo;re so like a fairy.&rdquo; &ldquo;Don&rsquo;t talk&mdash;flatterer!&rdquo; They danced on and Brazos thought that he was indeed sailing around an enchanted glade in the autumn woods. Yet he preserved his equilibrium enough to lead her through the whirling throng of dancers. But toward the end the sense of rhythmic dreamy movement, the murmuring voices of girls, the bright dresses, the scarlet flare of autumn leaves, and the entrancing feel of June so soft against his breast&mdash;these went to Brazos&rsquo; head like wine. &ldquo;Brazos&mdash;you&rsquo;re&mdash;hugging&mdash;me,&rdquo; June whispered, pantingly. &ldquo;Am I?... Doggone! I hadn&rsquo;t noticed it,&rdquo; he drawled, loosing his hold ever so little. &ldquo;Everybody&mdash;else&mdash;has.&rdquo; &ldquo;June, if my eyes air not pore, they&rsquo;re all doin&rsquo; the same.&rdquo; &ldquo;Jan saw. She looked&mdash;daggers at me.&rdquo; &ldquo;Wal, don&rsquo;t yu look.... Aw, it&rsquo;s over! How could thet happen?... June, I reckon I never had so wonderful a dance.&rdquo; &ldquo;Wal, thet was wonderful, too. But not like this one with yu. Yu see, Holly wasn&rsquo;t my sweetheart.&rdquo; &ldquo;I don&rsquo;t see. I should have thought she&rsquo;d be mad about you.... Brazos, do you want to please me?&rdquo; &ldquo;More than anyone.&rdquo; &ldquo;Make yourself agreeable to the girls who haven&rsquo;t beaux, and some of the older women. You can be so nice! It&rsquo;s a chance. It&rsquo;ll make them like you&mdash;and help our party to success. Will you, Brazos?&rdquo; &ldquo;Goodness only knows how I can, but I&rsquo;ll manage. But how aboot Janis?&rdquo; &ldquo;Jan will have three partners for every dance. But you must get a part of one, at least. She&rsquo;d blame me if you didn&rsquo;t.&rdquo; &ldquo;I&rsquo;ll bet I cain&rsquo;t get within a mile of her... or yu, either, after this. Look at these buckaroos pilin&rsquo; over each other to get at yu!&rdquo; &ldquo;Brazos, I&rsquo;ll hunt you up,&rdquo; she flashed, and was whirled away. Glowing and eager, with all his doubt fled, Brazos turned to the duty imposed upon him. He gave himself such an impetus that he found real pleasure in dancing with an unattractive girl no longer young. Thus launched, Brazos bent all his efforts to helping make the party of the Neece twins a success. Now and then, when a moment offered, he would stop to gaze among the circle of dancers, who grew gayer as the evening wore on, to pick out the lovely flushed face that flashed like a flower and then was gone. Once out of that same face, or its counterpart, he met dark reproachful seductive eyes, and he drew up with a sharp breath. They must have belonged to Janis. That fleeting glimpse was the first Brazos had had of her. He did not look again after that incident. The hours fled like the dances; the pine cones burned out; the big bonfires were replenished; many of the young men and some of the girls went often to the great bowl of punch that Neece had ordered must be kept full; and the moon climbed high to burn white in the blue dome above. At midnight supper was served for the younger folk on the wide porch, and in the long sitting room of the ranch house for the elders. Brazos ate his food standing, as many thousands of times he had done beside a chuck wagon on the Old Trail. Soon the dancers flocked back to the barn, lured by the strains of music. Brazos watched them from the porch, a little wistfully, wondering when June would hunt him up. Then a white hand slipped under his arm. &ldquo;Come, cowboy,&rdquo; called a challenging voice. &ldquo;Aw, heah yu air!&rdquo; cried Brazos. &ldquo;Quick. They&rsquo;re after me. Run!&rdquo; she said, with a giggle, and led him into the pines instead of down the lane. In a moment they were out of sight of the ranch house, the lights, and in another almost out of hearing of the merry hum. Then they walked hand in hand. Brazos&rsquo; heart seemed full to bursting. There was no need of talk. The pines black-barred the silver glades. The girl stopped to confront Brazos, though she did not let go of his hand. &ldquo;Where have you been all these hours?&rdquo; she asked. The moonlight enhanced her loveliness, blanched her oval face, darkened her unfathomable eyes. Brazos divined that she had given him the opportunity he had longed for. &ldquo;I&rsquo;ve been helpin&rsquo; make yore party a success. Didn&rsquo;t think it was in me! Dancin&rsquo; with old maids, doin&rsquo; the elegant with the wives and mothers, makin&rsquo; a waiter oot of myself. But it was fun, an&rsquo; did me good.&rdquo; &ldquo;Brazos, that was sweet of you,&rdquo; she returned, warmly. &ldquo;Wal, don&rsquo;t yu want to reward me?&rdquo; he drawled, softly. &ldquo;Yes.&rdquo; As she spoke that forceful word Brazos caught a hint of something as strange as lovely about her. In the magic of the moonlight all her charm and mystery appeared magnified. &ldquo;Would a&mdash;a kiss be too much?&rdquo; he asked, hesitatingly. &ldquo;Too little!&rdquo; Brazos kissed her and trembled on the brink of the unknown. She stood there, slender and white in the silvered radiance, eyes intent on him, lips upturned. &ldquo;Girl, don&rsquo;t look at me like thet... you said yes.&rdquo; &ldquo;I also said &lsquo;too little&rsquo;!&rdquo; Brazos&rsquo; restraint broke at that, and he took her in his arms, but did not avail himself of the repetition of that challenging surrender. Still she held back from closer contact with him, somewhat stiffly. &ldquo;Tell me yu&rsquo;ll marry me?&rdquo; he demanded, suddenly strong and vibrant with released emotion. &ldquo;Ah-h!&rdquo; she gasped, and as if the strength had left her limbs she sank upon his breast. Brazos held her closer and closer, bending his head over hers, to put his cheek upon her fragrant hair. &ldquo;Did thet surprise yu, darlin?&rdquo; She stirred her head in soft motion Brazos took to be affirmation. &ldquo;But it shouldn&rsquo;t have?&rdquo; &ldquo;Who could be sure of&mdash;Brazos Keene?&rdquo; she whispered. &ldquo;Wal, yu should be.... I love yu turrible, lass.... Say yu love me.&rdquo; &ldquo;I adore&mdash;you!&rdquo; &ldquo;Say when yu&rsquo;ll marry me?&rdquo; &ldquo;Oh, what will Dad and sister say?&rdquo; &ldquo;They&rsquo;ll be glad. But never mind them. Honey, I cain&rsquo;t wait much longer. Yu blessed twins air drivin&rsquo; me crazy.... Say when, precious?&rdquo; &ldquo;When do you&mdash;want me?&rdquo; she whispered, very low. &ldquo;Aw!... Why, I hate to rush yu, darlin&rsquo;. But there&rsquo;s a reason, yu know. I&rsquo;m a marked man in Las Animas. I oughta go away &rsquo;till those hombres forget they wanted to kill me.&rdquo; &ldquo;I told yu, darlin&rsquo;,&rdquo; he expostulated. &ldquo;Oh, my Brazos! I&mdash;I will marry you.&rdquo; &ldquo;When? The sooner the better.&rdquo; &ldquo;We&rsquo;ll elope!&rdquo; she cried, thrillingly. &ldquo;Wal, thet&rsquo;d be easiest an&rsquo; safest for me. Yore Dad wouldn&rsquo;t hold it against me.&rdquo; &ldquo;We&rsquo;ll do it!&rdquo; Suddenly she appeared transformed into a little whirlwind, throwing her arms around his neck, rumpling his hair with furious little hands, at last to draw his head down to kiss him with lips of sweet fire. &ldquo;Oh, Brazos! I&rsquo;ve been dying for you,&rdquo; she burst out with inarticulate cry. &ldquo;You won me&mdash;even though I thought you a devil with girls... a trifler!... All the time&mdash;all the time I thought it was June you loved!&rdquo; &ldquo;Don&rsquo;t kill&mdash;me,&rdquo; Janis managed to utter, faintly. &ldquo;Aw&mdash;I&rsquo;m sorry. I&mdash;I just went off my haid,&rdquo; replied Brazos, in a smothered voice, as he released his clasp. But Janis did not let go her clinging hold of him nor take her head from his breast. &ldquo;Oh-h!... Brazos, a girl in love&mdash;even as terribly as&mdash;I am&mdash;has to breathe.&rdquo; &ldquo;Wal, I didn&rsquo;t know yu loved me&mdash;turrible.&rdquo; &ldquo;My Gawd, how I know it!&rdquo; exclaimed Brazos, huskily, horror-stricken with a sense of his guilt and the exultant madness that enveloped him. He bent his head over her and again enfolded her slender form, while he gazed unseeingly out into the silver-black shadows of the woods. The white pitiless moon looked down upon him like an accusing eye. The night breeze moaned pitifully in the tips of the pines. Only a soft strain of music in the distance gave that strange solitude reality. &ldquo;Darling, this is perfect,&rdquo; said Janis, stirring, and trying to look up at him. &ldquo;It pays me for my anguish. It sustains me&mdash;until the next time. But we mustn&rsquo;t stay longer.&rdquo; &ldquo;No,&rdquo; agreed Brazos, and stood like a stone.
