Brazos wrenched his gaze from the shadows to look down upon her, fully conscious now that he was as weak as guilty, that he loved her the same as June, that she had a devastating power he had never felt in the shy sister. And suddenly she manifested it again, flaring up like a flame, to cling with round arms as strong as steel, to tear her fingers through his hair, to lock them behind his head and draw him down to those lips of fire that he would have faced doom to meet. Brazos surrendered to a moment of transport. Whatever came of this mad folly, he would have that to dream of. Janis was intensely alive. He could feel the bursting swell of her breast, the throb of her heart, the burn of her blood through the skin of her bare arms. He could hear her broken utterances of endearment, low, deep, with a strange rich hoarseness. And he spent all the passion of his lonely unsatisfied heart&mdash;the endless hours of longing by day and night in the saddle&mdash;the bitter fatality that a fulfillment of love was not for him&mdash;in his response to her kisses. Then it seemed somehow that this ecstasy waved away and she was smoothing his hair. &ldquo;I was always crazy to muss your hair like that,&rdquo; she was murmuring, her eyes like dark stars. &ldquo;Jan&mdash;I&rsquo;ve kinda&mdash;mussed yu&mdash;too,&rdquo; he replied, hoarsely. All down the lane she held his hand, while he strode fast to keep up with her tripping steps. Even the blazing bonfire did not deter her. Twice she turned to look at him with wonderful eyes, the secret of which was for him alone. The music grew louder. Brazos saw as in a dream the moving figures, pale, unreal, like disembodied spirits. Then he heard Janis&rsquo; sweet mocking little laugh: &ldquo;Henry! how did you ever miss me?&rdquo; &ldquo;I did. But this cowboy didn&rsquo;t,&rdquo; growled Henry, at which Brazos awoke to life and reality. &ldquo;Wal, Sisk, what one man misses, another hits,&rdquo; drawled Brazos, caustic in his sudden awakening. &ldquo;Never mind, Henry. We will dance this.... Adios, Brazos, until&mdash;&mdash;&rdquo; And Brazos stood watching in a light that had lost its dim unreality&mdash;watching the girl as she glided away with Henry, her lovely face turned, piquant and moon-blanched, lit by great dark alluring eyes. The crowd swallowed them up. And Brazos plodded away, his head bowed, like a man lost on an endless shingle of shore, in the weird pale night, aimless and hopeless. Corrals, fences, gates, gardens, orchards, ditches and woods and rocks were as if they were not. Brazos&rsquo; new gray suit, of which he had been so proud, became a tattered muddy garment. He came out into the road and heedless of direction he walked on. Miles west on the hill he saw the cabin where he had slept that fatal night Allen Neece was murdered. He had no thought for himself now. He would have been an easy prey for novices at the game of killing. But he turned at the hilltop and plodded back. The moon slanted low and its dimming light showed the road winding pale between black borders of trees and brush. Coyotes began their hue and cry. In open places Brazos saw faint streaks of gray over the eastern range. Dawn was not far away. Brazos walked on slower and slower. His feet became leaden. This was not his accustomed mode of locomotion. Sunrise found him skulking through the woods and fields, up the corral lane, to the little cabin he shared with Bilyen. Brazos heard the music end; he saw the thinned crowd of dancers file in couples out of the barn; he saw June and Janis, dragging homeward on the arms of their escorts. And bitterly he recognized them to be Janis with Henry Sisk and June with Jack Sain. Sick with anguish, numb with cold, staggering with exhaustion Brazos turned from that sight into the cabin, to divest himself of his ruined clothes and crawl into his blankets. Bilyen snored like a man seldom used to keeping late hours. But that did not keep Brazos awake. He did not hear it. He was trying to free his brain from unbearable thoughts. What was it that had happened? June and Janis Neece! They were twin sisters, nineteen years old. It had been the ambition of a proud and loving father to send them east to give them an education. They had come back unspoiled, still Western, beautiful as the dreams of cowboys beside lonely prairie campfires. They were amber-eyed, and no cowboy could look into those eyes and tell the twins apart, or ever have any peace of mind again. They were perfectly, absolutely, damnably alike. Their shapely forms, their pearly skin, their brown hair, their voices, looks, smiles, mannerisms, all were enhanced irresistibly by this marvelous likeness. They had an infernal habit, or coquetry, or some instinct of self-preservation, to dress precisely the same. But to the cowboy who had been blessed or cursed by fate to be loved by both of them, this similarity so unsupportable to him did not extend to character. They seemed as far apart as the poles. One was the south and the other the north. June was cool, sweet, shy, reserved, deep as the sea, adoring and unselfish, strangely and passionately devoted to her sister. Janis was fire hidden under a cold provocative exterior, selfish in her need of conquest, vain where her twin was modest, conscious of her alluring charm, a little devil in her chary practice with it, maddeningly irresistible and royal in her final abandonment to love. Brazos knew them both now&mdash;knew them to his doom. He worshiped them both. He could not have made a choice. June called to all the courage, nobility&mdash;to the unplumbed depths of his power to help, to serve, to protect, to the cowboy&rsquo;s dream of range and ranch, home and happiness, to tranquillity and peace after his hard life on the trails. And he would not have had that otherwise. Janis enveloped him in a flame, as he had seen prairie fire race through the windy grass. She had brought out in him a side he had not known he possessed, a tremendously strong primitive self, impossible to resist, a dark demanding master, up from the savage, engendering a yearning and a need stronger than habit, more compelling than any force he had ever experienced. At last Brazos saw the situation in all its stark naked reality, and he voiced it to himself, as was his habit. &ldquo;Wal, Brazos Keene, heah&rsquo;s where yu get off the woman trail for good. Yu love this June Neece. She&rsquo;s yore ideal&mdash;the girl of the long trail&rsquo;s dreams. An&rsquo; yu&rsquo;re engaged to marry her.... Yu love her sister, too, this bewilderin&rsquo; Janis&mdash;an&rsquo; yu wouldn&rsquo;t stop lovin&rsquo; her if yu could. An&rsquo; yu told her you&rsquo;d elope with her.... Yu love them both.... Yu wouldn&rsquo;t have it different.... Yu cain&rsquo;t tell them apart.... Wal, it&rsquo;s aboot time to go oot an&rsquo; get yoreself shot!&rdquo; * * * * * A voice pierced dimly into Brazos&rsquo; sleep, stirring old associations so intimately related with dead slumbers and early calls. &ldquo;My guard? Awl-l right.... A cowboy&rsquo;s life is ha-ard.&rdquo; &ldquo;Wake up, Brazos. If I don&rsquo;t miss my guess, yours this evenin&rsquo; will be harder than hell.&rdquo; &ldquo;Huh?&rdquo; &ldquo;It&rsquo;s four o&rsquo;clock an&rsquo; you&rsquo;re wanted,&rdquo; said the curt voice. Brazos rolled over and opened his eyes to see Jack Sain standing beside his bunk, a quite different person from the usual cheerful cowboy. &ldquo;Who wants me?&rdquo; &ldquo;June an&rsquo; Jan. They sent me. They&rsquo;re waitin&rsquo; for you where the trail turns off the lane into the woods.&rdquo; &ldquo;Ahuh.... An&rsquo; yu have a hunch my life is gonna be harder&rsquo;n hell pronto,&rdquo; drawled Brazos, sliding his long legs out of bed. &ldquo;I&rsquo;ll bet you get the spur-rakin&rsquo; of your ridin&rsquo; days.&rdquo; &ldquo;Boy, yu shore look like life was kinda hard for yu this mawnin&rsquo;.... I mean this evenin&rsquo; after the dance.&rdquo; &ldquo;I&rsquo;d just as lief be dead,&rdquo; returned Sain, hopelessly. Then Brazos took a second look at him, and felt remorse gnaw at his own heart. &ldquo;What&rsquo;s yore trouble, cowboy?&rdquo; asked Brazos, kindly. &ldquo;You know. It&rsquo;s the same as yours.&rdquo; &ldquo;Ump-umm. Don&rsquo;t yu get thet idee. Mine is double yores.... All the same I can help yu.&rdquo; &ldquo;Thanks, Brazos... I just can&rsquo;t help likin&rsquo; you&mdash;though you&rsquo;ve ruined my life.&rdquo; &ldquo;Turrible extravagant talk, Jack.... Yu mean June hasn&rsquo;t been so&mdash;so nice to yu since I rode along?&rdquo; &ldquo;Brazos, she al-almost loved me before you came,&rdquo; replied Sain, miserably. &ldquo;Since then she&rsquo;s been&mdash;Oh, hell! nice an&rsquo; kind, yes, but different. It just hurts, Brazos. I&rsquo;m not sore at you. I think you&rsquo;re the grandest fellow I ever knew. An&rsquo; even if you wasn&rsquo;t I&rsquo;d have to feel square toward you because of what you&rsquo;ve done for June&mdash;an&rsquo; all the Neeces. It&rsquo;s only&mdash;&mdash;&rdquo; &ldquo;Only what, Jack?&rdquo; &ldquo;I&rsquo;m afraid to tell you, Brazos, but&mdash;but they all say it. An&rsquo; you&rsquo;re bound to hear it.&rdquo; &ldquo;Go ahaid. My gun is way back under my pillow, so I cain&rsquo;t bore yu.&rdquo; &ldquo;Brazos, they say you&rsquo;re playin&rsquo; hell with the twins,&rdquo; replied Sain, huskily&mdash;&ldquo;that you&rsquo;re payin&rsquo; them up for their fun&mdash;their lettin&rsquo; us all take one for the other.&rdquo; &ldquo;Wal, who says thet?&rdquo; &ldquo;Jack, I kinda had thet hunch myself,&rdquo; replied Brazos, pulling on his boots. His mind seemed to scintillate with the sparks of an inspiration Sain had given him. He stood up, reached for his gun belt and buckled it on. Then he stepped to the little mirror, and had a look at his face. &ldquo;My Gawd! what a mug!... Did yu ever see a pictoor of thet gazabo Lucifer?... Wal, I shore look like him this mawnin&rsquo;.&rdquo; &ldquo;It&rsquo;s evenin&rsquo;, Brazos. Everybody has been up since noon. Bilyen rode to town, worried about somethin&rsquo;&mdash;an&rsquo; the girls are waitin&rsquo; for you.&rdquo; &ldquo;Let &rsquo;em wait,&rdquo; said Brazos, and he turned piercing eyes upon his friend. &ldquo;Jack, yu&rsquo;re a good boy. I like yu heaps. An&rsquo; I&rsquo;m damn sorry I upset yore courtin&rsquo;. But thet was only an incident in yore romance. Let me give yu a hunch, boy. Don&rsquo;t be sick an&rsquo; jealous an&rsquo; black. Be yore real self to June. Thet girl is gonna rebound into yore arms like a rubber ball off a dobe wall.&rdquo; &ldquo;Oh, Brazos. Don&rsquo;t lie&mdash;don&rsquo;t rave just to cheer me up.&rdquo; &ldquo;Keep this under yore sombrero, cowboy. I did give the girls a dose of their own medicine. I shore played a low-down trick on them. Why, Jack, it was apple pie for me to tell them&mdash;one from the other. An&rsquo; I let on I couldn&rsquo;t.... Wal, heah&rsquo;s what no one else but yu will ever know&mdash;except Neece, an&rsquo; I give yu leave to tell him.... I got burned turrible bad in thet little game of makin&rsquo; love.&rdquo; &ldquo;Boy, yu hit it plumb on the haid.&rdquo; &ldquo;Oh, Lord!... But, Brazos, damn it, I&rsquo;m not glad. I couldn&rsquo;t stand your&mdash;that you didn&rsquo;t really care!&rdquo; &ldquo;Gosh, Jack, yu&rsquo;re a heartless hombre,&rdquo; drawled Brazos. &ldquo;Wal, I&rsquo;ll trot along to my little rendezvoo.&rdquo; But Brazos knew in his heart, with grim anguish, that no man ever presented such a false exterior. His mind was set on one thing&mdash;to look and act and talk the character the cowboys at Twin Sombreros had given him. To make June and Janis hate him! He caught sight of them before they saw him, and then his thoughts raced, and his feelings kept pace. They were waiting in a grove of pines off the lane. He flipped his cigarette away and leisurely took off his sombrero. &ldquo;Mawnin&rsquo;, girls&mdash;aw, I mean good evenin&rsquo;,&rdquo; he drawled, as true to his frank careless winning way as ever in his life. &ldquo;I shore am glad to see yu so&mdash;so fresh an&rsquo; pretty after thet all-night dance.&rdquo; But his conscience smote him as with a terrific mace. Incredible as it seemed, he recognized instantly which girl was June and which was Janis. &ldquo;Brazos, Jan&mdash;we have something serious to ask you,&rdquo; said June, sad searching eyes on his. She was pale, composed, surprisingly strong. Brazos divined he was to learn the depth of her. Janis was white as snow and her eyes were great black baleful orbs of fire. She had no reserve. She was ready to burst into flame. &ldquo;Brazos,&rdquo; she whispered, hoarsely. &ldquo;I&mdash;I told June.&rdquo; &ldquo;Ahuh. I reckon yu girls been comparin&rsquo; notes... what&rsquo;d yu tell her, Jan?&rdquo; &ldquo;About last&mdash;night... that you begged me&mdash;to elope with you... and I promised.&rdquo; &ldquo;Wal, June, what&rsquo;d yu say to thet?&rdquo; &ldquo;Brazos! Oh, it&rsquo;s true&mdash;then.... I told Jan that I was in love with you&mdash;and engaged to marry you.&rdquo; &ldquo;What happened then?&rdquo; &ldquo;We had a terrible quarrel.&rdquo; &ldquo;Brazos Keene, is she telling the truth?&rdquo; flashed Janis, furiously. &ldquo;Shore she is,&rdquo; drawled Brazos. &ldquo;I&rsquo;m enjoyin&rsquo; the honor of bein&rsquo; engaged to June an&rsquo; plannin&rsquo; to elope with yu.&rdquo; &ldquo;Jan, did it ever occur to yu thet yu&rsquo;ve broken some hearts yoreself?&rdquo; &ldquo;Don&rsquo;t add insult to injury,&rdquo; she cried, passionately. &ldquo;Wal, I figured thet yu an&rsquo; June needed a lesson&mdash;a dose of yore own medicine,&rdquo; said Brazos slowly. But he felt June&rsquo;s eyes upon him and inwardly he began to weaken in this preposterous deceit. &ldquo;This game of yores&mdash;bein&rsquo; one girl when yu air really two girls&mdash;thet&rsquo;s shore not fair to us boys. We never could tell yu apart. An&rsquo; yu built yore house of mirth on thet. Yu were havin&rsquo; fun at our expense. Yu dressed exactly alike an&rsquo; talked an&rsquo; looked an&rsquo; acted thet way. Yu played tricks on us.... I reckon thet would have been all right when yu were kids&mdash;but yu&rsquo;re grown girls now&mdash;women in face an&rsquo; form an&rsquo; feelin&rsquo;, an&rsquo; most distractin&rsquo; lovely. An&rsquo; thet makes yore trick pretty damn low-down, in my way of thinkin&rsquo;.... Every cowboy on this range, an&rsquo; I&rsquo;ll gamble a lot of older men, air lovesick over yu two.... So little Brazos rode along an&rsquo; thought he&rsquo;d break up yore game.&rdquo; &ldquo;If you hadn&rsquo;t saved Dad&mdash;made him happy again&mdash;I&rsquo;d kill you!&rdquo; burst out Janis, in helpless rage. &ldquo;Jan, you see,&rdquo; interposed June, gravely, her hands going out to her sister. &ldquo;I always told you it would get us into trouble.&rdquo; &ldquo;It has&mdash;ruined me,&rdquo; sobbed Janis, covering her face. &ldquo;June&mdash;I&rsquo;m sorry. But it was such fun&mdash;until this devil came.... He never played any game&mdash;for fun.... He was deadly earnest&mdash;and he m-made me l-love him so&mdash;horribly.... I know now&mdash;maybe he served me right.... But that doesn&rsquo;t help&mdash;this&mdash;this&mdash;&mdash;&rdquo; She left off and suddenly uncovered her convulsed face, to fasten a gaze on Brazos that appeared to blaze through tears. &ldquo;You carried your poor joke too far.... You&rsquo;re a heartless villain&mdash;a shameless trickster. You disgrace the very name of cowboy.&rdquo; Brazos winced under that last jibe, the justice of which he recognized; and he was fighting to keep up his shallow pretense when June confronted him with soul-searching eyes. She came close. She laid a steady hold on him, looking up with the clearest, the most solemn eyes he had ever met. Their expression changed on the instant. In their amber depths came a shining little glint of woman&rsquo;s divination. &ldquo;Brazos Keene, you lie!&rdquo; &ldquo;Aw&mdash;June!&rdquo; &ldquo;You&rsquo;re lying. You&rsquo;re trying to save us&mdash;to make us despise you. But you can&rsquo;t do it.&rdquo; Brazos sat down on a log as if his legs had weakened as had his will. His cigarette fell from shaking fingers&mdash;his sombrero rolled on the grass. And he dropped his head, unable longer to stand June&rsquo;s look of love and pity. His very life seemed to fall a wreck about him. &ldquo;Shore. I&rsquo;m&mdash;a liar... an&rsquo; a miserable hombre.&rdquo; &ldquo;You loved me&mdash;thinking I was June?&rdquo; she asked, her voice breaking. &ldquo;I reckon I did.&rdquo; &ldquo;But you love me, too?&rdquo; &ldquo;Yes, I love yu, Jan.&rdquo; &ldquo;Just as much as you do June?&rdquo; &ldquo;I cain&rsquo;t tell my love apart any more than I can yu girls.&rdquo; &ldquo;But Brazos,&rdquo; cried Janis, frantically. &ldquo;We can&rsquo;t be absolutely the same to you.&rdquo; &ldquo;Yes, yu air. Only June makes me happy, quiet, shore of myself&mdash;an&rsquo; yu drive me wild with yore kisses.... Jan, I&rsquo;d go to hell for one of those kisses.&rdquo; &ldquo;Jan, you need not give Brazos up,&rdquo; said June, her voice strong and sweet. &ldquo;You shall marry him.&rdquo; Brazos heard aright and he sprang up, almost lifting Janis with him. &ldquo;What&rsquo;s thet?&rdquo; he demanded, roughly. &ldquo;Jan shall have you, Brazos.&rdquo; He stared at her, only conscious through sight of her drawn face and wonderful eyes that for the first time he was realizing the true June Neece. &ldquo;I cain&rsquo;t consent to thet.&rdquo; &ldquo;Nor I, June,&rdquo; added Janis. &ldquo;It wouldn&rsquo;t be fair. To cheat you of everything? No, no! All my life I have let you put me first. I won&rsquo;t do it here.... But I&rsquo;m not big enough to give him to you.... We must be brokenhearted together.&rdquo; &ldquo;Janis, neither of us needs be brokenhearted. He shall marry you and we&rsquo;ll all be happy.&rdquo; &ldquo;But&mdash;but&mdash;&rdquo; faltered Janis. &ldquo;What yu got in yore haid, girl?&rdquo; interposed Brazos, sternly, and letting go of Janis he squared toward June, studying her pale face with narrow piercing eyes. She was proof against his scrutiny. She was the strongest of the three. &ldquo;Brazos, I&rsquo;d give my very life to make Jan happy.&rdquo; &ldquo;Shore. But it cain&rsquo;t be done.&rdquo; &ldquo;Jan shall be your wife, Brazos... and you can have me, too.&rdquo; Janis leaped to her. &ldquo;June!... I&mdash;he&mdash;Oh, if it could only be!&rdquo; &ldquo;It can, sister.&rdquo; Brazos seized her shoulders in rough grasp. He felt the blood rush back to his heart leaving his skin tight and cold. &ldquo;What air&mdash;yu sayin&rsquo;?&rdquo; he demanded, huskily. &ldquo;My Gawd!&rdquo; gasped Brazos and he fell back overcome. June&rsquo;s solution to their problem was as dazzling and blinding and shocking as a thunderbolt. It caught him unprepared. He had no time to think, to weigh, to reason before he was caught in a tumultuous wave. The blood that had crowded back to his heart, numbing his senses, suddenly leaped like wildfire through his veins. Burning, dizzy, he saw the twins through dim reddened sight. &ldquo;Aw, June! for Gawd&rsquo;s sake! don&rsquo;t say thet!&rdquo; &ldquo;I do. I mean it.&rdquo; &ldquo;But girl!... listen. I&rsquo;m only human. I love yu turrible.... I love Jan wuss. An&rsquo; I cain&rsquo;t stand up&mdash;like a man&mdash;against such temptation as thet.... I beg yu&mdash;June&mdash;&mdash;&rdquo; &ldquo;No! I will be yours&mdash;too.&rdquo; Shaken, spent, Brazos faced them in eloquent importunity, holding out his hands, mute with the appalling thing he divined. &ldquo;Oh, Brazos&mdash;how can I save you&mdash;when I&rsquo;m lost myself?&rdquo; cried Janis, rapt with ecstasy, scarlet with guilt. &ldquo;If June can be happy so&mdash;Oh, Brazos!...&rdquo; Brazos bowed to that, utterly beaten. A man might contend with one woman&mdash;fight to save her from the havoc of love&mdash;but two women&mdash;twin girls, young, beautiful, physically perfect and equally fascinating, one of them deep, the other shallow&mdash;one of them sweet and noble, the other sweet and bad&mdash;how could he ever beat down the insupportable fact of being loved and desired by both? It was too much for Brazos Keene. He had lived unloved and lonely for this miracle. He bowed to what he could not break. And even the arraignment of self and fate could not detract from his transport. &ldquo;Wal, girls, yu&rsquo;ve knocked the pins oot from under me,&rdquo; he said finally, after he had recovered his sombrero and stood brushing it when it did not need brushing. Then at length he stood erect with steady eyes on them. They stood each with an arm around the other, and though one face was opal white and the other glowing crimson he again had the sense of confusion as to their identity. &ldquo;Yu wonderful girls! I&rsquo;ve shore had glorious fortune thrust upon me.... I&rsquo;ll slope along now an&rsquo; see if I cain&rsquo;t find the Brazos Keene who used to be.&rdquo; &ldquo;What&rsquo;s thet, Pedro?&rdquo; queried Brazos, blankly. He had ridden all the way from Twin Sombreros in a chimera in which, if a still small voice of conscience made itself heard, a clamor of passionate exultance howled it down. &ldquo;Thar ees beeg shoot in the town thees day.&rdquo; &ldquo;Ahuh. Yu see it, Pedro?&rdquo; &ldquo;No, señor. I hear the gun&mdash;the yell. Then thees men she run.&rdquo; &ldquo;Who got bored?&rdquo; &ldquo;I don&rsquo;t hear, señor.&rdquo; &ldquo;Take my hawse, Pedro.&rdquo; Brazos wended his way uptown in a gathering thoughtful mood. Shooting frays were not uncommon. To be sure, Las Animas was not Dodge or Lincoln, where seldom a day passed without a smell of brimstone, and never a week without some man biting the dust with his boots on. Nevertheless this news of Pedro&rsquo;s stuck in Brazos&rsquo; consciousness. He argued that it was because, for very long, it seemed he had forgotten the vital relation of guns to Brazos Keene. But before he turned into the main street he had a regurgitation of keen perception. After midday the street appeared mostly deserted and the warm autumn air drowsily hung upon the stores and saloons. &ldquo;Wonder who?&rdquo; muttered Brazos, and somehow the query concerned itself more with who had done the shooting than with the person who had been shot. A little somber cast came over him. Mexican Joe&rsquo;s would be the place to find out. But he had not gone halfway down the block when he encountered Inskip emerging from a store. The instant Brazos met Inskip&rsquo;s gray eyes, he understood the strange creeping shadow that had come between the vividness of his emotion and his thought. Inskip wore the look of hard Texas brotherhood. &ldquo;Howdy, Brazos. Talk aboot the devil an&rsquo; he pops up.&rdquo; &ldquo;Howdy, Inskip. Yu been talkin&rsquo; aboot me?&rdquo; &ldquo;No. But I jest heahed a man gamble yu wouldn&rsquo;t be long comin&rsquo;.&rdquo; &ldquo;Wal, I been pretty long at thet,&rdquo; said Brazos, ponderingly. &ldquo;Yu&rsquo;re lookin&rsquo; for Knight?&rdquo; &ldquo;No. Not particular just this minute.&rdquo; &ldquo;Then yu haven&rsquo;t heahed?&rdquo; &ldquo;Inskip, fact is I haven&rsquo;t heahed nothin&rsquo; but thet waltzy music oot at Twin Sombreros.&rdquo; &ldquo;Knight shot Hank Bilyen this mawnin&rsquo;.&rdquo; &ldquo;Aw!&rdquo; A rending pang in Brazos yielded to leaping fire. &ldquo;Hank!... Daid?&rdquo; &ldquo;No. Pretty close call, though. Doc says Hank ain&rsquo;t in danger.&rdquo; &ldquo;Wal, thet&rsquo;s a relief. Shore was sweatin&rsquo; cold.... This hombre Knight? He shot Surface, yu recollect.... What was it all aboot?&rdquo; &ldquo;Hank ain&rsquo;t tellin&rsquo;. But Knight has been roarin&rsquo; aboot town. He was drunk when he did the shootin&rsquo;, so I heahed.&rdquo; &ldquo;Drunk! What&rsquo;n hell was Hank doin&rsquo; all the time?&rdquo; &ldquo;He wasn&rsquo;t packin&rsquo; no gun.&rdquo; &ldquo;Ahuh. I&rsquo;ll shore cuss him.... An&rsquo; what&rsquo;s this gun-slinger Knight roarin&rsquo; aboot?&rdquo; &ldquo;Wal, it sobered him, I reckon. But he&rsquo;s mad or pretendin&rsquo; to be. Tellin&rsquo; everywhere he thought Bilyen had a gun an&rsquo; was drawin&rsquo; it... thet he told Bilyen he was goin&rsquo; to hold Neece for cattle Surface owed him... thet Bilyen began to curse an&rsquo; threaten.&rdquo; &ldquo;Ahuh. An&rsquo; what&rsquo;s the talk aboot town?&rdquo; &ldquo;Wal, there&rsquo;s not much talkin&rsquo;, but a lot of lookin&rsquo;.... What I heahed, though, was daid agin Knight. He&rsquo;s not liked by the substantial residents of Las Animas, thet&rsquo;s shore.&rdquo; &ldquo;Any talk connectin&rsquo; Knight with Bodkin?&rdquo; &ldquo;Not thet I heahed. But they&rsquo;re thick as hops, Brazos. Take it from me.&rdquo; &ldquo;Bodkin is the nigger in the woodpile. Inskip, how yu reckon thet nigger has lasted so long with Texans?&rdquo; &ldquo;Meanin&rsquo; me an&rsquo; yu an&rsquo; Kiskadden?... Wal, Gawd only knows how he&rsquo;s lasted with yu. But Kis an&rsquo; I have responsibilities&mdash;business an&rsquo; family. Then Bodkin had a strong followin&rsquo;, for a while, long enough to elect him. Sooner or later everybody heah in Las Animas will know he&rsquo;s crookeder than a rail fence, same as we know now.&rdquo; &ldquo;Where is Hank?&rdquo; queried Brazos, his brow knit heavily. &ldquo;At Gage&rsquo;s. I&rsquo;ll go with yu.&rdquo; &ldquo;Reckon yu&rsquo;d better locate Knight for me.&rdquo; &ldquo;No need. He&rsquo;s been walkin&rsquo; the streets. In stores an&rsquo; oot. Shy of saloons. All yu got to do is wait somewhere &rsquo;till he comes along.&rdquo; Brazos found his friend Bilyen lying on an improvised bed of blankets on the floor of a room back of Gage&rsquo;s store. The Texan&rsquo;s rugged visage lacked color and was clammy. &ldquo;Would yu men mind leavin&rsquo; us alone with Bilyen?&rdquo; queried Brazos, to the group present. They left and Brazos knelt by the prostrate man. &ldquo;Wal, old-timer, how yu makin&rsquo; oot?&rdquo; he drawled, with deep feeling. &ldquo;Howdy, Brazos. I been wonderin&rsquo; when yu&rsquo;d roll in.... Me? What&rsquo;s a gunshot to a Texan?... I&rsquo;m all right. I ducked when he shot, or he&rsquo;d killed me shore. If ever I seen red murder in a man&rsquo;s eyes it was Knight&rsquo;s. An&rsquo; I dropped like I was bored plumb center.&rdquo; &ldquo;Ahuh. Yu&rsquo;re pretty smart when yore hawse has got oot of the barn.... Let me see.... Right side.... Hank, don&rsquo;t tell me it&rsquo;s low down.&rdquo; &ldquo;Right under my collarbone an&rsquo; clear through. Sorer than a stubbed toe!... But it&rsquo;s nothin&rsquo; atall, Brazos. I wouldn&rsquo;t lie to yu, boy.&rdquo; &ldquo;Spit any blood?&rdquo; &ldquo;Nary a drop. Thet forty-five slug just missed my lung. Doc Williamson says I can be moved tomorrow.&rdquo; &ldquo;Gosh, thet&rsquo;s fine, Hank. I was kinda worried.... How come yu wasn&rsquo;t packin&rsquo; yore gun?&rdquo; &ldquo;June an&rsquo; Jan&mdash;they been coaxin&rsquo; me to lay it off,&rdquo; replied Hank, visibly confused. &ldquo;Hank Bilyen, yu listened to those little softies?&rdquo; &ldquo;Hell yes! What&rsquo;re you gonna do?&rdquo; &ldquo;Wal, yu&rsquo;re gonna listen to some plain talk. Yu &mdash;&mdash;! &mdash;&mdash;!... &mdash;&mdash;!!&rdquo; &ldquo;Thet&rsquo;ll do, pard,&rdquo; ejaculated Hank, no longer pallid. &ldquo;An&rsquo; I&rsquo;m askin&rsquo; yu&mdash;couldn&rsquo;t them twins wind yu around their little fingers?&rdquo; &ldquo;Not atall, old-timer,&rdquo; replied Brazos, imperturbably. &ldquo;Humph! Yu&rsquo;re such a liar, Brazos.... I had it figgered&mdash;an&rsquo; so did Neece&mdash;thet yu was the plumbedest, locoedest, lovesickest damn fool there ever was over June an&rsquo; Jan.&rdquo; &ldquo;Yu don&rsquo;t say? Funny aboot how men think!... Hank, I&rsquo;m doggone glad yu&rsquo;re not in a bad way.... Now let&rsquo;s get down to business, onless yu reckon yu better leave off talkin&rsquo; for a little an&rsquo; rest.&rdquo; &ldquo;Cowboy, I shore won&rsquo;t rest or sleep till yu shoot the gizzards oot of thet black buzzard.&rdquo; &ldquo;Good. If yu talk short an&rsquo; sweet I reckon yu can have a sleep in less&rsquo;n two wags of a lamb&rsquo;s tail... what was it aboot?&rdquo; &ldquo;Nothin&rsquo; I said or did. Knight braced me. Said he was demandin&rsquo; two thousand haid of yearlin&rsquo;s. From Neece through me I gave him the laugh till I seen thet red light come to his eye. Then if I&rsquo;d only had a gun!&rdquo; &ldquo;Did yu say anythin&rsquo;?&rdquo; &ldquo;I cussed him right pert.&rdquo; &ldquo;Anythin&rsquo; more than cussin&rsquo;?&rdquo; &ldquo;Wal, I was riled. An&rsquo; before I seen he meant murder I told him to lay off Neece or he&rsquo;d have yu to deal with. At thet he gave me the hawse laugh. Said he an&rsquo; Bodkin (he&rsquo;s not smart atall, Brazos. He gave Bodkin away) knowed yore hands was tied. Thet yore gunnin&rsquo; for sheriffs was over!&rdquo; &ldquo;An&rsquo; what did yu say to thet?&rdquo; &ldquo;I told him we knowed he an&rsquo; Bodkin was in cahoots&mdash;thet yu knowed he was the rustler Brad yu heahed with Bodkin thet night at Hailey&rsquo;s. Brazos, it was a random shot, but it shore went home. I jest guessed it... an&rsquo; thet crack of mine almost settled my hash. For murder leaped to Knight&rsquo;s eyes. I seen it an&rsquo; shet up pronto. But I was too late. When he drew I ducked.&rdquo; &ldquo;So... I&rsquo;ll shore know if he&rsquo;s thet Bard the instant I heah his voice. Not thet it matters. But it sort of dovetails in.... An&rsquo; thet&rsquo;s what I got on Bodkin.&rdquo; &ldquo;Brazos, this man Brad must have ruled Surface an&rsquo; Bodkin both. He struck me strong, cunnin&rsquo;, vicious. But he&rsquo;s no gunman. I could have shot him three times runnin&rsquo;.... But Bodkin. I told yu before to lay off him.&rdquo; Inskip interposed here. &ldquo;Right, Bilyen. Unless Brazos has proof&mdash;material proof, or a witness, he&rsquo;d better let Bodkin alone. For he has been elected sheriff by the citizens of this county. He&rsquo;s an officer of law in this territory.&rdquo; &ldquo;It cain&rsquo;t be done,&rdquo; drawled Brazos. &ldquo;Have you anythin&rsquo; on Bodkin thet&rsquo;d clear yu in court?&rdquo; &ldquo;I know him.&rdquo; &ldquo;But your word only is not enough, Brazos,&rdquo; declared Inskip, impressively. &ldquo;Pard, Inskip is talkin&rsquo; sense,&rdquo; added Bilyen, earnestly. &ldquo;Listen, cowboy. If&mdash;if things oot at Twin Sombreros air the way they seemed to Neece an&rsquo; me&mdash;an&rsquo; the way we hoped&mdash;for Gawd&rsquo;s sake, leave Bodkin alone. He&rsquo;ll hang himself pronto.&rdquo; &ldquo;It just cain&rsquo;t be done. I see thet now,&rdquo; replied Brazos, strangely. &ldquo;I am thinkin&rsquo; of June&mdash;an&rsquo; Jan, too,&rdquo; responded Brazos, as he pressed a strong hand upon Bilyen&rsquo;s. And Brazos knew, if Hank did not, that gesture was one of affection and farewell. &ldquo;So long, yu Texans.&rdquo; Brazos strode out leaving Inskip there. He passed the group of men, and went through the store to halt to one side of the open door. He wanted another moment to think before he gave all consciousness to a deadly set of faculties. All in an instant, kneeling there beside his friend, Brazos had received a revealing illumination. By its light he saw the unmistakable, the inevitable part left for him to play. A bitterness, a sadness, and yet an ecstasy waved through his soul as he accepted the gauge. It had all been plotted out beforehand for him. He had had his paradise and he had left it fine and pure, the better for his blunder, his love, his renunciation. The truth had been long hidden. He was Brazos Keene. He never could be anyone else but Brazos Keene. And he strode out that open door Brazos Keene again, cold and ruthless, with all his peculiar gifts magnified. Brazos had in mind an image of this man Brad&mdash;tall, heavy, dark of face with beady black eyes&mdash;black garb&mdash;forceful presence. He was not the first man Brazos had tracked without ever having seen him in the flesh. It was possible for him to be mistaken in a description, but never in a voice. He had heard Brad talk. The sidewalks were deserted. A farm wagon, drawn by big horses, appeared at the end of the street, raising slow clouds of dust. At the opposite end two horsemen rode out of town. The stillness seemed unusually pronounced, oppressive, full of suspense. But Brazos knew his mood&mdash;how it magnified his senses. Halfway between Hall&rsquo;s saloon and the Happy Days there stood an unoccupied adobe structure, one of the old landmarks of Las Animas, yellow and crumbling with age. Brazos took his station there in the doorway, from which he could not readily be seen except from a point almost directly opposite. Answering to Inskip&rsquo;s suggestion he meant to wait there a little while. So Brazos waited with hawk eyes alert, wholly now in the grip of this strange mood that had become a part of him&mdash;of faculties which made him so dangerous. He was conscious of enhanced physical activity, a strain, a gathering of nerve forces, of augmented heartbeats and throbbing pulse, of his tight cool skin. In fact, except for his keen thought and cold resolve, he was a tiger in ambush. He did not have long to wait for the quiet of Las Animas to be broken. There came a movement of vehicles up and down the street, and of boot-thumping, spur-jangling pedestrians along the sidewalk. Two cowboys went by with their awkward gait and they saw Brazos, started to check their talk; and when Brazos made a slight gesture, they hurried on whispering, their heads together. It would not be long from that moment when a wave of expectancy would run along this street. Brazos decided to forestall that, and he was about to start out when a tall man emerged from Hall&rsquo;s. He answered to the description Brazos had in mind as fitting Knight. Three men followed him out of the saloon. They talked. And Brazos detected a nervous excitement in the way they stood and spoke. Then Knight turned his dark face in Brazos&rsquo; direction. One of his comrades accompanied him, a lean man apparently used to the saddle. He was in his shirt sleeves and his vest was open. Knight wore a long black frock coat. It bulged over his right hip. Brazos smiled scornfully at the folly and blind arrogance of a man who packed his gun like that. The lean man took no such chances. They came on. It looked to Brazos as if Knight was on parade for the benefit of Las Animas. The other man showed nothing of such a mien. He would bear watching. As they came on, Brazos made his final estimate of Knight. In another moment, Brazos stepped out to confront them. &ldquo;Howdy, Brad,&rdquo; he drawled. If that name did not belong to this man, it certainly had power to halt him with a stiffening jerk. &ldquo;My name&rsquo;s&mdash;Knight,&rdquo; he rasped out. &ldquo;Aw, hell!&rdquo; ejaculated Brazos in cold derision. The voice was the one he expected. &ldquo;Who are you?&rdquo; demanded the other, suddenly. &ldquo;Wal, if yu don&rsquo;t know now, yu haven&rsquo;t got a long time to get acquainted.&rdquo; The lean man, staring hard at Brazos, said quietly: &ldquo;It&rsquo;s Brazos Keene.&rdquo; &ldquo;Good guess, stranger.... Slope damn pronto or I&rsquo;ll bore yu,&rdquo; returned Brazos, just as quietly. The man wheeled as on a pivot and his boots rang on the hard sidewalk. &ldquo;Wal, Mr. Knight, yu&rsquo;ve met up with Brazos Keene at last.&rdquo; &ldquo;What of that?&rdquo; retorted Knight. &ldquo;I cain&rsquo;t say for yu, but I can guess tolerable wal what of it for me.&rdquo; &ldquo;You&rsquo;re this Texas cowboy I hear so much about?&rdquo; &ldquo;How much do yu heah?&rdquo; &ldquo;I&rsquo;m fed up on it.&rdquo; &ldquo;Ahuh. Wal, yu kinda look like it disagreed with yu. Bad stomach, I reckon.... An&rsquo; yu don&rsquo;t strike me very healthy in yore mind,&rdquo; drawled Brazos, sarcastically. &ldquo;Is that so?&rdquo; snapped Knight, his queer voice whistling. &ldquo;Shore. &rsquo;Cause if yu were very bright yu&rsquo;d savvy what yu&rsquo;re up against.&rdquo; &ldquo;Brazos Keene, ah? Ha! Ha! It doesn&rsquo;t impress me, you bragging cowpuncher.&rdquo; &ldquo;Wal, it&rsquo;s agonna, Brad.&rdquo; &ldquo;Damn you! My name&rsquo;s Knight,&rdquo; burst out the other, fiercely. Brazos saw the leap of thought in those beady black eyes. It was a steely red glint, a compass needle wavering and fixing&mdash;the intent to kill. Brad would attempt to draw on him, Brazos knew, and he felt profound amaze at this man&rsquo;s ignorance of real gunmen. &ldquo;Wal, it&rsquo;s Brad too.&rdquo; &ldquo;Who told you that?&rdquo; &ldquo;Nobody. I just heahed Bodkin an&rsquo; thet other hombre call yu Brad.&rdquo; &ldquo;When and where?&rdquo; queried Brad, heatedly, but he had begun to whiten. &ldquo;Thet night at Hailey&rsquo;s. Just after the midnight train had pulled in from the East. I was in the next room an&rsquo; had a hole cut in the wall.&rdquo; &ldquo;You meddling cow hand!&rdquo; Knight vibrated to that. He blazed with passion. It was fury, not fear, that dominated him. &ldquo;An&rsquo; I&rsquo;m packin&rsquo; a gun, too! Which is what yu knew my Texas pard, Bilyen, wasn&rsquo;t.&rdquo; &ldquo;To hell with you Texans!&rdquo; &ldquo;Ump-umm. Texans don&rsquo;t go to hell for depletin&rsquo; the West of such two-bit hombres as yu.... Brad, yu an&rsquo; Bodkin an&rsquo; Surface&mdash;all yore ilk air nothin&rsquo; but a lot of blood-suckin&rsquo; cattle ticks.&rdquo; Knight appeared to be beyond speech, clamped in his rage, slowly awakening to the inevitableness of something sinister that loomed like a specter. Still he had no fear. But it was rage, not nerve. &ldquo;Why, man alive!&rdquo; went on Brazos, in his cold taunting voice of absolute assurance. &ldquo;I&rsquo;ve met up with some real men in my day. Yu&rsquo;re nothin&rsquo; but a low-down coward that shoots unarmed men....&rdquo; With a grating curse Knight jerked for his gun. * * * * * Brazos stepped through the drifting pall of smoke to look down upon the fallen man. But he was too late to see Brad die. The rustler boss lay on his back, his right arm pinned under him, clutching his half-drawn gun, his visage distorted in its convulsive change from life to death. And at that instant his sombrero, which had rolled on its rim off the sidewalk, tilted and flopped to a standstill. It was not until then that the blood lust in Brazos, the passion to slay, his implacable hatred of these parasites of the cattle range, all of which had been developed by circumstances over which Brazos had no control, leaped out of his controlling restraint to make him terrible. He slipped a fresh cartridge into the one empty chamber of his still smoking gun. A crowd blocked the sidewalk in front of Hall&rsquo;s. Across the street white faces appeared at doors and windows. A noise, like a single expelled breath, arose among the men outside, to augment and swell into voices. It expressed release of suspense, a tragedy enacted. &ldquo;Atta boy, Brazos!&rdquo; yelled a lout at the back, in hoarse venting of his passion, and a laugh, nervous, not mirthful, ran through the crowd. Sheathing his gun Brazos whirled on his heel to stride rapidly in the direction of the sheriff&rsquo;s office. It was locked. The dispenser of Las Animas justice wasted little of his valuable time there. Brazos burst into three places before some one told him where to locate Bodkin. &ldquo;Seen him go in Twin Sombreros restaurant,&rdquo; called out this individual. Brazos laughed. Of all places for Bodkin to be cornered by Brazos Keene! There was a fate that waited upon evil men. Bodkin, on the hour that his ally Knight had tried to murder Bilyen, and himself lay dead in the street, should have been in his office surrounded by his deputies and guns, or in the saloon where he drank and gambled and planned with members of his secret contingent. It boded ill for him that he was entertaining visitors from Denver and business men he desired to impress. Brazos opened the door of the restaurant, slipped in, then slammed it behind him. This eating house of the Neece twins was full of customers. On the right side, facing the street from which Brazos had entered, several of the small tables had been placed together, round which sat ten or a dozen men. Brazos&rsquo; lightning eye had scanned them to locate his victim. His appearance had as much to do with the sudden petrified silence of those present as had his stentorian voice. He surveyed the men at table. Miller he recognized. His passion was such that even the presence of the banker Henderson among them occasioned him no surprise. Several other faces were familiar, evidently belonging to new business men of Las Animas. The rest were strangers. &ldquo;Haw! Haw! Haw!&rdquo; laughed Brazos, lustily, wild as a maniac. But a keen observer would have noted that the cowboy swerved not the slightest hair from his slight crouch&mdash;that his hands were low and the right spread a little from his body. Far removed indeed was Brazos from irrationality. He was a death-dealing machine, as impersonal as a lightning stroke. &ldquo;Haw! Haw! Funny aboot findin&rsquo; yu heah, Bodkin!&rdquo; The guests at that table rose so hurriedly that half their chairs turned over. They split, some on each side, leaving Bodkin alone at the head, his ox eyes rolling at Brazos, his leather visage losing its swarthy line. &ldquo;Keene, this hyar&rsquo;s an intrusion.... Insult to my guests.... I&mdash;&mdash;&rdquo; &ldquo;Haw! Haw!... yore guests, huh? Wal, they must be crooked as yu or the damnedest fools in Colorado.&rdquo; &ldquo;Drunk again! Same old Keene!... You get out or I&rsquo;ll clap you in jail.&rdquo; Brazos spat like a cat. &ldquo;Jail?... By Gawd, yu make me remember I got thet on yu too!... Wal, Bodkin, my rustlin&rsquo; sheriff, yu&rsquo;ll never clap me in jail again... or any other cowboy!&rdquo; Still it did not quite dawn upon Bodkin that he was in for more than abuse at the hands of Brazos Keene. There must have been a very strong conviction locked in his dense mind&mdash;no doubt the gossip that if Brazos was to marry into the Neece family his gun-throwing days were over in Colorado. &ldquo;Get out, Keene. You&rsquo;re drunk an&rsquo; you&rsquo;re blowin&rsquo; off. Why do you pick on me?&rdquo; &ldquo;Wal, I didn&rsquo;t feel full of talk when I busted in heah,&rdquo; drawled Brazos, bitingly. &ldquo;But seein&rsquo; yu all dressed up, throwin&rsquo; all this bluff, I just feel like crowin&rsquo;.&rdquo; &ldquo;Well, you can go out in the pasture an&rsquo; crow,&rdquo; replied Bodkin, angrily. &ldquo;Let me alone. You can&rsquo;t want anythin&rsquo; of me.&rdquo; &ldquo;Hell, I cain&rsquo;t!&rdquo; There ensued a pause of suspense, fraught with the significance of the cowboy&rsquo;s icy voice. &ldquo;What you want&mdash;then?&rdquo; demanded Bodkin, hoarsely. &ldquo;Wal, first off I wanted to tell yu, Bodkin,&rdquo; drawled Brazos, with irritating slowness. He paused. Then he leaned a little more, like an eagle about to strike, to launch words swift as bullets. &ldquo;Yore pard Brad is layin&rsquo; oot there in the street daid!&rdquo; &ldquo;Brad?&rdquo; Bodkin choked out the name. &ldquo;Yes. Brad. He calls himself Knight. He&rsquo;s yore new man. I savvy thet empty chair I seen heah next yu was for him. Wal, he couldn&rsquo;t set in yore little game.... He&rsquo;s daid!&rdquo; &ldquo;Who shot him?&rdquo; &ldquo;Some hombre from Texas.&rdquo; &ldquo;You!&rdquo; &ldquo;Bodkin, yu&rsquo;re so good a guesser maybe yu can guess some more.&rdquo; &ldquo;Well, that&rsquo;s no great concern of mine,&rdquo; returned Bodkin, harshly, &ldquo;You&rsquo;re one of these even-break gunmen, so I can&rsquo;t arrest you. I knew him as Knight. Now get out&mdash;&mdash;&rdquo; &ldquo;Aw, Bodkin, yu&rsquo;re all lie,&rdquo; flung out Brazos, and in two long strides he reached the table. He lifted his boot against it and shoved powerfully. The laden tables slid and tumbled with a crash, overturning Bodkin and half covering his burly form. &ldquo;Come up with yore gun!&rdquo; ordered Brazos. Bodkin floundered to his feet and would have made a ludicrous figure but for a stark and ghastly terror that was etched on his face. He made no move for his gun, which swung free without coat to hamper it. &ldquo;I&rsquo;m not fightin&rsquo; you&mdash;gun slinger,&rdquo; he panted. &ldquo;Yes, yu air&mdash;or be the first man I ever bored withoot it.&rdquo; &ldquo;Let me by. If you&rsquo;re spoilin&rsquo; for a fight I&rsquo;ll find men&mdash;&mdash;&rdquo; &ldquo;Bah, yu chicken-hearted greaser! Cain&rsquo;t yu make no better stand before yore guests? Cain&rsquo;t yu die game?&rdquo; &ldquo;Brazos Keene, I&rsquo;ll not add another notch to your gun handle.&rdquo; &ldquo;Wal, I&rsquo;ll break my rule an&rsquo; cut just one notch for yu, Bodkin. An&rsquo; wherever I ride I&rsquo;ll show it an&rsquo; say thet&rsquo;s for the yellowest, dirtiest skunk I ever shot.&rdquo; &ldquo;I tell you I won&rsquo;t draw,&rdquo; shouted Bodkin, desperate in his fear. &ldquo;Air yu gonna take it by inches?&rdquo; demanded the cowboy. Bodkin gazed balefully, with wobbling jaw. Horribly plain his love of life, his fear of death! And still it eluded him&mdash;the destroying truth of this cowboy. &ldquo;You&rsquo;re the crazy&mdash;one,&rdquo; gasped Bodkin. &ldquo;Listen man. Cain&rsquo;t yu see things? I could kill yu on a personal grudge. But I&rsquo;m gonna kill yu for better reasons.&rdquo; &ldquo;Keene, you can&rsquo;t prove.... You have no case....&rdquo; Damning guilt worked upon the lessening fear and agony in Bodkin&rsquo;s visage. &ldquo;Now will yu go for yore gun?&rdquo; added Brazos, sardonically. &ldquo;No&mdash;you&mdash;hydrophobia-bitten cow hand!&rdquo; Then the cowboy faced the ill-assorted group of men who had assembled there as Bodkin&rsquo;s guests. There was not the sign of a movement among them. They stood as if petrified. &ldquo;Henderson, yu&rsquo;re in bad company,&rdquo; rang out Brazos, &ldquo;an&rsquo; no matter what yore excuse, it&rsquo;ll be remembered in Las Animas.... Miller, I&rsquo;m brandin&rsquo; yu as hand an&rsquo; glove with this Surface ootfit.... Yu business men an&rsquo; yu strangers all know Bodkin now for what he was.... An&rsquo; I reckon thet&rsquo;ll be aboot all for Brazos Keene in Colorado.&rdquo; His heading for the south, however, with the lean eager nose of his horse Bay turned toward Texas, had an air of finality. Thirst for adventure and even for romance, had been effectually killed. As Brazos took to the well-worn cattle trail, he felt sick and old and unhappy. He reflected that he would recover from the former, which was mostly revulsion at the shedding of blood, but he doubted that he would ever be young or happy again. For long days he rode alone, shunning the cow camps, going around several little towns, camping at water holes and living upon the food he had packed. He made a gloomy and strange traveler. The gray miles filed past and every one of them seemed a dropped fetter. And in due course the bitter dregs of his last killing orgy wore out of his stomach. But the sense of loss still pressed heavily upon him. He forgot that it had been wise for him to ride away from the scene that had outlawed him, however unjust the law was, and despite the multitude of friends he had left behind. Brazos knew his despair had to do with a broken heart. That susceptible member had been broken often before, and the time he had ridden away from Holly Ripple he thought it could never be mended. Nevertheless he had been mistaken. Despite the stress of his emotions, however, Brazos could not prevent the influence of the open and the solitude. There was something healing in the waving grass, the swales and green flats, the winding tree-bordered river bottoms, and the distant uplands. Autumn, his favorite season, met him with its golden and gorgeous Indian summer. The dry fragrant breeze soft in his face, the poise of hawk and bound of jack rabbit, and the slipping away into the willows of deer, the gradual drawing from sight of cattle herds, the warming color in the distance, the smell of dust that at times rose from the clip-clop of Bay&rsquo;s tireless hoofs, the cottonwood grove where he had once camped, the great sycamore tree on the bank of the Purgatory where he had helped to hang a horse thief, the subtle essence of the lovely land which had been so much in his life&mdash;these wore upon him more and more until his old philosophy got hold of him again, and reason made clear the change in his life, and that with his marvelous memory he could not be anything else but appreciative even with his broken heart. The day came when Brazos&rsquo; trail led to the sleepy village of Hooker, where he purchased a blanket and as much food supply as Bay could carry. Once over the line into the Panhandle, towns would be few and far between. When he rode across the Texas line it seemed as definite a break as riding out of Las Animas. He was in his beloved Texas land. Yet how he had loved Colorado! Thereafter travel became leisurely. There was now no need of hurry, if there had ever been, and he could think of Bay and be easy on the great horse. When one late afternoon Brazos rode down toward a gold and scarlet creek bottom, he experienced a singular revivifying sensation. All that Texas range beyond he knew as well as if it had been a treasured book. Camp that night was one of divided memories&mdash;the old trail-driving days vied with the poignant experience he had left behind in Colorado. He sat up late before his ruddy little campfire, remembering, dreaming, yet unable to banish the thought of June and Janis. It would not be long now, as days went, until he arrived at Camp Supply, on the Chisholm Trail. After that there would not be much more lonely traveling for him. He would meet cattle trains coming north across Texas on their way to Abilene and Dodge. And he would meet trail drivers riding south. He would find company. At the posts he would be known. The deep wide rut in the prairie that was the old Chisholm Trail, which he had ridden so often with cowboys as wild as himself, led on and on down through the gray vastness of Texas. There was a comradeship in the old trail. One day was like another. He continued to make wide detours around settlements and wagon trains and herds of cattle. Brazos came at length to Doan&rsquo;s Crossing. He was amazed to find himself so far down in Texas, arriving at one of the famous old posts of the frontier. Hungry and travel-stained, with Bay gone lame and needing rest, Brazos was forced to a halt. &ldquo;Jest as wal,&rdquo; he soliloquized. &ldquo;Reckon I cain&rsquo;t be a lone wolf forever. I gotta live. A little more of this lone prairie will make me dotty.&rdquo; Turning away from the great trail at the crossing, Brazos rode toward the post. It stood back from the river and appeared different. Then he saw that Doan&rsquo;s Crossing had grown to be a settlement. The huge rambling trading post, its adobe walls red in the westering sun, appeared the same as the picture in his memory. But it fronted on the corner of a wide street that stretched far between gray flat houses and red-walled buildings. Back from this typical Western street, full of dust and vehicles and houses, scattered cottages and shacks and tents grew up to the rise of the gray prairie. &ldquo;Wal, doggone me!&rdquo; ejaculated Brazos, mildly. &ldquo;Tom Doan has shore thrown up a metropolis.&rdquo; If there were a trail herd in from the south it would account for the saddle horses standing bridles down, and the lounging riders, and the sloe-eyed swarthy Indians, but hardly for the life and bustle of that street. As Brazos slid wearily out of his saddle a lanky young Texan met him with a keen gaze. &ldquo;Howdy, rider. Air you stayin&rsquo; over?&rdquo; &ldquo;Howdy, young feller. I reckon. My hawse is lame. Will you put him up and look after him?&rdquo; &ldquo;Yu bet,&rdquo; replied the lad, taking the bridle. &ldquo;Tom Doan heah yet?&rdquo; &ldquo;Shore, Tom&rsquo;s heah, big as life. Yu been heah before?&rdquo; &ldquo;Say, Tex, this old dobe post wouldn&rsquo;t be heah now but for me,&rdquo; drawled Brazos, as he untied his coat and saddle bags. &ldquo;Wal, you don&rsquo;t say?&rdquo; queried the lad, his keen blue eyes taking Brazos in with Texas perspicuity. &ldquo;Mister, there&rsquo;s Doan comin&rsquo; oot.&rdquo; Brazos&rsquo; glance lighted upon a tall Texan approaching. Same old Tom Doan! Brazos could have picked him out of a hundred Texans, though they all were sandy-haired, sallow-faced, with slits of gray fire for eyes. Brazos was used to scrutiny and he met it here in full measure. It gave him the first quick beat of pulse for days. He was home in Texas all right. &ldquo;Howdy, stranger. Git down an&rsquo; come in,&rdquo; was the greeting. &ldquo;Hevn&rsquo;t I seen you before?&rdquo; &ldquo;Tom, I reckon I&rsquo;m starved and thin and black with this heah dust and beard. But it&rsquo;s a downright insult for you not to know me,&rdquo; drawled Brazos. Doan straightened up from his close scrutiny. His still lined visage broke into a broad smile. &ldquo;Wal, talk of the devil an&rsquo; heah he is! Brazos Keene!&rdquo; &ldquo;Yep, it&rsquo;s Keene all right, only not the boy you knowed. How air you, Tom?&rdquo; The warm smile, the glad flash of Texas eyes, the hard grip, and the hand on his shoulder thrilled some of the cold weariness out of Brazos. &ldquo;I reckon I&rsquo;m downright glad to see yu, Tom,&rdquo; he responded, hoarsely. &ldquo;Say, yu&rsquo;re spittin&rsquo; cotton. Come in, boy, an&rsquo; hev a drink.&rdquo; &ldquo;Wal, I need one, Tom. But not red likker.&rdquo; Doan led Brazos through a lane of curious riders, into the post. The huge interior, its adobe walls decorated with Indian designs and ornaments, the colored blankets and utensils hanging from the rafters, the counters laden with merchandise, and the shelves packed with a miscellaneous collection of stores, and especially the great open fireplace at the end&mdash;all these appeared just the same as if he had seen them yesterday. But there was a wide door that Brazos did not remember. It led into a saloon full of smoke and noise. Dusty-booted and shirt-sleeved Westerners stood at a long bar; Indians in buckskin lounged back along the wall; gamblers sat intent at their tables. &ldquo;Tom, what the hell has come off about heah?&rdquo; asked Brazos, after he had quenched his thirst. &ldquo;Brazos, we&rsquo;ve growed up. Doan&rsquo;s Crossing is a town,&rdquo; replied the host, proudly. &ldquo;Hell, Tom, I ain&rsquo;t blind. But how come? There never was nothin&rsquo; heah. Wal, nothin&rsquo; but buffalo, Injuns, and trail herd rustlers.&rdquo; Doan laughed. &ldquo;So we used to think, cowboy. But we was blind. There&rsquo;s rich land heah. Lots of farms. Plenty ranches. Fine grass an&rsquo; water. We&rsquo;ve got a growin&rsquo; town. A dozen stores an&rsquo; more, too many saloons, a school an&rsquo; a church an&rsquo; a doctor. I&rsquo;ve added a hotel to my post. Turnin&rsquo; &rsquo;em away some days. Two stages a week, herds still trailin&rsquo; north, travel heavy. Aw, Doan Crossin&rsquo; is boomin&rsquo;.&rdquo; &ldquo;Wal, doggone! I&rsquo;m shore glad, Tom. But who ever would have reckoned on it?&rdquo; &ldquo;I did for one, Brazos.... Where yu headin&rsquo;?&rdquo; &ldquo;West of the Pecos,&rdquo; replied Keene, ponderingly, his gaze averted. &ldquo;Aw! Don&rsquo;t tell me yu are on the dodge, Brazos?&rdquo; &ldquo;Not atall. I reckon I did get in bad up Colorado way. But thet county I cleaned up will be so glad about it they&rsquo;d fire any sheriff who put oot strings for me.&rdquo; &ldquo;Ah-huh. Wal, I hain&rsquo;t heerd nothin&rsquo; an&rsquo; I ain&rsquo;t askin&rsquo;.&rdquo; &ldquo;Good.... Tom, I want a room and hot water. Last time I was heah I slept on the counter oot there. Recollect thet?&rdquo; &ldquo;I shore do. An&rsquo; you didn&rsquo;t need no bath, &rsquo;cause yu an&rsquo; Herb Ellerslie got piled off in the river.&rdquo; &ldquo;Gosh! Tom, yu do remember heaps. Did I by any chance owe you some money?&rdquo; &ldquo;Nope. Anyway thet&rsquo;s too long ago to remember.&rdquo; &ldquo;Tom, yu&rsquo;re a liar. What become of Herb Ellerslie?&rdquo; &ldquo;Shot, Brazos. Shot at Dodge by a gambler named Cardigan.&rdquo; &ldquo;Aw, no!... I&rsquo;m sorry. Herb and I were pretty thick on the trail.... Cardigan?... I&rsquo;ll remember thet name.... How aboot Wess Tanner?&rdquo; &ldquo;Jest fine. Wess drove through&mdash;let&rsquo;s see&mdash;along in August. He raved aboot one of them electric storms. Come to think of it Wess will be along any day now.&rdquo; &ldquo;Wouldn&rsquo;t I like to see Wess!&rdquo; ejaculated Brazos, dreamily, following his host out of the saloon into a long corridor. The whitewashed walls were colorful with Indian blankets, as was also the earthen floor. There were windows on one side and doors on the other. Doan halted at the end of the corridor, which apparently opened into a green and flowery patio. Brazos heard the tinkle of running water. He was ushered into a room that spoke eloquently of the advance Doan&rsquo;s Crossing had made toward civilization. &ldquo;Doggone!... Tom, this heah is mighty stylish for me. Wonder if I can sleep in thet bed.&rdquo; &ldquo;Wal, you look like you needed to,&rdquo; replied Doan, with a laugh. &ldquo;I&rsquo;ll send some hot water. You got about a half hour before supper.&rdquo; Brazos laid off his sombrero, his gun, spurs and chaps. Then he opened his saddle bags to take out his last clean shirt, scarf and socks, and also his shaving outfit. &ldquo;Heigho!&rdquo; he sighed, and sat down on the bed. &ldquo;Doan&rsquo;s Crossing.... Jesse Chisholm&rsquo;s Trail.... And I&rsquo;m a broken old man!&rdquo; A Mexican lad brought a bucket of hot water and towels. Then Brazos indulged in the luxury of a bath, a shave and some clean clothes. He was scrutinizing his lean brown face in the mirror, shaking his head dubiously, when the supper bell rang. Brazos did not forget to strap on his gun belt. Then he went out. He had to be directed to the dining room, and found a dozen or more men ahead of him. Most of them were merry riders. Finding an empty seat Brazos stepped across the bench and sat down. The fellow next to him on the right was friendly, though not curious. Still Brazos found himself the cynosure of all eyes. That Doan had mentioned his name seemed evident. A middle-aged man, surely a rancher, sitting at Brazos&rsquo; left, made himself agreeable. But presently Brazos discovered that he was a starved wolf, and that the meal was sumptuous. He ate until he felt ashamed of himself, and was the last to leave the table. After supper he went outdoors to walk a little. In the cool darkness Indians had a fire burning. It was pleasant for Brazos to watch and listen. The night wind was cold. He heard coyotes out on the prairie. Presently Brazos went into the post, to lounge around in the background listening to the gossip of the trail that had once been his world. He shunned the saloon with its bright lights and gay riders. Then he strolled down the wide street, along the board walk, from one end to the other, finding it like any other promenade of a frontier town. The brightly lighted stores, the passers-by, a few of whom were giggling girls who made shy eyes at him, the rattle of a roulette wheel and the metallic clink of silver coins, the sound of music and gay song, loud voices, the bang of a gun, and then silence&mdash;all these things left Brazos uninterested and he returned to his room with the pang in his breast more leaden and heavy than when he had sat around his lonely campfires. He went to bed. But tired as he was, he could not sleep. The bed felt too soft, too comfortable. He lay awake, thinking. It was as dark as pitch in his room. Only a hum of noises penetrated the thick walls. And June and Jan Neece filled his mind. Strangely, though, of all the nights he had been riding away, this one began to assume different and tremendous importance. Perhaps contact with men again had dispelled the dream, the fantasy, the unreality. Something had clarified his clouded mind. It seemed that through all these days and nights he had been nearing the truth of his problem without being conscious of it. Jan and June Neece no longer existed in his consciousness as a single entity&mdash;one girl. He had separated them into the twins once again, as they had been at first. In the dead of night in the blackness of his room at Doan&rsquo;s post, hundreds of miles from the scene of his downfall, he at last saw clearly. All the time, it had been June, and June alone. He had worshiped her, and worshiped her still. At the very onset both girls had won his sympathy, his championship. But it had been June who had uplifted and inspired him, called so deeply and poignantly to the finer side of him, that he had never known really existed. He had thought of June as a girl to work for, to change his nature, to make a home for him and be the mother of his children. All dream! but he saw through it clearly now. Jan, the devil, the sweet counterpart of her twin sister, the natural coquette, had stimulated all the physical and the wild in Brazos. He had loved her, too. It was impossible not to. Yet he saw it now as singularly different. If it had been possible, failing to get June, he would have chosen Jan for a wife, realizing that instead of peace he would have found turmoil. The truth seemed to be a relief. He was not so ashamed as he had been. What chance had he had against those lovely girls, both in their way equally devastating? Not one man in a hundred, not one cowboy in a thousand, similarly situated, could have resisted the appalling temptation June Neece had visited upon him. Brazos shook in his bed. That old wave of fire passed through every fiber of his being. He feared that he would feel that until he was old, perhaps to his grave. And yet he would not have had it otherwise. There had been something great in his downfall. Yet what folly to call it downfall or ruin! Those twins had been his salvation. He had to be worthy of what they had made him. Yet all this did not make clear to Brazos the subtleties of the present. For days he had traveled more and more slowly. It seemed the reverse of that old step on his trail. Doan&rsquo;s Crossing marked some kind of a dividing line for him. Had his subconscious mind been harboring a hidden thought for him to take the stage north? This query shook Brazos anew. How was he to know what he might do? But that was unthinkable. Still there was something that haunted and mocked him&mdash;something which seemed to hold aloof inexplicably, to damn him with vague conviction that he had no control over his wretched destiny. Sleep came very late to Brazos that night. He was awakened from a dead slumber of exhaustion by a pounding on his door. He sat up rubbing his eyes. &ldquo;Hey, Mister Keene, air yu daid?&rdquo; called a voice Brazos recognized as belonging to the Texas lad. &ldquo;Mawnin&rsquo;, Tex. No I ain&rsquo;t daid yet. What&rsquo;s the row about?&rdquo; &ldquo;I been tryin&rsquo; to wake you.&rdquo; &ldquo;Say, Kid, don&rsquo;t tell me you called me more&rsquo;n once.&rdquo; &ldquo;I have, though.&rdquo; &ldquo;What time of day is it?&rdquo; &ldquo;Long after midday. I reckon two o&rsquo;clock.&rdquo; &ldquo;Wal, what&rsquo;s the idee? Injuns or fire?&rdquo; &ldquo;Hell you say! Look heah, Tex, I&rsquo;m liable to bounce somethin&rsquo; off yore haid. What you mean&mdash;wuss?&rdquo; &ldquo;I&mdash;yu... fact is, Brazos, the Dodge City stage rolled in&mdash;with... an&rsquo;&mdash;some old friends of yores rode in with it.&rdquo; The lad&rsquo;s voice betrayed excitement if not confusion. &ldquo;Friends?&rdquo; flashed Brazos, his slow blood quickening. &ldquo;Tanner an&rsquo; some of his riders. I heerd Doan tell him to come wake yu up. An&rsquo; Wess said: &lsquo;Me wake Brazos Keene? Mebbe out on the trail, but not heah. Ump-umm!&rsquo;... They was afraid to wake yu, Brazos. An&rsquo; I wasn&rsquo;t so damn happy about it, nuther. Yu see....&rdquo; &ldquo;What&rsquo;n hell you all think&mdash;thet I ain&rsquo;t human?&rdquo; yelled Brazos, as he leaped out of bed. &ldquo;Tell Wess I&rsquo;ll be there in a jiffy. Rustle, you towhaid.&rdquo; Brazos laughed at the quick footfalls of the lad fading down the corridor. He washed and dressed as swiftly as if this summons had been a trail call. Warm thoughts attended his actions. He wagered that he would be as glad to see Wess Tanner as Wess.... Afraid to wake him? That was a good one. The damned old maverick hunter. Brazos belted on his gun belt and strode out into the corridor. He halted at the door of the saloon. There were a dozen or more men at the bar, all strange to see, facing the corridor entrance. All unknown to Brazos, he noted at a glance; but that keen glance registered also that they knew him and looked uneasy. Brazos swore under his breath and strode on into the trading post. A hubbub of voices stilled as he entered. That also stilled Brazos&rsquo; pleasant sensations. What was amiss? Even if he was Brazos Keene.... Indians and riders grouped around the wide door. Outside there appeared to be a crowd. At the far and lighter end of the big post Doan appeared to have stopped waiting on a customer. Brazos saw him jerk a thumb toward the entrance, and following that cue Brazos espied half a dozen riders, standing a little to the left of a tall blond fellow, lean and intent of face, with eyes like daggers. Brazos recognized Wess Tanner and saw him suddenly jerk as if frozen blood had sustained a hot release. One other of the riders Brazos recognized in that quick glance. They all appeared under a restraint. It irritated Brazos. Why should friends act like strangers when he stalked in? That was one bitter price Brazos Keene had to pay. But he shook it off and advanced. When he reached Tanner only genuine gladness possessed him. They clasped hands and locked glances. It was a meeting between tried and true Texans who had slept and fought and toiled together through unforgettable days. Whatever had made Tanner strange and backward vanished the instant Brazos faced him. &ldquo;Brazos&mdash;meet my ootfit,&rdquo; said Tanner, presently. &ldquo;Yu ought to remember Sam heah.&rdquo; &ldquo;Sam Jenkins. I shore do. Howdy, Sam,&rdquo; replied Brazos, offering his hand to the sleek dark Jenkins. &ldquo;It shore is good to meet you again. Can you palm aces like you used to and sing the herd quiet?&rdquo; &ldquo;Howdy&mdash;Brazos,&rdquo; replied Jenkins, warming brightly. &ldquo;Darn glad to see you.... Shore I can do them same old tricks.&rdquo; Brazos was introduced to the other riders, most of them striplings such as he had been when he first started herd driving. Obviously they were overcome at this meeting, and though pleased and excited, they did not succeed in throwing off the restraint. &ldquo;Wal, Wess, I reckon you&rsquo;re ridin&rsquo; back to Santone for the winter. No more trail drivin&rsquo; this year?&rdquo; &ldquo;Not till spring, Brazos. An&rsquo; mebbe not then. Pard, I shore have the grandest ranch bargain there is in all Texas. If I can only raise the backin&rsquo;!&rdquo; &ldquo;Same old Wess. Always dreamin&rsquo; of thet grand ranch. I shore want to heah about it. Reckon you&rsquo;ll lay over heah today?&rdquo; &ldquo;We ain&rsquo;t in any hurry, Brazos.&rdquo; &ldquo;I&rsquo;d kinda like to ride south with you, for a while. It&rsquo;s been lonely.&rdquo; Tanner gave him a keen kindly glance that baffled Brazos. He expected a warm response to his suggestion that he accompany Tanner and his boys down the Old Trail, and as that was not instantly forthcoming Brazos sustained a little shock of surprise and disappointment. &ldquo;Don&rsquo;t be hurt, pard. It ain&rsquo;t likely you&rsquo;ll want to ride with us. But I&rsquo;d shore like thet.... Brazos, come aside over heah. I&rsquo;ve news for yu.... I&rsquo;m scared stiff, yet&mdash;&mdash;&rdquo; Wess led Brazos to a corner beside a window and faced him there hopefully yet apprehensively, with a pale face full of suppressed agitation that nonplussed Brazos and gave rise again to his former irritation. &ldquo;Scared stiff, you?&rdquo; he queried darkly, almost bitterly. &ldquo;Wess, I know I&rsquo;m an ootlaw&mdash;an&rsquo; unfairly, by Gawd&mdash;but you, my old pard who wouldn&rsquo;t have thet blond scalp but for me&mdash;&mdash;&rdquo; &ldquo;Shet up, you fire-eater,&rdquo; interposed Wess. &ldquo;I couldn&rsquo;t be no more ashamed of yu, Brazos Keene, than of my own brother.&rdquo; &ldquo;Sorry, old-timer. Reckon I&rsquo;m kinda testy.... What&rsquo;s on yore mind?&rdquo; Manifestly Wess labored under some stress that rendered liberation extremely difficult. He lit a cigarette with visibly unsteady fingers and he swallowed a lump in his throat. But the paleness under his tan had begun to tinge with red. &ldquo;Hell, man!&rdquo; exploded Brazos. &ldquo;You didn&rsquo;t use to be so damn squeamish.... You&rsquo;ve heahed about thet little Las Animas mess.&rdquo; &ldquo;Shore, Brazos&mdash;shore,&rdquo; agreed Wess, hurriedly. &ldquo;Only it didn&rsquo;t seem little to me. Fact is&mdash;it was big&mdash;big as Texas.&rdquo; &ldquo;Yeah? An&rsquo; what of it?&rdquo; rasped Brazos. &ldquo;Wal, for one thing Dodge City took it fine. Wild Bill hisself said to me: &lsquo;Wess, thet&rsquo;s the sheriff for Dodge when I get mine!&rsquo;&rdquo; &ldquo;Hell he did? Wild Bill Hickock!... Kind of a compliment at thet.&rdquo; &ldquo;Mebbe you shouldn&rsquo;t have rode away from Las Animas so quick.&rdquo; &ldquo;I reckon you think I should have got up a party and swelled around town,&rdquo; said Brazos, sarcastically. &ldquo;Nope, not jest thet, though the deal shore called for some red-eye. Where&rsquo;d yu stop an&rsquo; soak up a load of likker?&rdquo; &ldquo;Wess, I haven&rsquo;t taken one dod-blasted drink,&rdquo; declared Brazos. &ldquo;Thet settles it. Yu air crazy. I been afraid of it ever since&mdash;since&mdash;&mdash;&rdquo; &ldquo;Since what, you tongue-tied hombre? I&rsquo;m gonna get sore pretty pronto.&rdquo; &ldquo;Brazos, for the life of me I cain&rsquo;t see why. If I was in yore boots I&rsquo;d be so dod-blasted happy&mdash;&mdash;&rdquo; &ldquo;You been afraid since what? Since what?&rdquo; flashed Brazos, grasping Wess&rsquo;s wrist with fingers of steel. There was something wrong about this old friend&mdash;something that had to be solved. &ldquo;Wal, then, old pard&mdash;since&mdash;since Miss Neece braced me on the street in Dodge.&rdquo; &ldquo;Shore. Yore fiancée,&rdquo; replied Wess. &ldquo;Pard, if I&rsquo;d never heerd of yu I&rsquo;d have been stricken by sight of thet lovely gurl.&rdquo; &ldquo;My&mdash;my fiancée?... How&rsquo;d you know&mdash;thet?&rdquo; &ldquo;She told me.&rdquo; &ldquo;Good Gawd!... Wess, wasn&rsquo;t she ashamed of thet?&rdquo; &ldquo;Ha! Ha! I should smile she wasn&rsquo;t.&rdquo; &ldquo;Aw!... But what for?... How come? Was she visitin&rsquo; Dodge with her Dad or friends... and heahed you might know me?&rdquo; &ldquo;No, she shore wasn&rsquo;t visitin&rsquo; an&rsquo; as for her Dad.... Wal, never mind about him.... Miss Neece was hot on yore trail, Brazos.&rdquo; At that Brazos began to shake. What was this? His mind began to whirl. &ldquo;Hot on my&mdash;trail?&rdquo; he echoed, in a whisper. &ldquo;I said hot, pardner.... It was this way. I happened to run into the Hotel Dodge to see Jeff&mdash;you cain&rsquo;t have forgot Jeff Davis? He shore hadn&rsquo;t forgot you. Wal, before I could say howdy even, Jeff grabbed me an&rsquo; turned to a gurl standin&rsquo; there. I went stiff at sight of her. &lsquo;What luck!&rsquo; burst out Jeff. &lsquo;Heah he is now.... Wess, this is Miss Neece. She has been askin&rsquo; if any Texas trail driver might know Brazos Keene. An&rsquo; I told her yu&mdash;Wess Tanner&mdash;was an old pardner of his.&rsquo;... The gurl&rsquo;s white face went red, then paled again. &lsquo;Please come,&rsquo; she said, and led me off out of the lobby into the parlor. &ldquo;&lsquo;Yu know Brazos?&rsquo; she asked, and she was trembling. &ldquo;&lsquo;Wal, I used to, Miss,&rsquo; I said. &ldquo;&lsquo;Yu&rsquo;ve heerd about me?&rsquo; &ldquo;&lsquo;No. Sorry to confess I haven&rsquo;t,&rsquo; I had to tell her. &ldquo;&lsquo;Yes, Miss. Thet&rsquo;s town talk heah. But I never believe range gossip, much less about Brazos Keene.&rsquo; &ldquo;&lsquo;Oh! But it is all true... and I am his&mdash;fiancée.&rsquo; &ldquo;Right you were&mdash;Wess,&rdquo; mumbled Brazos, thickly, fighting the wave on wave of emotion that swayed him. How terrible and sweet this news! &ldquo;I&rsquo;ll shoot&mdash;yore laig off. Damn you! Wasn&rsquo;t I miserable enough?... But tell the rest now, if there is any.&rdquo; &ldquo;There&rsquo;s plenty, pard. Let&rsquo;s have a drink first.&rdquo; &ldquo;No! Plenty?... Wess, I cain&rsquo;t stand much more.... But the idee? What was her crazy idee&mdash;coaxin&rsquo; you to find me?&rdquo; &ldquo;What do you think, old pard?&rdquo; queried Wess, drawing a deep breath. &ldquo;Think? I cain&rsquo;t think. Only thet it was sweet of June.... Tell me, or I&rsquo;ll choke it out of you.&rdquo; Wess clapped a heavy hand on Brazos&rsquo; shoulder. &ldquo;Pard, Miss Neece&rsquo;s idee was to come with me&mdash;till I found you,&rdquo; replied Wess, his voice ringing. &ldquo;Come with&mdash;you?... Heavens above!&rdquo; &ldquo;Thet was her idee, Brazos. An&rsquo; she did come.&rdquo; Brazos could only stare in fearful stupefaction into the pale face of his friend. &ldquo;She&rsquo;s heah!&rdquo; rang out Wess. Brazos went blind. His shaking hand groped for Wess, who met it with his own and steadied him. &ldquo;Right now, pard. She&rsquo;s in with Mrs. Doan.&rdquo; &ldquo;Right now! She&rsquo;s heah!&rdquo; echoed Brazos, huskily. His legs went unsteady under him. He had to hold on to Wess. These were his physical reactions, which possessed him momentarily. Then, his mind released, wonder and joy followed, to lift him to seventh heaven. &ldquo;Brazos! For the good Lord&rsquo;s sake!&rdquo; Wess was saying in faraway voice, as he shook Brazos. &ldquo;What ails yu? Man, yu should be the happiest man in all Texas. Why, I never seen yu like this. An&rsquo; how many gurls have I seen yu crazy over? Shore, pard, this is different. This gurl is the real an&rsquo; the last one.... But... hell&rsquo;s fire, Brazos, yu haven&rsquo;t held up a bank or stole a hawse? Somethin&rsquo; bad to clinch thet outlaw name?&rdquo; &ldquo;Which&mdash;one?&rdquo; whispered Brazos, his eyes closed tight. Wess&rsquo;s piercing whisper penetrated Brazos&rsquo; tortured consciousness. The stupefying shock passed, if not its wake of emotion. Brazos let go of Wess and turned to the window. Gradually his dim sight cleared. Outside he saw Indians and horses, a sweep of gray slope leading up to the horizon. He found himself. What was this that had happened? The nameless thing he had felt had held him here at Doan&rsquo;s Crossing. For what? Retribution had caught up with him. One of the Neece twins had followed him. He had imagined it was June&mdash;the good and quiet&mdash;the sweet and noble girl whom he had worshiped. But June could never have undertaken this wild chase. She would never have deserted her father for an outlaw. It was Jan. It was Jan&mdash;that passionate little devil who at last had given rein to the wildness in her. &ldquo;All right, Wess,&rdquo; declared Brazos, finally, turning to his friend. &ldquo;What&rsquo;s the rest?&rdquo; &ldquo;Wal! Thet&rsquo;s more like my old pard,&rdquo; replied Wess greatly relieved. &ldquo;There ain&rsquo;t so much more. Yore gurl had a lot of baggage, Brazos. Easy to see she wasn&rsquo;t goin&rsquo; back home. Ha! Ha!... Wal, we loaded it on the stage. An&rsquo; we rode with thet stage all the way from Dodge. There was several other passengers off an&rsquo; on. They an&rsquo; my boys, an&rsquo; me too, fell turrible hard for yore sweetheart. I reckon the stage come purt&rsquo; near bein&rsquo; held up once by road agents. At least Bill Hempstead, our driver, said he knowed thet outfit. But we was too many. Afterward Miss Neece confessed she had a lot of money with her an&rsquo; thet it was great luck for her thet we happened to be her bodyguard.... I reckon thet&rsquo;s about all. Yu bet I never enjoyed the Old Trail like I did this time.&rdquo; &ldquo;Lot of baggage and money!&rdquo; exclaimed Brazos, bewildered again. &ldquo;Wess, tell me this is a nightmare.&rdquo; &ldquo;Nightmare, my eye! Look at thet&mdash;where yu bruised my wrist, squeezin&rsquo; it so hard.&rdquo; &ldquo;I&rsquo;m sorry, pard. Thanks for everythin&rsquo;. I reckon I won&rsquo;t shoot yore laig off.&rdquo; &ldquo;Wal, I&rsquo;m tolerable glad about thet. Brazos, I got a gurl myself, an&rsquo; when yu&rsquo;ve time yu must heah about her.&rdquo; &ldquo;Aw, thet&rsquo;s fine, Wess. I&rsquo;ll be glad to heah.&rdquo; &ldquo;Brazos, there&rsquo;s Mrs. Doan,&rdquo; went on Wess, quickly. &ldquo;She&rsquo;s lookin&rsquo; for yu, I&rsquo;ll bet. Come, pard, yu better get it over. I cain&rsquo;t help sayin&rsquo; I wouldn&rsquo;t mind bein&rsquo; in yore boots.&rdquo; Doan introduced Brazos to his wife, a comely sturdy pioneer type, blonde and buxom. She certainly gave Brazos a looking over before she relaxed into friendliness and sympathy. &ldquo;I think you had better see your fiancée at once. She is under a strain. I hope&mdash;nothing&rsquo;s wrong. She is sweet and she must care greatly for you.&rdquo; &ldquo;Cowboy, I seen thet an&rsquo; I had it figured when she stepped off the stage. Such eyes! Black an&rsquo; hungry as a starved Indian&rsquo;s!&rdquo; added Doan, with his hearty smile. &ldquo;Wal, friends, she must think a lot of me,&rdquo; replied Brazos, gravely. &ldquo;It&rsquo;s too late now for me to worry about not bein&rsquo; good enough for her an&rsquo; ridin&rsquo; away like I did.... Take me to her.&rdquo; &ldquo;Wal, cowboy,&rdquo; interposed Doan, impressively, &ldquo;take it from me. A Texan like you is worth any two Yankee gurls thet ever was!&rdquo; Two girls! Brazos suffered a piercing stab. His quick flash of eyes assured him that the frontiersman was just bragging in the simplicity of his loyalty and pride. She led him to a door at the south end of the post. &ldquo;This is my room, Brazos. You&rsquo;ll be secluded there. Make it up to her. Try to realize your great good fortune.&rdquo; In the moment before he stepped into the room, Brazos faced his ultimatum. It was June he loved most and wanted for his wife, but it could never have been June who had the adventurous spirit to follow him. So Jan must never know. And love her he did, too, but not as he did June. In all humbleness, he told himself that he was lucky to have either of the twins give up everything to come to him. Brazos was tense and tingling when he opened the door. The room appeared large and bright. Sunlight streamed in the several windows, to give the furnishing a touch of warmth and vividness.
